HEADLINE: Weicker Honeymoon Over as Governor's Race Heats Up

BYLINE: By NICK RAVO

DATELINE: HARTFORD, Aug. 26

 BODY:
Former United States Senator Lowell P. Weicker Jr., an independent running for governor, has enjoyed nothing less than a political honeymoon for the last six months. He has held wide leads in all public opinion polls. His campaign has been gentlemanly and issue-oriented. He also has been successful at raising funds, frequently attracting the news media and collecting endorsements from Democrats and Republicans.
Last week, however, Mr. Weicker's candidacy became engaged in a volatile volley of charges and countercharges with the Democratic Party's endorsed candidate, United States Representative Bruce A. Morrison of Hamden, over Mr. Weicker's personal finances.
The exchanges, which included unusually strong language from both candidates, also appeared to start what will likely become a fiercely fought three-way contest that could easily escalate into the kind of tit-for-tat negative campaigning that helped unseat Mr. Weicker in 1988. 
''It was Hell Week for Lowell Weicker,'' said Jack R. Goldberg, the press secretary for the Republican Party's nominee for governor, United States Representative John G. Rowland of Waterbury. ''He wants to stay lofty and above the battle, but if he wants to be governor he's going to have to fight for it.''
 
Fund-Raising Role Disputed
Mr. Weicker, a Greenwich resident who has remained a registered Republican and is running under the Connecticut Party banner, came under attack by Mr. Morrison for failing to make public his 1989 Federal and state tax returns. Mr. Rowland, Mr. Morrison and the Democrat challenging Mr. Morrison in the party's Sept. 11 primary, State Representative William J. Cibes Jr. of New London, have released their returns. Mr. Morrison said Mr. Weicker's refusal was inconsistent with his past practice of disclosing his taxes. He also intimated that Mr. Weicker's holdings of oil and pharmaceutical company stocks could be a conflict of interest.
Moreover, he suggested that the fund-raising company for medical and scientific research, based in Alexandria, Va., that employed Mr. Weicker as chief executive in 1989 was a ''sham'' created solely to provide Mr. Weicker with employment following the end of his 18-year Senate career.
Mr. Morrison also noted that some public service advertisements featuring Mr. Weicker and the medical organization, Research! America, had been seen in Fairfield County on New York television stations and on some cable channels elsewhere in Connecticut. The broadcasts, he said, raise questions about requirements for equal time.
''The people of Connecticut need a governor they can trust,'' Mr. Morrison said in a news conference at the State Capitol on Thursday.
Earlier that day, Mr. Weicker, under persistent questioning from reporters, Mr. Morrison and the Republican candidate for lieutenant governor, State Representative Robert G. Jaekle of Stratford, released part of his 1989 tax records, stating that he earned $521,172 and paid $86,616 in Federal taxes.
Mr. Weicker did not supply any tax forms supplementing form 1040, including Schedule A, which would detail his $137,482 in deductions, or Schedule B, which would state the origin of the $58,561 he said he earned in taxable interest and $22,467 in dividends.
Instead, Mr. Weicker, whose grandfather, E. R. Squibb, helped found the pharmaceutical company now called the Squibb Corporation, said that he earned $173,000 from Research!America and about $100,000 from a teaching post at George Washington University in Washington, lecture fees, royalties and other part-time positions.
 
Sensitive Family Information
He said the rest of his income, and that of his wife, Claudia, came from pensions, annuities, dividends, interest and capital gains on more than 30 securities. He also received payments as a beneficiary of two trust funds.
He said he held less than 250 shares of stock in a few pharmaceutical companies. He also said he owned no oil company stocks, although he received a $348 oil company dividend from his mother's trust fund.
Mr. Weicker said he was declining to release other tax forms because they would reveal sensitive family information, like how much alimony he pays to his two former wives. He said he released such information in the past because he was a public official and had chosen to disclose more than he had to.
As for Research! America, Mr. Weicker said Mr. Morrison was ''throwing slime.'' He described the group as a nonprofit alliance that raises money for grass-roots public awareness programs about the benefits of medical research. He listed its directors, including former Surgeon General C. Everett Koop, and 130 supporting foundations, hospitals, universities and pharmaceutical concerns.
Mr. Weicker said the alliance raised almost $1.1 million in 1989 and spent slightly more than half that amount on salaries and other administrative costs. He also said that he was on an unpaid leave and that any broadcasting in Connecticut since he became a candidate was not deliberate.
To further blunt Mr. Morrison's criticisms, Mr. Weicker released documents showing that Mr. Morrison in 1989 bought and sold millions of dollars worth of stocks in oil and pharmaceutical concerns.
Mr. Weicker, speaking near the end of a 90-minute news conference that included the playing of several Research! America commercials, also criticized the news media for publicizing, and thus, he said, giving credence to, Mr. Morrison's accusations.
Mr. Weicker's press secretary, Avice A. Meehan, said Mr. Morrison's charges were ''desperate.'' Mr. Morrison has been trailing both Mr. Weicker and Mr. Rowland in all public opinion polls conducted since March, when Mr. Weicker unexpectedly entered the race and Gov. William A. O'Neill, a Democrat, unexpectedly announced he would not seek a third full term.
 
Weicker Leads in Latest Poll
Earlier this month, in the most recent poll taken by the University of Connecticut's Institute for Social Inquiry and published in The Hartford Courant, Mr. Weicker led by 37 percent of those polled, with Mr. Rowland receiving 20 percent and Mr. Morrison receiving 13 percent. The poll of 500 randomly selected state residents, which was taken between July 23 and 30, had a margin of error of plus or minus five percentage points.
''He has not even broken 20 percent in a public poll,'' Ms. Meehan said of Mr. Morrison. ''He is a man really struggling along, and he has yet to find a public message. He is floundering, so he has flailed out.''
 
Rowland Silent and Pleased
Mr. Morrison said his comments did not amount to negative campaigning. ''I asked a lot of questions about Research! America, and I don't think there is anything wrong with him answering those questions,'' he said.
When asked by reporters if he had ''slimed'' Mr. Weicker, Mr. Morrison laughed and looked at his hands to show that they were clean. He also said the stocks in Mr. Morrison's transactions mentioned by Mr. Weicker were all owned by his former wife, Jane.
Mr. Rowland had little involvement in the week's debate, although he was apparently pleased by the attention Mr. Morrison's charges had attracted, and said he did not consider them to be negative campaigning.
Both Democrats and Republicans have made Mr. Weicker the target of most attacks so far, in particular charging that he was being evasive in stating how he would deal with the state's growing economic crisis. Both parties likened the burly former Senator to a ''dancing bear'' for his refusal to say under what circumstances, if any, he would seek a state income tax.
The attacks are meant to recall Mr. Weicker's failed 1988 campaign for a fourth term in office, when he was repeatedly ridiculed as a sleeping bear in several television commercials broadcast by the Democratic candidate, Joseph I. Lieberman of New Haven. Mr. Lieberman won the election by less than 1 percent of the vote.
Ms. Meehan said the current Weicker campaign was not daunted by the specter of a replay of 1988's negative attacks. ''The efficacy of the negative campaign has been overblown,'' she said. ''People who feel desperate enough to fall into that tactic use it like a bogyman to scare children.''

CORRECTION-DATE: August 30, 1990, Thursday, Late Edition - Final

 CORRECTION:
An article on Monday about financial disclosures by Lowell P. Weicker Jr., the independent candidate for governor in Connecticut, misidentified his grandfather. The grandfather was not E. R. Squibb but Theodore Weicker, one of three partners who founded the Squibb Corporation.

HEADLINE: A Market Slumps and Real-Estate Lawyers Scramble

BYLINE: By ANTHONY DePALMA

 BODY:
When houses and apartments were selling in days or weeks during the mid-1980's, real-estate closings were the bread and butter of many law practices in the New York metropolitan region. Some lawyers conducted hundreds of closings a year, collecting from $750 to $1,000 for each one.
But since the real-estate market was laid low and houses began to remain unsold for a year or more, that steady source of income has dried up, and many of those lawyers have had to reorganize the way they practice law.
Lawyers have abandoned the freedom of practicing alone for the security of firms that deal with more than just real estate, branching out into new legal fields. At the same time some firms that handle real-estate matters are laying off lawyers and paralegals. 
In many firms, lawyers have replaced closings with foreclosures, more often than not properties that were the focus of mid-1980's real-estate deals that have soured.
 
More Golfing Now
Like real-estate agents, architects and title-insurance companies that thrived through the region's real-estate boom, lawyers have had to find ways to cope with the bust.
''I don't know of anyone who has gotten out of the business completely, but they certainly are looking at other ways of making a living,'' said Allan D. Grecco, a lawyer in Rocky Point, L.I., who is co-chairman of the Suffolk County Bar Association's real property committee. ''Me? My golf game is improving.''
House buyers and sellers are not required to have lawyers, but most want legal representation as soon as they enter a deal. The closing is the final part of a sale, in which the lawyers supervise the signing of legal documents and calculate adjustments for items like heating oil, property taxes and repairs outlined in contracts.
Statistics on the decline in house sales suggest how lawyers have been hurt, since every sale usually involves three lawyers, representing the buyer, seller and lender. According to the National Association of Realtors, since the peak of the market in 1987, sales of existing houses and condominiums have declined 11 percent in Connecticut, 20 percent in New York and 25 percent in New Jersey.
 
'It's a Terrible Market'
In New Jersey, that drop means there will be 40,000 fewer closings this year than in 1987.
''It's a terrible market,'' said Paul E. Roberts, a partner in the New York office of Morgan, Lewis & Bockius, a large national law firm. ''Most development lawyers are not too busy.''
Without closings or new real-estate projects, lawyers have had to take hard looks at their practices. Until this year, for example, Mr. Grecco had his own practice, related primarily to real estate. He represented builders, speculators and homeowners.
But as the number of projects dropped and closings disappeared, he needed new avenues of support. So Mr. Grecco and four other lawyers who also had been working on their own joined to form a new five-person firm. About 10 percent of the firm's practice now is real estate, but a more difficult and contentious form of real estate.
''What used to be a real-estate practice of contracts and closings,'' Mr. Grecco said, ''now is one of litigation, negotiations and aggravation.''
 
More Complex Deals
Many lawyers say that not only are closings fewer, but also that the deals that are being completed tend to be far more complex.
Most complications arise from the advantages of buyers over sellers in the current market. Buyers can and do demand many special provisions, such as making the purchase contingent on the sale of their own house. They also tend to be tougher when the results come in from home inspections, demanding extra work or deep price reductions. And much of the negotiating now falls to the lawyers.
''Each deal is harder and harder to put together,'' said Ed Wacks, a lawyer in Morris Plains who is head of the New Jersey Bar Association's Real Property, Probate and Trust law section.
Mr. Wacks said that until 1988, real estate had accounted for 45 percent of the practice in his firm, Wacks Mullen Kartzman & Craig. That has dropped by half in the last few years, replaced increasingly by foreclosure proceedings. He said he also has picked up much more work in the probate field: wills and estates.
''Each time there's a downturn in the economy people think the worst,'' Mr. Wacks said. ''And when they think of the worst they think of death.''
 
Drastic Drop in Income
Mr. Wacks said that some big firms have been laying off associates, especially those who had been hired for the real-estate practice. Other small firms unable to shift to new types of litigation have watched their income drop drastically.
In some practices, it has taken an infusion of new blood to keep things going. Ronald King, a veteran real-estate lawyer in Stamford, Conn., who during the peak was doing more than 400 closings a year, saw his practice cut in half in the last few years.
''You come almost to depend upon the practice growing every year,'' Mr. King said. ''It came as a shock to me when it stopped.''
Like other lawyers who had built practices almost entirely on real-estate transactions, Mr. King, 64, sought new ways to expand his practice. But it was not easy. ''It's been hard for an old horse to learn new tricks,'' he said.
Things started to turn around for him last December when he was joined by his 30-year-old son, Peter. The younger Mr. King had just passed the Connecticut bar and had an interest in probate and copyright law, which helped bring in new clients.
''Usually we were so busy with real-estate work that we virtually threw other business out the door,'' the older Mr. King said. ''Now we're being a little more careful about replacing our income.''
The legal profession, recognizing that some of its members are in danger of being stuck high and dry by the receding market, is ready to throw out a life preserver. The Stamford Regional Bar Association plans to hold seminars in the fall to help lawyers who had concentrated on real estate refresh their skills in other areas like probate and corporate law.
''As the regional economy has shifted, many people who had emphasized real estate as an important part of their practice find it necessary also to shift,'' said Michael J. Cacace, a partner in the firm of D'Andrea & Cacace, and president of the Stamford Regional Bar Association. ''The legal community, as with any business, is adapting to the economy.''

HEADLINE: Jesse Fried Wed To Naomi Super

 BODY:
Naomi Anne Super, a doctoral candidate in materials science at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, and Jesse Mackenzie Fried, a Harvard University law student, were married yesterday at Congregation Shaarei Tefilla in Las Vegas, Nev. Rabbi Abner Weiss officiated. 
Mrs. Fried, 23 years old, is a daughter of Mr. and Mrs. Jack Super of Las Vegas. She is a summa cum laude graduate of the University of California at Berkeley, where she was elected to Phi Beta Kappa. Her father is a real-estate developer and her mother, Marlene Super, is a school librarian.
Mr. Fried, 27, is a son of Lena Fried of Woburn, Mass., and Zoltan Fried of Lexington, Mass. He graduated from Harvard, where he also received a master's degree in economics. His mother is a systems analyst for the Mitre Corporation in Bedford, Mass. His father is a professor of physics at the University of Lowell.

HEADLINE: ON YOUR OWN;
Take a Hike, on Cross-Country Trails

BYLINE: By BARBARA LLOYD

 BODY:
A SCOUTING party has taken to the woods and backroads to mark a trail that will offer, if you are fit and eager, the opportunity to take an eight-month nature walk across the United States. But the purpose for staking out the new path has to do as much with image as reality.
''It's important for people to know that they can hike the entire trail if they want to,'' said John Viehman, executive editor of Backpacker magazine. ''But it's more the dream we're creating here. 
''This is a time when people are starting to look out their living room window and question how they fit into the grand scheme of the world. For all those with a short amount of time, backpacking is the key. But there has to be a hard core of trails, or they will make other choices.''
Backpacker, a national outdoors magazine published by Rodale Press of Emmaus, Pa., has joined with the American Hiking Society of Washington to organize the scouting trek. Their goal is to lay out a 5,500-mile American Discovery Trail, which will stretch from ocean to ocean: from the Point Reyes National Seashore near San Francisco to the Prime Hook National Wildlife Refuge on the coast of Delaware. The route across America's heartland will be created from existing trails and logging roads rather than new terrain.
 
Water Supply Is Scarce
The plan is grand, but the day-to-day job of dogging it out on the trail has had its ups and downs. The drought in the West has curtailed progress, making the scouting party's daily search for water tantamount to counting cracks in a riverbed. And occasionally, marked trails don't exist, or at least are thick with weed and brush.
A three-person team led by Eric Seaborg, a former president of the hiking society, set out June 2. The group is using a four-door Chevrolet Blazer truck and 15-foot trailer as its roving headquarters. While concentrating on a network of footpaths for hiking, the team also carries mountain bikes. At times, it has joined local equestrians for trips on horseback through established trails.
Organizers see the pathway as a way of hiking, bicycling and horseback riding through a vast network joining more than 30,000 miles of existing trails. It is designed to link the nation's three major trails running north and south: the Appalachian Trail in the East; the Continental Divide Trail through the Rocky Mountains, and the Pacific Crest Trail in the West.
The inspiration for a coast-to-coast trail came from Hike-a-Nation, an effort by the American Hiking Society nearly a decade ago to publicize the need for such a trail. Sponsors like Chevrolet Truck Sales and the Coleman Company, a Kansas manufacturer of camping equipment, are subsidizing the current project. A trail map and guide book published by Rodale is scheduled for release in 1992.
 
Shorter, Less Expensive Trips
The time is right for developing a trail, said Chuck Sloan, president of the hiking society. ''We have found that in every economic downturn, the number of people hiking increases,'' Sloan said. ''It's a way for people to get out and do something; to take shorter trips that are less expensive.'' The American Hiking Society, formed in 1977 as an outdoors advocacy group, has about 250,000 members.
So far, the trip has had all sorts of surprises. Heather Douglass, assistant trails coordinator, joined the scouting team for two weeks when it was heading northeast of Foresthill, Calif., near Lake Tahoe. In a clearing along the trail, the scouting team came across Michigan Bluff.
''It was a street in the middle of the woods,'' said Douglass. ''There was an old house in the middle of town that had been letting people camp in their backyard for years without charging. Here these people sit, and the world comes to them. The town was our best discovery, a neat, funny place.''
Seaborg and his team are sometimes out of contact for days. An unusually prolonged stretch of nine days caused some concern among organizers last week. Seaborg finally surfaced last Friday, and reported that his group had been more than 80 miles from the nearest telephone. They had trekked from the Big Smokey Valley near the Toouima Range in central Nevada to the Duckwater Indian Reservation southwest of Ely.
 
Rain and Snow
In an 11-hour hike Thursday, they passed over three ridges in the Hot Creek Range, from the desert floor to an altitude of 9,500 feet. They had been soaked earlier in the week in a two-day rain spell, unusual for that area. In the mountains, they had a dusting of snow.
A local forest ranger gave them directions for a trail that still was being forged across the ridge. ''He told us to go up a trail that was just roughed out last week,'' said Seaborg, ''but that ran out. From there, we bushwacked through the brush and got onto a jeep trail.''
Despite the hardships, Seaborg's spirits were running high. ''It's a physical challenge, but it's real pretty out here,'' he said by telephone from Duckwater. ''It's neat to be on the desert. And it was fun to be away from phones.''
For recorded information on the progress of the scouting trip, which is expected to end next February, telephone the American Discovery Trail Scouting Expedition line at (703) 754-9008.

HEADLINE: Critic's Notebook;
Theater in Britain Is No Longer a Bargain

BYLINE: By FRANK RICH; Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LONDON

 BODY:
Even when the plays have been less than perfect here, the price of a ticket has always been exactly right for American visitors. Not this year.
Inflation and a weak dollar have sent West End box-office scales soaring. An orchestra seat to a musical in London now approaches $50, and the additional cost of $2 to $3 for a program brings the real price close to the $55 top for some Broadway musicals.
Good seats for plays cost only $15 less than musicals, and sometimes the price is higher if there's a star name on the marquee. Things have gotten so humiliating for some bargain-seeking American culture-vultures abroad that the McDonald's in Stratford-on-Avon, the last refuge for Shakespeare enthusiasts on a tight budget, now levies a 12-cent surcharge for ketchup. 
In the West End, the rise in prices has not been matched by a commensurate increase in theatrical quality. The season has been notorious for expensive bombs, Broadway-style, including two very short-lived musical extravaganzas devoted to spiritual figures, ''King'' (as in Martin Luther) and ''Bernadette'' (as in Lourdes). Local critics still write about these fiascoes, uncommonly large in economic scale for London, with a bewilderment suggesting that they were in some way mystical experiences after all. The two most acclaimed dramas of the London season, David Hare's ''Racing Demon'' and Peter Flannery's ''Singer,'' have both avoided the financial pressures of the West End entirely: Rather than transfer into commercial theaters, these two plays have simply moved from the small houses at the National Theater and the Royal Shakespeare Company to larger auditoriums at the same repertory institutions. This leaves the West End with no dramatic offerings remotely comparable to, say, ''The Piano Lesson,'' ''The Grapes of Wrath'' or ''Prelude to a Kiss'' on Broadway. With occasional exceptions, what the West End presents instead is tourist fare, much of it far less fresh than the equivalent products across the ocean. While ''Me and My Girl'' and ''Starlight Express'' have long since come and gone in New York, they continue unabated here, with their umpteenth casts. In England, the sun apparently never sets on any Andrew Lloyd Webber musical, Agatha Christie thriller or sex farce by Ray Cooney, beloved author of ''Run for Your Wife!'' (now in its seventh year).
Revivals are also far more plentiful here than in New York. They range from the limited engagements of stars in modern classics - Richard Harris in Pirandello's ''Henry IV,'' Derek Jacobi in Sartre's ''Kean'' - to less-than-stellar retreads of 1980's hits that were not so wonderful the first time around, such as ''Blood Brothers'' and ''Stepping Out.'' Perhaps reflecting the ambiguous position of Brecht in a world pondering a united Germany, a ''Mother Courage'' starring Glenda Jackson advertises itself with a critic's quote promising a ''dazzling un-Brechtian production.'' In a category by itself is a staging of Noel Coward's ''Private Lives,'' scheduled for mid-September, in which Keith Baxter will co-star with Joan Collins. As Miss Collins owes her latter-day celebrity to American television, so American pop culture dominates much of the West End. Even the long-running ''Les Liaisons Dangereuses'' is featuring an American television star, Daniel J. Travanti of ''Hill Street Blues.'' By far the most talked-about actor in a London theater right now is another American interpreter of Valmont, John Malkovich, who is re-creating his Broadway role of Pale in Lanford Wilson's ''Burn This'' on Shaftesbury Avenue. Along with ''Private Lives,'' the most awaited West End opening of the fall is an English production of the Stephen Sondheim-James Lapine ''Into the Woods,'' directed by Richard Jones and starring Julia McKenzie.
Golden oldies of American rock-and-roll are at the core of three recent West End musicals (''Return to the Forbidden Planet,'' ''Buddy'' and ''Stop in the Name of Love''). The most warmly received new revival in London is ''Show Boat,'' with the highest praise reserved for its American stars, Bruce Hubbard and Karla Burns. When one looks beyond the West End to London's subsidized companies, where no fewer than three Arthur Miller plays are running, it's hard to imagine how anyone can accuse the English of colonizing the American theater. If anything, the reverse may be true: London cannot get enough of the rude vitality of Steppenwolf, ''Can't Help Lovin' Dat Man,'' ''Peggy Sue'' and even ''The Crucible.'' Certainly in contrast to the American competition, the two top-rung English playwrights who have ventured into the West End this season, Alan Ayckbourn and Simon Gray, seem to speak in subdued voices.
This is especially true of Mr. Gray, whose ''Hidden Laughter,'' at the Vaudeville Theater, tries to explore a bankrupt marriage in quiet terms that can be regarded either as Chekhovian or genteel to a fault.
As one expects from the author of ''Butley'' and ''Otherwise Engaged,'' Mr. Gray places his focus on a London literary couple, a budding novelist (Felicity Kendal) and an agent (Kevin McNally). In the manner of ''The Common Pursuit,'' the play jumps several years with each scene, gradually filling in the decadelong chronology of a family's wasteful decline as played out by representatives of three generations in a weekend getaway cottage in Devon. The play's title is from T. S. Eliot, and its central, neo-''Cherry Orchard'' image is a center-stage tree stump. Its dramatic events often concern what one character defensively describes as ''the usual middle-class stuff'' - petty infidelities and alienated teen-age children. ''Hidden Laughter'' is the kind of schematically constructed play in which characters constantly reassure one another that ''everything will be all right'' right up to the moment when someone histrionically asks, ''So why is everything going wrong?'' That question is never answered, so Miss Kendal, under the author's awkward direction, tries to raise the emotional stakes by delivering almost every speech as if she were laughing through her tears. Eventually this novelist's cries prove more wolf than Woolf.
No wonder the show is taken over by Peter Barkworth, who, like Michael Bryant in the new Hare play, is giving one of London's two standout performances in the seemingly ubiquitous role of a wry, silver-haired Church of England minister with agnostic and homosexual tendencies. Mr. Barkworth's vicar, somewhat reminiscent of the title character in Mr. Gray's ''Quartermaine's Terms,'' dispenses wisdom and compassion to all the selfish strivers around him only to be forgotten by his supposed friends when he is in desperate straits of his own. In the play's funniest if most irrelevant scene he finds himself in a full-fledged brawl with an egomaniacal young novelist; it's as if the Archbishop of Canterbury were rolling in the dirt with Martin Amis. Mr. Ayckbourn's new West End production, ''Man of the Moment,'' is also written in a low key - or as much so as a play with a frequently used on-stage swimming pool can be. Yet the writer's gentle tone of voice does not belie his bitterness. As this comedy's nasty premise has it, a fatuous television documentary program titled ''Their Paths Crossed'' is staging a reunion between a nebbishy former bank clerk named Douglas Beechey (played by Michael Gambon) and the onetime armed robber (Peter Bowles) whose crime he foiled 17 years earlier. Though a national hero for a few weeks after his noble gesture, Beechey is now an anonymous suburbanite, a double-glazing salesman in a sexless marriage to the reclusive woman whose face was disfigured by the robber's gunshot. The criminal, meanwhile, has become the real media star over the long haul: After exploiting his nine-year jail term in best-selling memoirs, he was rewarded with a hit television talk show that has bought him the servant-crammed Spanish villa where Mr. Ayckbourn's play is set. If the cynical irony of ''Man of the Moment'' sounds a bit cheap - and sometimes is - it is mitigated by the author's unexpected characterization of Beechey. While a nerd and a bore, the good Samaritan is also a genuinely good soul who, to the astonishment of his slick television packagers, harbors no resentment of the vulgar, pontificating bully whose criminality has proved so extravagantly profitable. Indeed, the genial, contented Beechey is grateful for the free trip to sunny Spain, dazzled by his first exposure to show business and eager to regard his long-ago antagonist as a reformed citizen, a first class pundit and a generous host.
The part of Beechey was a tour de force for Mr. Gambon, the frequent Ayckbourn collaborator who concluded his run in ''Man of the Moment'' at the Globe Theater in mid-August. Shuffling on at first as a timid, faceless nonentity, compulsively smiling and mouthing platitudes, the star more resembled the Alec Guinness of 1950's Ealing comedies and the Peter Sellers of ''Being There'' than the aggressive, rough-edged actor still best known to Americans for ''The Singing Detective.'' But for all his priceless farcical bits of meekness, including a hilarious Art Carney-ish attempt to appear nonchalant in front of a television camera, Mr. Gambon never let the audience laugh condescendingly at his character: When Beechey twitches for a split-second with regret while recounting his unfulfilled desire for children or when he must rise above 17 years of lethargy to stand up for right one more time, the actor's performance became more touching than clownish and, finally, more heroic than pathetic.
If ''Man of the Moment,'' like some other recent Ayckbourn plays such as ''Woman in Mind'' and ''Henceforward . . .,'' is circumscribed by the gimmick of its premise, it shares the preoccupation with good and evil that has distinguished this amazingly prolific playwright's most expansive efforts of the decade, ''A Small Family Business'' and ''The Revengers' Comedies.'' As always, the evening is flecked with its creator's dramatic daring as both author and director. Among his characters is a grotesquely overweight nanny who proves both comic (in a scuba-diving outfit) and tragic (in unrequited love), and among the theatrical experiments is an outlandish if unsatisfying Pirandellian denouement that casts the audience in the theater in the part of an easily cowed television audience. Until the finale, ''Man of the Moment'' is one of the precious few productions in the West End where the audience is not asked to sink to that lowly role.

The Oil Jolt
 
U.S. officials said economic sanctions against Iraq, while starting to have some effect, will probably not have a decisive impact for months. The Economy

HEADLINE: Key Talks On Output For OPEC

BYLINE: By YOUSSEF M. IBRAHIM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: VIENNA, Aug. 26

 BODY:
As members of the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries gathered informally here today to confront some of the most crucial issues in the group's 30-year history, some predicted that a formal meeting would be announced on Monday to address questions of oil production and prices.
But regardless of the forum, a majority of OPEC members expect at least three member nations to carry out their plan to raise oil production to help meet the supply shortfall caused by Iraq's invasion of Kuwait on Aug. 2.
There is a growing sentiment here that the member nations would be better off to permit production increases within an OPEC framework instead of allowing a free-for-all that might be difficult to control once the Persian Gulf crisis is over. Once the conflict comes to an end, this logic holds, OPEC can retake control and regulate production under its quota system. 
While there are serious differences among members on how to respond to the turmoil in the world petroleum markets, Saudi Arabia, the United Arab Emirates and Venezuela are pressing to increase their production substantially when the meeting ends.
 
Long-Dormant Animosities
The Iraqi-Kuwaiti conflict has rekindled long-dormant animosities within OPEC that reflect the political schisms separating poor and rich countries and pro-Western and anti-Western nations.
OPEC officials from Algeria, Libya, Iran, Nigeria and Iraq, for example, argue that it is not OPEC's mission to save the West from an economic crisis caused by rapidly rising oil prices. They say the West has done little for indebted third world nations in the last decade and argue that there is enough oil in world markets to compensate for the interruption of supplies caused by the Iraqi invasion.
Delegates from Saudi Arabia, Venezuela, Kuwait, the United Arab Emirates and Qatar, however, say that unless OPEC allows members that can raise their output to do so immediately, OPEC itself will pay a high price if demand drops.
These delegates say higher oil prices will eventually create a backlash as they have done in the past, when industrialized countries countered rising prices with conservation measures that reduced oil consumption.
A number of member countries, including Indonesia, Algeria and Nigeria, seem to favor a middle ground in which OPEC would raise output to make up for only part of the shortages in the markets.
Their view is that oil prices had been quite weak for a long time earlier this year, and higher prices now are needed to compensate for the earlier losses.
 
A Fundamental Split
But behind the issues of output and prices is a more fundamental split along political and ideological lines.
Among the poorer, more militant members of OPEC, there is an unquestionable antipathy toward the United States and a conviction that Saudi Arabia and Venezuela, the prime advocates of higher production, are acting under strong American pressure.
''We are not here to respond to the wishes of the Americans,'' said an Arab delegate from one of the militant countries. ''This is OPEC, and our mission is to serve the best interest of our members.''
In an argument echoed by several moderate countries, including Nigeria and Indonesia, which are allies of the West, an OPEC official from Algeria said: ''There is no real shortage of oil in the market. If the American and Western oil companies release their stocks of oil instead of hanging on to them to make more money, there will be no shortage.''
Advocates of this view say that even if OPEC now authorized all its members with surplus capacity to produce all the oil they could, this would not amount to the full four million barrels a day missing from Kuwait and Iraq.
 
Delegate From Kuwait
Among the delegates who gathered today, Kuwait's representative, Ali Khalifa al Sabah, the Finance Minister and a senior member of the Kuwaiti ruling family who has been an architect of OPEC policies for a decade, was greeted warmly by most other OPEC ministers.
So far, there has been no sign of the Iraqis, who have indicated that they would wait for the meeting to be elevated into a formal session.
The general sentiment here is that even if Iraq took part, it would not be able to prevent other OPEC members from raising output to compensate for the loss of the four million barrels of oil a day.
In pressing their case for increased production, Saudi Arabia and Venezuela concede that international oil companies, the United States, Japan and Western European countries hold large stocks of oil that could be released to help make up for the shortfall.
But they argue that these stocks are held by rich industrialized nations and that poorer third world countries, like India, Pakistan, Brazil and many African nations, which do not have stocks of oil, are experiencing a real shortage and are facing huge, untenable price increases.
 
A Middle Ground
One OPEC delegate, who is among those who are unable to raise production but strongly favor higher prices, advocates bargaining for a smaller increase in output by Saudi Arabia, Venezuela and the United Arab Emirates. ''Perhaps something on the order of two million barrels instead of say 3.5 or so,'' he said.
But Saudi Arabia and Venezuela have said that under the existing OPEC agreement, which mandates the production of a total of 22.5 million barrels a day by all 13 members at a price of $21 a barrel, there is plenty of room to produce all they can now. Prices now stand at about $31 a barrel and production is substantially below 22.5 million barrels a day.
Some delegates are hoping that the talks do not raise emotions too high. Sadek Boussena, the OPEC conference president and Algeria's oil minister, said in an interview tonight on CNN: ''I have big hopes that all of us will try to remain calm and stay out of matters that do not concern us.''
 
Making a Decision
Mr. Boussena added, ''I think we have to make this decision in a way that allows all OPEC members not to consider this decision as against them.''
The Algerian oil minister noted that the rise in oil prices is not something OPEC can remedy by itself.
He and others have said psychology and market speculation are driving prices up and that these elements have little to do with how much oil is on the market or how much production is missing from Kuwait and Iraq.

LENGTH: 1557 words

HEADLINE: International Report;
Huge Withdrawals Hurt Gulf Banks

BYLINE: By STEVEN PROKESCH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LONDON, Aug. 26

 BODY:
A huge withdrawal of deposits and the cutting of vital Western and Japanese credit lines have dealt severe blows to the banking systems and economies of the Persian Gulf region.
Much of the capital that has left Kuwait, Saudi Arabia, Bahrain and the United Arab Emirates since the Iraqi invasion of Kuwait on Aug. 2 is believed to have been deposited in Western havens like London, Switzerland and the United States.
As a result of the financial panic, analysts predict, many banks in the region will become insolvent, there will be a wave of bank mergers and the decline of Bahrain as an international banking center, which was already under way, will accelerate.
The weakening of the banks also may signal a bigger economic setback for the region: many governments had ambitious plans to develop their fledgling capital markets and to vastly expand their private sectors to create jobs for their youthful populations. Many of these plans depended on attracting back the huge sums of capital their citizens had invested abroad. 
The private sector in Saudi Arabia, Kuwait, the United Arab Emirates, Bahrain, Qatar and Oman has about $150 billion invested abroad, said Stephen Timewell, editor of Arab Banker, a London-based periodical. ''Governments had hoped for that to come back,'' he said. ''At this stage, it does not look likely.''
And the capital invested abroad is growing. Analysts estimate that $3.7 billion to $5.9 billion, or 25 percent to 40 percent of total deposits, have been withdrawn from banks in the United Arab Emirates since the invasion.
In Saudi Arabia, 10 percent to 15 percent, or $1.6 billion to $2.4 billion, of private-sector deposits in domestic branches may have been withdrawn.
In Bahrain, private customers are believed to have sent abroad 30 to 40 percent of their deposits in commercial banks, or $1.1 billion to $1.5 billion.
Withdrawals from the 55 offshore banks based in Bahrain, which had assets of $72.6 billion, appear not to have been proportionately as large. But analysts say that is because their main depositors - financial institutions and other corporations - have longer-term deposits that cannot be withdrawn instantly.
A story told by Elisabeth Jackson-Moore, general manager of Capital Intelligence, a Cyprus-based bank-rating agency, reflects how little confidence there is in Persian Gulf banks. ''I was speaking with one bank in Dubai who wanted to deposit money with a Japanese bank,'' she said. ''The Japanese bank said: 'No thank you.' ''
 
Hard Currency Sought
The loss of credit lines and the capital flight are not the only blows to gulf banks. After the invasion, the value of local currencies plummeted and trading in them was halted in international financial centers like London. Local ''demand for hard currency, especially dollars, is already putting banking systems under pressure,'' said the London-based Middle East Economic Digest.
Before the invasion, some gulf banks were already having difficulty collecting on loans to Kuwaiti businesses and individuals and to Iraq. Now, repayment is even more problematic.
Some Arab bankers are struggling to sound upbeat. They point out that their profits have typically reflected the level of oil prices. With oil prices, which were $18 a barrel right before the crisis, expected to stay at least in the $20's no matter how the conflict is resolved, their future is promising, they say.
They add that declining investor confidence in the Middle East means that the business of channeling Arab money abroad should remain strong and that the withdrawal of Western and Japanese banks from the region means less competition.
 
A Different View
Analysts are not so sanguine.
''Even if Saddam Hussein were assassinated in Baghdad today, the damage has already been done to gulf banking,'' said Mr. Timewell of Arab Banker. ''It is difficult to see how confidence can be restored over the next few years.''
The crisis hit just as many gulf banks were recovering from their problems of the 1980's: a slump in oil prices, the 1982 collapse of the Kuwaiti stock market and problem loans to Latin America and local borrowers, including members of the Saudi royal family.
They also faced the happy prospect of helping finance the reconstruction of Iraq and Iran. But several banks that loaned Iraq money during its eight-year war with Iran discovered that it was easier to lend the money than to get it back.
There are other lingering problems. With 48 commercial banks for about two million people, said Mr. Timewell, the United Arab Emirates is ''the most overbanked country in the world.''
A lot of banks there will and should go under,'' he said.
 
Insolvencies in Kuwait
Kuwait had one of the most highly developed banking industries in the Middle East; its six domestic banks had assets of about $37.5 billion at the end of last year. But that included about $28 billion at five banks that had been technically insolvent since the stock market collapse there but had been propped up by the Government while they dealt with a mountain of problem loans.
With Iraq in control of Kuwait, and the Kuwaiti dinar, which was worth nearly $3.48 before the invasion, is now essentially valueless, the five banks are not operating for all intents and purposes.
Kuwait's Finance Minister has pledged to use the exiled Government's huge resources outside of Kuwait - believed to total $90 billion to $100 billion - to make sure that Kuwait-controlled companies remain ''secure and stable.'' But it is unclear how the banks could be helped.
The outlook is much brighter for the National Bank of Kuwait, the country's largest domestic bank. Analysts believe that at least 40 percent of its assets, which totaled $13.3 billion at the end of 1989, is invested abroad. The bank is privately owned, has long been profitable and much of its top management is believed to be regrouping in London.
 
Restrictions on Assets
But running the bank from afar, without any records, could be difficult. And many banks in the gulf region with Kuwaiti shareholders have to contend with the freeze or restrictions on their assets in Western countries, which were imposed to keep the assets out of Iraqi hands.
The London-based United Bank of Kuwait is also in relatively good shape. While its shareholders are Kuwaiti financial institutions, it is a British bank and its $4.2 billion in assets are outside Kuwait. Its assets include British residential and commercial mortgages and American real estate.
Yet its leaders are expecting a big drop in deposits as exiled Kuwaitis withdraw money to survive. Christopher Keen, general manager of the United Bank of Kuwait, told The Financial Times that he expects to lose about a third of deposits in coming weeks. ''But we can handle that,'' he said, ''because we have always been a fairly liquid bank and we'll be able to scale down our balance sheet.''
Western banks do not seem to have much to worry about from the gulf crisis: Kuwait was a net creditor to international banks. And data from the Bank for International Settlements indicate that the net lending exposure of Western banks to Iraq is only $5.4 billion, with much of that held by Italy's Banca Nazionale del Lavoro.
 
Strength in Saudi Arabia
With the Saudi central bank firmly behind it and a fairly developed domestic economy, the Saudi banking system is relatively strong. But Mr. Timewell said four or five smaller banks are ''quite weak'' and their long-term viability is dubious.
The Riyadh-based Saudi American Bank may actually come out ahead as a result of the crisis. The nation's third-largest bank, with assets of $7.1 billion, Saudi American is 40 percent owned by Citicorp, so Saudis may see it as a safe place for their money, analysts say.
Much more vulnerable are Bahrain's 19 domestic banks and 55 offshore banks. Bahrain became an international banking center in the late 1970's and early 1980's mainly because it served as a gateway to Saudi Arabia. But that role became increasingly unnecessary as Saudi banks became more sophisticated and as the Saudis cut back on development projects when oil prices declined in the 1980's.
 
Two Dominant Banks
The offshore banks are dominated by the Arab Banking Corporation and the Gulf International Bank, which had assets of $21.7 billion and $9.9 billion, respectively, at the end of 1989, according to The Banker, a magazine published by The Financial Times.
''Obviously, they must have been badly affected by the gulf crisis, but both are in a strong enough position to overcome their problems,'' Mrs. Jackson-Moore of Capital Intelligence said.
Both have big amounts of Latin American loans but have been setting aside large reserves. Mr. Timewell said Gulf International also has as much as $500 million in loans to Iraq, although Mrs. Jackson-Moore thinks it is much less.
She said the two banks have ''quite a wide geographical spread'' in their lending. Arab Banking has also made acquisitions outside the Middle East, including a West German trade bank and retail banks like International Bank of Asia in Hong Kong and Banco Atlantico in Spai.
Still, analysts say it will be a long time before the banking climate returns to where it was before Iraq invaded. Western banks will not rush to restore credit lines, they predict.

LENGTH: 1089 words

HEADLINE: Czech Brings Enthusiasm To Fed Event

BYLINE: By LOUIS UCHITELLE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: JACKSON HOLE, Wyo., Aug. 26

 BODY:
The star of the proceedings at the Federal Reserve's annual summer retreat, dedicated this year to a discussion of central banking in Eastern Europe, turned out to be the Finance Minister of Czechoslovakia, Vaclav Klaus.
An enthusiastic and energetic man, Mr. Klaus gave a rousing luncheon speech in which he endorsed principles that American central bankers hold dear.
''We have all been enchanted by Vaclav Klaus,'' said Michael W. Keran, chief economist at the Prudential Insurance Company of America and a former Fed official. ''His personality is charming. He is very articulate and forceful in giving his view. And his view is that the invisible hand - free markets -works.'' 
Casting aside the go-slow approach toward free markets that has been preferred, for example, by Hungary, Mr. Klaus said that his ministry was orchestrating a rapid switch from central planning. Simultaneously, stiff anti-inflation measures were being applied in the classic style of Western central banks.
Although ''anti-reformers'' have thrown up roadblocks, Mr. Klaus said, price controls and subsidies are quickly being ended and permission is being sought for the rapid sale of state enterprises to private owners. And the Czechoslovak currency, the koruna, is to be made convertible into dollars and other hard currencies soon.
''I would have preferred a gradual, step-by-step approach to the dismantling of central planning, but the situation dictated otherwise,'' Mr. Klaus said. ''History is unfolding before our eyes, and with all necessary fears and risks we have to move forward very quickly.''
Federal Reserve officials spoke of Mr. Klaus's speech as a textbook approach to the design of free markets and said his anti-inflation policy was a lot tougher than might be tried in free-market countries. But they applauded his efforts to fight the inflation that inevitably results from drastic economic changes like those taking place in Czechoslovakia. Mr. Klaus reported that growth in the money supply was being reduced to zero, a measure that often produces a recession in industrial countries.
His speech came on the opening day of a two-day conference that ended on Saturday at a lodge in the mountains of Grand Teton National Park. Twenty-five Federal Reserve officials exchanged views for two days with economists and central bankers from Western Europe and Canada. Each annual conference centers on a theme, and this year's was the role of a central bank in the emerging market economies of the Soviet Union and Eastern Europe.
Central bank officials and ministers from these countries traveled here for the discussions, but Mr. Klaus, more than any other, seemed to match the American sense of the way things should be done.
 
Backed by Volcker and Greenspan
''The central banks are going to have to be tough, keeping some kind of limits on inflation and money growth,'' Paul A. Volcker, the former chairman of the Fed, said in the opening speech. And in the closing summation, Alan Greenspan, the current Fed chairman, wished Mr. Klaus well in his efforts to educate the anti-reformists.
Mr. Klaus, who is 49 years old, asserted that economic growth is not possible while Czechoslovakia is part state-run and part free-market, so the conversion should be rapidly achieved, whatever the disruption and hardship. He passed out a graph to illustrate his argument that inflation must be cured before economic growth can begin, although the cure - roughly equivalent to high interest rates in the United States -discourages purchases and investment.
''There is no way to accelerate growth with the structural difficulties we have,'' Mr. Klaus said in an interview, referring to the state enterprises and other institutions of central planning that remain in place. ''And to have stability during the transition to free markets you have to have slow growth, maybe even zero growth. But I would never use the term recession. Growth in Czechoslovakia is either faster or slower.''
Poland preceded Czechoslovakia in trying to convert quickly to free markets, and it is currently in a severe recession. Mr. Klaus said his country would suffer less because it was beginning the changeover in better shape, without a foreign debt, with a budget surplus and low inflation.
 
Havel Less Enthusiastic
He also said that while his Government was committed to a rapid transition, some officials, including President Vaclav Havel, were less enthusiastic about moving toward free markets than he was.
Mr. Klaus's enthusiasm for free markets was especially vivid in the blunt answer he gave to a question from Mr. Greenspan. The Fed chairman said he wondered about the problems of measuring economic growth in Eastern Europe during a period of transition and uncertainty and asked how Mr. Klaus would deal with this issue: in effect, how would Mr. Klaus measure the impact of his transition policies?
Mr. Klaus, who once worked as an economic forecaster deeply involved in statistics, said he would not even try to measure economic performance until a free-market system was in place. Whatever the impact, the rapid transition to a free-market system, with a minimum of inflation, is clearly the proper strategy, he said, giving Mr. Greenspan a ''damn the torpedos - full speed ahead'' answer.
 
The Savings Overhang
Through the 10 hours of conference sessions, there was much discussion of matters such as how Eastern European currencies could be made convertible to hard currencies and of ways to absorb the savings that Eastern Europeans accumulated during years when they had little to buy. The savings overhang, as it is called, is potentially inflationary, as people rush to buy goods.
But partly because of Mr. Klaus, there was little discussion of the go-slow strategy for developing free markets - putting a tax system in place first, for example, then a credit system and so forth. And among the various speakers, only Richard Cooper of Harvard University advanced the proposition that the hardships of transition might be lessened if a little inflation were permitted.
For the most part the Fed officials and others among the more than 120 people at the conference felt that the people of Eastern Europe and the Soviet Union were so disenchanted with the failures of centrally planned economies that they were prepared to endure the hardships created by Mr. Klaus's strategy.
''The step-by-step approach seems to be losing status,'' said Philippe Lagayette, first deputy governor of the Bank of France.

HEADLINE: Machine Tool Orders Fell 11.9% Last Month

BYLINE: By JONATHAN P. HICKS

 BODY:
New orders for American-made machine tools dropped by 11.9 percent in July, according to a monthly report by the Association for Manufacturing Technology. Economists said the figures showed that the nation's economy had begun slowing before the Iraqi invasion of Kuwait.
The decline in new orders reflected lower corporate profits, economists said. The lower profits led to continued reductions in capital spending by the nation's automobile makers, which account for more than a third of the nation's machine tool orders. 
The level of machine tool orders stood at $233.7 million in July, compared with June's total of $265.2 million. Shipments plunged by 33.4 percent in July, to $233.4 million, from $250.2 million in June.
 
Typical Summer Slowdown
Albert W. Moore, the president of the Association for Manufacturing Technology, said the decline in orders was largely the result of a typical slowdown in the industry during the summer. ''Machine tool orders are hanging tough, considering that July is usually a slow month, as many manufacturing plants have summer shutdowns and the auto industry is normally in the process of model changeovers,'' he said.
The level of new orders increased by 22.5 percent in July, compared with the month last year, said the association, based in McLean, Va.
But economists said that despite the year-to-year increase, the July numbers reflected troubles within the economy. In recent months, as car sales have slumped, auto makers have sharply curtailed their orders for machine tools. That has particularly affected tool producers, because of the dependence of machine tool makers on the automobile industry.
''My reading of the total package of numbers is that economic growth was extremely slow and fragile before the Middle East situation,'' said Robert J. Stalla, the chief economist with McDonald & Company, a forecasting firm in Cleveland. ''I think that from here on out, there will be higher oil prices, fueling inflation and slowing economic growth. Unless something happens quickly, we're likely to have a recession.''
 
Foreign Orders Off 54%
The July figures included a 54 percent plunge in orders from foreign customers. The analysts said that a single large order from a foreign customer could cause sharp fluctuations in the foreign totals, adding that they did not consider the drop to be significant. The figures also included a 3.5 percent decline in orders from domestic customers.
Many tool makers were concerned about how the Middle East situation might affect oil supplies and whether that would have any impact on the size of the engines to be used by auto makers in Detroit for new models.
Walter J. Aspatore, the president of Cross & Trecker Inc., a large machine tool maker based in Bloomfield Hills, Mich., said the crisis would likely lead machine tool manufacturers to seek more business in the oil industry, making tools that aid in drilling and exploration.
''That is one area that is a big user of machine tools that many in the industry have overlooked,'' Mr. Aspatore said. ''I'd look for more emphasis to be placed in that area, and on business overseas.''
 
Indication of Spending
Machine tools are power-driven devices for cutting or shaping metal parts that are used in products ranging from appliances and vehicles to aircraft and construction materials. The strength of tool orders generally reflects the strength of industrial production, and economists consider patterns of machine tool sales to be critical indicators of capital spending by manufacturers.
Orders for metal-cutting tools, which include boring mills and lathes, dropped by 22.5 percent in July from June's level. That segment of the industry typically accounts for three-fourths of new orders. Orders for metal-forming tools were up 14.2 percent in July from June's level.
The machine tool order backlog stood at $1.56 billion in July, down slightly from the June level. The backlog in July of last year was $2.13 billion.

HEADLINE: Business Scene;
Mexico's Vibrant Untaxed Sector

BYLINE: By Louis Uchitelle

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY

 BODY:
EIGHT blocks from Mexico City's ancient central plaza and historic cathedral is a major shopping district. The streets are as packed with shoppers as Fifth Avenue in New York before Christmas. The shops are mostly makeshift sidewalk stalls, but they display a huge variety of clothing, shoes, household wares, toys and electronic products. Buyers come from all walks of life, and rare is the upper-middle-class home that does not have a videocassette recorder, a refrigerator or a stereo system purchased from vendors like those here, who are prominent participants in Mexico's ''informal economy.''
Tepito, as this shopping district is called, lies at the high end of an off-the-books economy that, if it were on the books, would increase Mexico's gross domestic product by nearly 40 percent, or $80 billion a year, according to estimates of Government and private economists. It is clearly a big source of income, not only for the ''shopkeepers'' of Tepito, but for the tens of thousands of raggedly dressed people who sell Chiclets, toys, wool sweaters, jewelry, packaged snacks and much other new merchandise. 
''Just selling Chiclets or holding out their hands to beg, they can make more than the minimum wage for a formal job,'' said Luis A. Pazos, who owns an economics research center. The national minimum wage is 10,000 pesos a day, or $3.50.
But there is much more to the informal economy than providing income for the unemployed, although that function is a major one in a country where half of the 24 million people of working age do not hold on-the-books jobs. Many of these unemployed migrated to Mexico City in recent years from farms that failed.
Behind these ''salespeople'' are their suppliers, often wealthy wholesalers who get their merchandise from two sources. One is the United States, where electronics products and appliances are bought in quantity and brought into Mexico free of duty - a procedure that requires bribes and partnerships with Government officials. The second source is off-the-books manufacturers, people who make clothing or toys and sometimes employ as many as 200 workers, also off the books.
The key to success for this network is avoiding taxes - not only customs duties, but also sales and income taxes and the various social welfare taxes that legal businesses must pay. Since they do not make tax payments, or pay rent, these underground merchants can sell at 10 to 40 percent below the prices charged by legal shopkeepers. Not surprisingly, there is endless friction between the two groups.
Still, Mexican shoppers are turning to the informal economy in growing numbers, driven to seek bargains because inflation and curbs on wage increases have eroded the purchasing power of many people in recent years.
Given the demand, the informal economy is producing some rather enterprising merchants.
For instance, Rene Daniel Hernandez, 35 years old, a graduate of the University of Mexico, sells television sets, hi-fi systems, compact-disk players and video cameras in Tepito. Leaving his brother to mind the stall, Mr. Hernandez sometimes travels to Los Angeles to buy merchandise, which he brings home in suitcases. But frequently he turns to a wholesaler, who, because he buys in bulk, can give Mr. Hernandez a better price, even after the wholesaler's markup. Mr. Hernandez's own markup might be $15 on a Sony VCR that he sells for $260.
''My prices and profits vary with the flow of shoppers,'' he said. ''The profit margins aren't big, but I can sell 10 VCR's in a day.''
And for the shopper who cannot find a particular item, Mr. Hernandez has a catalogue, from Sony or another manufacturer. The shopper picks out the desired item and makes a down payment. A few days later, Mr. Hernandez makes delivery to the shopper's home, having obtained the item from a wholesaler. Nothing is done on credit, although wholesalers often wait a week to be paid.
So far the Government has tolerated the informal economy; indeed, these merchants are a large source of support for the governing Institutional Revolutionary Party. The party even gives out ''credentials'' to vendors as protection against possible Government harassment.
But this sort of protection deprives the economy of hundreds of millions in tax revenue, assuming that a way could be found to legalize the informal economy. The problem is that Mexico City, where the informal economy is concentrated, lacks the industry and the jobs to absorb the number of people, estimated as high as two million or more, who are operating off the books.
That leaves two alternatives for most of the vendors. One is to migrate to the United States, which they have been doing for years. The other is to migrate to northern Mexico, to cities like Monterrey, Saltillo and Chihuahua, where manufacturing is booming. This migration is now gaining steam.
''The north is an escape from a city that can't support its huge population,'' said Homer Aridjis, a novelist and historian. ''Decentralization is a solution.''

HEADLINE: Tokyo Stocks Set a Record

BYLINE: Reuters

DATELINE: TOKYO, Monday, Aug. 27

 BODY:
Share prices in Tokyo today rose to its ninth-largest one-day gain on techincal factors and investor relief after a weekend without news of military conflict in the Middle East.
The Nikkei index of 225 issues closed up 976 points, or 4.04 percent, to 25,141.76. The index ended the morning session up 398.89 points, or 1.65 percent, to 24,564.65. On Friday, the average rose 428.13. 
Late this morning, the Nikkei was up as much as 535.12 points, but the earlier gains were trimmed on profit taking, traders said.
''The market has clearly been oversold, so a modest technical rebound is natural,'' a trader said. ''But the major investors are still very cautious.''
Today's one-day gain was the ninth largest in the Nikkei's history. The index is now 35 percent below its record high of 38,915.87, set on Dec. 29.
In the morning session, volume totaled about 170 million shares, compared with 300 million on Friday. Advancing shares outnumbered declining ones in the early session by 18 to 5, with 679 higher, 189 lower and 105 unchanged. Trading remained thin throughout the remainder of the day.
In Tokyo's money markets, the dollar opened lower against all primary currencies this morning.
''Dollar-yen trading will not be active here today,'' said Kaoru Kondo, a dealer at Security Pacific National Bank after the morning session ended. ''Instead, activity will be led by cross-trading again.''
The dollar opened at 145.20 yen and 1.5483 West German marks, after closing at 145.70 yen and 1.5470 marks here on Friday.
The currency market continues to watch the Persian Gulf situation, with most dealers expecting dollar buying to emerge if fighting occurs. Most of the dealers said underlying dollar sentiment was negative because of concerns over a weakening American economy.
The mark opened at 93.77 yen against its Tokyo close of 94.17 on Friday. The British pound opened at 283.39 yen, after closing at 284.88 yen on Friday.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
Reviewing Deals After the Invasion

BYLINE: By Kurt Eichenwald

 BODY:
THE best of Wall Street's merger experts in the 1980's specialized in determining how much debt their target companies could take on in a leveraged buyout. To do so, they made assumptions about the future, including the direction of interest rates, economic growth, inflation, even oil prices. But none of them figured on Saddam Hussein. The Iraqi invasion of Kuwait has sent Wall Street and corporate America back to the calculators, and a number of the assumptions that determined the prices of takeover deals have been discarded. Since the invasion, oil prices have soared more than 70 percent, increasing inflationary pressure. And the economy, already slowing, appears to many economists to have been pushed to the edge of recession.
Among the deals that investment bankers say are most likely to be affected by the Iraqi invasion are the airline buyouts or merger agreements made in the last year, including the proposed employee-led buyout of the UAL Corporation, the parent of United Airlines, and the leveraged buyout of NWA Inc., the parent of Northwest Airlines. 
The reasons for the potential troubles in those deals are simple: barring a turnaround, airlines will pay much more for fuel at a time when potential passengers have less money to spend on air travel.
The impact on the UAL deal, which has yet to obtain financing, has been the most striking since the Iraqi invasion. UAL's stock price has tumbled from $140 a share on the last day of trading in July to $93.50 a share on Friday. And the deadline to arrange financing for the $4.38 billion bid had to be extended for two months.
The situation is not so difficult at NWA, which was purchased in 1989 by a consortium of buyers led by Alfred Checchi, a former Marriott Corporation executive from Beverly Hills, Calif. Although that deal has been completed, with the most of the $3.35 billion in debt financing coming from the Bankers Trust Company, some fear that the rise in jet fuel costs could cause problems that were not anticipated during the buyout.
''Northwest could fall into trouble,'' said one investment banker who has examined the deal. ''The only question is: What are they going to do about it?''
A number of analysts said that while they had expected the company to try to go public again by next year, the situation in Iraq had changed their assumptions. ''They are likely to try and refinance,'' said Helane Becker, an airline analyst with Lehman Brothers.
John Dasburg, the executive vice president of finance and administation for NWA, said through a spokesman that the company had ''no plans to refinance the acquisition debt.'' But Wall Street executives involved in the talks said that a number of commercial banks had begun to scrutinize NWA's financial position, apparently with an interest in putting capital into the company.
Unlike UAL, which is trying to raise its debt now, NWA has been paying off its obligations. The company has paid down its debt faster than had been required under its agreement with Bankers Trust, with the result, one banker said, that the company will not be required to make any additional payments until 1992.
NWA reduced its $3.35 billion in debt to $2.1 billion as of the end of March, and it projects a debt level of $1.75 billion by the end of the year, said Bob Gibons, a spokesman for the company.
''They are way ahead of schedule with the banks,'' said Paul P. Karos, an airline analyst with the First Boston Corporation. ''So maybe there is some breathing room here for them in this situation.''
That debt reduction would put the company in a better debt position than a number of other airlines, analysts said. Kevin Murphy, an industry analyst with Morgan Stanley & Company, said there were several airlines whose debt levels, combined with their business prospects, put them in worse shape than Northwest. ''If they have to walk the gangplank,'' he said, referring to NWA, ''they have a lot of people in front of them.''
Moreover, NWA has assets that it has turned to its advantage. For example, the company got $384 million earlier this year by mortgaging property it owns in Japan through the Tobishima Corporation, a construction company based in Tokyo.
But NWA faces some financial hurdles that have been raised a little higher by the crisis in the Persian Gulf. By Sept. 29, KLM Royal Dutch Airlines, one of the equity investors in the consortium of buyers, must significantly reduce its $400 million equity stake in NWA as part of an agreement between the company and the Transportation Department. KLM has announced that it will cut back its equity holdings by $250 million.
Wall Street had expected the Checci group to try to repurchase the stake, although now some say it is unclear whether another equity partner will have to be brought in.
Nevertheless, bankers and analysts said, Northwest's management, which had been exploring plans to buy a large part of Eastern Airlines and had been expected to issue shares by 1991, will most likely have to scale back its plans.
''I don't think they'll be bidding on Eastern anymore,'' Mr. Murphy said. ''And this may thwart Checci's ability to go public anytime in the near term.''

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Higher Long-Term Rates Predicted

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
The week before Labor Day is normally one of Wall Street's slowest periods. But with the Persian Gulf crisis becoming increasingly volatile, the atmosphere in the financial markets will hardly be holiday-like in the coming days.
''It will be one of the slowest, but one of the most anxious weeks of the year,'' said Robert A. Brusca, chief economist at Nikko Securities International.
''In effect,'' writes David Hale, chief economist at Kemper Financial Services, in his weekly letter to clients, ''financial asset prices are now being driven by the looming struggle between the eagle and the scorpion, not Wall Street's traditional bidding competition between bulls and bears.'' 
 
Worsening Is Seen
Most traders and analysts in the credit markets seemed fairly certain last week that the crisis would probably get worse, And long-term interest rates, which by Friday had spurted to their highest levels in 17 months, would almost certainly move higher, they said. ''If we have a war, rates will go higher,'' said Robert J. Barbera, chief economist at Shearson Lehman Brothers. ''If we don't, this sell-off is vastly overdone.''
In the three weeks since Iraq invaded Kuwait, bond yields, tracking the surge in oil prices, have risen by 80 basis points, or hundredths of a point, to 9.15 percent.
Even with the possibility that shooting in the Middle East could easily push interest rates higher, some money managers said that current yields were very attractive. ''On Monday I intend to put some money into long bonds,'' said Marshall B. Front, an executive vice president at Stein, Roe & Farnham, a Chicago-based investment advisory firm that manages close to $10 billion in fixed-income assets. ''Even though uncertainties remain high, we think that interest rates are attractive.''
 
'Pretty Good' Outlook
William H. Gross, a managing director at the Pacific Investment Management Company in Newport Beach, Calif., said, ''Oil and the potential for war are two giant factors in the market right now.
Nevertheless, ''the outlook for bonds six months down the road is pretty good,'' said Mr. Gross, whose firm manages a $16 billion portfolio of fixed-income securities.
Underpinning the positive longer-term outlook is a very soggy economy, one that may be pushed into a recession by the rise in oil prices.
Although the markets waved off the information, the Commerce Department did little to alter the view that growth was tepid at best even before the outbreak of the current crisis when it reported on Friday that the economy grew at a 1.2 percent rate in the second quarter. The new figure was unchanged from a preliminary estimate released in late July.
''Oil prices are above $30 a barrel not because we lost Kuwait, but because the world is waiting for war,'' said Mr. Barbera of Shearson. ''If oil falls back to $23 to $24 a barrel, we will be in for a mild recession, and bonds will be at the point where they can stop worrying about oil, and rally.''
 
Effects Called Minimal
The recent rise in interest rates can only increase the chances of a recession, even though analysts said the effects of the increase thus far had been minimal. ''There are some people who will be staring at higher mortgage costs,'' said Mr. Brusca of Nikko Securities, ''but the run-up in long rates has not caused much harm. The question is how long this will last.''
The Persian Gulf crisis is likely to be the sole driving force in the credit markets this week. Auctions of new two-year and five-year Treasury notes will be held tomorrow and on Wednesday, but the economic calendar is very light.
Residents of the troubled Northeast hardly need more bad news, but the rise in oil prices and interest rates is almost certain to make conditions worse. To highlight the region's poor economic health, analysts at Salomon Brothers have rejiggered the ''misery index,'' a simple indicator that in previous incarnations was compiled by adding year-to-year changes in consumer prices to the civilian unemployment rate.
 
Time Lag in Job Data
Because of big time lags in reporting regional unemployment rates, Brian Jones, a Salomon economist, chose for this exercise to subtract the annual percentage change in nonfarm payroll employment from the annual percentage change in consumer prices. For the 12 months ended in June, the most recent month for which state payroll data are available, consumer price inflation outpaced employment growth by 3 percent nationally, Mr. Jones said, placing the misery index 3 percentage points above the 1986 rate.
In the Northeast, by comparison, consumer prices rose by 5.2 percentge points more than employment growth over the same period. ''Declining employment and the erosion of real consumer spending power is reflected in confidence surveys in the Northeast,'' Mr. Jones wrote in Salomon's weekly ''Comments on Credit.''
For the 12 months ended in July, the Conference Board's consumer confidence index for New England fell by more than 64 percent. The index for the mid-Atlantic region declined by 25 percent.

HEADLINE: Treasury to Auction Only Bills and Notes

 BODY:
Treasury financings this week are confined to bill and note auctions, beginning with today's regular weekly auction of three- and six-month bills. Tomorrow there will be an auction of new two-year notes and on Wednesday an auction of new five-year notes.
By late Friday the rate on the three-month bill was 7.46 percent and 7.48 percent for a six-month bill. In when-issued trading the two-year note was offered at a price to yield 8.26 percent, while the yield on a five-year note was 8.67 percent.

HEADLINE: Official Urges Lower Rates

BYLINE: Reuters

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug 26

 BODY:
Commerce Secretary Robert A. Mosbacher said today in a television interview that interest rates should be cut to help avoid recession.
''I think we have a slow-growth period here, and we can keep it from going into recession, but easing interest rates would certainly help a lot,'' Mr. Mosbacher said on the ABC program ''Business World.'' He said the Federal Reserve should lower rates.
The Commerce Secretary also said a free-trade agreement with Mexico was a priority now that the United States needed to find new oil sources because of the Persian Gulf crisis.
''Mexico is willing to give us all the extra oil they have, but 100,000 barrels a day is all they talk about, which is not much in the total mix. We lost over four million barrels a day worldwide,'' Mr. Mosbacher said.
