HEADLINE: The Media Business: Ad Scene;
Sponsors' New Message: Buy It, but Have Patience

BYLINE: By Randall Rothenberg

 BODY:
ADVERTISING, with its brassy promise of immediate, when-it-absolutely-positively-has-to-be-there-overnight results, has begun to croon a softer, sweeter tune: Buy now, be satisfied later.
Consumer-product manufacturers who once vowed instantaneous answers to bad breath and engine knock are now using their television commercials and print ads to ask for a little time. The pledge of fast change embodied in the old Crest toothpaste anthem - ''Look, Ma! No cavities!'' - has given way to appeals to patience, as illustrated by the new Texaco System 3 gasoline campaign that pleads, ''Five tanks is all we ask.'' Where it once took a spoonful of Cocoa Puffs to send its tufted spokesbird into paroxysms of delight, today Grape Nuts cereal is entreating, ''Try it for a week.'' 
Audi automobiles are even requesting a ''three-year test drive.''
John Neiman, the chief creative officer at the D'Arcy Masius Benton & Bowles advertising agency in New York, believes the ad industry, if not the nation, has entered an ''era of less-than-instant gratification,'' which he contrasts to a past decade stuffed with quick fixes like ''junk bonds'' and tax cuts.
He dates the transition in the marketing Zeitgeist to 1988, a year that some political philosophers find notable for the election of George Bush as President, but that Mr. Neiman recalls as the time that Kelly Lebrock began appearing in ads for Pantene shampoo and hair conditioner.
As in conventional ads featuring celebrity spokesmen, Ms. Lebrock, a raven-haired model and actress, confessed to occasionally unruly hair and promoted Pantene as the solution. But in a startling departure from the norm, she conceded: ''It won't happen overnight. But it will happen.''
It may be hard to believe that the kingdom of one-hour Martinizing and fast-food franchises is becoming the land of deferred gratification. But there are tantalizing, if anecdotal, bits of evidence. Sales of some microwave products have begun to plummet; microwave pizza sales dropped 16.6 percent by the end of March from a year earlier, according to Arbitron/ SAMI, the research service.
More telling, perhaps, is that sales of ground coffee increased by two million pounds last year, while instant coffee sales fell by six million pounds, according to John C. Maxwell, an analyst with Wheat First Securities in Richmond.
D.M.B.&B.'s own campaigns for Clearasil acne medication well represent the shift. About a year and a half ago, the agency released a commercial entitled ''Double Zap.'' A panoply of fast-moving images set against a throbbing musical undercurrent, the rock-video-style ad opened with an announcer asserting: ''You can run but you can't hide. You zap one pimple, another one appears. You need to zap, zap, double zap your zits.'' Sure enough, with one brush of a Clearasil pad, the commercial's distraught protagonist is out and about, his arm around his girlfriend, his face miraculously unblemished.
This year, the agency introduced a new commercial - a week in the life of an auburn-haired lass played out to the more amiable early 60's song, ''Da Do Run Run Run.'' On Monday, she discovers a facial imperfection. On Tuesday, Wednesday and Thursday, she applies Clearasil, allowing her to venture out happily on Friday with ''a guy named Bill.''
The ad concludes, ''Clearasil guarantees you fewer pimples in five days or your money back.''
Mr. Neiman attributes the change in marketing strategies to consumer cynicism that has been fed by television programming and advertising. ''We are in an era saturated by public-service ads and other images showing the problems of AIDS and drugs, which tell people that solutions will take years,'' he said. ''A decade ago, everything was 'Ozzie and Harriet' happy endings. But today, you try to run a commercial that says, 'Try this - you'll feel good overnight,' after an AIDS or anti-drug ad, and consumers will turn you off.''
Barbara Feigin, research director at Grey Advertising, the agency responsible for the Pantene and Grape Nuts campaigns, puts a more positive spin on the social changes driving the deferred-gratification ads. Americans aren't more cynical, she says; they are more mature.
''America is really getting older and wiser,'' she said, noting that the median age of the American population today is 33, up from about 30 a decade ago. ''As we asked the marketing implications, we realized you don't really need to argue the consumer into believing.''
A Grey survey of 477 households released earlier this year even determined that more than half the American population, representing two-thirds of all spending power in the country, fitted in a category the agency called ''the New Grown-Ups,'' who are more concerned than the generation that preceded them about home, hearth, family and the future.
Of course, the ''era of less-than-instant gratification'' may be nothing more than Madison Avenue chasing its own video shadows. F. Thomas Juster, an economist at the University of Michigan's Survey Research Center, which conducts monthly surveys of consumer attitudes and behavior, noted that the savings rate, which has been declining for years, continued to fall, and that home-equity loans and other forms of borrowing continued to rise.
''The casual evidence all goes in the direction of saying that people are, if anything, more consumption-oriented and more instant-gratification-oriented,'' Mr. Juster said. ''People are spending more and saving less. Everything is, 'let's get it today.' ''

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL REPORT;
Gulf Crisis Has Brazil in a Tailspin

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: RIO DE JANEIRO

 BODY:
Shock waves from the crisis in the Persian Gulf are rippling through Brazil's economy, swelling the bill on oil imports, threatening an anti-inflation program, deepening a recession and freezing a close trade relationship with Iraq.
The rise in oil prices, to more than $30 a barrel from $18, is expected to cost the cash-strapped nation an extra $1.6 billion for this year. Last year, Brazil imported $3.4 billion worth of oil.
To pay the extra oil costs, Brazil is expected to draw down its foreign exchange reserves that would otherwise have gone to service the foreign debt or to pay for productive imports. 
 
Drop in G.N.P. Seen
''Brazil has become poorer,'' said Antonio Kandir, the Secretary of Economic Policy. Before the oil price increases, economists predicted that Brazil's gross national product would shrink by 3 percent this year.
Before Iraq invaded Kuwait on Aug. 2, the two countries supplied a third of Brazil's oil imports. Brazil is negotiating replacement purchases from Iran, Malaysia and Venezuela. Brazil imports about half of the oil it uses, down from 85 percent during the 1973 oil crisis.
To cope with the higher oil prices, Brazil's Government has raised domestic oil prices. In the coming days, it is expected to accelerate the price increases for domestic fuel, impose limited rationing and increase the efficiency of an existing alternative energy program that uses alcohol as automobile fuel.
 
Inflation Plan at Risk
The sharp rise in oil prices may shake the program of President Fernando Collor de Mello to reduce inflation. Since taking office in March, Mr. Collor has cut Brazil's monthly inflation rate to about 10 percent, from 80 percent.
A spurt of inflation next month would be politically inopportune for Mr. Collor, since congressional and gubernatorial elections are on Oct. 3.
Although Brazil has maintained an extensive trading relationship with Iraq, Brazilian diplomats have vowed to abide by the United Nations embargo. Last year, Brazil was Iraq's second-largest source of industrial imports, after the United States. And Iraq was Brazil's eighth-largest trading partner.
In the 1980's, Brazil was an important supplier to Iraq of tanks, rockets, cars, canned beef products and frozen chickens. Since the 1970's, Brazilians have built hotels, highways and a railroad in Iraq, and have conducted an engineering study for Baghdad's subway.
During the eight-year war between Iraq and Iran, heavy Iraqi arms purchases virtually created an arms industry outside Sao Paulo. But last year, Iraq defaulted on weapons bills totaling about $120 million. Because of the defaults, a rocket maker, Avibras Aerospacial S.A., and a tank manufacturer, Engesa, filed for protection from creditors this year.
In a deal that could save the tank maker, Engesa is negotiating to sell 318 Engesa Osorio tanks to Saudi Arabia for $2.2 billion.
 
Company Is Owed $530 Million
In another Brazilian venture, Construtora Mendes Junior, a construction company, is building a highway and an irrigation system in Iraq. But the company is reportedly owed $530 million by the country, part of a larger $1.3 billion debt owed by Iraq to public and private business creditors.
This year, as Iraq's reputation as a delinquent debtor spread, Brazilian sales tumbled. Last year, Brazil sold $343 million in manufactured goods to Iraq. In the first six months of this year, sales totaled $84 million - the worst such period in a decade.
Besides the sweeping economic and political problems facing Brazil, 290 Brazilians are trapped in Iraq, a legacy of the once-flourishing trade relationship. Many Brazilians say the Iraqis have delayed issuing exit visas to put pressure on Brazil to resume arms and food shipments.
''Iraq blackmails Brazil,'' read a recent front-page headline in Rio's independent daily newspaper, Jornal do Brasil.
The term ''hostages'' has also crept into news accounts about the Brazilians in Iraq, largely engineers and construction workers.
 
Pressure From Employers
Demanding ''the liberation of all Brazilians'' in Iraq, five Brazilian companies with employees in Iraq have written to Brazil's Foreign Minister, Francisco Rezek, objecting to ''the nonissuance of exit visas by Iraqi authorities.''
Counseling patience, the Foreign Minister said in a recently published interview: ''The use of the word 'hostage' represents an expansion. It would be an abuse of the word's meaning.''
Brazil ''never imagined that breaking the commercial embargo of Iraq would help the Brazilians who are there,'' Jose Vicente Pimentel, a Foreign Ministry spokesman, said.
In a front-page editorial, Jornal do Brasil recently called Iraq's action ''crazy.''
Magazines have also printed sympathetic interviews with Kuwait's Ambassador in Brasilia, Faisal Rashe al-Ghais. After Iraqi diplomats demanded to take possession of the Kuwaiti Embassy last week, the Kuwaiti diplomat gave interviews with an automatic weapon by his desk.

Some 290 Brazilians are still trapped in Iraq. (Associated Press); graph of Brazil's foreign oil shipments (Source: Petrobas, Petroleum Intelligence Weekly)

PUB-DATE: August 28, 1990

SECTION: Section A; Page 1, Column 6; Foreign Desk

LENGTH: 1642 words

HEADLINE: CONFRONTATION IN THE GULF;
IRAQ IS SAID TO ORDER SHIPS NOT TO OPPOSE INSPECTIONS; U.S. EXPELS BAGHDAD ENVOYS

BYLINE: By ANDREW ROSENTHAL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: KENNEBUNKPORT, Me., Aug. 27

 BODY:
Iraq has reversed orders to its merchant ships, instructing them not to resist if American or other naval forces try to board the vessels for inspections as part of the international blockade, United States officials said today.
Officials in Washington interpreted the new orders as a possibly conciliatory move by Iraq in advance of a mediation mission to the Middle East by the United Nations Secretary General, Javier Perez de Cuellar.
But President Bush said today that he saw no immediate hope for ''fruitful negotiations'' to end the 26-day-old Persian Gulf standoff. The Administration ordered two-thirds of the staff of the Iraqi Embassy in Washington to leave the country, and there were new reports of roundups of foreigners in Iraq and Kuwait.
 
Bush Firm on Iraqi Pullout
In remarks that seemed intended to avoid any sign of faltering as the United States expands its presence in the gulf, Mr. Bush said at a news conference at his vacation home here that there was ''no flexibility'' on the United States' basic demand that President Saddam Hussein unconditionally withdraw Iraqi forces from Kuwait. 
Still, Mr. Bush said that the United States was willing to open a dialogue with Baghdad. To achieve a Iraqi withdrawal, he said, ''you have to talk.''
Mr. Perez de Cuellar said today that he would seek the ''full implementation'' of Security Council resolutions demanding that Baghdad withdraw from Kuwait and free all detained foreigners. [Page A13.] Some Administration officials cited the Secretary General's impending visit to the Middle East as a sign that the gulf crisis might enter a lull in which the focus would shift from military tensions to diplomatic overtures.
 
U.S. Commanders 'on Hold'
Although the American military buildup in Saudi Arabia and nearby waters continued unabated today, Pentagon officials said their commanders in the Middle East were essentially ''on hold'' while diplomats renew efforts to solve the crisis.
Western European nations also continued to bolster their forces. France announced that it was sending combat helicopters to Saudi Arabia, the first ground forces committed by that country since Iraq invaded Kuwait on Aug. 2, and Qatar joined other Arab states in the Persian Gulf in making its military installations available to the multinational force.
Administration and Pentagon officials in Washington said they had received intelligence reports from the Middle East indicating that Iraq had rescinded an order that the captains of its merchant ships continue to defy the blockade of Iraqi shipping.
The source of the reports was not disclosed, but it was presumed that they came from intercepted radio messages. The officials said that the Iraqi captains, who have already ignored two sets of warning shots from United States Navy vessels, have since been ordered to stop on demand and to cooperate with boarding parties. Public Relations Move? The officials said the new orders might simply be a public relations gesture by Baghdad in advance of Mr. Perez de Cuellar's trip to Jordan, where he will meet on Thursday with Foreign Minister Tariq Aziz of Iraq. But the officials said the new orders might genuinely be intended to avert the possibility of warfare at a potential flashpoint in the Middle East, the waters around the Arabian Peninsula where scores of warships are trying to cut off all Iraqi shipping.
Administration officials noted that the Iraqi military has made no overt move on the United States Embassy in Kuwait City. The mission is one of several that have been surrounded by Iraqi troops.
But Margaret D. Tutwiler, the State Department spokeswoman, said in Washington that Iraqi forces had detained another 7 American citizens in Kuwait since Friday, taking 6 of them to the Regency Hotel there and the seventh to an undisclosed location. That brings the numbered of detained Americans to 63, with 42 in Iraq and 21 in Kuwait.
The State Department said it learned late tonight that an American man in his 50s died in Basra, Iraq, of a heart attack. The Iraqi Foreign Ministry informed the American Embassy of the death and officials were trying to confirm the man's identity. It was not clear whether he was one of the Americans held by the Iraqis, a State Department spokeswoman said.
 
Detention of 3 Denounced
Earlier, Marlin Fitzwater, the White House spokesman, denounced an Iraqi decision to prevent three young American men from leaving Iraq late Sunday with 52 other dependents of diplomats who crossed into Turkey. Mr. Fitzwater said Iraq had ''once again reneged on its pledge'' to allow the dependents to leave the country.
The three were sent back to the United States Embassy in Baghdad after they were denied permission to cross the border with the rest of their convoy. A State Department spokesman said the three dependents were ''apparently detained because they were males over the age of 18.''
Of the 52 freed, 47 are expected to arrive around midday Tuesday on a chartered military plane at Andrews Air Force Base in suburban Maryland, outside Washington. The other 5 will travel to points outside the United States. The Americans released included 19 women and 33 children, including 10 under the age of 3.
In London, the Foreign Office said the Iraqis rounded up 8 more Britons in Kuwait on Sunday night and detained them at a ''civilian establishment'' in Kuwait City. It said it had learned that two others had been detained at an earlier date, increasing the number of Britons held to 157.
Mr. Bush said he was confident that Iraq would not retaliate militarily for the United States' decision to expel 36 of the 55 Iraqi diplomats and nondiplomatic staff members of the embassy in Washington. The State Department gave the Iraqi Embassy until 4:30 P.M. Thursday to comply with the expulsion, which was ordered in retaliation for the Iraqi crackdown on the American Embassy in Kuwait.
Iraq announced the annexation of Kuwait and subsequently ordered all foreign embassies closed. The United States and many other countries have defied that order, saying that they do not recognize the annexation. #7 of 36 Are Diplomats The State Department said that 7 of the 36 Iraqis covered by the expulsion order are accredited diplomats, and that the other 29 are nondiplomatic staff members.
Miss Tutwiler said the Deputy Secretary of State, Lawrence S. Eagleburger, summoned the Iraqi Ambassador, Mohammed al-Mashat, to the State Department today to inform him of the expulsion order. The Iraqi envoy called the action ''unfair, unjust, uncalled for, because we haven't touched your embassy in Baghdad.''
The State Department was also preparing a diplomatic note that would restrict the 19 remaining diplomatic personnel to a 25-mile radius around the Iraqi Embassy, revoke multiple-entry privileges for Iraqi diplomats, and restrict the dispersal of money by the Iraqi Embassy to Iraqi students, Miss Tutwiler said.
She said that ''a number'' of other nations had ''indicated that they will be taking actions similar to our own.'' She did not name the other countries.
Mr. Bush, who has been traveling between Washington and his vacation home here over the past three weeks, planned to return to Washington on Tuesday to meet with members of Congress on the gulf crisis and to remain in the capital until Thursday. The President said today that he would also meet with aides to discuss energy supplies and efforts to reduce the Federal budget deficit.
 
Avoids Raising Hopes
Addressing reporters today after meeting with Prime Minister Brian Mulroney of Canada, Mr. Bush seemed anxious not to raise public expectations about the trip to the Middle East by Mr. Perez de Cuellar.
The United States is open to talks between Iraqi officials and the American charge d'affaires in Baghdad, Joseph C. Wilson 4th, Mr. Bush said. He said that if Mr. Perez de Cuellar tries to persuade the Iraqi Government to comply with United Nations resolutions demanding a withdrawal from Kuwait, ''so much the better.''
But when asked if he had become more optimistic about the hopes for a diplomatic settlement, Mr. Bush said: ''I don't particularly see more hope now because it's so clear what the world is demanding of Saddam Hussein. Clearly the objectives remain the same: Get out of Kuwait and restore the rightful leaders to their place.''
He added, ''Saddam Hussein has been so resistant to complying with international law that I don't yet see fruitful negotiations.''
Mr. Bush said he did not plan to seek assurances from Mr. Perez de Cuellar that he would not discuss any compromise with the Iraqis on the United Nations' basic demands.
''It's inconceivable to me that the Secretary General, an experienced diplomat, a good leader, would do that,'' he said. ''I think it would be gratuitous for me to discuss that with him.''
Mr. Bush said, ''There is no flexibility on Iraq getting out of Kuwait and the rulers being permitted to come back to Kuwait.''
But the Administration's position beyond that point remains unclear. Although Mr. Bush insists that the deposed Emir of Kuwait be restored to his throne, the Administration acknowledges that the gulf crisis is likely to create new pressure to democratize Kuwait's Government in some way.
 
Jordan Says U.S. Halted Ships
AMMAN, Jordan, Aug. 27 (Reuters) - Jordan said today that United States warships had intercepted three cargo ships bound for the Red Sea port of Aqaba in the last two days and forced one to turn back.
The official news agency Petra quoted the port director of Aqaba, Awad Tell, as saying that the Zorba Express, registered in St. Vincent, and the Kalidos, registered in India, were inspected and allowed to proceed but that a third ship, the SGM Paris, had been denied entry to the port. Aqaba is Jordan's only outlet to the sea.

HEADLINE: CONFRONTATION IN THE GULF;
Bush Facing Scrutiny by Congress On His Policy in the Persian Gulf

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 27

 BODY:
After 25 days of remarkably clear sailing, perhaps unparalleled in a foreign policy crisis in the last quarter-century, President Bush is about to face closer scrutiny from Congress for his policy in the Middle East.
Many members of Congress, though scattered across the country and the world on vacations or other trips, have intently followed the events in the Persian Gulf. So far, most have approved of most that Mr. Bush has said and done, but they do not see clearly where the United States goes from here.
Few are openly critical, many are apprehensive, some are fearful.
''This is the easy time for the President,'' said Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato of New York, a conservative Republican. ''He's done terrific so far, rallying the world behind his policy, responding vigorously to aggression. But now the real test starts. A lot of people in Congress are going to start to ask pointed questions.'' 
Representative Dante B. Fascell, the Florida Democrat who heads the House Foreign Affairs Committee, said that ''the time has come to deepen and broaden the national dialogue on steps yet to be taken.''
The questioning may begin in earnest Tuesday, when Mr. Bush, again interrupting his vacation in Kennebunkport, Me., returns to Washington to meet at the White House with the Congressional leadership and then at the Old Executive Office Building with senators and representatives from committees concerned with foreign and military policy.
At first, the inquiries are likely to be mild, and the Administration can count on stalwarts like Senator Sam Nunn, the Georgia Democrat who heads the Senate Armed Services Committee, to mute whatever doubts they have. Visiting American troops in Saudi Arabia today, the influential Mr. Nunn was supportive, telling a small group of marines, ''This is the world against Saddam Hussein.''
 
Some of the Questions
From comments made already, and all but drowned out by the chorus of praise for Mr. Bush, it is possible to discern some of the things on the minds of the lawmakers: Who's paying for this? What's the long-term goal? What about the hostages?
A more aggressive American policy, involving an attack on Iraqi military or Governmental installations, would certainly arouse passions on Capitol Hill. Representative Les Aspin, the Wisconsin Democrat who heads the House Armed Services Committee, remarked that the President does not now have ''the political mandate to conduct a military attack on objectives inside Kuwait or inside Iraq.''
But even without such a catalyst, many foresee much more intense Congressional scrutiny when the current recess ends and Congress as a whole returns to Washington on Sept. 5.
A prominent Republican Representative, speaking on condition that he not be identified, said that time was the enemy of the President's policy, and that ''as the Iraqis work on the hostage families and whip up emotions in this country, George Bush is going to feel plenty of heat from people on the Hill.''
Hoping to delay that as long as possible, Mr. Bush has been reluctant to talk very much about the fate of the Americans trapped in Iraq and Kuwait. His aides say that anything the President might say would inflame the situation.
On television on Sunday, Senator Bob Kerrey, a Nebraska Democrat who won the Medal of Honor during the Vietnam War, inquired into a potential weak spot, saying that he could not support a policy based on the idea that ''we're going to go there and have our young people die so that we can have cheap oil here at home.''
Anticipating that criticism, Administration officials in recent days have been emphasizing the need to set right a political wrong - Iraq's occupation of Kuwait - rather than the need to avoid an economic peril: the loss of the Arabian peninsula's vast oil reserves, which Mr. Bush made much of in a speech in Houston two weeks ago.
 
Concern About Objectives
Then there is the question of objectives. Some members of Congress, mainly conservatives, have expressed concern about the President's pledge to reinstate the Emir of Kuwait, whom they regard as an antidemocratic feudal oligarch, as the ruler of a little sheikdom.
Others, led by Mr. Aspin, argue not that the President has set dubious goals but that he has failed to aim far enough. The Wisconsin Democrat said the President should state a clear goal: ''Saddam Hussein has got to go, or his army has got to go.'' Otherwise, he said, the United States will be faced with the problem of Baghdad more or less permanently in a position to threaten Saudi Arabia and influence its oil prices.
''I'm afraid we're in a no-win situation,'' Mr. D'Amato said in a telephone interview from Cairo. ''We say the embargo will eventually force Saddam to pull back because he won't be able to feed his people. But if there is famine, that will create a moral dilemma for us. Can you imagine the TV pictures of starving kids?
''That will inevitably force a lifting of the blockade on food.''
Sen. Daniel Patrick Moynihan, Democrat of New York, argues that Congress has been quiescent at least partly because it approves of handling the problem through the United Nations, ''without the surreptitiousness shown by the White House in many past crises.'' It is therefore willing, he said, to back off from any intensive examination of what the White House does.
But most people on Capitol Hill see little disposition in Congress to abandon the role in making foreign policy that it has hacked out for itself in a series of struggles with the Executive Branch since the Vietnam War.
Instead, they see a Presidential policy that has so far won wide support - in Congress and among foreign governments - but that will almost certainly lose some of that support as time goes by. By moving to reassure Congress through Tuesday's large-scale briefing, the first of its kind since the crisis began, Mr. Bush is trying to solidify his support while his political leverage is greatest.
 
Nunn Wants More Arabs
IN SAUDI ARABIA, Aug. 27 (Special to The New York Times) - Senator Nunn said today that there was not enough Arab participation in the international force here and that a greater effort had to be made to bring Arab troops to Saudi Arabia.
Mr. Nunn said he would bring up the matter with President Hosni Mubarak of Egypt. Arab forces are assigned in the north, but in relatively small numbers. Egypt's forces here are believed to number several thousand.
Senator Nunn was part of the first Congressional group to visit here. Other members of the group were the Senate minority leader, Bob Dole of Kansas; Senator John W. Warner of Virginia, the ranking Republican member of the Armed Services Committee; Senator Jake Garn, a Utah Republican; Senator D'Amato; Senator Don Nickles, Republican of Oklahoma; Senator Steve Symms, Republican of Idaho, and Senator Connie Mack, Republican of Florida.
The Senators mingled with Marine and Army troops, taking down phone numbers to call wives and friends back home. Under the rules imposed by the Pentagon on reporters here, the location cannot be discloed.
The four-hour visit here was one stop in a six-nation trip, which includes visits to Eastern Europe and the Soviet Union. Before speaking to reporters, the Senators were briefed by the troops' commander, Gen. Norman Schwarzkopf, the head of the Central Command, who is eager to maintain Congressional support for the mission.
Senator Nunn said the United States had assembled a sufficent force to deter an Iraqi attack.
''An Iraqi attack on Saudi Arabia would be national suicide,'' Mr. Nunn said. ''You cannot say that the man won't do it. But if he is logical he won't do it.''

LENGTH: 1719 words

HEADLINE: Tiny Tonga Seeks Satellite Empire in Space

BYLINE: By EDMUND L. ANDREWS

 BODY:
Spurred by an ambitious American entrepreneur, a tiny South Pacific kingdom has started what amounts to a turf battle in space that could affect the future control of satellite communications between Asia and the United States.
The island nation of Tonga has seized on a loophole in international law to lay claim to the last 16 desirable unoccupied orbital parking spaces for satellites that can link Asia, the Pacific and the United States. Control of the spaces, called ''slots,'' determines who can provide satellite communications to a given part of the world. The number of these spaces above the earth is sharply limited by international law to prevent interference between satellites, but they can be reserved at no cost by nearly any nation on an essentially first-come, first-served basis.
Before Tonga's move caught it by surprise, the 119-nation consortium that provides most international satellite service, known as Intelsat, pretty much thought it had a lock on the slots. 
Intelsat officials now contend that Tonga, with an economy based largely on fishing, coconuts and foreign aid, is merely a front for financial speculation by a slick American operator, Matt C. Nilson. Intelsat, of which the United States is a member, contends Mr. Nilson's goal is to make a quick profit by leasing the orbital slots to the highest bidders.
Intelsat is trying to persuade the international communications authorities to dismiss Tonga's ambitious claim, but experts say not much can be done unless the regulations themselves are changed. That cannot happen until the next scheduled meeting by the World Administrative Radio Conference, in 1992.
Tonga's venture into satellite communications began in 1987 when Mr. Nilson, who had been in the satellite business, persuaded King Taufa'ahau Tupou 4th to sponsor an ambitious satellite system over the Pacific.
A Government-sponsored company, Tongasat, was formed to join foreign partners to launch a fleet of satellites capable of reaching from the Middle East to Hawaii. Mr. Nilson is Tongsat's managing director and has a 20 percent stake in the venture.
Tongan officials concede that they can put up none of the money for the satellites they are proposing, and that Tongasat has only six employees. But the princess who is Tongasat's chairman seemed offended by accusations that the satellite positions are being hoarded to make a quick profit. ''Countries in Asia and the Pacific region have a need for better communications,'' said the princess, Salote Pilolevu Tuita. ''They make it sound as if we are only interested in financial gain.''
 
Sounds Like Fiction
In some ways, the dispute is reminiscent of ''The Mouse That Roared,'' a novel about a tiny country that declares war on the United States in hopes of obtaining foreign aid. But it highlights the growing clash of interests created by the soaring demand for global telecommunications, in particular between North America and the Pacific Rim nations.
The Federal Communications Commission estimates that the market for telecommunications traffic -including telephone, data and television signals - between the United States and Asia totaled $2.5 billion in 1989 and is growing 21 percent a year. Traffic within the Pacific region is growing nearly 40 percent a year. As demand for satellite capacity continues to swell, the scarcity of slots is becoming more acute and the conflicts among business and government enterprises more frequent.
Until now, the kingdom of Tonga, near the Samoan Islands and whose 100,000 citizens could all find seats at the Rose Bowl, has rarely attracted notice on the world stage. Tonga's tallest building is four stories, and its estimated gross national product of $70 million falls far short of the sales of any Fortune 500 company. Foreign aid, mostly from Australia and New Zealand, accounts for much of the national budget.
 
Out of Retirement
The driving force behind Tonga's emergence as a power in the satellite market has clearly been Mr. Nilson, the 51-year-old American who heads the Nilson Research Corporation, a telecommunications consulting firm with offices in Tonga, San Diego and Manila. He said he conceived of the venture after he retired and moved to Tonga, where he lived for two years before returning to the United States.
Mr. Nilson, who had worked for Intelsat in several jobs during the 1970's, is well known within the satellite industry. In the early 1980's, he founded a Washington company, Advanced Business Communications Inc., which aspired to develop a private domestic satellite system in the United States.
But by challenging Comsat, the Communications Satellite Corporation, which Congress created to control American satellite service, Mr. Nilson made some enemies in the industry. Still, he succeeded in obtaining licenses for orbital slots over the United States and enlisted the Hughes Aircraft Company to build and launch satellites to rival Comsat's. Hughes backed out before any satellite was launched because of concerns about an overcrowded satellite market.
 
Limitations in the Market
''It became obvious at that time the market could not have supported an additional satellite system,'' Mr. Nilson said.
Control over orbital slots determines who has the right to launch satellites serving a particular region. These satellites orbit 22,300 miles above the earth at the same speed as the earth's rotation, and are called geostationary because they appear to hover over one location. All geostationary satellites are positioned along the equator.
Under international law, satellites using the same spectrum of frequencies can be no closer than 2 degrees in longitude, limiting the total number of worldwide slots to 180. But international law also provides that any government has the right to reserve any unused slots for satellites it plans to launch, simply by registering its intention with an organization in Geneva called the International Frequency Registration Board.
Orbital slots are allocated on request to either individual sovern nations or to Intelsat on behalf of the governments that are its members.
Because Intelsat did not think it had much competition for the orbital slots, it did not rush to reserve the 16 unclaimed positions most economically feasible for a trans-Pacific communications network that Tonga now claims. And because three positions overlap, Tonga as a practical matter can use 13 for actual satellites. Besides the 16, Tonga and Britain have competing claim on two slots.
As described by Mr. Nilson, those slots would be filled by a fleet of high-powered satellites capable of sending signals strong enough to be received by small antennas in remote villages and desolate islands. To allow more optimum spacing in orbit, he said fewer than 13 satellites might be launched, to keep the strong signals from overlapping. Mr. Nilson said the service would bring communications to places that cannot be served by land-based phone lines and would be much less costly than services relying on large, expensive ground stations.
Mr. Nilson and Tongan officials say they think the system could be started for $500 million, but they concede that they have virtually none of the money themselves.
No one seemed to pay attention to Tongasat until earlier this year, when it abruptly announced that it held the rights to all remaining Pacific slots and that it was seeking investors.
 
The Last Slots Available
''Tonga currently possesses all C-band positions that were still available as of early 1988 over the Pacific,'' the company proclaimed in a news release. ''Since most of Tonga's slots are the last that can illuminate all of East Asia and still reach the U.S.A., they are attractive to investor groups.''
The statement did not mention a price, but Mr. Nilson said in a recent interview that $2 million a year for each slot might be reasonable. That would be $26 million annually for all 13 positions. If Tonga retained about half of that amount after Mr. Nilson and others got their cuts, it would still increase the Government's budget by about 20 percent.
Mr. Nilson also contended that a tracking and data relay satellite for the United States Government was encroaching on Tonga's territory. The American Government plans to let Intelsat use channels on the disputed satellite to carry some Pacific traffic. But Mr. Nilson informed the National Aeronautics and Space Administration that such a use could interfere with one of Tonga's proposed satellites. Although he did not explicitly demand a payment, he acknowledged last week that ''we should at least have some compensation if we're going to lose'' the satellite position.
Tiny Tonga has thus become a top-level concern of Intelsat, which provides most trans-Pacific satellite service.
In June, Intelsat's director general, Dean Burch, harshly denounced Tonga's plans as an abuse of international regulations that ''would set a dangerous precedent if not effectively challenged.''
Mr. Burch charged that the ''extremely large number of orbital slots involved is greatly in excess'' of Tonga's needs and that the country's real motivation was to sell them to the highest bidder.
 
Charge Adamantly Denied
Mr. Nilson adamantly denied that charge, although he acknowledged that Tonga was ready to lease its slots while the nation continued work on the system. Mr. Nilson insisted, however, that Tonga did indeed plan to sponsor the proposed system.
Intelsat is trying to persuade the International Telecommunications Union and the International Frequency Registration Board to deny recognition to Tongasat's claims to slots. Satellite experts said, however, that Tonga's actions thus far were consistent with international regulations.
''Clearly, Tongasat is a cleverly designed scheme to take advantage of loopholes in the law to benefit the Government of Tonga and the principals of Tongasat,'' said Scott Chase, editor of Via Satellite, a trade magazine based in Potomac, Md.
But Mr. Chase added that the scarcity of orbital positions might increasingly dictate that positions be allocated on the basis of price in a market-like fashion. ''Selling slots may be the wave of the future,'' he said. ''If so, Tongasat is the pioneer in financial speculation for orbital slots.''

PUB-DATE: August 28, 1990

SECTION: Section A; Page 1, Column 1; National Desk

LENGTH: 2057 words

HEADLINE: Efforts to Reshape Teaching Focus on Finding New Talent

SERIES: TOMORROW'S TEACHERS: SECOND OF THREE ARTICLES.

BYLINE: By SUSAN CHIRA

DATELINE: COLLEGE PARK, Md.

 BODY:
To become a teacher, John Davis gave up his job as a treasurer for the teamsters' union and took a pay cut of $10,000 a year.
''So many children grow up now without any male role model,'' said Mr. Davis, who is 35 years old. ''I'm starting out teaching with what I started out with eight years ago at the union. But if you're really not enjoying what you're doing, you can't pay for that.''
Mr. Davis is one of a small but growing number of people who are switching to teaching in mid-career. He was able to make that change because of an intensive master's degree program at the University of Maryland in one of dozens of innovative approaches across the country aimed at attracting new talent to teaching and helping such people qualify faster for teaching jobs. 
Acute shortages of minority teachers, of teachers who excel academically and of those with math and science expertise continue in the nation's schools, and teaching's low status as a profession is hobbling efforts to recruit and retain the best teachers.
In response, the struggle to attract and train potential teachers has taken new turns. On one track, programs across the country are luring people with strong math and science backgrounds into teaching.
On another track, to increase the number of minority teachers, educators are reaching into high schools to promote teaching as a career path for academically talented black and Hispanic students. And historically black colleges are revamping their teacher education programs to help their graduates meet tougher state standards for teacher entry.
Lastly, to make sure America's schools are attractive to the very kinds of people they need, a nationwide effort to reshape schools and improve teachers' working conditions is under way.
A Central Issue: How Much Training Beaming and clutching balloons, Mr. Davis and his fellow graduates at the University of Maryland marched before their families this summer as they received their master's degrees in education. Although the university's program is not aimed specifically at math and science teachers, many of its graduates do have strong math and science backgrounds.
Brenda Stamps, 31, majored in biology and chemistry in college and went on to work in scientific laboratories. Sarah Nobis Baden, 26, put her economics degree to work with the General Accounting Office, analyzing the effects of the Clean Air Act.
Both had good jobs, but found them unsatisfying. ''One of the reasons I joined the G.A.O. was to serve the public,'' Mrs. Baden said. ''But it takes about a year for the reports to get out, and I found more immediate rewards in teaching.''
Spurred by their own idealism, publicity about an education crisis and the hope that ongoing changes are improving teachers' lot, others like Mrs. Baden are signing on. Reaching New Teachers, a nationwide recruitment campaign, receives more than 600 calls a day on its toll-free number. The Association of American Colleges for Teacher Education reports a 200 percent rise in enrollments of students who already have college degrees.
Dozens of other programs at the nation's universities offer new programs for a master's degree in math and science education; others retrain teachers for math and science education.
Most of these programs are designed as quicker alternatives to traditional teacher education courses. The programs vary widely in quality and include intensive master's programs as well as emergency certification programs that allow career-switchers or bright college graduates into the classroom after only a summer, or even a few days, of training.
At least 32 states have adopted alternative certification programs. Researchers have found that such programs attract more members of minorities and more people who are willing to teach in inner-city schools. And advocates say that career-switchers or people who could take higher-paying jobs will not be willing to invest the time and money for traditional teacher-training programs.
But Prof. Linda Darling-Hammond of Columbia University's Teachers College, among others, believes the programs with the scantest training undermine efforts to improve the status and professionalism of teaching by suggesting that practically anyone can walk into a classroom and start teaching. Her research showed that many graduates of such programs were not prepared for the demands of teaching.
The only solution, she said, is to find money for better training.
C. Emily Feistritzer, director of the National Center for Education Information, sees another pitfall: Many school administrators are reluctant to hire career-switchers, even if they are better qualified. Although some estimates are higher, her research indicates that 12,000 of one million teachers hired in the last five years came from such certification programs.
''I think there's a lot of anecdotal evidence that many of the brightest people are being turned away from teaching,'' she said. ''They cost more, and to some school officials, they're a threat. I've had superintendents tell me, 'I don't want those people in my school.' ''
Minority Teachers: A Daunting Task Of all the efforts to recruit new teachers nationwide, few are more intensive, or more daunting, than the search for talented minority teachers.
Aspiring teachers like Sondra Kendricks, a 25-year-old education major at Illinois State University, are all too scarce. One of the few blacks in her class, Ms. Kendricks is a single mother who intends to teach in Chicago.
''With the birth of my son, I became worried about what would happen to young boys,'' she said. ''I feel like I owe a debt.''
At least one-third of the nation's schoolchildren will be members of minorities by the end of this decade, but only about 10 percent of the nation's teachers are black, Asian or Hispanic. The number of aspiring teachers who are members of minorities is even smaller, about 8 percent.
High failure rates for some minority children, especially black males, have convinced many educators that students need to see examples of successful minority teachers and that some students may be more effectively motivated by people with backgrounds similar to their own. Experts in minority recruitment, like Barbara Hatton of the Ford Foundation, argue that more minority teachers are also necessary to demonstrate to white children that members of minorities can hold positions of authority.
A combination of poverty, high school failure rates and cutbacks in Federal student loans means that fewer minority members have the college degree necessary to become a teacher, said Nathaniel Jackson of the Southern Education Foundation, which administers several minority teacher recruitment programs.
Even more alarming, the nationwide move to raise standards for teachers has disqualified many minority members who aspire to be teachers. A study in 19 states found that the average pass rate for the National Teachers Examination, one of the most widely used entry tests, was 15 percent to 50 percent for blacks. The rate for whites ranged from 71 percent to 96 percent.
''Many of our students come with a deficit in basic skills,'' said Burnett Joiner, executive academic dean and dean of the college of education at Grambling State University in Louisiana. ''Many have difficulty reading, writing, in math and English. Many of our students have not been socialized into working hard.''
When Mr. Joiner arrived at Grambling State in 1980, only 5 percent of its students were able to pass the National Teachers Examination. Part of the reason, he and others said, is that the test is culturally biased and does not address teaching skills.
In 1992, the Educational Testing Service, which writes the exam, will offer a redesigned version that addresses these complaints. Carol Dwyer, who has overseen the planned changes, said that the new, three-stage test would be fairer because it would include observations of teachers' performance in the classrooms as well as results from testing basic skills and knowledge of subject matter.
After analyzing his students' test results, Mr. Joiner revamped the entire teacher education program at Grambling, imposing tough general education requirements, designing remedial courses in basic skills and including courses that covered the subjects students would see on the test.
Now 85 percent of the students pass. But there is a cost. Grambling State now graduates only half as many teacher education students.
New Places to Look, New Ways to Train To increase the number of minority teachers, educators and foundations are sponsoring programs that help minority children succeed in school, and are looking for prospective minority teachers in new places, including among retiring soldiers and teaching assistants in public schools.
Vicki Rodgers-Tamakloe spent 17 years climbing the ranks at a child care center before becoming a teacher's aide in the Montgomery County schools in Maryland. Thanks to a new program jointly sponsored by the schools and the University of Maryland at College Park, Mrs. Rodgers-Tamakloe, who is 36, could work at night and during the summers toward a master's degree in education, while continuing in her job.
Other programs reach out to the large pool of teaching assistants with degrees from two-year community colleges.
Louisiana has a four-year commitment to encourage minority teachers, beginning with a future teachers club in middle school, Mr. Jackson said. If students do well in the club, they may enter a paid teaching internship program and then move on to summer courses at Xavier or Tulane Universities. The best are offered scholarships at Grambling or other colleges.
For all the encouraging programs under way, there is a final obstacle: Minority members now have broader career choices. In 1985, Professor Darling-Hammond said, 8,800 minority students who earned college degrees in the previous year entered teaching, but 24,000 went to law or medical schools, and 30,000 were in other professional degree programs.
Trend in Schools: Reshaping Teaching The most ambitious efforts to find and keep good teachers center on reshaping the profession.
As long as teachers have no voice in shaping the rules that bind them and no chance to move up without leaving the classroom, experts say, many of the brightest people will continue to shun teaching.
That is one driving force behind the school restructuring movement that is under way across the country. In Kentucky, New York, Florida and elsewhere, the chance to work with principals and parents to improve schools is being offered to teachers.
In some schools in Miami, New York City and Rochester, for example, teachers join management teams that run schools, choose textbooks, decide discipline policy and set budgets. Kentucky is preparing to repeal all its current education regulations. Instead, it will set learning goals and allow school management teams to plan on how to achieve them.
A new nationwide poll of teachers released Monday by the National Center for Education Information showed that teachers overwhelmingly approve changes that would give them more voice in running schools.
Teachers unions and school districts across the country are also cooperating on programs that will allow teachers to earn more money and take more responsibility as they grow more experienced.
In the Ohio cities of Toledo and Cincinnati, for example, the American Federation of Teachers and local school districts have established ''career ladder'' programs, allowing those who demonstrate exceptional teaching ability to become master teachers.
Master teachers earn more and might assume a range of responsibilities. At least 29 states are trying career ladder programs or merit pay to reward and retain excellent teachers.
While it is still too early to judge the restructuring efforts, they are clearly inspiring many of tomorrow's teachers and attracting more and better-qualified candidates into teaching. Sarah Nobis Baden, the former Government economist, said she hoped to avoid burnout by becoming a master teacher. And Brenda Stamps, who left laboratory work for teaching, said she is confident about her choice.
''In all the time I've been out working,'' she said, ''finally I am where I need to be.''

HEADLINE: CONFRONTATION IN THE GULF;
Peacekeeping in a New Era: The Superpowers Act in Harmony

BYLINE: By ELAINE SCIOLINO, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 27

 BODY:
The new spirit of unanimity at the United Nations reflects an emerging partnership between the two superpowers at the end of the cold war era. But it also affirms a basic principle of international relations: collective action works when national interests coincide.
 
Sweeping Security
Council resolutions passed since Iraq's invasion of Kuwait have quickly turned the United Nations into a vehicle that may prevent war from breaking out on the Arabian Peninsula.
But unity is motivated less by a willingness of the five permanent members of the Security Council to relinquish power than by the realization in an international diplomatic environment conditioned by American-Soviet agreement on the need for stability, a global approach is a less costly and more efficient way to manage conflict. 
 
'Convergent Interests'
''We've had some pretty egregious violations of international law in the past, but the great powers had different interests, and in those conditions this U.N. system cannot work,'' said Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan, a New York Democrat and author of a new book on international law. ''Now the major powers have convergent interests and the mechanism of the U.N. is there waiting to be used.''
President Bush made clear today that the United Nations, however useful when it serves American interests, has its limits and that he has no intention of surrendering any authority to Secretary General Javier Perez de Cuellar when the United Nations leader begins a mission to the Middle East on Thursday.
But Mr. Bush has no cause for worry. Mr. Perez de Cuellar scrupulously avoids making any move that goes beyond the explicit instructions of the Security Council, to such an extent that he has often been criticized for inaction at times of crisis.
 
Taking the Risks
The peace mission also allows Mr. Bush to sound warlike and uncompromising while Mr. Perez de Cuellar risks nothing except the loss of face by trying to make peace abroad. ''One of the main purposes of the Secretary General is to take some of the risks that the leaders of nation states won't or can't,'' said Charles William Maynes, editor of Foreign Policy magazine and former Assistanct Secretary of State for International Organziaitons.
As for the Security Council resolutions, particularly one adopted over the weekend approving the use of force in the blockade against Iraq, they give a desirable cover of international legitimacy to an American-driven policy.
The Bush strategy contrasts with the Reagan policy. The Bush Administration is legitimizing the American response to Iraqi aggression through the United Nations. Three years ago, the Reagan Administration rejected a Soviet proposal to jointly reflag Kuwaiti tankers to protect them against Iranian attack during the Iran-Iraq war.
Even longtime diplomats suspicious of American intentions give the Bush Administration high marks for its skill and doggedness in pushing through the resolution over the weekend. The breakthrough was attributed in large part to conversations between Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d and Foreign Minister Eduard A. Shevardnadze of the Soviet Union and in messages between Washington and Beijing.
 
'Emergence of a New Order'
In an interview with Cable News Network on Saturday, Brent Scowcroft, the national security adviser, explained the diplomatic successes as resulting perhaps from ''the emergence of a new order.''
''That's a strong term, but what we're seeing is the United Nations beginning to operate as it was foreseen to operate when it was established in 1945-1946,'' he said.
But it is important to remember that the ''new order'' suits the Soviets. For the past two years, the Kremlin has become the most forceful advocate for a dynamic United Nations. Moscow's enthusiam for the world organization stems directly from a feeling that it can cut military spending and develop its own economy only in a more peaceful world.
''For the Soviets today, the U.N. is emerging not as a vehicle of retreat but as a less costly and more legitimate way to influence events,'' wrote Thomas G. Weiss of Brown University and Meryl A. Kessler of Harvard University, in a recent article on Soviet policy and the United Nations.
 
A Role in the Middle East
The Soviet decision to embrace the resolution over the weekend after days of foot-dragging also reflects Moscow's desire to take part in decision-making in the Middle East, where Moscow has been denied a role in the past.
''Everybody knew that the Americans would have intercepted the ships anyway and the Soviets would be without any influence in the crisis,'' said one Western diplomat familiar with the negotiations. ''The United Nations was the only way for Soviets to have a role.''
More and more, the interests of the superpowers have converged, and more and more the United Nations is being used to resolve regional disputes. The United Nations gave the Soviet Union a face-saving way out of Afghanistan, monitored elections in Nicaragua and has begun to mediate in the war in El Salvador.
In unguarded moments, however, some Administration officials and Western diplomats have suggested that the response of the Security Council to the crisis in the Persian Gulf may be a matter of degree as much as principle. The annexation of Kuwait is seen not just a regional conflict, but as a threat to global order and the world supply of oil, and these officials acknowledge that the international response would never have been so strong if Iraq had moved only to seize the oil fields just over the Kuwaiti border.
When Iraq invaded Iran a decade ago and seized Iran's oil-producing Khuzistan province, for example, the Security Council passed a lukewarm resolution that did not even call for Iraq to return to internationally recognized borders. There was little sympathy worldwide for Iran, which was holding American hostages at the time. In the 1980's, the Security Council reacted slowly and did not impose sanctions against Iraq when it used chemical weapons against Iranian soldiers and later against its own civilians.
Finally, in the Falkland conflict of 1982, there was a certain feeling in the Security Council that Argentina and Britain had to fight it out among themselves, despite a personal mediation effort by Mr. Perez de Cuellar.
''The Falklands didn't engage the same kind of outrage in the world,'' said one Adminsitration official. ''It was an obscure little piece of island off in the middle of nowhere. There's no way you can just sit back and let the locals fight it out in the Persian Gulf the way you could in the Falklands.''

HEADLINE: CONFRONTATION IN THE GULF: JORDAN HOPES U.N. LEADER CUTS TENSION;
In Egypt, Some Skepticism

BYLINE: By JOHN F. BURNS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CAIRO, Aug. 27

 BODY:
Egypt is eager for a breakthrough but skeptical that King Hussein will find a formula that will persuade Iraq to move toward an early withdrawal of its troops from Kuwait, diplomats here said today.
Egypt has played a leading role in Arab diplomacy from the beginning of the crisis, and its top officials, led by President Hosni Mubarak, have maintained a hectic pace of consultations with other Arab nations in an effort to avoid what Mr. Mubarak said last week would be ''a vicious war.'' 
Diplomats say that for now the Egyptians will continue to emphasize an approach of tightened economic and other pressures on Iraq, in the belief that any hint of concessions may fortify Baghdad in its repeated vow to hold on to Kuwait.
As King Hussein continued his peace mission, Mr. Mubarak sent his foreign minister, Ahmed Esmat Abdel Maguid, to Moscow on Sunday for talks with Soviet leaders.
Diplomats said this signaled Egypt's belief that the best hope for persuading President Hussein to withdraw from Kuwait was pressure from the United States and the Soviet Union, combined with a continued show of resolve by the Arab nations that voted at a summit meeting here on Aug. 10 to condemn Iraq.
Diplomats say Egypt will be wary of any diplomatic initiative that seeks to lure Baghdad into withdrawing from Kuwait by offering incentives, like the territorial concessions, loan write-offs and other payments demanded by President Hussein before the invasion.
The diplomats said Egyptian officials monitoring the latest statements by President Hussein and other top Iraqi officials had sensed that the Iraqi leader might be ''casting about'' for a way to extricate himself from the crisis, and that signaling an offer of concessions might stiffen his resolve just as his isolation was beginning to sink in.

HEADLINE: CONFRONTATION IN THE GULF;
Jordan Hopes U.N. Leader Cuts Tension

BYLINE: By JOSEPH B. TREASTER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: AMMAN, Jordan, Aug. 27

 BODY:
Jordanian officials and others expressed hope today that Secretary General Javier Perez de Cuellar of the United Nations could create a breathing space in the Persian Gulf crisis where diplomacy might, at least for now, take center stage.
Tajer Maseri, a former Jordanian Foreign Minister and now head of the foreign affairs committee in Parliament, said the crisis had reached a turning point.
''For 10 days you had the building of the war machine,'' he said this morning. ''For a couple of days it was war and talk. Now we're really moving into the diplomatic thing.'' 
A Western diplomat said, ''You get the impression that the crisis is not escalating at this moment.''
 
'A Little Bit of Hope'
An Iraqi diplomat, speaking in a telephone interview from London, said the meeting scheduled for Thursday in Amman between Mr. Perez de Cuellar and Foreign Minister Tariq Aziz of Iraq suggested ''a little bit of hope'' that war might be avoided.
''There is a small light in the matter,'' the Iraqi diplomat said. ''Let's hope the light will become bigger.''
Mr. Perez de Cuellar is respected in Iraq for having played an important role in reaching a cease-fire in the war with Iran in 1988. Iraq and Jordan are taking his visit here as a serious effort and not just as an instance in which a senior diplomat goes through what might be regarded as expected motions.
Iraq and Jordan are also hoping that the Secretary General will arrive with some sign of leeway in Washington's stand, although Washington has said this is not the case.
''If Perez de Cuellar has any mandate from the United States, overtly or covertly, I think we're on the right track,'' a Jordanian official said. ''This could be a way out for all parties, face saving and practical.''
 
Choice of Meeting Site
Diplomats and Jordanian officials speculated that Mr. Perez de Cuellar chose a site outside Iraq for the meeting because he wanted to avoid any hint of leaning toward one side. They note that despite a long friendship with Iraq, Jordan has tried to steer a neutral course in this crisis and it is situated just next door to Iraq, should the Secretary General decide to open talks directly with President Saddam Hussein.
King Hussein of Jordan, who has been insisting that the Arab leaders themselves can find a diplomatic solution, was in Libya today and planned to travel to Algeria, Tunisia, Morocco and Mauritania before going to Europe and then to Baghdad.
Some diplomats were skeptical of the effectiveness of the King's travels. Aides said he would not be in Jordan to welcome Mr. Perez de Cuellar and Mr. Aziz.
Some diplomats said the King might find it convenient to be away when foreign critics were continuing to raise questions about Jordan's reluctance to apply the United Nations economic sanctions.
While Jordan is indeed anxious about the possibility of war, all indications are that the King's popularity remains high. In a country that overwhelmingly supports President Hussein, posters with twin color portraits of the Iraqi leader and King Hussein have begun appearing in shop windows and at pro-Iraq demonstrations.
 
Seeking a Consensus
Jordanians close to the Government said the goal of the King's trip is to develop a consensus, urging withdrawal from Kuwait, among Arab countries that have appeared more understanding than others of Iraq's position. Of the countries he is visiting, only Morocco has strongly condemned Iraq.
''He wants to be able to go to Saddam and say, 'Look, this is what the countries think, let's find a solution,' '' a member of Parliament said. A Western diplomat said the King would probably try to broaden the appeal for withdrawal with ammunition gathered on his swing through Europe.
President Hussein has been admamant on staying in Kuwait. But the King and other Jordanians say they believe he is more flexible than he indicates in public. They say they believe that President Hussein was willing to withdraw from Kuwait shortly after his troops invaded but stiffened his position in response to the condemnation from a majority of leaders in the Arab League.
At the time, United States intelligence indicated that Iraqi troops were massing on the Saudi border in what was thought to be preparation for an attack. The Jordanians said then, as they do now, that they believe President Hussein has never intended to invade Saudi Arabia.
The United States, Britain and France say Iraq's departure from Kuwait is mandatory for any direct negotiations.
Mr. Perez de Cuellar has not spelled out what he plans to discuss with Mr. Aziz.
 
Americans Less Optimistic
American diplomats here would not discuss Mr. Perez de Cuellar's visit on the record, but, reflecting Washington's official position, they did not appear to share the optimism of many others here.
The United States has made clear that as it strengthens an already awesome combat force in Saudi Arabia each day, its strategy is to try to bring President Hussein to his knees with the economic blockade. It cannot reject the possibility of talks with Iraq out of hand, diplomats said, but is unlikely to encourage negotiations until it believes that concessions are more predictable.

HEADLINE: Amid Jitters, Luminaries Hunt for Dialogue

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: OSLO, Aug. 27

 BODY:
If you don't think it's tough holding a peace conference on ''resolving conflict through dialogue and democracy'' in the middle of war jitters, then just listen to this.
''Dialogue alone is not going to get Saddam Hussein out of Kuwait,'' said Conor Cruise O'Brien, the Irish diplomat, scholar and writer, giving one of the conference's keynote speeches, called ''The Anatomy of Hate.''
''Neither dialogue nor democracy are necessarily the answer to hatred or conflict,'' former President Jimmy Carter told the 50 assembled luminaries this morning, as they convened at the invitation of the Elie Wiesel Foundation for Humanity and the Norwegian Nobel Committee.
Mr. Carter asserted that Western democracies had often personalized hatred in the past, and were doing it again now, with President Hussein of Iraq joining the list of enemies ''stripped of any redeeming human characteristics.'' 
After Mr. Carter left Oslo, Mr. Wiesel, a survivor of the Holocaust who established his foundation in New York City after winning the Nobel Peace Prize from the Norwegian Committee in 1986, said he had toyed with the idea of asking the former President to undertake a Middle East peace mission.
That seemed unlikely today, with foreign troops reinforcing Saudi Arabia in the crisis caused by President Hussein's invasion of Kuwait.
Even Mr. Wiesel, who hoped the conference would adopt a declaration condemning hatred before the meeting ends on Wednesday, said privately that he also wished it could declare Mr. Hussein a war criminal who should be tried by an international tribunal.
The West German writer Gunter Grass offered his own list of potential war criminals - the German companies that he said had shipped arms to Iraq that would enable it to fire long-range rockets delivering poison gas and possibly even nuclear warheads to targets in Israel. Uncontrolled lust for profits, not hate, was the motivating force here, he said.
At a news conference outside the hotel ballroom where hate was being discussed, Nelson Mandela, visiting deputy leader of the African National Congress, was asked today why he had not talked with Chief Mangosuthu Gatsha Buthelezi of the Inkatha movement about the violence that has killed more than 500 people in clashes between the two groups this month.
''If Mr. Buthelezi wants to talk to me, I am here,'' said Mr. Mandela, who later conferred with Norwegian officials and then was to go on to Algeria. He spent much of the afternoon, at Mr. Wiesel's request, clarifying his position on the controversial 1975 resolution of the United Nations General Assembly equating Zionism and racism, gradually making it tougher as he went along, settling on this:
''If Zionism means the right of the Jewish state to retain territories which they have conquered from others, to refuse to deal with the acknowledged leaders of the Palestinians, in particular with the P.L.O.; if the Jewish state has decided not to allow the Arabs in the conquered territories to exercise the right of self determination; if protest demonstrations by the youth of the Arabs are met with brutal oppression, then of course we condemn Zionism in the strongest terms.''
''But if Zionism, as I believe, means the struggle by the Jewish people for their national home, for their own state within secure borders,'' he said, ''then Zionism is a very commendable movement which we all would like to support.''
Ehud Olmert, the Israeli Minister of Health, said, ''I expected to hear an unequivocal repudiation.'' He added that among the things Israel and Zionism were up against was leaders like President Hussein, who had openly threatened to use poison gas to kill Jews.
Some participants thought the Persian Gulf crisis had deprived the conference of its real point. Yelena Bonner, widow of the Soviet Nobel Peace Prize winner and human rights champion Andrei D. Sakharov, seemed more concerned about racism, anti-Semitism, civil strife and economic chaos in her own country. ''A lot of the talk here is just meaningless platitudes,'' she said.

LENGTH: 767 words

HEADLINE: Auto Workers Expected to Make G.M. a 'Negotiation Target'

BYLINE: By DORON P. LEVIN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DETROIT, Aug. 27

 BODY:
The United Automobile Workers is expected to name the General Motors Corporation on Tuesday as its ''negotiation target'' in talks covering half a million industry workers in the United States, just two weeks after the No. 1 auto maker granted concessions to strikers in Flint, Mich.
The naming of a target company, sometimes referred to as a ''strike target,'' sets the stage for intensive bargaining that would begin this week, leading to the Sept. 14 expiration date on the current three-year contract between the union and Big Three auto makers, G.M., the Ford Motor Company and the Chrysler Corporation.
If an agreement is not reached by Sept. 14, the union may call a strike at its designated target or continue bargaining.
The union, while declining to comment on reports that it has already selected G.M., said it would announce the target here Tuesday morning. In the last week G.M., Ford and Chrysler have each submitted tentative contract proposals to union bargainers. Each company acknowledges that it wishes to be the union's negotiating target so it can shape what typically becomes the model contract for the industry. 
 
Theory About the Target
Labor experts and union members close to bargaining talks say G.M. has been selected because it recently demonstrated in the Flint strike and by its contract proposal a surprisingly conciliatory attitude toward improving relations with blue-collar workers.
G.M. settled the Flint strike by granting workers there a job security guarantee and a reprieve from a previously announced layoff. The workers responded with an overwhelming vote to end the strike.
By making G.M. the target, the union may be seizing the opportunity of a harmonious period to significantly improve industrywide job security provisions, its highest priority, for the greatest number of workers. G.M. employs about 300,000 hourly workers. The company's relations with hourly workers historically have been the most acrimonious in the industry.
Specifically, G.M. is trying to find a way in the next labor contract to assure hourly workers guaranteed employment until they have 30 years of service, while retaining the flexibility to shut down unneeded production lines.
 
Factors in G.M. Talks
Richard Block, professor of labor relations at Michigan State University, said the aging of G.M.'s work force has made it economically feasible for the company to consider a blanket guarantee of job security, which means either providing work or paying workers who are laid-off a salary close to what they would earn on the job.
In return, Mr. Block said, G.M. may seek contract provisions that give it broad freedom to transfer workers to plants where they are needed.
''All the folks they hired in the 1960's are going to be retired in the next few years,'' said Mr. Block. ''What G.M. wants is the ability to close plants if it has to without all the political pressure and pressure from the union. If G.M. promises to keep everyone on the payroll, it may be able to do that.''
As factors strongly influencing G.M. to be more conciliatory than in the past, Mr. Block also cited the promotion of Robert Stempel as company chairman earlier this year, and G.M.'s recent gains in the domestic market. Mr. Block presumes that Mr. Stempel will want to start his post on amicable terms with the union.
''I think G.M. wants to settle the job security issue for the years to come,'' Mr. Block said. ''I know I would want to settle it if I were in Stempel's shoes.''
The current contract for the Big Three provides unemployment benefits that cover laid-off workers, depending on seniority and the reasons for layoff, for a period of several months or years; eventually, however, the benefits expire.
While the specifics of the G.M. proposal were not given, union sources who spoke on condition of anonymity said it provided for a salary rise in the first year, followed by two annual bonuses. They said G.M. is also proposing to reduce labor-related costs in parts and components factories, where workers are paid much more than in nonunion plants.
The union sources also said Chrysler's contract proposal had evoked anger and frustration among U.A.W. bargainers. Chrysler's proposal reportedly said that blanket medical insurance coverage would be replaced by health maintenance organizations and other coverage. The Big Three have targeted medical costs as a major competitive disadvantage with Japanese auto makers.
The union has said that it would help to reduce medical costs, but that it considers comprehensive medical coverage non-negotiable.

SECTION: Section A; Page 20, Column 1; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 529 words

HEADLINE: This Embargo Can Work

 BODY:
Trade embargoes have often proved feeble instruments of coercion. Those who favor quick military action against Iraq argue that the present blockade won't work, or will take many months to bite. They may prove right. 
Yet this blockade is already unique in its worldwide support and breadth. Iraq is uniquely vulnerable. The Bush Administration continues daily to detect and plug holes. And victory may not require bringing the Iraqi economy to its knees.
Perhaps more than any comparable action this century, the embargo against Iraq can work, can break President Saddam Hussein's will - provided that those who demand instant military solutions are held at bay.
Other embargoes had fatal flaws. The League of Nations approved sanctions to punish Mussolini for invading Ethiopia, but then failed to deny oil to Italy. The United Nations embargoed oil shipments to Rhodesia, which obtained its supplies through South Africa instead. The siege of Iraq falls in a quite different category from these half-measures.
Oil and refined products account for 97 percent of Iraq's export earnings. But Baghdad cannot now sell a drop of oil. Its pipelines through Saudi Arabia and Turkey are blocked, and its tankers will now be denied passage through the gulf, if necessary by force. Thus Iraq's income has dwindled close to zero, and its financial assets abroad are frozen.
Even if Iraq could pay for any imports, its shipping routes are blocked and little is coming in by air, since many countries as part of the economic blockade have canceled flights to Iraq and denied it their airspace. The United States is making every effort to close all loopholes as soon as it learns of them. Land routes, including local trade with Jordan and Syria and smugglers' routes through Iran, are still open but probably provide a tiny fraction of Iraq's present needs.
Iraq does have some stockpiles of food, including three months' worth of rice and two months' wheat, and is expecting a good harvest. It's also true that the shared sacrifice of a siege often boosts morale, at least to start with. But home-grown food and hope will last only so far. The current sanctions have a substantial chance of forcing President Hussein to the belief that he cannot win.
It is also essential that Mr. Hussein continue to feel that he has no way around the embargo, especially no military alternative. That's why it's critical to continue the buildup of American and other forces in the Persian Gulf area. Its effects were demonstrated when U.S. officials disclosed yesterday that, in a policy reversal, he had instructed his merchant ships not to resist naval inspection.
The blockade still may prove unsuccessful, if Iraq can stretch out its stockpiles and develop local supplies - or if the will of the world community erodes. But there is little basis now for supposing either eventuality.
Iraq soon will find it painfully difficult to operate without imports. And for the embargo to achieve its purposes, it may not be necessary to bring Iraq's economy to a halt, let alone cause starvation in the streets of Baghdad. The pain for Iraq does not have to be total; it only has to be enough.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Swiss Know How to Conserve Gasoline

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Americans use ''about 350 gallons of gasoline a person'' (in a year, presumably), we learn in the Aug. 12 Week in Review. According to your adjacent graph, estimated gasoline consumption for 1989 was 2.75 billion barrels. Multiplied by 42 gallons a barrel and divided by 247.1 million Americans (1990 World Almanac), that's 467 gallons a person.
The higher figure, probably more accurate, shows dramatically the shocking wastefulness of United States transportation, in contrast not only to countries you tabulate in the accompanying graphic, but in particular to Switzerland. There a system is in use so sensible and so fuel-economical that it is a priori almost ruled out of the American way of life.
The system's basis is the simple idea that, since both people and mail have to be moved from any one town to all the other towns in the same area, why not use the same vehicles for both? Do you commute to work or want to shop or visit? Go to your post office and take the postal bus, which has appropriate capacity and leaves on schedule. Behind the bus is a small trailer, which the driver has unloaded with mail for that office and reloads for later destinations. You and the others (and the mail) get there (the post office, of course) quickly and comfortably. Service for passengers and mail is frequent.
Obviously, the two countries differ. But there must be parts of this one where similar logistics are applicable. With so much at stake, and despite our bad habits, a trial seems warranted.
 
JEROME S. SCHAUL
 Caldwell, N.J., Aug. 13, 1990
