HEADLINE: Risk in 'Boot Camp' Handling of Inmates

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Your Aug. 14 editorial in support of the ''boot camp'' approach for Rikers Island inmates fails to elaborate on its significant drawbacks. A ''bootcamp'' inmate is released without having received any additional education or having developed any additional employment skills. These programs simply release strong, healthy, unemployed young men into the community after only a brief jail term.
Particularly in light of the fact that the military has moved away from abusive or degrading training methods, using physically and verbally aggressive tactics in an effort to ''train'' inmates to act responsibly in the community is full of contradictions. In fact, the program runs the risk of turning out young men who are more aggressive and hostile than they would have been under routine imprisonment. Aggressive and hostile behavior is why many of these men are incarcerated.
While ''boot camp'' might not meet the needs of rehabilitation or community safety, it seems to meet the needs of political officials who can boast of ''doing something'' about crime.
 
HOWARD ABADINSKY
Professor of Criminal Justice
Saint Xavier College
 Chicago, Aug. 15, 1990

HEADLINE: FOREIGN AFFAIRS;
Moscow Can Help

BYLINE: By Flora Lewis

DATELINE: VENICE

 BODY:
The Security Council vote endorsing military enforcement of the U.N. blockade on Iraq was an impressive feat of diplomacy. It not only strengthened the international coalition against President Saddam Hussein; it blocked the temptation of American hawks to plunge into war.
The way the Persian Gulf crisis is resolved is going to have deep impact on the conduct of world affairs for a long time. Instead of confirming the U.S. as leading partner of all who seek a peaceful world order, going it alone could have reversed the remarkable consensus creating a new weapon for peace. 
Participants at a meeting here of Aspen Institute Italia on ''Economic Policies for a New Era: West-West and East-West'' reflected widespread relief that the era probably will not start with a bang after all. The meeting was planned long ago, but everybody came preoccupied with Iraq, oil and the questions they provoked.
A major gain so far has been the advance in American-Soviet diplomatic cooperation. Paul Nitze, former U.S. arms negotiator, has called for using the momentum to speed up more far-reaching arms reduction agreements. Mr. Nitze was once a prominent member of the ''Committee on the Present Danger,'' critical of most deals with the Soviets. Now he says President Bush ''should try radically to improve the bases for U.S.-Soviet collaboration.''
That is a turnaround. Mr. Nitze's careful proposals should weigh against the peculiar idea that the gulf threat makes further arms control and drastic restructuring of American forces undesirable. This crisis is in part a result of the global arms race, and should argue instead for a much sterner, broader attempt to block it everywhere.
The Soviets seem willing. Moscow is eager to reinforce the U.N. as the center for action. ''Everything that can be done through international or multilateral means should be done that way, and everything we do we should do together,'' said a Soviet diplomat, meaning the U.S. and the Soviets should always consult and can probably agree on measures to take.
There are some crucial contributions Moscow can make right now to advance resolution of the crisis. They are delicate and might have to be discreet, but they are a new test.
They concern intelligence. There are still several hundred Soviet military technicians in Iraq. They probably know where the hostages are. More important, Moscow knows all about Iraqi forces, air defenses, missiles, equipment, most of it Soviet-made. That information won't disarm Saddam Hussein, but it must gravely undermine his confidence if he has to suppose the U.S. has it.
Most important, no intelligence service anywhere has the K.G.B.'s intimate knowledge of Iraqi politics, especially rivalries and fault lines within the military and police establishments, some of them also organized by former Eastern European security services.
The one way to avoid war is for the Iraqis themselves to bring down their dictator. It is an illusion to think that outside pressure can induce President Hussein to pull out of Kuwait and let his forces be neutralized. That would be failure, and he knows his own people would not let him survive a show of impotence. He would more likely sacrifice them.
So the key is to convince others that their chance of survival is to get rid of him, or go down with him. He came to power through a coup. It is not an alien idea in Iraq. There is always hidden opposition, secret cliques in a tyranny. They need to be given a sense that they have more to gain than to lose in risking an overthrow.
The West, including Israel's vaunted Mossad, knows very little about the hidden intricacies of President Hussein's regime. Moscow can get word to the right people.
It is wrong to suppose the Soviets have an interest in prolonged crisis because of the windfall in high oil prices. It helps them, but not nearly enough to solve their foreign currency shortage, and their production has been declining sharply over the past two years. Prices will remain high anyway, and Western recession would hurt them too. What they need is improved production.
Before the gulf crisis, Prime Minister Ruud Lubbers of the Netherlands proposed technical aid to improve the Soviet oil industry as a way to help Eastern European democracies that will have to pay hard currency for these imports next year. West Europeans like the idea. The U.S. should support it. That would be yet another innovative example of cooperation for mutual and general benefit. It is a new era. The Soviets need help and they can contribute.

HEADLINE: Fight to House Homeless at Old Missile Base Turns Nasty

BYLINE: By ROBERT HANLEY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TAPPAN, N.Y.

 BODY:
Someone poured gasoline outside Sgt. Johnnie Peters's home at a defunct Nike missile site here last week in another expression of vandalism and hostility surrounding a plan to turn the old military base and its 36 bungalow-style homes into a project for the homeless.
The Pentagon has declared the site surplus in a cost-cutting move, and a nasty political and personal fight has ensued between town officials and an anti-poverty agency that is sponsoring the plan for the homeless.
Windows of the empty military housing have been spray-painted, mailboxes and trash cans have been stolen, and men driving past at night with bullhorns have shouted at the last five National Guard families on the base to get out.
Town officials theorize that neighbors who want to see the base bungalows razed and middle-income housing built may be behind the vandalism. 
 
Two Other Bases Affected
Similar plans for the homeless have been proposed at other surplus former Nike bases in Mahwah, N.J., and Westport, Conn. But tensions in Tappan are the highest. Until the 1960's, the three bases were part of a defensive missile ring around New York City.
The hostility here has stunned Sergeant Peters and four other Guard families left on a post that resembles a ghost town. Sergeant Peters, who has been in the New York National Guard for 15 years and whose husband has been hospitalized, says the community is indifferent to their plight.
''If the community cares about us, help us,'' she said. ''Military people can't function under conditions such as these. You don't have your freedom. Where's their heart? I need to know - where do I go? This is America. What do I do?'' The five Guard families here are not the only ones in the region on the verge of being made homeless for the sake of the civilian homeless.
 
Deadline in Mahwah
At the surplus base in Mahwah, seven New Jersey National Guard families face a Sept. 15 deadline to leave unless Bergen County, which has proposed housing the homeless at the site, the military and Mahwah officials can untangle themselves from a bureaucratic Catch 22.
Bergen County wants the seven Guard families to live with homeless people it would place at the base under a Federal law, the Stewart B. McKinney Homeless Assistance Act, which gives the homeless top priority on surplus Federal property.
But the military, apparently concerned about image, will not permit its personnel to live in projects for the homeless created under the McKinney Act. However, they would be permitted in developments that included some homeless people but created under different auspices.
''Military people cannot qualify for housing under the McKinney Act,'' said one military official familiar with the Mahwah case. ''The only way the soldiers can stay is if the town buys the property for affordable housing.''
 
Decision in Westport
Westport resolved the status of its old Nike base in that fashion. After negotiations, a advocacy group for the homeless withdrew its McKinney Act application for the site, and Westport agreed to buy it and its 16 military homes, with 13 homes set aside for low-and moderate-income residents and the three others for the homeless.
The plan will not go into effect until 11 military families now on the site are transferred to new posts by 1993.
Bergen officials say they are considering withdrawing their McKinney Act applications. Mahwah would then be clear to buy or lease the land from the Pentagon for an affordable-housing project for elderly and young people squeezed out of the private market and for Guard families unable to find new housing.
Like the five families in limbo in Tappan, the seven in Mahwah say they cannot afford private rental housing on their military housing allowance, which ranges from $500 to $700, depending on rank.
 
'We Don't Understand'
''We're devastated,'' said Sgt. Robert Vuinovich, a New Jersey National Guard member for 18 years who has a wife and two teen-aged children. ''We work for the state and Federal Government, and we don't understand why they're putting us on the outside. But we've been fighting this since January, and we're going to go down fighting.''
After the Nike missiles were phased out, the 36 homes in Tappan and the 24 in Mahwah were filled by recruiting sergeants for the regular military branches and full-time National Guard sergeants who run the administrative and training affairs of Guard companies. All the regular military personnel at Tappan and Mahwah have been transferred to new duty stations and homes, and Guard personnel with nearby families have moved in with them.
The remaining 12 families are isolated now.
''My family is in South Carolina,'' Sergeant Peters said. ''That's 856 miles from here.''
 
Leasing of Site Approved
A New York National Guard housing official, Maj. Richard Vargus, said the five Tappan families had rejected military housing at either Fort Hamilton in the Bay Ridge section of Brooklyn or Fort Totten in Bayside, Queens, in hopes that the Town of Orangetown would obtain the site and allow them to remain.
But the fate of the old base is now embroiled in a bitter struggle between Orangetown officials and Joseph J. Lundy, head of a private anti-poverty group, the Rockland Community Action Council.
The council has a McKinney Act application, approved by the Federal Department of Health and Human Services, to lease the site and put 36 homeless families there. Mr. Lundy expects that monthly state aid of about $3,200 for each of the 36 families would give the council, as the landlord, net annual income of about $1 million.
Mr. Lundy's plan drew stiff opposition in Orangetown, a community with a long tradition of self-reliance and abhorrence of Federal grants. Amid the opposition, Mr. Lundy offered a compromise on the 36 houses if the McKinney application was withdrawn: 17 houses would be preserved as affordable homes for town volunteer firemen and Civil Service workers, 14 would be set aside for Guard families, and five would be for the homeless.
 
Compromise Talks Collapse
Orangetown officials accepted that ratio but rejected Mr. Lundy's plan to keep control of the land and his refusal to restrict the five houses for the homeless to local residents.
Compromise talks have collapsed. The Rockland County Department of Social Services has withdrawn its support of the council's McKinney application, citing concern about creation of a ''welfare ghetto'' and Mr. Lundy's $1 million income projection from a 36-unit project for the homeless.
''All he talks about is money and control,'' an Orangetown Councilman, Thomas J. Swift, said of Mr. Lundy.
Mr. Lundy said he now planned a project entirely for the homeless.
''I wanted a win-win situation for everybody,'' Mr. Lundy said. ''I got my nose bloodied. My training is as a businessman with a social conscience, and my job is making capitalism and the economic system work for the benefit of the poor.'

HEADLINE: Auto Racing;
CART Eliminates Detroit Race From 1991 Indy-Car Schedule

BYLINE: By JOSEPH SIANO

 BODY:
The city of Detroit, which lost the Formula One auto race through its downtown streets in 1988, lost the race that replaced it for the last two years when Championship Auto Racing Teams voted this past weekend to drop the event from its 1991 Indy-car schedule.
The CART race, which was run this June for the second consecutive year, replaced the International Grand Prix that was run along public streets past the Detroit River and around the towering Renaissance Center. In both cases the races were dropped because the possibility of downtown development clashed with demands for track improvements and other long-term commitments that were made by the respective sanctioning bodies. 
CART was seeking a long-term contract with the race's organizers, said A. William Stokkan, CART's chairman and chief executive officer, so that it could present television networks with a firm schedule at least three years in advance when negotiating broadcast fees. But the organization could not reach an agreement with Detroit Renaissance, the nonprofit group that promotes development of downtown Detroit.
CART, the Indy-car sanctioning group, usually seeks three-year renewals with race promoters.
 
Rough Pavement
Another issue that came up after this year's race was the roughness of the pavement, which caused some drivers to suffer severe blisters on their hands. Some team owners also thought the rough track surface was causing too much damage to their cars, Stokkan said last night from Chicago.
But with the high cost of repaving the track and the uncertainty over whether the race would be run over the same roads, it became impractical for the race promoters to repave the course, Stokkan said.
Because the Detroit race course used public streets, any major downtown construction had the potential to disrupt the event. In November 1988, Formula One racing officials decided to cancel the grand prix there after seven years because Detroit Renaissance could not provide the property on which to build permanent garages.
 
No Previous Indications
Although the CART race was not a sellout either year, attendance was not considered disappointing by either side, and there were no previous indications that the race was in peril.
Robert E. McCabe, president of Detroit Renaissance, said he was surprised by CART's decision to drop the event. ''We were in negotiations with Mr. Stokkan as late as midweek last week,'' McCabe said yesterday.
Stokkan admitted that before the 1991 schedule was released yesterday, he was was unable to get to McCabe to tell him of the CART Board of Directors' decision, which was reached on Sunday before CART's race in Denver.

HEADLINE: Alternatives to Oil Move From the Lab to the Road

BYLINE: By WILLIAM K. STEVENS

 BODY:
IN the last 10 years, scientists and engineers have taken long strides toward the day when alternative energy sources can compete with oil from the Middle East, and some of the more promising alternatives are on the verge of practicality.
But a drastic cut in funds for energy research over the past decade has slowed progress in many areas. And even if the research effort is accelerated now, experts say, the energy system that has grown up around petroleum is so vast, so pervasive and so entrenched that it will be years and in most cases decades before the alternatives replace oil on a large scale.
It is clear, say the experts, that the biggest and most important gains in the struggle to wean the country from petroleum are to be made in transportation, which accounts for nearly two-thirds of all oil consumed in the United States. If alternative fuels and technologies could somehow magically displace gasoline today, they would eliminate most of the need for imported oil at one stroke. 
Now, stimulated in the last few years mostly by environmental concerns, scientists and engineers have brought to the brink of fruition, or close to it, a number of alternative ways to power motor vehicles. Many of them are based on resources that are renewable rather than depletable and that emit less pollution than gasoline.
Scientists working with advanced enzymatic techniques are steadily improving the process by which wood is converted to ethanol, a form of alcohol fuel. By making the process more efficient, they have reduced the cost of wood-derived ethanol to the equivalent of about $2.15 for a gallon of gasoline, from $5 in 1980, and they expect to reduce it to about $1.15 by the end of this decade. American gasoline prices have been around that level in the last few years and are now higher. The use of wood and other vegetation opens up a potentially vast source of renewable raw materials, or feedstocks, and researchers are developing ways to produce the wood more efficiently.
Commercially produced methanol, another form of alcohol fuel, is already as cheap or cheaper than gasoline. Recent analyses by the Environmental Protection Agency have found that it now costs 90 cents to $1.10 to buy methanol that will propel a car as far as a gallon of gasoline. And as part of an attempt to capitalize on this economic fact, the agency's emission-control technology laboratory in Ann Arbor, Mich., expects in about two weeks to begin testing the first prototype of an engine especially designed for methanol.
American automobile manufacturers, meanwhile, have built engines designed especially for ethanol-fueled autos and installed them extensively in Brazilian cars. Within the last five years auto makers here and abroad have also brought to technological maturity flexible-fuel engines that burn methanol, ethanol and gasoline interchangeably, or any mixture of the three. The blending makes it possible for the alcohol fuels to replace gasoline gradually, with a minimum of disruption in the supply system. As part of its stringent new policy against air pollution, California is to serve as a pre-production testing ground for the cars.
By the end of the century, electric vehicles may also be making a dent in urban transportation. All three major American auto companies have been working on new electric vans and cars. As part of a demonstration project, General Motors, for example, plans to begin production next month of the first 60 modern electric vehicles produced in this country.
The years and decades ahead are likely to see a vigorous competitive struggle among these fuels and technologies, as well as compressed natural gas, which is being sold experimentally as an auto fuel in the United States.
The potential of these alternatives as an antidote to dependence on imported petroleum is clear: foreign oil and petroleum products contributed 15.3 quadrillion B.T.U.'s, or ''quads,'' to the nation's energy consumption last year; the burning of gasoline, measured last year at 13.74 quads, can be said to have soaked up most of the imports.
The extent to which alternatives could displace oil in energy sectors besides transportation is less clear, some experts say. ''In theory,'' alternatives based on renewable resources ''could be directed to the replacement of oil in all sectors,'' said Dr. Thomas D. Bath of the Solar Energy Research Institute, a Government-supported national laboratory in Golden, Colo. But it would take more money and time to direct alternative resources to specific oil targets in industry, businesses and homes than in transportation, he said.
The gains would probably be incremental and piecemeal - gradually replacing oil heat in homes with solar heat, natural gas or electricity, for example, or displacing some of the remaining oil in the generation of electricity with renewable alternatives like solar energy or wind power, or with nuclear power. But oil now accounts for only 5 percent of electricity generation, leaving relatively less room for improvement in that sector. Some oil, moreover, is used as feedstocks in the production of petrochemicals and asphalt and is not energy-related. Research Progress Depends On Spending Although some of the alternatives not based on fossil fuels may soon challenge both the technology and the economics of oil, experts say, none is likely to supplant oil in a widespread fashion for some time.
''You are very quickly struck by the fact that none of them are ready at all to compete at large scale with fossil fuels,'' said Dr. William Fulkerson, an associate director of the Oak Ridge National Laboratory who oversees the lab's programs in advanced energy systems and who directed a study of the subject last year.
Federal funds for research on energy supplies declined to about $1.5 billion in 1990 from about $5 billion in 1980, in constant 1990 dollars, according to a study by Dr. John P. Holdren, professor of energy and resources at the University of California at Berkeley. ''We've allowed our programs for research and development on these new options to deteriorate drastically,'' he said. ''We are just not making the progress that would have been possible if we'd maintained those 1980 levels.''
Without an increase in research funds, according to a study published last March by five national laboratories, energy derived from renewable sources would probably grow to 15 percent of the total by 2030. The study included a wide range of renewable energy sources, including, for instance, solar energy, wind power and fuels made from wood and other types of vegetation, or biomass.
If Federal expenditures for research, development and demonstration projects were doubled, to about $3 billion a year over the next 20 years, the study calculated, renewable energy's share of total energy use could more than triple, from 8 percent in 1988 to 28 percent, by the year 2030. It found that under these conditions of accelerated research, the use of transportation fuels made from wood and other vegetation would grow the fastest, accounting for 8 quads a year by 2030.
''That seems like an awful lot of time,'' said Dr. Bath, who headed the study and whose laboratory participated in it. ''But what most people don't have a good feel for is how big the energy system is, how much capital is sunk into it. It's 5 percent of gross national product, and changes in anything that big take a lot of time.'' Because of this, he said, years are required for a new technology or fuel to spread through the system on a large scale even though it works well and its cost competes with existing energy sources.
The process might be speeded up, experts say, if the price of oil rose high enough and remained there over the long term, either because of market workings or tax policies. But energy experts say that suppliers are not inclined to convert to alternatives more quickly unless they are convinced that oil will be permanently more expensive. Temporary price shocks brought about by Middle Eastern crises are not enough, they say.
''Prudent people aren't going to go out and spend some fraction of a trillion dollars based on a dictator grabbing control and manipulating things,'' said Dr. Robert L. Hirsch, the manager of research at ARCO Oil and Gas Company in Plano, Tex., who was formerly assistant administrator of the Energy Research and Development Administration, the predecessor of the Department of Energy. Obstacles Other Fossil Fuels Have Drawbacks Generally speaking, experts say, fossil-fuel alternatives to imported oil are not likely to compete on a broad scale as transportation fuels any sooner than renewables. The fossil-fuel alternatives include, chiefly, natural gas and petroleum derived from coal and oil shale. Synfuels, as these latter have been called, were the object of an intensive federally sponsored research and development program undertaken after the 1979 oil-price shock but abandoned later as prices of crude oil plummeted. For the most part, their use also emits more air pollution than other alternatives, and some energy experts believe their emergence is unlikely in the foreseeable future.
Natural gas may play at least an interim role. Until wood and other vegetation can be more fully developed as feedstocks, for instance, it seems likely to provide the main feedstock for methanol. And coal may not be completely ruled out. Methanol's proponents say that advanced conversion techniques coupled with more efficient engines would make it possible to derive methanol from coal. They say it would emit 80 percent less carbon dioxide than gasoline, reducing an emission that many scientists fear will cause the Earth's climate to warm.
In the 1980's, the electric power industry largely weaned itself from oil, but did so mostly through increased reliance on coal, one of the most polluting of fossil fuels. From 1978 to 1988, according to Federal figures, oil's share of electricity production dropped to 5.5 percent from 16.5 percent, while coal's share rose to 60 percent from 44 percent.
With nuclear energy on hold and fusion power decades away, the industry is now straining to reduce coal's share by expanding renewable sources of energy like wind and solar power, and to develop clean-coal technologies. Some experts say it would be a mistake for the transportation industry, in switching to alternatives to oil, to go the same route. Liquid Fuels Alternatives To Gasoline As transportation fuels, methanol and ethanol share an advantage in that they can each be produced from a variety of feedstocks - for example, natural gas, garbage and even sludge for methanol, grain for ethanol, wood for both. Neither alcohol fuel emits as much pollution as gasoline when burned.
Both ethanol and methanol can also be used in existing cars if some engine modifications are made, but experts say that new engines are necessary to make the alcohol fuels fully competitive. ''We are not suggesting retrofitting,'' said Charles L. Gray Jr., the director of the E.P.A.'s Ann Arbor laboratory, where the prototype of the new methanol engine is about to be tested. Both alcohol fuels pack less energy per gallon than gasoline, and the new engines are designed to help compensate for this.
''We would probably say methanol is the fuel to beat,'' said Mr. Gray. This is largely because the enlistment of natural gas as a ready feedstock has already made methanol economically competitive. But pipeline capacity makes natural gas in the United States vulnerable to sudden shortages in times of extraordinary demand, and much of the gas envisioned as methanol feedstocks would probably come from Canada, Mexico and the Soviet Union. Moreover, the burning of methanol has been criticized as emitting twice as much formaldehyde gas, a possible carcinogen, as does the burning of gasoline. Mr. Gray says this can be readily controlled, and that methanol emits far fewer other carcinogens than gasoline.
In the United States, ethanol has so far been produced from corn and is sold as an additive that, when combined with gasoline, produces gasohol. It receives substantial Federal subsidies, however, and some experts say that ethanol production would be limited by the size of the corn crop. Scientists are therefore looking toward ethanol derived from wood and other vegetation. At the Solar Energy Research Institute, scientists are using a variety of enzymes to break down the cellulose components of wood more efficiently, producing higher yields of ethanol that in turn bring down its cost per unit of feedstock.
''If ethanol from cellulose can be commercialized,'' said Mr. Gray, the proponent of methanol, ''it will be very competitive with methanol.''
If either ethanol or methanol were to rely heavily on wood as a feedstock, some scientists say, it could put heavy pressure on forests and other stands of vegetation and could create conflicts in land use. Anticipating this, scientists at the Oak Ridge laboratory are working with universities and forestry companies to improve the per-acre productivity of trees by growing and harvesting them, much like crops, on a cycle of six to eight years. Since the growing trees would absorb carbon dioxide, they would offset the carbon dioxide emitted by the burning of ethanol or methanol.
''If you ran the system right,'' said Dr. Fulkerson, ''there would be no net addition of carbon dioxide to the atmosphere.'' Looking Ahead Electricity And Hydrogen Electric vehicles, of course, emit no pollutants at all - except for those emitted by the electric power plants that recharge the vehicles' batteries - and their development by auto companies is proceeding apace.
''I think that by 2000, the impact of electric vehicles should begin to be felt,'' said Larry O'Connell, the manager of the transportation program at the Electric Power Research Institute, who has been working with auto companies in developing electric cars and vans.
For the most part, their range is still limited by battery technology. General Motors' new van that will go into limited, preliminary production next month, for instance, has a range of 60 miles when half laden, said Mr. O'Connell. Its conventional lead-acid battery can be charged overnight; if it is charged when the van is idle throughout the day, it has achieved 175 miles in a day. Its top speed is about 50 miles an hour, half laden. The van is envisioned as an around-town delivery vehicle, and will be tested first as part of electric utilities' vehicle fleets.
The Chrysler Corporation is working on a minivan with a nickel-iron battery that represents the next advance. Its range with an overnight charge is between 110 and 120 miles and its speed is about 70 miles an hour - about the same as a gasoline version of the minivan with a 2.2-liter engine.
Cars are also being developed that use electricity for city driving but switch to gasoline for longer trips, but all of the electric vehicles are perhaps two decades away, at a minimum, from any large-scale impact since it takes years for existing vehicles to be replaced.
In the farther future, perhaps, are hydrogen-fueled vehicles. Auto companies have experimented with them off and on for years. ''It's quite a clean energy system, but the cost has always been regarded as too high,'' says Dr. Joan Ogden, a research physicist at the Center for Energy and Environmental Studies at Princeton University, who has long studied the problem. The cost could come down close to the range of gasoline, she said, if photovoltaic technology could be harnessed to the production of hydrogen, cutting its cost. A number of European companies are working in that direction, she said, but practical application will not come until some time in the next century.
In the end, given the slow rate at which any of the alternatives are likely to be adopted, many of the scientists and engineers who work with them urge a time-tested interim strategy for reducing dependence on oil: conservation and increased efficiency in the use of petroleum.

HEADLINE: PERIPHERALS;
Interesting Way to See Interest On Money

BYLINE: By L. R. SHANNON

 BODY:
FOR anyone who often deals with matters of interest - interest on money, that is - PercentEdge can be a great timesaver.
The interested party might be an accountant, lawyer or business executive dealing with such things professionally, or any consumer making large purchases or frequent investments.
PercentEdge consists of four spreadsheets that have already been set up to handle calculations for mortgages, amortization, present value and variable rate. You enter the numbers you know - in the case of a mortgage, the price of the property, the down payment and terms of the loan and so forth - and the program fills in the blanks, like the monthly payment required. 
As with any spreadsheet, you can readily play ''what if'' games with PercentEdge. If you raise the monthly payment by 10 percent, what does that do to the total price of the loan? What if you make a larger down payment? Since the answers appear almost instantly, the program encourages such explorations.
PercentEdge is easy enough to use that the manual is practically superfluous, unless you're hazy about the exact meaning of present value (''a tool for computing structured legal settlements, annuities, pensions and internal rate of return''). On-screen help is always available, and there are plenty of examples to call up. In addition, an electronic calendar and simple calculator are part of the program.
PercentEdge runs on any PC, XT, AT, PS/2 or compatible computer with at least 320 kilobytes of internal memory. A hard disk is desirable, but not essential. With a hard disk or expanded memory, you can run the program as a memory-resident utility, one that pops up in the middle of another program.
PercentEdge is an enhanced version of Josh Mitteldorf's Interest Machine, discussed here last summer. It is published by the Timeslips Corporation, 239 Western Avenue, Essex, Mass. 01929. The suggested retail price is $99.95. For the name of a dealer near you, or to order directly, call (800) 338-5314 or, in Massachusetts, (508) 768-6100. SmartGuide for DOS is a program that doesn't make the operating system of most computers any easier. It just makes it far more understandable.
Instead of hunting for the manual and trying to puzzle out programmer's prose, you just call up SmartGuide from where it is lurking in the computer's memory and look up the correct syntax for the command you want to issue. If you're not sure what a command does, the program will tell you. It even keeps track of the 15 most recent commands, so you can repeat them without retyping. That would be especially handy when you're doing housekeeping chores like copying files from one disk to another and issuing similar commands a number of times.
The manual is slim; the ways to work the program are almost perfectly obvious from the screen. It is slightly marred by the one error I noticed: You must type ''SGDOS,'' not ''SDOS,'' to load the program. But anyone who is already slightly smart about DOS could discover that.
SmartGuide for DOS works on any I.B.M.-compatible computer with at least 256 kilobytes of internal memory and DOS 2.0 or later. A hard disk is a convenience, but not required.
The program is a product of Individual Software, 125 Shoreway Road, No. 3000, San Carlos, Calif. 94070. For more information, or for the name of a dealer, call (800) 331-3313 or, in California, (800) 874-2042. The suggested list price is $29.95.
''Here it is - the last foam peanut you'll ever see from us,'' Pat Gallup, the president of MacConnection, says on the cover of the mail-order house's fall catalog.
MacConnection, which sells software and hardware for the Apple Macintosh line of computers from its headquarters in Marlow, N.H., has sworn off using the pesky packing material in its shipments. The company will switch to recyclable newsprint to stuff its boxes safely. In addition, it is stocking a greater variety of box sizes so it can package orders with as little waste space as possible.
The fall catalog, which is 51 pages plus index, may look a bit dull. That is because it is printed on recycled paper, in an experiment. However, it is full of merchandise that should make a Mac owner's mouth water. You can get a copy by calling (800) 800-4438 or writing MacConnection, 14 Mill Street, Marlow, N.H. 03456.

HEADLINE: Greenpeace Faults U.N. Panel

BYLINE: Reuters

DATELINE: STOCKHOLM, Aug. 27

 BODY:
The environmental group Greenpeace sharply criticized a key United Nations conference on global warming today, saying plans to prevent climate change were inadequate.
Greenpeace issued a statement on the first day of the four-day conference, asserting that the plans under discussion did not contain any concrete steps to prevent release of heat-trapping gases into the atmosphere. These ''greenhouse'' gases are expected to cause global warming, scientists say.
About 300 delegates from 75 countries, including Britain, the United States and Japan, are attending the session hosted by Sweden in the central town of Sundsvall. Greenpeace has observer status at the conference.
The meeting's aim is to lay the groundwork for a treaty on global warming to be adopted at the U.N. conference on environment and development in Brasilia in June 1992. The report being discussed is based on research by more than 1,000 atmospheric experts and has been issued by the U.N. Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change. It proposes that individual nations should attempt to limit or reduce the emission of greenhouse gases and says measures should also be taken to increase efficiency in energy production and energy use.
''The panel has been used by a handful of industrial countries which think in short-term economics instead of planning for coming generations,'' Greenpeace said.

LENGTH: 1217 words

HEADLINE: Critic's Notebook;
The Future of the Philadelphia And Germanism on the Rise

BYLINE: By JOHN ROCKWELL

 BODY:
Although the management of the Philadelphia Orchestra, still enmeshed in its labor negotiations, will neither comment nor confirm, it seems likely that shortly after Labor Day, Wolfgang Sawallisch will be named to succeed Riccardo Muti as the orchestra's music director.
Mr. Sawallisch, who turned 67 on Sunday, is the director of the Bavarian State Opera in Munich and is said by several music-business executives to have agreed to accept the Philadelphia post, although contract details are still to be worked out.
Known primarily as a solid and steady if not particularly imaginative opera conductor, Mr. Sawallisch will bring something rather different to the sumptuous Philadelphia ensemble than the fiery Mr. Muti has brought. Mr. Sawallisch is a German through and through, with all the debits and credits any national stereotype entails. 
He is also not alone. After a generation in which almost every nationality but the Germans won prominent music directorships in this country, North America suddenly seems flush with them and their Germanic allies. There is, of course, Kurt Masur as the newly appointed music director of the New York Philharmonic. Gunther Herbig, having spent time in Detroit, has moved on to Toronto. Christoph Eschenbach seems to be a hit in Houston; the Swede Herbert Blomstedt spent years in Dresden before going to the San Francisco Symphony; Claus Peter Flor and Bruno Weil and Christof Perick are rising stars in this country, and so is the Viennese Nikolaus Harnoncourt. And, to be sure, there are Klaus Tennstedt and Carlos Kleiber, both of whom appear here frequently (Mr. Kleiber has just been announced for the Metropolitan Opera's ''Rosenkavalier'' this fall) but who seem just a bit too mercurial to settle down to any one post. What binds these conductors together? Naturally, each is an individual, and Mr. Tennstedt and Mr. Kleiber are too flamboyant to conform neatly to any stodgy Kapellmeister stereotype.
But it is just the solid, steady kind of music making implied by the term Kapellmeister that seems to make these men desirable to American orchestras. What might seem boring to some sounds refreshingly sane to others. German conductors are sought for their supposed high seriousness, their idiomatic sympathy with the Germanic masterpieces at the core of the repertory and their skills as orchestra disciplinarians.
Indeed, an interesting possible aspect of the surge in German music directors involves the American orchestra musicians themselves. As the players participate more and more in the selection of music directors, they seem to crave the musical and even moral authority that a German conductor still symbolizes. As unionized musicians press for more power and benefits, they also seek more guidance and authority.
This shift in fashion follows a generation in which seemingly everyone but the Germans commanded music directorships. English, French, French-Swiss, Italian, Estonian, Finnish, Mexican, Indian, Japanese, Korean, even American - the whole world crowded American podiums. Mr. Tennstedt once referred to this rainbow coalition of conductors as ''the exotics.'' Germans, conversely, seemed to fall into two categories. There were old masters - Herbert von Karajan, Karl Bohm, Eugen Jochum - who didn't travel much. And there were younger conductors who didn't seem to be courted abroad. Clearly, a backlash against Nazism lingered in the musical world. But that seems to be fading in this new era of excitement about a soon to be united Germany. A disproportionate number of the best-known German conductors today are either East Germans or built their early careers there - Mr. Masur, Mr. Tennstedt, Mr. Blomstedt and Mr. Herbig among them. Paradoxically, the supposedly materialist (philosophically speaking, that is) and internationalist Communist regime in East Germany served to retain the old German provincialism and artistic idealism that seems so desirable in today's commercialized, deracinated international musical culture.
The reason for this odd juxtaposition of old-fashioned musicality and official progressivism was partly political, in that enforced isolation artificially re-created the circumstances of the pre-World War II era - as with Mr. Masur's Leipzig Gewandhaus Orchestra being largely stocked with graduates of the Leipzig Conservatory. It was partly economic, in that poverty and a worthless currency forced East Germans to stay at home and cultivate a local musical culture. And it was partly a reaction against the harshness of the Communist regime, as musicians and audiences turned inward, in classic German fashion, to find solace in art from the travails of life.
The new popularity of German conductors and things German in general makes life harder for non-Germans. One has to feel a little sorry, for instance, for Charles Dutoit. Perhaps this Swiss-French conductor is perfectly happy in Montreal, where he has been music director since 1977. But one can't help think he might have been chosen in either New York or Philadelphia, where he is known to have been a candidate.
Yet he was not, and the reason seems to be a perception among many musicians and administrators that he is not strong enough for the task. Maybe he is, maybe he isn't. But orchestras play beautifully for him; he conducts plenty of interesting 20th-century music, and he sells far more records than either Mr. Masur or Mr. Sawallisch. One plausible explanation for his image problem is that, as a specialist in French music, he is slighted in a climate that has newly come to prefer solid German musicians playing solid German music. In that sense, the stall in Mr. Dutoit's career, if it is indeed a stall, amounts to a setback for the diehard Francophiles among us, those eager exponents of French culture (the late Virgil Thomson, Ned Rorem et al.) who fought against the unthinking admiration for all things Teutonic that had prevailed in American culture before World War I.
But do German conductors really play German music with any greater authenticity or native insight than anyone else? It might seem unlikely, in our internationalized musical culture, in which conductors and performers of every nationality address every school of music. But there are those who regret this cosmopolitanism as a loss of national authenticity. For them, the return of the Germans may be a source of profound satisfaction, given the centricity of Germanic music in our orchestral life.
As far as Mr. Masur in New York and Mr. Sawallisch in Philadelphia are concerned, one must simply wait and see. I myself, however, recall with fondness a Mostly Mozart Festival concert a couple of summers ago in which Mr. Masur led the Israel Philharmonic in, of all tired warhorses, Beethoven's Fifth Symphony.
His interpretation was in no way flashy or attention-grabbing. He played the score neither particularly fast nor particularly slow. He brought no special musicological insight to his conception. He and the Israelis just played the music, but with a rock-solid authority and confidence and, in the end, conviction that proved utterly engrossing. If that's what German music-making in German masterpieces can mean for New York and Philadelphia, then both cities should have many satisfying concerts in store.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

The Oil Jolt
The price of oil fell $4, to $26.91. The 12.9 percent decline was the largest daily selloff in the crude oil futures markets in four years. The price declines for many refined products were even more pronounced. [Page D1.] Some promising alternatives to oil are on the verge of practicality. [C1.] 
OPEC is close to authorizing its members that can produce more oil to do so immediately, up to a total ceiling of 22.5 million barrels a day. That would replace most of the four million barrels a day lost as a result of the embargo of Iraq. [D6.]
 
 
 
Markets
Stocks gained back much of last week's loss in the wake of a big drop in oil prices. The Dow rose 78.71, or 3.1 percent, to 2,611.63. Added to Friday's 49-point gain, the Dow has now recovered 128 points, or 5.2 percent, erasing much of the 173-point loss in last week's three-day plunge. [D1.]
Stock markets around the world continued to climb from their recent losses, with Japanese stocks adding 4 percent.[D9.]
Prices of Treasury notes and bonds rose sharply and interest rates fell. [D15.]
Precious metals prices plunged, with gold posting its biggest one-day drop since early 1983. [D16.]
The dollar fell more than two and a half yen as a slide in oil prices relieved some inflation anxiety. [D16.]
 
 
 
The Economy
Reflecting the further tightening of credit, the Fed said commercial banks had further restricted lending since May. [D1.]
Personal incomes grew 0.6 percent in July and consumer spending rose 0.5 percent, the Government said.[D7.]
 
 
 
Companies
 G.M. said it would install air bags on the driver's side in all its U.S.-built cars starting in 1995. [A1.] The U.A.W. is expected to name G.M. as its ''negotiation target.'' [A18.]
Merrill Lynch and its insurers have quietly settled a set of claims filed after the brokerage firm stunned Wall Street by announcing in April 1987 that it had lost more than $250 million trading mortgage-backed securities. [D1.]
E-Systems pleaded guilty to conspiring to defraud the Government by falsifying test results on Army radios. [D2.]
A Federal judge overturned a $148.8 million award to Liggett from Brown & Williamson. Liggett had accused its larger rival of predatory pricing. [D4.]

HEADLINE: Dow Gains 78.71 Points, To 2,611.63

BYLINE: By ROBERT J. COLE

 BODY:
Stocks demonstrated surprising bounce yesterday, gaining back much of last week's loss in the wake of a big drop in crude oil prices and tentative signs of a relaxation of tensions in the Persian Gulf.
By the end of the day, every market barometer had made significant gains. The Dow Jones industrial average rose 78.71 points, or 3.1 percent, to 2,611.63.
Oil seemed to be by far the main reason for optimism among investors. But market analysts, traders and technicians said a range of factors contributed to the strong rally. The Treasury's 30-year bond rose more than 1 1/2 points, to 97 15/32. And the spot price of gold fell $26.70 an ounce - its largest drop since 1983 - to $388.30. [Pages D15 and D16.] Up 5.2% in Last Two Sessions Adding Friday's 49-point increase to the Dow's rise today gives it a gain of 128 points, or 5.2 percent, erasing much of the 173-point loss in last week's three-day plunge. It was the biggest one-day gain in both percentage and points since Oct. 16, when the Dow jumped nearly 3.5 percent. 
The Nasdaq composite index of over-the-counter stocks showed even stronger gains, rising 13.94 points, or 3.8 percent, to 381.27.
Barely nine minutes after the 9:30 A.M. opening bell and for the rest of the day, the New York Stock Exchange intervened against program traders under its 50-point rule. To prevent market activity from feeding on itself, the rule prohibits such traders from selling on a downtick whenever the Dow falls 50 points or more, or from buying on an uptick when it rises at least 50 points.
 
Effect of Programs Debated
Birinyi Associates, which tracks program trading, said the net effect of such activity lifted the market only 1.74 points yesterday. But Jack Barbanel, president of a Princeton, N.J., money management company, First Global Asset Management, felt otherwise.
''I don't think that everybody got up at 7 A.M. and decided to be bullish at the same time,'' he said. ''To a great extent, most of the activity was related to program trading and professional activity and not to euphoric optimism the way Wall Street characterized it.''
Mr. Barbanel said that even with the 50-point circuit breakers, ''there's still enough professional activity in the market'' to lift it until it gets to that limit.
John A. Conlon Jr., head of the equities division at Rothschild Inc., said he felt the market responded basically to ''improved hopes for a negotiated settlement'' of the crisis in the Middle East, a $4-a-barrel decline in crude oil prices and the drop in the price of gold.
Stock prices also improved, he said, because ''the Fed is reported to be leaning toward a less restrictive posture on monetary policy - toward a slight easing in interest rates'' and also because stock prices had fallen too much.
Investors had strong reason to be exceptionally bullish when they got to work yesterday. In the wake of increasing sentiment that a shooting war may be avoided and that OPEC members, meeting in Vienna, were working on getting more oil to market, foreign stock markets everywhere recorded big gains.
With investors anxiously looking for bargains and pessimists buying back stock they had sold short, the market had jumped 88 points by 11 A.M. By 2 P.M., it had moved a bit higher, falling back only slightly by the close.
Despite the strong rise, trading volume stood at a relatively anemic 160.2 million shares, down from 199 million on Friday. Advances heavily outweighed declines - 1,453 to 256 - but a troubling 60 stocks touched new lows while only 4 reached new highs.
The same stocks that have historically been market leaders led the market again yesterday. Topping the 10-most-active stocks on the New York Stock Exchange, with 2.9 million shares changing hands, Philip Morris regained 1 1/2, to 44 3/4.
The nine other active Big Board stocks also advanced, with Wal-Mart Stores up 2 1/4, to 29; General Electric 2 1/8, to 63 1/2; Texas Utilities 3/8, to 35; Boeing 2 1/2, to 48 3/8; General Motors 1 3/4, to 39 1/8; A.T.& T. 1 1/4, to 33 1/8; Fannie Mae 1 1/4, to 30 1/4; I.B.M. 4, to 104 3/8, and Ford Motor 1 1/8, to 35 1/2.
With investors looking for safety in an uncertain economic environment, drug stocks were big favorites. Merck rose 3 3/8, to 81; Pfizer 3 1/8, to 69 7/8; Eli Lilly 3 3/8, to 72 3/8; Johnson & Johnson 2 5/8, to 64 5/8; Syntex 3 1/8, to 55 1/8, and Warner Lambert 3 5/8, to 61 1/2.
Traders said heavy buying came both from private investors and from the big financial institutions. Much of it appeared concentrated in the safe, blue-chip ''big name'' stocks, traders said, but the rise in the Standard & Poor's index of 500 stocks showed that buying was widespread.
The S.& P. index, much in line with the Dow industrials, rose 9.93 points, or 3.2 percent, to 321.44.
Like the Big Board, the over-the-counter market, which rose even more impressively, moved ahead on the same market leaders. MCI Communications, the long-distance phone company, rose 1, to 33 7/8; Microsoft, the computer software producer, gained 5 1/4, to 60 3/4; Apple Computer jumped 2, to 37 3/4, and Nike, the athletic shoe maker, soared 6 1/2, to 68.
Gold shares, battered by the sharp drop in the metal price, also fell. On the Big Board, ASA fell 4 3/8, to 48 1/8; Newmont Mining 3 5/8, to 47, and Newmont Gold 4 3/8, to 47. On the American Stock Exchange, Echo Bay Mining lost 1 3/8, to 12 7/8.
Investors once again appeared to be paying attention to stock market recommendations. McDonald's rose 1, to 28 1/4, in heavy trading after Leslie Steppel of Prudential-Bache Securities Inc. eliminated her recommendation to sell.
 
P.&G. and Luxottica Rise
Procter & Gamble rose 4, to 75 7/8, and Luxottica of Italy, which sells designer eyeglass frames, rose 2 1/2, to 23 1/2, after Deepak Raj of Merrill Lynch recommended it. Among Mr. Raj's other recommendations, Gillette rose 3, to 58 1/4, and Tambrands 2 7/8, to 80 1/4.
Commonwealth Edison, the Illinois utility, rose 1 1/2, to 30 1/4, after a request to raise rates won initial approval. Disney, a market favorite, rose 5 1/4, to 103 1/8.
Among other averages, the Dow transportation one climbed 38.32 points, to 908.03, with UAL, Airborne Freight, Delta Airlines, Federal Express, Conrail and Norfolk Southern aiding the rise. The Amex composite index of smaller stocks rose 4.44, or 1.4 percent, to 320.73.

HEADLINE: Fed Reports Bank Loan Tightening

BYLINE: By STEPHEN LABATON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 27

 BODY:
Reflecting a further tightening of credit conditions for commercial borrowers, the Federal Reserve Board said today that the nation's banks had increased restrictions on lending since May.
In its most recent survey of senior bank loan officers at American banks and foreign branches in the United States, the Fed said that there had been a ''reduced willingness on the part of banks to lend'' in the last three months. It said that 43 percent of those questioned had established more stringent conditions for their middle-sized customers and more than a third had instituted tighter standards for both large and small customers.
 
Sign of Slowdown
The survey is the latest sign that economic growth is slowing considerably, just as economists are expressing concern that the steep rise in oil prices since July could worsen inflation. 
''By far, the most important reasons domestic respondents gave for tightening their credit standards in the last three months were a less favorable economic outlook and industry-specific problems,'' the survey said. ''Also important were current pressures on banks' capital positions and capital pressures anticipated to result from a deterioration in the quality of their loan portfolios.''
Some analysts said that the latest survey could provide a further basis for action by the Fed to ease interest rates, although rates do not appear to be the sole reason for the banks' reluctance to extend credit or to issue new loans. The Fed last moved to lower interest rates in mid-July to stimulate the economy and alleviate a tightening of credit.
''It's clear that the Fed has now moved this up on the list of things that are important,'' said Robert F. Dieli, an economist at the Northern Trust Company in Chicago. ''Throughout the year, banks have faced a real increase in regulatory pressure, plus the feeling that no one wants to make the headlines. It has now gotten to the point where the Fed, rightly so, has become concerned about it.''
The Fed has so far resisted pressure from the Bush Administration to lower interest rates to encourage economic growth. In recent testimony before the Senate Banking Committee, Alan Greenspan, chairman of the Federal Reserve, suggested that rates would not be lowered soon, except to offset unusually restrictive commercial lending practices.
Senior Fed officials met last week and appeared to have decided not to change interest rates until the Middle East turmoil began to subside.
''The Fed has been reacting cautiously, just as the banks have been, to the climate of uncertainty,'' said James Chessen, an economist at the American Bankers Association. ''It's not at all surprising that when there are fears about the economy, the banks become cautious.''
The latest study, of senior loan officers at 60 domestic banks and 18 branches of foreign institutions, found that lenders had become more restrictive in recent months, after earlier growth moderated as ''some loan quality problems have emerged.''
 
Tougher Standards for Some
For consumers, the survey found no change in the banks' attitude since the May survey, about extending installment credit. It said that about three-quarters of the banks had imposed tougher standards for lending money to finance commercial buildings.
The study's release comes four days after the disclosure of the minutes of the July meeting of the Federal Open Market Committee, in which Fed members expressed some concern about restrictive lending practices.
''The members continued to hear numerous reports that some businesses were finding it more difficult to obtain credit from banks, notably builders in many areas but also other businesses, including auto dealers, in some parts of the country,'' the minutes said.
''On the basis of still fragmentary information, reduced credit availability appeared to have had some, but quite limited, effects on the economy. A tightening of credit standards, however, could affect credit flow and spending with a lag, and, in addition, there was some concern that the trend to greater restraint in the provision of credit might continue.''

HEADLINE: Strong Mark Forcing Dollar to Move Over

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER

 BODY:
The German mark, the currency of the soon-to-be-united Germanys, is rivaling the dollar in its pre-eminent role in the world economy.
The Deutsche mark, which is now at its historic high against the dollar, is unlikely to replace it as the world's dominant currency. But as the mark's role in daily foreign exchange trading grows and as Europe unifies, the dollar, economists say, will no longer be the single currency of choice in the world market.
''The mark is very much the crucial currency in Europe,'' said John Williamson, a senior fellow at the Institute for International Economics in Washington.
The rise of the mark comes at time when the dollar's dominance is also beginning to be challenged by the Japanese yen, leading to predictions by an increasing number of economists of a tripartite currency system with a yen bloc, a Deutsche mark (or eventually a European) bloc and a dollar bloc. 
For the Germans, a rising mark is desirable now to help control inflation. It makes imports less expensive at a time when prices are vulnerable to inflation because of rising oil prices and the cost of reunification.
In addition, a strong mark, which the Bundesbank has been pursuing aggressively all year, will help the country attract capital needed to pay for the rebuilding of East Germany.
The main reason for the rise of the mark is the central role that Germany will play in a united Europe. Germany has the strongest economy in Europe, and more important it has a reputation for sound economic policy founded on the credibility of the German central bank, the Bundesbank.
The Bundesbank made itself the indispensable central bank in Europe by setting an anti-inflation tone for economic policy there. The mark is also the keystone currency of the European Monetary System.
 
Kohl's New Stature
Politically, the new prominence of Chancellor Helmut Kohl, which has been enhanced by his handling of the difficult issues of German unification, has added to the mark's luster and the global confidence in the currency. Mr. Kohl played a central role at the economic summit meeting last month and fashioned an agreement with the Soviet Union allowing a unified Germany to stay in the North Atlantic Treaty Organization.
The mark has also been helped by the opening and growth of the financial markets in West Germany, which a united Germany should make larger. The more the markets open and the bigger and more liquid they become, the more attractive they are to investors. And this makes the currency more attractive.
But as fate might have it, just as the Deutsche mark attains its new status, it could be gone. The event that is increasing its importance in the world - the economic unification of Europe - could result in its demise, as the mark and other European currencies are replaced by a single European currency.
German political leaders have committed themselves to the eventual demise of the mark as part of the process to unify Europe economically. The Bundesbank is obviously leary about such a prospect. But it is fighting for a European central bank that will be modeled on it, meaning that such a bank will be politically independent and staunchly anti-inflationary.
 
Trading Surges
Beyond the strength of the mark and its central role in Europe, there are several other signs of the emerging status of the mark.
One is the growth in trading of the mark in the $500 billion-a-day foreign exchange markets. According to a survey of foreign exchange markets by the Federal Reserve Bank of New York, the trading of marks between banks rose to $50 billion a day in April 1989, the latest survey, from $21.6 billion in March 1986. Trading in the mark by investment banks and other nonbank institutions doubled, to $8.8 billion a day, over that period, and mark trading at foreign currency brokers almost doubled, to $18.4 billion a day, from $9.8 billion.
In addition, the mark dominates the currency trades in which the dollar is not involved. According to the report, the mark was involved in 95 percent of all reported trades of this kind, known as cross-trading.
Although there are no official numbers, foreign exchange traders contend that this cross-trading is growing rapidly - a sign of both the growing importance of the mark and the yen, and a declining interest in the dollar.
 
Concern About Deficit
In its most recent report, The Foreign Exchange Committee, an informal adviser to the Federal Reserve Bank of New York on currency issues, concluded: ''The markets, as early as 1989, experienced a significant rise in non-dollar cross-currency trading and a corresponding relative decline in the importance of dollar-trading activity. Several members talked of the possibility that the United States may face increasing difficulty in attracting sufficient foreign capital to finance its current account deficit if the dollar's relative importance declines too rapidly.''
Another measure is the rise of the mark as a reserve currency held by central banks, which use currencies to intervene in the foreign exchange market. The dollar is still the main reserve currency but its share of total reserves is declining while Germany's is growing.
In 1980, according to the International Monetary Fund, the mark made up 14.9 percent of the total currency reserves held around the world and 14.3 percent of the reserves held by industrial countries. In 1988, the latest figures available, the percentage had risen to 16.2 percent around the world, an 8.7 percent increase, and 18.3 percent among the industrial countries, a 28 percent gain.
The Bundesbank had resisted a greater international role for the mark, including its use as a reserve currency, before the mid-1980's. Use of the mark for reserves can make it more difficult for the Bundesbank to manage domestic monetary policy because large holdings are out reach of the central bank. These difficulties are aggravated as more foreigners begin to hold large blocks of marks because of the attractiveness of Germany's financial markets.
But because the preference for a currency is set by market forces, the Bundesbank had to accede to the demand for a greater role as German financial markets opened and European unity become a possibility.
Now, the Bundesbank will be under more pressure, as has the United States, to keep its currency stable and attractive.
 
Mark Becomes a Haven
And a final measure of the rise of the mark is its newer status as a haven in times of political or military crisis. Until the Iraqi invasion of Kuwait, the dollar had been the traditional haven. It was backed by a stable Government and traded in a large liquid market.
But instead of rising, the dollar has fallen since the crisis began, as investors fled to the mark and some other European currencies, like the Swiss franc. ''With the end of the cold war, the Deutsche mark no longer suffers from the jitters when there is a crisis,'' said Robert Hormats, vice chairman of Goldman, Sachs International.
For the dollar and the foreign exchange markets, the rise of the mark and the move toward a three-part monetary system including the yen will mean that the dollar will be traded less. With more attractive currencies to move to in times of crisis or change, the markets could become more volatile. But economists doubt that the change will make currency markets harder to manage.
''The pecking order isn't such a crucial element of the world monetary system,'' said Mr. Williamson of the Institute for International Economics. He added that he grew up in England in the 1950's, when the pound was No. 2 to the dollar and declining. ''If one really got the world compartmentalized into blocs that would be hostile, that would be bad. But that would assume there was a breakdown in trade relations.''
 
Three Distinct Blocs Foreseen
The evolution to a three-part system will take time but will be bolstered by the development of the three regions - the United States and North and South America, Germany and Europe, and Japan and East Asia - into distinct economic blocs even as the world economy becomes more global.
''If we accept the fact that the United States is one of the three major areas, it is hard to imagine that the dollar will remain as dominant,'' Mr. Hormats said.
Economists add that the strong mark might make it harder for the United States to attract the foreign investment needed to cover its trade and budget deficits. Yet at the same time, the weaker dollar will make American goods less costly, and more attractive abroad.
''For Americans, it is something to be pleased about because it tells us we'll be able to be more competitive with Europe and in third world countries,'' said Martin S. Feldstein, a former chairman of the Council of Economic Advisers in the Reagan Administration.

HEADLINE: Business and Health;
Method of Pricing Drug Is Assailed

BYLINE: By Milt Freudenheim

 BODY:
IN the latest battle over drug pricing tactics, state and Federal officials, doctors and advocates for patients say one company is opening the door to a novel and inflationary marketing method. The company, the Sandoz Pharmaceuticals Corporation, requires patients who purchase clozapine, a promising treatment for schizophrenia, to buy a Sandoz-licensed program of weekly blood tests. With the tests, a year's supply of clozapine, which Sandoz markets as Clozaril, costs $8,944.
Critics say that the price is excessive and that if the clozapine precedent stands, at least seven new drugs now in the research pipeline could be tied into similar arrangements. They say that Sandoz sells clozapine in Europe for one-third its American price and that European doctors monitor their own patients, as is done with other drugs. 
Sandoz Pharmaceuticals, a unit of the Swiss company Sandoz Ltd., said its unique system is necessary because clozapine is very dangerous and schizophrenia patients may not otherwise show up for a weekly blood count. Sandoz requires that the tests be done by the Caremark division of Baxter International Inc., using laboratories of Hoffmann-La Roche Inc.
A spokesman for Sandoz, Gilbert Honigfeld, said the company could be forced to withdraw the drug if it lost control of the monitoring, because of possible high awards in American medical malpractice lawsuits. Outside the United States, where high malpractice awards are rare, Sandoz said, three to five patients using clozapine die each year. Samuel D. Isaly, a securities analyst with Mehta & Isaly, said about 20,000 patients abroad used the drug. Critics say Sandoz may be violating antitrust laws. The Federal Trade Commission and state attorneys general in New York, Minnesota and other states are examining the Sandoz-Baxter contract. Mr. Honigfeld said the company was cooperating in the investigations. He added, ''Our legal opinion is that there is no violation.''
Dr. John M. Kane, a psychiatry professor at the Albert Einstein College of Medicine in New York who has studied clozapine, said it might benefit 100,000 to 300,000 Americans. Schizophrenia often causes severe delusions, depression and other serious symptoms, which the drug can relieve. In some cases, patients have been able to resume normal activities.
Sandoz said that 5,500 American patients were now using clozapine and that 1,300 receive it free. In theory, Sandoz, Baxter and Hoffmann-La Roche could divide potential annual sales of $894 million to $2.68 billion, but time is short: exclusive marketing rights for clozapine run out in 1994.
Moreover, because of the debilitating symptoms, many schizophrenia patients are destitute. Most state programs for the indigent are reluctant to spend their limited funds on the drug. Dr. Herndon P. Harding Jr., medical director of the Ohio State Department of Mental Health, said the $8,944 cost was ''obviously exorbitant.''
In California, the state's Medi-Cal program is pressing Sandoz to agree to cut the price, as Merck & Company and Glaxo Holdings P.L.C. have done for their drugs. Yesterday, the Upjohn Company announced that it would also offer reductions on Medicaid drug prices. A nonprofit advocacy group, New York Lawyers for the Public Interest, said it was preparing a lawsuit in a Federal district court to try to force Medicaid to pay for clozapine.
When Arkansas mental health officials said they could not afford clozapine, Senator David Pryor, Democrat of Arkansas, introduced a measure that would help Medicaid programs buy the drug without paying for the monitoring system. Two Senate subcommittees have scheduled hearings next month on the bill.
The Federal veterans hospitals, which make up one of the nation's largest customers for drugs, are also challenging Sandoz. The Department of Veterans Affairs created its own independent monitoring system to insure that veterans would not be endangered by clozapine.
Sandoz said it might make a deal with the veterans hospitals if the Government agreed to protect the company against potential lawsuits. Dr. Laurent S. Lehmann, the veterans affairs department's associate director of psychiatry, said the Sandoz demand was unprecedented and unacceptable. ''Sandoz is gouging,'' he said.
Mr. Honigfeld of Sandoz said the company was ''seriously considering alternative proposals'' for protecting clozapine patients and the company. But he added, ''$9,000 is not excess in protecting the lives of patients receiving breakthrough therapy.'' Spokesmen for Baxter and Hoffmann-La Roche declined to disclose their share, which they said was set by Sandoz.

HEADLINE: OPEC Close To Raising Output Level

BYLINE: By YOUSSEF M. IBRAHIM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: VIENNA, Aug. 27

 BODY:
The Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries is close to authorizing its members that can produce more oil to do so immediately, up to a total OPEC production ceiling of 22.5 million barrels a day.
That would replace most of the 4 million barrels a day that have been lost as a result of the international trade embargo on Iraq and Iraqi-occupied Kuwait mandated by the United Nations. OPEC is currently producing a little less than 19 million barrels a day.
At least nine of the organization's 13 members are ready to back an accord to allow extra production to offset that loss, senior OPEC officials meeting here said today. The agreement is widely viewed as a positive response to pressure for more production by Saudi Arabia and its prime allies, particularly Venezuela, acting out of concern for Western economies to which their political and strategic interests are linked. 
It came two weeks after Washington agreed with Riyadh to station American troops in Saudi Arabia to defend the nation from any Iraqi aggression. The United States has also pressed Venezuela for additional supplies of crude.
 
Iraqi and Libyan Opposition
Iraq and Libya appear certain to oppose the production agreement, the officials said. Indeed, neither country has sent a representative to the ''consultative'' meeting that has been in progress here since Saturday.
Those two countries, and a number of other OPEC members, like Iran, Algeria, Indonesia and Nigeria, have strong feelings that the aggressive Saudi-Venezuelan initiative to raise production was in response to pressure from the United States. As one African delegate said, it amounts to a ''direct order.''
Several OPEC members, while wishing to preserve the group, feel the West is not entitled to demand help from a third world organization like OPEC because it has not shown enough sensitivity toward third-world debt problems. Consequently, much of the politicking today was aimed at circumventing any appearance of giving in to Western pressures.
 
Non-Producers Cited
In pressing their case for more production, Saudi Arabia and Venezuela cited concerns for non-oil-producing third world countries like Brazil and India that encounter extreme hardship in times of oil shortages.
And OPEC will insist on language in its final communique that states that it is acting out of a desire to be a responsible economic entity and to ''stabilize'' world economies, officials said.
''We want to keep the majority of OPEC behind this accord, and we will word it any way we can to achieve this aim,'' said a senior delegate from a key member of OPEC who spoke on the condition that he not be identified. ''The basic aim is to realize a substantial increase in current OPEC output, while keeping OPEC together,'' the official added.
Saudi Arabia is expected to raise output by 2 million barrels a day, to a total of about 7.5 million barrels a day, once the accord is reached; Venezuela and the United Arab Emirates are expected to lift their output by 600,000 barrels a day each, and other members of OPEC, including Nigeria, Iran, Ecuador and Gabon, would together contribute about 400,000 barrels a day, thus making up for most of the missing oil.
OPEC is also expected to call upon international oil companies to release supplies held in commercial storage and upon the industrialized countries to sell from their strategic reserves.
 
Prices Down Sharply
On world oil markets, word of the likely OPEC accord - coupled with a perception that tensions in the gulf crisis have eased - brought oil prices sharply down today.
Prices fell by as much as $4 a barrel on several international oil markets, but oil analysts said this could be reversed almost instantly if world oil markets perceived any danger of a war interrupting oil supplies.
''Many oil traders expected the war to happen over the weekend,'' said Garry Ross, president of Petroleum Industry Research Associates in New York. ''Now that no war appears immediately within sight, in addition to the certainty that OPEC will produce more oil, the market has come down. But it could flare up again the moment tensions heighten again.''
 
Appearance of Consensus
Throughout much of the manuevering and the closed-door negotiations that occupied the 11 oil ministers present here today, a major topic of discussion was how to create the appearance of an OPEC consensus.
Both Saudi Arabia and Venezuela have insisted on what they describe as ''an OPEC umbrella,'' that is, that their drive for increased production be accepted with the organization's approval.
Venezuelan and Saudi officials said that was the reason they had sought this informal OPEC gathering and that they had postponed for days their declared intentions of increasing their oil output.
This tenacious desire to preserve the facade of OPEC unity is seen as serving the purposes of all its members, as it has since 1960, when OPEC was created.
 
Foreign Policy Tool
Virtually all OPEC members, who are third world countries, are known to view the organization as an important part of their foreign policy aparatus in terms of its prestige as the most powerful third world group of its kind.
Indeed, OPEC membership is considered to convey, or enhance, the legitimacy of all the governments belonging to it. Such an asset is seen as one that OPEC nations would want to preserve no matter how deep their differences.
''I hope to God we can save OPEC,'' said Sadek Boussena, OPEC's conference president, who is also Algeria's oil minister. He was referring less to the organization's existence than to its cohesiveness.
