HEADLINE: Personal Income Rose 0.6% in July

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 27

 BODY:
Personal incomes grew six-tenths of a percent in July and consumer spending rose five-tenths of a percent, the Government said today in a report that some analysts said pointed to a stronger economy than expected.
Stephen S. Roach, senior economist for Morgan Stanley & Company, said the growth in both areas was important because it could lessen the effect of the Middle East crisis.
''It holds out the hope that we might be able to scrape by without going into recession,'' he said. 
 
Home Sales Up
In another upbeat report, the National Association of Realtors said existing-home sales rose for a second straight month in July. House resales rose 3.3 percent after a nine-tenths of 1 percent gain in June. The Commerce Department said consumer spending, which represents two-thirds of the nation's economic activity, totaled $3.67 trillion. That was up $16.6 billion from the previous month although the pace was slower than June's 1 percent increase.
A spokesman for the Commerce Department said the slower pace resulted from fewer purchases of automobiles, food and clothing.
Nevertheless, William K. MacReynolds, forecasting director for the Chamber of Commerce, called the consumption expenditures for June and July a ''bright spot.''
''If the July consumption level can be maintained for the next two months, consumption in the third quarter will increase at an annual rate of 2.3 percent over the second quarter,'' he said. ''This would be the highest quarterly increase since the third quarter of 1989.''
But Mr. MacReynolds and others said the inflationary effects of rising oil prices likely will reduce spending.
 
No 'Horror Stories'
So far, however, there have been no ''horror stories,'' said economist Sandra Shaber of the Futures Group, a Washington management consulting firm.
''People haven't been canceling auto purchases,'' she said. ''Retailers are complaining about soft business, but there's no sign of further deterioration because of the Middle East crisis.''
The Commerce Department reported that personal incomes totaled a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $4.67 trillion, a $26.3 billion increase from June. It was the largest increase since a gain of seven-tenths of a percent in March.
The report said Americans' income after taxes increased five-tenths of a percent, down slightly from six-tenths of a percent a month earlier.
''The gains were primarily from wages and salaries, rather than dividends and interest payments and things like that,'' Ms. Shaber said. ''It suggests some strength in the economy, and that's money that people spend.''
Wages and salaries rose $16.2 billion after a $15.9 billion rise in June.
Americans' savings rate - savings as a percent of disposable income -was unchanged from 4.9 percent in June. Americans' savings rate for all of 1989 was 4.6 percent.
On the spending side, the increase in personal consumption, which includes everything except interest payments on debt, rose $16.6 billion, down from June's gain of $36.1 billion.
Spending on durable goods - big-ticket items expected to last more than three years - increased $5.3 billion. It was the second consecutive increase after five months of declines.
Spending for non-durable goods, however, slipped $1.7 billion after a $13.5 billion gain in June. Spending on services was up $13.0 billion.

HEADLINE: July Home Resales Up 3.3%

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 27

 BODY:
Sales of existing homes rose 3.3 percent in July, the second straight monthly increase, as mortgage rates dropped into the single digits, a real estate trade group reported today.
The group, the National Association of Realtors, said house resales totaled a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 3.44 million units, compared with 3.33 million in June. The gain in sales in June - nine-tenths of a percent - was the first advance in seven months.
Fixed-rate, 30-year mortgages fell to 9.8 percent in July from 10.06 percent, according to surveys by the Federal Home Loan Mortgage Corporation. But they moved up again after Iraq's invasion of Kuwait on Aug. 2 and reached 10.29 percent by Friday.
The national median existing-home price was $98,400 in July, up nine-tenths of 1 percent from June. The median price means half of the homes cost more, and half less.
Sales increases were posted in the Northeast and South. The Northeast had a 14.3 percent rise, to 560,000 units, and the South gained 6.9 percent, to 1.4 million units.
Sales fell 10.3 percent, to 520,000 units, in the West, and declined 3.1 percent in the Midwest, to 930,000 units.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasuries Make Strong Advance

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
A steep $4-a-barrel drop in oil prices and what traders saw as a slight easing of tension in the Persian Gulf served as a tonic to the credit markets yesterday, as prices of Treasury notes and bonds rose sharply and interest rates fell.
The benchmark 30-year bond rose more than a point and a half, with the yield dropping back under 9 percent, to 8.99 percent.
The hint of optimism about the Middle East was initially reflected in overnight trading in East Asia, where market participants pushed prices higher. Later, after activity shifted to Europe and then the United States, the big drop in oil prices served as a signal to buy securities. 
While welcome, dealers said that the gains were recorded on sparse trading volume and warned that the still-volatile situation in the Persian Gulf could easily spark a selloff.
 
'Still Being Held Hostage'
''We are still being held hostage to what goes on in the Middle East,'' one Government bond trader said. ''At the moment, people are more neutral on the market than they were before, when there was out-and-out fear. But there is still not much sense in taking a position when the very next news headline could wipe you out.''
William V. Sullivan Jr., a senior vice president at Dean Witter Reynolds Inc., said, ''There is a sense that conditions in the Middle East have improved, but it is much too early to say that we are out of the woods, even though all financial markets traded that way.''
Reports in The New York Times and The Wall Street Journal suggesting that the Federal Reserve Board is prepared to ease monetary policy once the crisis in the gulf subsides and oil prices stabilize also helped to lift spirits, analysts said. The reports, which appeared over the weekend and on Monday, were based on interviews with Fed governors attending a conference in Jackson Hole, Wyo.
''My guess is that the Fed governors gave themselves the leeway to ease policy at last week's Open Market Committee meeting and are looking for a convenient time to do so,'' said Brian J. Fabbri, the chief economist at Midland Montagu Economics.
''That is good news for the stock and bond markets, and if it comes on a day when there are positive developments elsewhere, so much the better.''
 
Long-term Prices Strongest
Long-term bond prices, which have been battered recently, were the biggest gainers yesterday.
By late in the day, the Treasury's 8 3/4 percent 30-year bonds were offered at a price of 97 15/32, up more than 1 1/2 points, or more than $15 for each $1,000 face amount of securities. The 8.99 percent yield compared with a yield late Friday of 9.15 percent, the highest level for the benchmark issue in more than 17 months. Note issues also fared well. Late yesterday, the 8 3/4 percent 10-year notes were offered at 99 6/32, up just more than 1 point, to yield 8.87 percent. And the 8 1/2 percent 7-year notes were offered at 98 23/32, up 3/4 of a point, to yield 8.56 percent.
Today and tommorrow, dealers will be asked to bid on $20 billion in new two-year and five-year notes. With conditions in the Middle East far from stable, the auctions could not come at a more inopportune time, traders said.
Adverse events in the Persian Gulf could rock financial markets and make bidding for the new securities a potentially perilous exercise.
''When we get done with the five-year auction on Wednesday, there will be a big sigh of relief on the Street,'' one trader said.
By late in the day the two-year notes, which will be auctioned today, were offered on a when-issued basis at a price to yield 8.16 percent. Meanwhile, the five-year notes were offered at a price to yield 8.54 percent.
As the market rallied, the weekly auction of three-month and six-month bills went smoothly yesterday.
Three-month bills were sold at an average discount rate of 7.49 percent, down from an average rate of 7.55 percent a week ago.
Meanwhile, bids on the new six-month bills produced an average discount rate of 7.48 percent, compared with 7.45 percent at last week's sale.
 
Other Bonds Rise
Prices in the secondary markets for investment-grade corporate bonds and tax-exempt municipal bonds also improved yesterday, but dealers said that activity was light.
Corporate bond prices rose by anywhere from 1/4 of a point for short-term securities to 3/4 of a point for long-term bonds, traders said.
Prices of actively-quoted municipal bonds rose by about 3/4 of a point after being hammered last week.

HEADLINE: FUTURES/OPTIONS;
Spot Gold Plummets $26.70, Its Biggest Drop Since 1983

BYLINE: By H. J. MAIDENBERG

 BODY:
Precious metals prices plunged yesterday, with gold bullion posting its biggest one-day drop since early 1983. Platinum and silver prices also dropped, as investors and traders who bought bullion in reaction to Iraq's seizure of Kuwait on Aug. 2 sought to unload their holdings.
At the close of the Commodity Exchange in New York, the spot August gold delivery, which expires tomorrow, settled at $388.30 an ounce, down $26.70. The far more active December contract lost $27, at $396.20. 
The drop in the price of spot gold was the largest since Feb. 28, 1983, when the spot contract plunged $42.50, to $400.50. During the week before that price break, gold had declined $105 an ounce.
 
Pressures of the Past
Weighing on the gold market then were both a drop in oil prices and the nation's gradual emergence from an inflationary period that began in the late 1970's. That period saw gold rise to a record $850 an ounce on Jan. 21, 1980, while silver hit a peak of $50.35 that day. Platinum advanced to just over $1,000 an ounce two weeks later.
Until yesterday, the biggest one-day drop in gold since 1983 came last March 26, when the spot contract tumbled $23.10, to $365.80. That selling came mostly from bullion dealers in the Persian Gulf who expected India to lift its 28-year-old ban on gold imports, which would have destroyed much of the region's lucrative smuggling industry.
Dealers said yesterday that many of the buyers of precious metals since the invasion of Kuwait were small investors and commodity funds who responded to daily news reports of heightening tension in the region. Those who sold the metal, at steadily rising prices, were more concerned about the longer-term implications of Iraq's action, dealers said.
They said professional sellers were concerned that the huge amount of bullion Iraq seized in Kuwait, which was a major gold market as well as an oil producer, would soon find its way into the world market, causing prices to fall. These concerns were heightened last week when many foreign workers who fled Kuwait told of being stripped of the gold they had accumulated as savings.
The seized gold is expected to be exchanged for imports of food and other supplies that Iraq cannot obtain because of the international blockade. Gold has historically been used to circumvent blockades and other trade restrictions.
''There is no doubt that Iraq got its hands on an incredible amount of gold from Kuwait's central bank, private banks, bullion merchants and individuals,'' said Steven J. Pepper, senior vice president at the Republic National Bank of New York, a leading international gold dealer.
''While that gold supply undoubtedly hangs over the market, the big sellers today were mainly locals,'' Mr. Pepper said of yesterday's gold trading. ''The big London bullion market was closed for a holday, so we won't get a reading on the sentiment there until Tuesday. We didn't see much feverish selling in the Swiss or Japanese markets.''

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Drops Over 2 1/2 Yen, Falls to Low Against Mark

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar fell more than two and a half yen in New York trading yesterday, as a slide in oil prices relieved some anxiety about the inflationary consequences of a prolonged Persian Gulf conflict.
In European trading, the dollar fell to a postwar low against the German mark. It fell in Frankfurt to 1.5423 marks, down from 1.5575 marks late Friday. The previous lowest closing level for the dollar was 1.5445 marks last Thursday in New York. 
When activity shifted later to North America, the dollar lost ground in New York from its late Friday close, falling to 1.5505 marks, from 1.5605 marks.
 
Focus on Yen
Currency trading was lighter than normal because markets in London were closed for a bank holiday.
Michael Faust of MMS International in New York said the Japanese yen took center stage in North American currency trading. The decline in world oil prices is a welcome development for Japan, which is dependent on oil imports, and a rebound in the Japanese stock market also helped to restore faith in the yen.
By the time trading was done in New York, the dollar was quoted at 144.20 yen, down from 146.75 late Friday and its lowest level since February. Earlier, in Tokyo, the dollar fell to a close of 144.80 yen, from 145.70 at Friday's close.
Currency analysts said optimism among traders about chances for a negotiated settlement to the conflict caused by Iraq's Aug. 2 invasion of Kuwait reduced demand for dollars. Traders also apparently gave high priority to worries about the United States economy, which had been put aside during a dollar-buying binge that followed the Iraqi invasion.
In Europe, the British pound rose to $1.9505, from Friday's $1.9445 close in London. Sterling gained in New York, where a pound brought $1.94335, compared to $1.9400 on Friday.
Selected late dollar rates in New York, compared with Friday's late quotes, included: 1.2735 Swiss francs, up from 1.2720; 5.2010 French francs, down from 5.2360; 1,149.50 Italian lire, down from 1,159.50, and 1.14315 Canadian dollars, up from 1.1353.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: Advertising;
New Tactics By Magazine Publishers

BYLINE: By KIM FOLTZ

 BODY:
WITH circulation down, publishers of many of the nation's best-known magazines are grappling with ways to keep the bad news from further scaring off skittish advertisers.
Analysts say a growing number of publishers are trying to placate advertisers by dropping or modifying the offers of cut-rate subscriptions, which go for as little as half of the cover price. Others are doing away with incentives like free telephones or calculators, used extensively in the past to lure subscribers - although some, like Time Warner, still offer them for magazines like Sports Illustrated.
The latest figures from the Audit Bureau of Circulation showed that for the first six months of this year the average circulation of many consumer magazines has slipped. At Time, TV Guide, McCall's and Better Homes & Gardens, total circulation fell by about 3 percent. The picture was worse for other publications: People magazine had a 5 percent drop and Sports Illustrated was down 7 percent. 
''It is an immediate signal to advertisers of the real vitality of magazines if subscribers are paying the full rate,'' said Paul DuCharme, the director of print media at Grey Advertising.
Michael Clinton, the publisher of GQ magazine, said that almost every copy of the monthly men's magazine was sold for the full cover price of $3 and that that gave GQ a competitive edge. ''It is a very strong selling point with advertisers,'' he said.
Publishers are also making these changes to improve their profits. By eliminating expensive premiums and charging subscribers the full price, publishers hope to compensate for revenue lost through steep declines in advertising pages this year.
''A lot more publishers are concerned about the bottom line,'' said Donald Kummerfeld, the president of the Magazine Publishers of America, a trade group. ''It doesn't make any sense to pump up circulation during tough economic times.''
Newsstand sales for many of the biggest magazines were down by 10 percent or more. Elle was down 18 percent, Glamour was off 14 perent and McCall's 30 percent.
Newsstand sales are important because publishers reap the full cover price. These sales are also a critical indication to advertisers of how much consumers value a publication. The theory is that readers who travel to a newsstand and pay full price are far more interested than those who get a magazine in the mail.
Analysts and publishers said consumers were buying fewer magazines mainly because prices had escalated - many cost $3 or more - in the last few years.
Even though circulation is declining at many magazines, publishers are expected to raise advertising rates from 5 percent to 10 percent or even more in 1991, primarily to offset increasing costs for paper and postage - and revenues lost through declines in ad pages.
But publishers may find it hard to persuade advertisers to pay more, analysts said. Ad pages have been steadily falling this year. In July, ad pages as measured by the Publisher's Information Bureau were down 5.7 percent, and ad pages are off 3.6 percent so far this year.
Most analysts said that the rise in rates was merely a bargaining ploy and that most publishers were willing to negotiate. Many magazines are already offering discounts of 30 percent or more on ad pages for the fourth quarter.
Dan Capell, a magazine consultant and the publisher of Capell's Circulation Report, said he expected newsstand circulation to continue to decline for many magazines. Some magazines like Family Circle and Woman's Day are cutting back on their newsstand sales and hoping to attract more subscribers.
Some publishers are abandoning newsstands because the competition has become fierce. Not only are there more titles fighting for the consumer's attention, but the owners of newsstands are alloting space that used to go to magazines to items like candy and razor blades, which have higher profit margins.
Still, newsstand sales are still important to some publishers. ''We are more committed than ever,'' said Mark Miller, executive vice president and general manager at the Hearst Corporation, which publishes Cosmopolitan, Redbook, Good Housekeeping and other magazines that rely heavily on newsstand sales. To bolster its position at newsstands, Hearst recently struck a deal with the field sales force of National Enquirer to place Hearst magazines in racks at supermarkets, Mr. Miller said.
Perhaps the most revolutionary change being contemplated by some publishers is a switch, in part, from paid circulation to ''qualified controlled circulation,'' in which the reader gets it free.
Avenue magazine, for example, operates on a controlled circulation. Avenue asks a group of selected readers whether they would like to receive the magazine, and those who say yes get a free copy. Controlled circulation has become more attractive because the Audit Bureau of Circulation recently said it would include controlled subscribers in its figures. Although publishers would lose some revenue, controlled subscribers are far less expensive to obtain than paid subscribers.
But there are risks. ''Publishers will have to be very careful in choosing the kind of people who get free copies,'' Mr. Capell said. ''Otherwise it will mean nothing to advertisers.''

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS;
Consumer Confidence Low

BYLINE: By Reuters

 BODY:
Increasingly worried about the economy and pessimistic about the future, Americans are demonstrating the lowest consumer confidence since the nation was recovering from recession more than seven years ago, a business research group said yesterday.
The Conference Board's Consumer Confidence Index fell dramatically, to 83.8 in August, from 101.7 in July. The index, based on a 1985 level of 100, is arrived at from monthly surveys of 5,000 households in the United States.
August's level is the lowest the index has sunk since April 1983, just five months after the economy hit its weakest level in the 1982 recession. The drop of nearly 18 points in August was even greater than the fall recorded immediately after the October 1987 stock market crash.
The board said about 24 percent of the respondents believed economic conditions were bad, up 3 percentage points from July. Only about 20 percent of respondents said conditions were good, a fall of 3 points.
Nearly 19 percent of respondents believed conditions would worsen in the next six months, up from 12 percent in July. Less than 14 percent of respondents felt conditions would improve.
The board said there had been a deterioration in the public's evaluation of the employment situation. It said 21 percent of those surveyed said jobs were plentiful, down from about 32 percent a year ago.
Moreover, buying plans recoiled, the board said. The number of people who planned to buy an automobile within six months fell in August to 7.5 percent, compared with 8.8 percent in July.

HEADLINE: W.C.T. Out of Business

BYLINE: >By The Associated Press

 BODY:
World Championship Tennis, which staged the Tournament of Champions at the West Side Tennis Club in Forest Hills, Queens, has gone out of business. 
The W.C.T. directors, Lamar Hunt and Al G. Hill Jr., announced yesterday that the 23-year-old Dallas-based company has discontinued its tennis tournaments effective immediately.
''The decision to terminate operations was a business judgment based on the realities of the economics in sports marketing as well as the difficult circumstances of an independent company operating a group of high-quality tennis tournament events on a financially viable basis,'' Hill said.
Tournaments scheduled for London in December, Scottsdale, Ariz., in March, and Dallas in April, have been canceled.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1, Column 1; Financial Desk

LENGTH: 1550 words

HEADLINE: California Sees Housing Boom Become Slump

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Aug. 28

 BODY:
On front lawns in many cities along the California coast, the for-sale sign has become almost as common as the palm tree, and to some sellers seemingly as permanent. After several years of breathtaking price increases and demand so strong that houses were snapped up within hours of being listed, California's giant real estate market has slowed drastically.
Just as in the Northeast in recent years, California sellers accustomed to huge annual increases in housing prices have been shocked by how low the offers have come in. Some are dropping their prices to meet the highest bid, and housing prices are now falling in many areas. But in other cases they are either taking their houses off the market or leaving them on and refusing to budge much on the price.
 
Volume of Sales Down
As a result, the pace of housing sales in California has been dropping even more quickly than the price. Figures released Monday by the California Association of Realtors indicated that the rate of houses sold fell in July to its lowest level since December 1985. The seasonally adjusted annualized rate of sales last month for existing single-family houses was 419,943, down 15 percent from July 1989. Construction of new houses is also off sharply. 
And while prices in some inland cities like Sacramento continue to rise, prices are dropping in the population centers along the Pacific Coast. The median price of a single-family house sold in Monterey was down 9.3 percent from July 1989, and in Los Angeles it was down 4.5 percent. Prices in Santa Barbara dropped 2.9 percent from a year earlier. In the San Francisco Bay area, the drop was 1.8 percent.
Statewide, the median house price in July, at $194,099, was down 3.7 percent from a year earlier, when the statewide figure peaked at $201,653.
After the heady atmosphere that made this one of the nation's most overheated housing markets for the past several years, the new reality has come as a shock. Starting in 1986, prices rose as much as 30 percent annually in some areas, with the sharpest increases in 1987 and 1988.
Even with such increases, buyers found themselves in bidding wars that often pushed the selling price above the original asking price. Their willingness to pay reflected a belief that the house would be worth even more within a few months.
The ''buy at any price'' mentality seemed by late last year to have finally outrun the ability or willingness of many people to buy a house, and has disappeared this year. With the sales rate down and so many houses on the market, the pressure to lower prices is even greater, and many analysts expect prices to fall further, particularly if the national economy, now burdened by the Middle East crisis, descends into a recession.
But while the rapid deceleration of the housing market on the California coast resembles that of the Northeast, there are a number of important differences. Since prices in the Northeast have been falling longer and farther than here, more sellers have taken losses. And the Northeast's economy is weaker than California's.
In the view of many real estate agents here, the slump is nothing more than a temporary cooling. Californians continue to recite a litany of factors they think will keep the housing market from going into a steep dive, including the state's diversified economy, widespread restrictions on building that have limited the supply of housing and its strong population growth. Early census figures show that the state's population has topped 29 million, for an explosive 23.7 percent rise in a decade.
 
No Strength Left
Despite such optimism, the housing market in California is so enervated that the Kaufman & Broad Home Corporation, the state's largest house builder, recently started offering to pay closing costs for new-house buyers, an incentive worth about $10,000. The Marina City Club, a condominium complex in Marina Del Rey, has cut prices up to 22 percent. A developer in Lancaster, in the Mojave Desert north of Los Angeles, recently cut prices on some houses to less than $200,000, from $245,000, infuriating owners of identical neighboring houses who paid the developer full price just a few months ago.
More than a year ago, Charles M. Harker put a three-bedroom, two-bath house in the Los Angeles suburb of La Canada on the market for $497,500. The house, which he bought and remodeled as an investment, remains unsold, with the price slashed to $445,000 and Mr. Harker and a partner close to the point where their chance for a profit would evaporate.
''The market just came unglued,'' said Mr. Harker, an accountant. ''Many of us looked at it as though the strong market would continue. We didn't see the fall coming quite so fast.''
In November, Ann Pettijohn, a broker in Orange County, listed a three-bedroom house in the planned community of Irvine. The owner was being transferred, and had turned down an offer to sell the house to his company for $325,000, convinced that he could get a higher price.
 
'No One Buying Anymore'
The house remained on the market for six months at $339,000 before the owner finally gave up and accepted the company's offer. The company has had the house on the market through another broker for $319,900 and just accepted an offer that Ms. Pettijohn said was probably in the $315,000 range.
''That's a common scenario,'' Ms. Pettijohn said. ''The house eventually did sell, but the market in 1989 was $339,000 and in 1990 it's $315,000 to $319,000. It's like someone blew a whistle that only dogs and buyers could hear, and suddenly there's no one buying anymore.''
Few sellers are actually losing money. Except for those who bought at the peak last year and must sell now, most homeowners are still far ahead on paper after the double-digit price increases of previous years. As a result, banks and savings and loan associations in California report no upswing in foreclosures on residences, and most analysts say the state's financial institutions will weather the slump without any significant damage.
 
The Economy Is Rattled
Still, the downturn is extremely unsettling in California, where analysts already see signs of vulnerability in an economy that has enjoyed robust growth longer than almost every other region of the country. The economy is being rattled by huge layoffs in the aerospace and military contracting industries. Commercial real estate developers and brokers are being battered by a glut of new office space. Permits for construction of residential housing declined to an annual rate of about 160,000 in June from an average of 238,000 last year, prompting the beginning of a decline in construction jobs.
And some analysts say California's economy and its housing market are in for tougher times.
''With the economy at risk in the future, we couldn't have hit bottom yet in the housing market,'' said David Hensley, an economist with the business forecasting project at the University of California at Los Angeles. ''I'd expect further erosion in sales and probably prices too.''
 
'Cyclical Downturn'
Richard A. Snyder, a broker in San Diego who is chairman of the California Association of Realtors' long-term planning committee, said: ''We are in an adjustment period. My personal view is that we are going into a cyclical downturn that could last one-and-a-half to two years.''
But, Mr. Snyder added, ''We will in fact weather the storm and we won't see the price decreases you have seen in the Northeast.''
There are a few bright spots in the state. Sacramento and other inland cities, where prices are far lower than along the coast, continue to see increases in sales volume and price. In a state where only 18 percent of households can afford the median-priced house, according to the Realtors association, the lowest-priced houses are selling briskly.
But most of the state is facing a wrenching readjustment. Jim and Marilou Brown put their house near Santa Barbara up for sale in June 1989, asking $289,000, roughly the same price that a similar house down the street had sold for a few months earlier. Mr. Brown, a newspaper editor, moved immediately to Florida with the couple's son to start a job there, while Mrs. Brown stayed behind for what they thought would be several months.
 
An 11-Month Ordeal
But the house did not sell until May, 11 months later, and only after the Browns dropped their price by nearly 10 percent, to $263,000. In the end, after accounting for capital gains taxes and $25,000 in improvements they made, Mr. Brown said they barely came out ahead despite having purchased the three-bedroom house at the beginning of the boom in 1986 for $187,000.
''It was a bust as far as our expectations were concerned,'' Mr. Brown said. ''We didn't lose, but we certainly didn't come out with diamonds and rubies and gold fillings. It was kind of a shock because when I left California, the boom was still going on.''
Real estate agents by and large say they remain optimistic. Ms. Pettijohn, the broker from Orange County, said she got a call the other day from an investor who had stopped buying properties last fall, asking her to suggest some good values.
''When your investor buyers start calling,'' she said, ''you begin to think they're sniffing the bottom of the market.''

HEADLINE: Confrontation in the Gulf;
IRAQI LEADER SAYS HE WILL FREE FOREIGN WOMEN AND CHILDREN

BYLINE: By JOHN F. BURNS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CAIRO, Aug. 28

 BODY:
President Saddam Hussein of Iraq announced tonight that he would allow the departure of all foreign women and children, whom he had previously barred from leaving his country.
The Iraqi leader's move, announced on Baghdad television and monitored here, appeared to mean that thousands of Westerners who have been described by President Bush as hostages - and by Mr. Hussein as ''guests'' of Iraq pending settlement of the Kuwait crisis - would be allowed to leave Iraq. The Iraqi statement said the decision would be effective on Wednesday.
It was assumed here that foreign women and children in Kuwait would also be allowed to leave, because Iraq has announced that it had annexed that nation it seized on Aug. 2, precipitating the crisis. But there was no immediate confirmation of this. [In Washington, senior State Department officials said they have been unable to confirm that there are any plans for a release of hostages.] At Least 11,000 Westerners Western diplomats speculated that the action was taken by Mr. Hussein as a way of countering the worldwide condemnation of his use of hostages without relinquishing his hold on Kuwait. It also seemed intended to improve the atmosphere for the talks scheduled for Thursday in Amman, Jordan, between Secretary General Javier Perez de Cuellar of the United Nations and Foreign Minister Tariq Aziz of Iraq. 
At least 11,000 people from the United States, Britain and about 20 other Western countries are trapped in Iraq and Kuwait, among them 2,500 to 3,000 Americans. The decision to hold foreigners also appears to have been applied to some of the nearly two million citizens of at least two dozen other nations who were caught up in the crisis, although tens of thousands of them have been flooding out of Iraq since the invasion.
It is impossible to say how many of those held are women and children, but the assumption here is that the number probably amounts to several thousand.
 
Day of Conflicting Signals
The announcement tonight that the women and children could leave came at the end of a day of conflicting signals from the Iraqi ruler, who gave growing indications of making policy on an hour-to-hour basis under tightening military and economic pressures.
But while Mr. Hussein presented his decision to let women and children leave as a humanitarian one, he gave no indication that he was ready to abandon his occupation of Kuwait.
Indeed, the Baghdad Government newspaper, Al Jumhuriya, printed a presidential decree announcing that Kuwait had been declared the 19th Province of Iraq, that Kuwait City had been given the 19th-century name of Kadhima that it had when it was a dusty trading fort at the outer reaches of the Ottoman Empire, and that a strip of Kuwaiti territory a few miles deep along what was formerly the border with Iraq had been renamed Saddamiyat Al-Mitlaa, in honor of President Hussein.
In a statement issued by the Kuwait News Agency, Kuwait's exiled government described the measure as a ''conspicuous challenge'' and as ''an act of scorn'' for the world community ahead of the Thursday talks in Amman.
 
Invitation to Bush and Thatcher
Mr. Hussein coupled his move with an invitation to President Bush and Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher of Britain to debate the crisis with him on television ''for the whole world to see.'' Mr. Bush and Mrs. Thatcher had responded to previous suggestions of negotiations from Baghdad with statements that rejected any talks as long as Iraq refuses to withdraw from Kuwait and let the hostages go.
But President Hussein said: ''I am prepared now - ready and prepared -for direct talks and dialogue with Mr. Bush and Mrs. Thatcher immediately.'' [The State Department spokeswoman said Mr. Hussein's proposal for a debate with Mr. Bush and Mrs. Thatcher was ''sick, and it doesn't deserve a response.'' Responding to the offer of freedom for some women and children, a White House aide commented, ''You can't take their word for anything, so we'll wait and see whether they let anyone go.''] Decision Is Hussein's The announcement that women and children would be allowed to leave was read in the name of Iraq's ruling Revolutionary Command Council, but the statement made it clear that the decision was President Hussein's.
It said that he had been ''deeply affected'' by meeting some of the detained families at a meeting earlier in the day in Baghdad, at which one woman, speaking English, responded to the Iraqi leader's justification of their detention by asking him how he could explain his decision to ''use children as a pawn.''
The television announcement repeated Mr. Hussein's earlier assertions that the decision to stop Westerners and other foreign groups from leaving was based on ''humanitarian reasons.''
 
Roundup of Hundreds
Starting 10 days ago, the Iraqi leader ordered his troops and officials to begin rounding up Westerners, apparently mainly Americans and Britons, and dispatching them to military installations and other strategic sites in Kuwait and Iraq. President Hussein justified the move by saying that he hoped to deter attack on the installations by the United States and other forces in the region. Recent Western estimates have put the number rounded up at several hundred.
Referring to President Hussein, the television announcement tonight said: ''He ordered that all women and children who are foreigners in Iraq enjoy the freedom of staying or leaving, including families which the National Council decided to host. Effective tomorrow, Wednesday, Aug. 29.''
Western officials, caught by surprise by the sudden announcement from Baghdad, said they had no immediate count of how many of the hostages were women and children.
 
'Some Pretty Children'
Only hours before the announcement, which constituted the first major break in a hostage-taking policy that Mr. Hussein declared 10 days ago, the Iraqi leader met with a group of about 25 hostages, including women and children, and implied that he intended to continue holding them as human shields against possible attack.
In a somber, half-hour defense of his decision to keep Westerners as hostages, President Hussein said that any attack on Iraq would put at risk ''some pretty children, just as pretty as the children that we have here.'' He added that by preventing the Westerners from leaving, ''the same way as Iraq would lose its sons, its women and its children, of course the aggressive parties, the aggressive countries, would also lose their own men and their women and children.''
He continued: ''So, if the United States were to attack our installations in which we have men, women, children accommodated, these people would be killed.''
The television announcement on letting women and children leave left Western governments with the problem of figuring out whether, and how, they could assist any departures from Iraq and Kuwait, which have been cut off from most scheduled air links with the outside world since the United Nations imposed a trade embargo on Iraq.
Unless an airlift is organized, there is the possibility of further chaos at Iraq's road border with Jordan at the remote outpost of Ruweished. About 20,000 people, mainly Asians, arrived today alone, according to reports from Jordan, following nearly a quarter of a million others who have crossed in the past two weeks.
 
A Sudden Announcement
Iraq's decision to allow family members to leave, while continuing to hold men, was a sudden one. Only a few hours before the announcement, the Iraqi News Agency said that Mr. Hussein had decided to allow one British family, which it did not name, to leave Iraq so as to be able to celebrate the birthday of an infant daughter at home. Then, only two hours before the television statement on the women and children, President Hussein appeared in the filmed meeting with the hostages, which was said to have taken place earlier today.
At the meeting, Mr. Hussein, dressed in a gray business suit and seated in an armchair, appeared at pains to strike a more formal tone than he adopted in his first meeting with hostages last week. In that encounter, also shown on Baghdad television and relayed around the world, he patted a British boy on the head, and said that the British youngsters could be ''proud'' one day of having played a part in preventing war. The scene was denounced by Mr. Bush and Prime Minister Thatcher, among other Western leaders, as an exploitation of defenseless people.
The criticism evidently stung Mr. Hussein, whose rambling defense today of his decision to hold the Westerners included a riposte to ''some British politicians,'' evidently meaning Mrs. Thatcher, who described the earlier televised meeting as ''repulsive.''
''I can't see why they should be annoyed,'' the Iraqi leader said. He added: ''If the situation were to be reversed, if Iraq were to invade Paris, Washington or London, can we imagine the kind of measures these countries would take.''
 
'Appreciate Your Feelings'
With the hostages, most of them apparently Westerners, seated in a circle around an ornately decorated room, Mr. Hussein said: ''I appreciate your feelings, feelings that probably make you feel restricted or unhappy, because you are not allowed to travel, to leave, because if I were in your place, or if my family were in your place, then I would have had the same feelings.'' But he added that ''feelings of humanity'' were not the only issue, and that matters should be seen in the ''larger context'' of Iraq's threatened position.
In Cairo, President Hosni Mubarak of Egypt, who has led the Arab states in their condemnation of Mr. Saddam, struck a pessimistic note about the mediation efforts of King Hussein of Jordan. Mr. Mubarak said that he doubted that the King would achieve anything with his ''flying around'' the North African states, a description that he accompanied with an airy wave of his hands. The Egyptian leader has made no secret of his disdain for King Hussein since the Jordanian leader appeared to align himself with President Hussein in the early days of the crisis.
Mr. Mubarak appeared to place most of his hopes on a change of heart by President Hussein. ''I am saying to him, please, please in the name of humanity, in the name of the Arabs, and the Islam of which you speak, please defuse the bomb, because you are about to destroy the world.'' He added: ''Sometimes common sense returns to people. Sometimes they become more pragmatic.''

HEADLINE: In Moscow, Cigarette Addicts Will Get Just Half Pack a Day

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: MOSCOW, Aug. 28

 BODY:
Facing a tobacco shortage that has produced street protests and riots in some cities, Moscow's City Council decided today to ration cigarettes starting this Saturday.
When Moscow residents pick up their monthly coupons for sugar, which is already rationed, they will also get coupons for 15 packs of cigarettes a person, the newspaper Vechernyaya Moskva said - an official limit of about one-half pack a day.
But nicotine addicts with money will be able to buy more cigarettes on the thriving black market, where a medium-quality brand that officially sells for 30 kopecks a pack goes for nearly 5 rubles. Higher-quality foreign cigarettes bring 20 rubles or more a pack, a small fortune in a nation where the average monthly wage is 240 rubles a month. 
Tobacco coupons apparently will be issued in the same indiscriminate manner as sugar coupons, which means that every resident - including children and nonsmokers - will get the same amount.
 
Trade Coupons for Sugar
People who don't use their coupons will be able to give them to friends, sell them on the black market or trade them officially the following month for 4.4 extra pounds of sugar.
The cigarette shortage, which began in early summer and got much worse this month, has forced Muscovites to line up for hours at tobacco kiosks.
Irate smokers have blocked traffic in Moscow's central Revolution Square, and street protests also have been reported in other cities, including Leningrad, Gorky and Yaroslavl.
 
Riots in Siberia
People demanding more tobacco, food and alcohol rioted Friday in the Siberian city of Chelyabinsk, smashing shop windows and throwing gasoline bombs, the official Soviet press agency Tass reported.
The authorities have announced price increases that will triple the official cost of most cigarette brands starting next month, but Moscow is believed to be the first city to ration tobacco.
Official spokesmen have blamed the shortage on factory breakdowns, hoarding by black marketeers and the near-depletion of the nation's reserves of foreign currency, which means that the Soviet Union has bought 5 billion fewer cigarettes from Bulgaria this year than it did last year.
Although the Soviet Union is the world's third largest tobacco grower, topped only by the United States and China, it imported about 55 billion cigarettes in 1988, with more than 95 percent coming from Bulgaria.

HEADLINE: SLOVAKS ARE HURT BY ARMS-SALES BAN

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MARTIN, Czechoslovakia

 BODY:
A hand-scrawled, blue-and-white banner hangs limply over the arms factory entrance, proclaiming, ''Democracy. Full Employment. Economic Welfare.''
Although this sounds like leftover sloganeering from the Stalinist era, it is instead a desperate plea from the new post-Communist era, a cry for help from the the 15,000 beleaguered workers at the Z.T.S. arms factory, known for producing sturdy tanks for the Soviet bloc. 
This city, nestled among the streams and lush green hills of northern Slovakia, has been in a state of shock since Foreign Minister Jiri Dienstbier, embarrassed by his nation's reputation as a merchant of death, announced early this year that Prague would halt arms exports as soon as possible.
This surprise announcement has jolted not just Martin, but also Detva, Trencin, Dubnica nad Vahom and many other communities that became boom towns as Czechoslovakia grew into the world's seventh-largest arms exporter. As the curtain closes on the cold war, what is happening in Martin is the Soviet bloc mirror image of what is happening in communities in Missouri, California and New York, where the factory gates say Lockheed, McDonnell Douglas and Grumman.
''When we first heard the news we couldn't believe it,'' said Stanislav Neboska, deputy director of the Z.T.S. factory in Martin. ''Now the big question is where do we get work to replace the special production?''
 
'Many of Us Grew Spoiled'
Industry officials still cling reflexively to the secretive term ''special production'' to describe what they long made for export: T-62 and T-72 tanks, BVP-1 and OT-75 armored personnel carriers, Skorpion machine pistols, Bren machine guns, L-39 jet trainers and self-propelled artillery. Czechoslovakia is also the manufacturer of Semtex, the infamous plastic explosive believed to have been used to blow up Pan American Flight 103 over Lockerbie, Scotland, in December 1988.
Czechoslovakia sold such wares to other Warsaw Pact countries and to third world arms buyers, including Iraq, Iran, Libya, Syria, Ethiopia and Afghanistan. Arms were one of Czechoslovakia's main sources of hard currency, providing it with $2.15 billion over the past five years.
''Many of us grew spoiled and lived comfortably thanks to arms sales,'' said Jan Maric, director of a 16,000-employee factory in nearby Dubnica, which makes armored personnel carriers. He added, however, that there was also deep-seated uneasiness about building wealth on such a foundation. ''To produce weapons is no glory psychologically,'' he said.
Many workers at the Martin plant view the decision to halt arms exports as a noble but vain gesture. One-third of Martin's workers face layoffs or retraining as a result. What is more, many are reluctant to move to new jobs because arms plants have traditionally paid 10 percent more than other factories.
''A lot of people are saying, 'It won't do much good if we disarm while everyone else doesn't disarm,' '' said Jan Zaborsky, a graying 46-year-old who works on Martin's tank assembly line. ''If only Czechoslovakia ceases to export arms, then the export markets we have will just be taken by other countries. We are not such a rich country that we can afford this.''
 
Conversion to Civilian Uses
Now many workers are wondering where their paychecks will come from when most arms production lines close down at the end of this year. Some production will be continued to fulfill long-term contracts and provide spare parts.
''It doesn't make sense to stop military production until we have taken some steps to find some civilian production to replace it,'' said Peter Truchly, a lanky, mustachioed, 30-year-old foreman on Martin's tank assembly line.
Halting arms exports is expected to affect 200,000 workers in 110 factories nationwide, said Ladislav Nemec, who is overseeing a conversion to civilian production for the Ministry of Machinery and Electronics. ''These people will end up doing less-qualified work and being paid less,'' he admitted.
Worker and manager alike in the industry are looking to two sources for salvation: Prague for aid and Western companies for investment and production contracts. ''I believe we can cushion the impact of all this,'' said Mr. Maric, head of the Dubnica armored personnel carrier factory. ''Unfortunately, officials in the Finance Ministry say they can't help much. They say money is very hard to come by nowadays.''
The Dubnica factory is negotiating with American and Western European companies about producing forklifts and equipment for manufacturing rubber, while the Martin plant is already making bulldozers for a West German company. It also plans to produce more road-building machinery.

HEADLINE: Confrontation in the Gulf;
Excerpts From Iraqi Chief's Remarks

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 28

 BODY:
Following are excerpts from remarks by President Saddam Hussein of Iraq during a visit with Western hostages, as broadcast by CNN and recorded by Federal News Service, a transcription company. Mr. Hussein's remarks were translated by an Iraqi interpreter.
I appreciate your feelings, feelings that probably make you feel restricted or unhappy because you are not allowed to travel, to leave, because if I were in your place or if my family were in your place then I would have had the same feelings.
But politicians are forced sometimes to take a position that goes beyond what they see directly in front of them, to visualize something that goes beyond what they see. For instance, if it were to do with the feelings of humanity . . . then I would have immediately said that you should leave, that you should go to your families. But when we put the issue in a larger context . . . in the event of a, God forbid, of a military clash between Iraq and the - and the armies of the countries that have come to the area. 
And if we visualize the sort of losses that would result from such a clash if it were to happen, and if we -and if we, along with this, were to also suppose that your presence here in Iraq . . . would be amongst the factors that would prevent such a collision . . . then we would find ourselves in a situation . . . in which we are forced to keep some - a number of the foreigners so that - in order to prevent the death of many more foreigners if the war were to erupt or come about.
 
Targets and Americans
Economic and scientific installations in Iraq are in operation . . . night and day. . . . Those working on those installations live on the same complexes, the same camps. . . . If war were to erupt, then, of course, these installations will be prime targets during this war. . . . So, through this we can clearly expect the number -a great number of people that are at risk, including some pretty children, just as pretty as the children that we have here.
Amongst the most important things which we should take into consideration also is the inhuman manner in which the President of the United States has behaved. It is he and his administration that should be asked why they have come to the area. Initially, they said they were coming to the area to defend Saudi Arabia. And once this pretext was discovered to be a flimsy one, now they say that they have come to defend the oil in the area. . . . The people of the area, the Arabs, do not want to drink their oil; they would want to sell it to other countries, to the United States, to Japan, to Europe. And the revenue that comes from oil . . . would . . . be employed in the development of the oil-producing countries. . . .
So, the oil would be sold to the West. . . . So, even this pretext, that is their coming to defend oil, is, in fact, another flimsy one.
 
Range of Weapons
In a war situation, the same way as Iraq would lose its sons, its women and its children, of course the aggressive parties, the aggressive countries would also lose their own men and their women and children. And by this I am talking about those parties perpetrating the offensive, the aggression, and those that ally themselves with them. . . . The weaponry, the kind of weaponry that Iraq is in possession of may not be capable of reaching the United States, as a territory, but it - our weaponry is capable of reaching areas in which the United States has allies here.
 
Objectives of Iraq
Some Western politicians say that the measures that Iraq has taken are inhuman. I would agree that it is an inhuman measure if we were - if we had any other alternatives through which we could prevent the scourge of war. I also agree that it would be inhuman if the objective behind us taking this measure was, in fact, inhuman. . . .
But, if it - if it is conducted in the manner that it is being conducted now, which is hosting you, that is, I know it is a difficult sort of situation, it is a difficult kind of hospitality.
If the situation were to be reversed, if Iraq were to invade Paris, Washington or London, can we imagine the kind of measures that these countries would take?
Have they forgotten all that they keep saying about democracy and about liberty? When - as soon as they heard that some of their citizens would not be allowed to leave Iraq they immediately detained Iraqi citizens that are there in their countries.
We may think together of sending messages - you as messages of peace to your government, European governments, the Western governments in which you ask them the direct question of why have you come to the area as invaders in view of the fact your interests, your legitimate interest are insured in all criteria past and - follow in the past and will follow in the present? In which situation those of you who take part in conveying this message will be champions of peace which will be contrary to the attempts made by those who want to become champions through adventures, adventures which would lead to nothing but loss and destruction.
It seems my that my talk has been heavy on some of you, especially the kids who do not really - are not interested in matters of politics. They seem to be going to sleep.
 
Children as Hostages
QUESTIONER. I'll ask you how you can use children as a pawn in something that they don't understand?
HUSSEIN. This is a - exactly -this is a very legitimate question and expected from a lady such as yourself. But I want to ask the same question, but in a different way, and then I will answer. . . . My question is, is it right to kill people without any legitimate reason or objective? . . .
We all know wars and we've seen films about wars. We know very well that if aircraft were to come and attack cities, they would kill children just as pretty as your own children who have their own mothers just like you, and they would also kill women. So, this - these huge armies that have been brought now to the - to our sacred places, they are meant to come here to kill children, women, and old people, to kill troops that have not really committed any aggression against the United States or against Britain. Now, I would answer your question. No one should cause any harm to anybody. And no one should cause any harm to anybody unless it is done in an unavoidable situation of self-defense and there was no other alternative through which you could avoid the confrontation So, for my part, as being responsible here in Iraq, I am - I'm prepared now - ready and prepared for direct talks and dialogue with Mr. Bush and with Mrs. Thatcher immediately. And if they so wish and in order to let the world know everything about the whole situation, then let us have the debate between me and them on television for the whole world to see.

HEADLINE: Confrontation in the Gulf;
Strains of Embargo on Iraqis Reported

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PARIS, Aug. 28

 BODY:
As a result of the trade embargo against Iraq, food lines are growing in Baghdad, hoarding is common, rationing has begun and the Government is urging Iraqis to make dates a larger part of their diet, a French newspaper reported today.
In a report by a correspondent in Baghdad, Le Figaro depicted a city where the trade embargo has already started to bite, but has not yet had dramatic effects.
''Waiting in line on the sidewalk outside the bakery has become part of the daily routine for the capital's inhabitants,'' the article said. ''They have already lived through eight years of war against Iran and easily renew old habits.'' 
The article said the atmosphere was one of resignation rather than panic in Baghdad's major commercial district, Al Karraba. Many Iraqi families have stocked up on food, with some having hoarded enough to last six months. While speculative hoarding is punishable by law, the article noted, ''The state cannot forbid a mother from preparing for the future.''
 
'Enthusiasm Is Very High'
Shortages are becoming more and more common. On Aug. 18, the Minister of Social Affairs said, ''The economic blockade would bring about shortages in imported foods, such as milk for children.'' The article, written by Claude Lorieux, quotes a foreign resident who said: ''Rice is distributed sparingly. Even in the big hotels, sugar and tea are rare, it's true. One must realize that people have been stocking up like maniacs for the last week and that rice is only delivered to the shops once a month.''
In the article, an Iraqi office worker, sounding like a hardened Soviet consumer, said that whenever she sees a line, she gets on it because it evidently means there is something worth waiting for.
The Iraqi Government is using today's scarcities to teach a lesson, the article suggests. It wants to prepare the population for the worst while pointing its finger at what it considers the guilty party: ''George Bush's America.''
Manal Yunis Abdul Razak al-Alussi, director of the Federation of Iraqi Women, said it was easy to mobilize women ''because enthusiasm is very high.'' She said women often chant slogans of solidarity, including, ''We are going to eat dates and drink water.''
 
Lists of Consumers
Mrs. Alussi said dates were a very rich product that ''give energy and cannot do harm.'' She said a team of specialists had compiled a book of date recipes. She was asked whether she had already prepared a date dinner for her four children, and replied, ''Our meals have definitely changed, but we haven't reached that level yet.''
Mrs. Alussi said a system of rationing had been created in which directors of each administrative district make up lists of families. The system allows consumers to obtain a minimum of rice, sugar and other household goods.
To show her nation's support of Iraq's leader, Saddam Hussein, Mrs. Alussi quoted a letter that a 10-year-old girl had sent to President Bush.
''Mister, I can go without Pepsi-Cola,'' she wrote. ''That's not serious. But you, without oil, what will you become?''

HEADLINE: Confrontation in the Gulf;
Japan Putting Limits on Aid To Nations Hurt by Embargo

BYLINE: By JAMES STERNGOLD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, Aug. 28

 BODY:
Japanese officials attached new conditions today to the financial aid pledge Japan made last week to help Middle Eastern countries hurt by the trade embargo on Iraq.
The shift is likely to slow the rate at which the support is delivered and make it contingent on contributions from other countries, principally the United States.
''Japan should make a significant contribution in this field, but there is a limit to what any one country can do,'' a senior Finance Ministry official said at a briefing here today for foreign reporters.
''An international network of support might be necessary,'' said the official, who insisted that he not be identified. 
He said that meant that Japan would try not to act on its own, but would seek to be part of an aid consortium that would include the World Bank, the International Monetary Fund and the United States and other industrial nations.
 
No 'Cash Dispenser Machine'
The comments marked a change in tone from last week, when Government officials said Japan was preparing to move swiftly to offer the equivalent of billions of dollars in financial support to nations that were taking part in the embargo on Iraq and were suffering economically because of it. Principally, the Government was focusing on Jordan, Egypt and Turkey, neighbors of Iraq and Kuwait.
The remarks thus appeared to be an attempt to temper global expectations about how much Japan would contribute and the terms under which it would make loans, grants or other forms of aid available. Japan has initiated programs to help recycle the vast trade surpluses achieved in recent years, but it has fought against developing an image as a ''cash dispenser machine'' for aid, as one official here put it.
Japan's Constitution restricts its use of military forces in international disputes, and the Government has been engaged in a fierce internal debate over how it can directly take part in the embargo without changing the law or jeopardizing political support at home.
A number of officials had seized on financial aid to the ailing Middle Eastern countries as a form of support not restricted under the law, but which would mark a positive Japanese contribution. Those officials had said that a package of low-interest loans and other forms of aid would be announced shortly to demonstrate Japan's commitment.
 
Scaling Back Expectations
On Wednesday, Prime Minister Toshiki Kaifu is expected to disclose his long-awaited package of measures for bolstering the multinational forces opposing Iraq. But a number of officials have said it would be just a preliminary step, and would include little of the financial aid pledged earlier.
Press reports here have said the other measures are expected to be: sending medical personnel to the Persian Gulf region, refugee aid and transportation equipment, perhaps chartered jets and ships. The second part of the package, the financial measures, is now expected to be incomplete and contingent on talks with other countries.
Japan is more dependent on Middle Eastern oil than any other industrialized nation, getting slightly more than 70 percent of its crude from the region. Shortly after Iraq invaded Kuwait, Japan froze the assets of the two countries and decided to join the trade embargo. Getting beyond those initial steps has proven difficult.
The note of caution sounded by the Finance Ministry official today irritated some in the Foreign Ministry, who had hoped for a quicker and more decisive response to promote Japan's image as a leading player in international issues.
''If we miss this wave, the wave will be gone,'' said a Foreign Ministry official, who backed a swifter commitment by the Government. ''This is frustrating for us.''

HEADLINE: Virginia Beach Journal;
City and Black Students Plan Holiday Together

BYLINE: By B. DRUMMOND AYRES Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: VIRGINIA BEACH, Va., Aug. 28

 BODY:
A year after racially charged rioting on the waterfront here left a hundred stores looted, dozens of people injured and scores more arrested, this resort city says it is trying to do the right thing.
On Labor Day weekend last year, thousands of black students from college campuses all over the East Coast converged on Virginia Beach for a final summer fling, much as they had on Labor Day weekends stretching back to the late 1970's.
But what started out as a youthful good time suddenly turned into a violent confrontation between blacks and whites when the crowd swelled to more than 50,000, twice as many as in years past, and many of them too young or too old to be in college. More than a thousand state troopers and National Guardsmen were summoned to stop the looting and disperse the crowds. 
The violent images on television screens focused attention on the city and racial problems here, and later a citizens' study panel cited failures of leadership and communication as causes of the trouble.
A year ago the city had almost no plans for handling or entertaining 50,000 young people. Indeed, officials had stated flatly that the resort, predominantly white and predominantly family-oriented, did not want to be host to a huge fraternity bash.
But now, with its economic future no longer solidly assured and its racial reputation tarnished, fairly or unfairly, Virginia Beach is taking a far different approach. Since last Labor Day prominent blacks and whites here, along with student leaders from a dozen black colleges in the East, have been talking frankly, acknowledging wrongs on both sides and laying careful plans to make the coming holiday one to be remembered for fun under the sun.
''This is a social experiment of great import for this country at this time, given the tensions that exist everywhere,'' said Mayor Meyera E. Oberndorf. ''The city and the students have accepted that they have co-responsibility for the weekend's outcome.''
The preparations have gone far beyond philosophical discussions about cultural differences and youthful desires. Where once the city planned no entertainment for student visitors, this year there will be parades, swimsuit contests, dances, job fairs, football and basketball games and music, music, music. The events will be scattered up and down the boardwalk and elsewhere in the city to hold down congestion and the size of crowds.
''Things are much different this year,'' said Arthur Jarrett, the student body president at nearby, predominantly black Norfolk State University, who is a major figure in preparations for the holiday.
''We've compromised, reached a middle ground,'' he added. ''No one has gotten all they wanted. Some people on both sides are still grumbling, and I'm not saying that nothing bad can happen. But the community has basically come together and I think things are going extremely well.''
While the biggest question hanging over the 1990 Labor Day weekend is whether the violence of 1989 can be avoided, there is also a question of just how many black students will come. Student groups in Washington, Baltimore and a few other college towns in the East have announced they will boycott the celebration.
''Virginia Beach may have come up with solutions for Labor Day 1990, but a lot of us don't think Virginia Beach has really changed in its heart,'' said Damita Coats, a senior at Howard University in Washington who is helping plan one boycott.
The boycotts may indeed have some impact. Thus far, only about 40 percent of the resort's hotel rooms are booked, about half the number usually booked at this point.
''But boycotts aren't really the issue,'' said Andrew S. Fine, a lawyer and developer who helped head the Laborfest planning committee. ''The issue was whether the city could come together and respond rationally, and it certainly did, with a few exceptions. Whatever happens, we accepted this as a huge test of our will to do what's right.''

SECTION: Section A; Page 19, Column 1; National Desk

LENGTH: 659 words

HEADLINE: Washington Talk;
2 Roads Diverge in Task Of Measuring Poverty

BYLINE: By JASON DePARLE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 28

 BODY:
Poverty, like beauty, resides in the eye of the beholder. Many Americans think they know it when they see it, but definition has proved difficult.
Intellectuals in universities, research centers and Government offices are now devising, or refining, two different yardsticks for defining poverty. One seeks to provide an overall measurement of poverty in America. The other tries to measure a smaller, though more severe, type of impoverishment that engulfs ''the underclass.'' 
Both are esoteric arts, undertaken by a covey of specialized researchers using census data and computer models. They spew pages of numbers as scintillating as a tax table and talk in obscure journals of coefficients and standard deviations. Why bother? At bottom, the effort to measure poverty is an attempt to measure how the idea of America is working, and the distance between its promise and its present. Moreover, it has practical implications for Government in making laws, budgets and regulations for the distribution of benefits.
As Patricia Ruggles, a leading expert, puts it, poverty statistics play ''an important role in shaping our perceptions both of the extent of real economic need and of the characteristics of those who are most deserving of our help.''
While related, the attempt to measure poverty in general is a very different undertaking from the effort to measure a specific underclass. The first concentrates exclusively on economics; the second often involves attempts to measure not just money but behavior, like extended joblessness or criminal misdeeds.
The tool for measuring poverty in general is a contentious concept, the poverty line, that was devised in the 1960's. The formula is grounded in groceries: it took the Agriculture Department's recommendations for the minimum amount of healthful food consumption, calculated the cost, and, noting that most people spent a third of their income on food, multiplied it by three. In 1969, the Government declared all Americans who earn less to be officially poor. Each year since, it has simply raised the line to account for inflation.
But many liberals say the formula underestimates the number of poor because it does not take account of changes in spending; specifically, the rising proportion that goes to housing and child care. Some conservatives contend that the current standard may exaggerate the extent of poverty because it fails to take into consideration benefits like food stamps, Medicaid and subsidized housing.
The attempt to define and measure the American underclass is a newer art and one that has produced no official Government standard. To many specialists, the underclass is a group of persistently poor inner-city residents who are most likely to become school dropouts, drug addicts, teen-age parents or criminals.
Some emphasize location as the major concept and have tried to measure the number of poor people living in neighborhoods where many others are poor. Others, like Isabel V. Sawhill at the Urban Institute, emphasize behavior as the crucial concept, and have tried to measure the number of people living in areas characterized by high dropout rates, unemployment, welfare dependency and households headed by women.
Still others argue that measuring behavior may confuse cause and effect and that the focus instead should be on the duration of poverty.
Nonetheless, some consensus is emerging that places the number of people in the underclass in the area of two million to three million, or less than 10 percent of the nation's poor.
Many people associate the underclass with an image of poverty's seeming intractability. But Prof. Sheldon Danziger, a specialist in poverty policy at the University of Michigan, says the measure in fact offers inspiration. Most poverty, he says, is not so entrenched and ''with housing and food and tax credits, one can do an awful lot for the vast majority of poor people who only lack economic resources.''

HEADLINE: Americans Can't Afford to Alienate the Saudis

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Your Aug. 11 military analysis of the problems of a multinational force in Saudi Arabia discusses the great cultural differences between Americans and Arabs, and how our military personnel will have to be extremely conscious of Arab sensibilities. How right you are.
In Vietnam, our troops were given perfunctory lectures on respecting the Vietnamese and their culture, without any defined policy to circumscribe their behavior or limit the resources they were able to expend on their pursuit of pleasure. 
Prime Minister Pham Van Dong of North Vietnam warned American policy makers in a January 1967 interview: ''The more American troops are put into South Vietnam, the more complicated problems they create, something that cannot even be foreseen. . . . The expeditionary corps is entirely upsetting the economy of South Vietnam. There is increasing inflation. People cannot live. And another thing. There is a bad effect on social and cultural life. You have heard it said that Saigon is now a very huge brothel. Even girls of the upper classes are prostitutes to American officers.''
Eventually, the lack of a firm American policy and individual discipline led to overwhelming alienation on the part of our Vietnamese allies, whose hearts and minds we were supposed to win. What had started as a legitimate war against Communist expansion was transformed, through our own incompetence and insensitivity, into an anticolonial war against the new imperialists: us.
''Islamic law,'' you state, ''rigidly enforced in Saudi Arabia, prohibits the possession or consumption of alcohol or pornography (including magazines that many Americans would consider merely racy) and bans the defiling of mosques and holy places.'' Also, ''another possible source of trouble has to do with women. Saudis strongly disapprove of unmarried women and men associating with each other in public.''
Saddam Hussein is attempting to convert his naked act of war against a neighbor into a jihad, or holy war. He has been quoted as saying: ''Rise up against evil, against the foreigner who has trampled our sacred right.''
Now all our personnel in Saudi Arabia have to do is behave like ordinary Americans out on the town on Saturday night, and we can make his wish for a holy war come true.
We are guests in Saudi Arabia; our every action and mode of behavior must reflect our genuine respect for Saudi customs and religion. To do any less is to invite the condemnation and resentment of the entire Arab world and convert our noble efforts to halt this aggression into a bloody jihad that will force us, as in Vietnam, to come home with our tails between our legs.
 
JAMES V. HALL
Garrett Park, Md., Aug. 14, 1990

HEADLINE: About New York;
A Crescendo In the Concerto Of Competition

BYLINE: By JAMES BARRON

 BODY:
''This is the Grand Central station of the New York music world,'' Michael Meltzer said. He said this in the Steinway & Sons showroom on West 57th Street, between playing a Scarlatti sonata and telling how the man who invented the windshield wiper and the shock absorber also dreamed up something that made the moving parts inside a grand piano move faster.
Maybe that is true; maybe it is just an artful salesman's perfect pitch. There are more than half a million Steinway pianos out there. Mr. Meltzer, a Steinway salesman since 1984, was playing No. 500,000 - with a modernistic-looking case of East Indian ebony, dyed Swiss pear and something called Bubinga wood. 
On the front lines in the piano wars, everything looks so friendly, so genteel, so expensive. But lately the competition has seemed a bit more intense, at least to sidewalk shoppers who like to look at long grands behind big plate glass windows. Yamaha has opened an ultramodern showroom across 57th Street, slightly to the left of Carnegie Hall. And a dealer selling Bosendorfers, an Austrian brand whose highly polished grands have a few more keys than a Steinway, has set up shop on the Upper East Side.
If only this were as simple as Cadillac versus Mercedes-Benz versus those expensive new Japanese cars. Horsepower can be quantified. Differences in tone cannot.
Steinway has a long list of artists who perform on nothing but Steinways - plain black ones, not like No. 500,000, which has the signatures of dozens of pianists etched into the case: Vladimir Horowitz, Maurizio Pollini and Van Cliburn to the right of the keyboard; Rudolf Serkin, Daniel Barenboim and Elton John (yes, Elton John) to the left.
Steinway is so sensitive about Steinway artists that it sent Yamaha a letter of complaint after Steinway executives got hold of a Yamaha newsletter that they felt implied that some Steinway artists had defected to Yamaha.
Bosendorfer, for its part, lays claim to Frank Sinatra and Stevie Wonder. And the tradition of piano-making.
''The things Steinway is most proud of, we still do,'' said Allan W. Buchman of Classical Keyboard Instruments, the Bosendorfer dealer at 406 East 79th Street.
He is a man who dreams of ''Anything You Can Do, I Can Do Better'' as a theme song. Bosendorfer has hired the designer Hans Hollein to build a rival to No. 500,000.
''We feel the best thing we can do for Steinway is provide viable competition, because it will raise the standards,'' said Mr. Buchman, who was the head technician at the Manhattan School of Music for 12 years. ''We don't want to move into 57th Street. We don't want to push Steinway out of 57th Street. We just want to make the very best possible instrument.''
The Bosendorfer Imperial Concert Grand is the one with the nine extra notes below the 88 on conventional pianos - and the one in the showroom has Leonard Bernstein's signature inside the case. ''The first time you sit down, it's like being at the helm of an ocean liner,'' Mr. Buchman said. The lowest of the extra notes is a C so low it rumbles - a foghorn comes to mind. Almost no music calls for those nine notes. They are there, Mr. Buchman said, to make the others sound better by shoving them closer to the center of the sounding board.
Mr. Buchman claims that Bosendorfer dries the wood that goes into its pianos longer than Steinway does, and uses no fans to speed things along. ''If wood is subjected to forced-air drying,'' he said, ''it disrupts the memory the cells have.'' That ''memory'' is what makes the wood in a Bosendorfer resonate more naturally than the wood in other pianos.
''This is Steinway country, O.K.?'' Mr. Buchman said. ''We're faced with a situation where we have artists who want to perform here and they're afraid.'' He said that he had offered an Imperial - list price $82,000 - to Carnegie Hall for its 100th anniversary season. Carnegie Hall, which is across the street from the Steinway showroom and has a Steinway on the stage, said no thanks. ''If you have an ongoing relationship with quality merchandise and everything is longstanding and positive,'' said Lois Cohn, a spokeswoman for Carnegie Hall, ''why look elsewhere?''
Steinway, pardon the expression, plays down the competition. Peter B. Goodrich, who manages Steinway's concert and artist department, disputes the idea that new Steinways fall short of the old standards.
''The public does love to hear that Steinway and Yamaha are tooth and nail or something,'' said Peter Becker, Steinway's director of retail sales and marketing. ''But, you know, the bottom line is, we have people waiting for Steinways, even in what seems to be a slow economic time.''
