HEADLINE: New York City In Violation On Diesel Soot

BYLINE: By ALLAN R. GOLD

 BODY:
New York City, already plagued with one of the nation's worst smog and carbon-monoxide problems, is also violating the Federal health standard for the soot and dirt emitted by the diesel engines of buses and trucks, new government data show.
The information is based on a measurement taken in midtown Manhattan along Madison Avenue, one of the busiest bus routes in the city, but a city environmental official said violations would probably be found at other spots in New York where bus and truck traffic is heavy.
The black smoke belching from diesel-powered vehicles - the sight commonly associated with urban air pollution - can produce respiratory problems and has been identified by the Federal Government as a potential cause of cancer. Such particle emissions, many times greater than those produced in gasoline engines, also reduce visibility and smudge buildings with grime. 
The information, collected by a state-operated, street-level monitor at 47th Street and Madison, shows that levels of soot and dirt in the air routinely exceeded health standards during the first six months of this year. The monitor also found violations in 1988 and 1989, the only other years for which data are available. The latest results were made available by the Natural Resources Defense Council, which had requested them from New York State.
''Diesel pollution is one of the worst pollution problems in urban areas,'' said S. William Becker, executive director of State and Territorial Air Pollution Program Administrators, a Washington-based lobbying group. ''It's diesel that more than any other problem gets the attention of the public.''
The new data on soot come as discussions are going on in New York City about using cleaner-burning fuels in vehicles. On Monday, United Parcel Service agreed to convert the engines in some of its Manhattan fleet so that they could operate on fuels other than diesel and gasoline, like methanol and compressed natural gas. Also, the city's Department of Environmental Protection is exploring whether city buses and other city vehicle fleets could also operate on such alternative fuels.
Ozone smog can blanket the entire metropolitan area, but such pollutants as soot particles from diesel engines are more localized. In New York the particles are generated mostly by buses and trucks and are most concentrated where they are trapped by tall buildings. The highest levels are in Manhattan and on busy thoroughfares in other boroughs. The number of diesel-powered trucks and buses in use in New York City grew substantially during the 1980's.
The monitor at Madison Avenue and 47th Street is in a neighborhood that environmental officials say might have the city's worst pollution from diesel engines because of the high number of buses there and the skyscrapers lining the avenue.
Although more are planned, no other street-level monitors are in place around the city, said Dr. Mira Barer, a deputy commissioner for the Department of Environmental Protection. But violations of the Federal health standard for soot particles are likely at other locations that have similar traffic and buildings, she said. Street-level measurements are important because they reflect what people are actually breathing.
New York is the nation's diesel bus capital, said Eric A. Goldstein, a lawyer with the Natural Resources Defense Council. The city has more than 5,000 private and public vehicles traveling city roads each day. There are also thousands of diesel-powered trucks that roll through New York.
Diesel engines are popular because they are fuel-efficient and economical. But they emit 30 to 70 times more soot particles than gasoline-powered engines of a similar size, Mr. Goldstein said.
 
Violations Started in '87
For most of the 1970's and 80's, New York City met the Federal health standard for diesel emissions. Violations started to occur after 1987, when the Environmental Protection Agency adjusted its health standard to account more accurately for health risks posed by soot particles. On one day last February, a reading at the Madison Avenue site found levels almost double the national standard. The national standard is 50 micrograms of soot per cubic meter, measured as an annual average.
Until 1987, the Federal environmental agency standard addressed particles of all sizes. But scientists found it was particles less than 10 microns -those with diameters less than one-tenth the width of a human hair - that were riskiest.
When inhaled, the smaller soot particles pass through natural filters in the nose, mouth and throat and may lodge in the lungs. The inhaled particles can irritate or damage the respiratory system, much as ozone and carbon monoxide do. Children, the elderly and people with respiratory disease are more vulnerable to the soot particles than the rest of the population.
 
Steps to Correct Problem
Movement is occurring on both the city and Federal levels to deal with diesel pollution.
New buses purchased by the Metropolitan Transportation Authority must be equipped with tailpipe devices to cut soot emissions, Dr. Barer said. Consideration is being given to attaching the same devices to existing buses, she said.
In Washington, the Federal Environmental Protection Agency recently announced that it would require an 80-percent reduction in sulfur in diesel fuel, beginning Oct. 1, 1993. The Transit Authority now uses such fuel.
The change, in connection with an earlier agency decision to tighten standards for tailpipe emissions for bus and truck diesel engines beginning in 1991, could eventually improve urban air quality. Nationally, the actions will reduce particulates pollution by 90 percent, the environmental agency said.
But Mr. Goldstein was not optimistic about great advances.
''Without additional controls or cleaner-burning fuels, it will be very hard to improve air quality because of the increasing number of diesel-powered vehicles,'' he said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Heavy bus traffic at Madison Avenue and 47th Street contributes to a chronic diesel-pollution problem that is measured by a street-level monitor, right. Soot and dirt levels exceeded health standards during the first six months of this year. (William E. Sauro/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Rinfret Attacks Cuomo's Flights As Free Junkets

BYLINE: By SAM HOWE VERHOVEK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ALBANY, Aug. 28

 BODY:
Attacking one of the more cherished spoils of incumbency, Pierre A. Rinfret today demanded that the State Board of Elections investigate Gov. Mario M. Cuomo's use of state aircraft for what Mr. Rinfret described as ''political campaign activities.''
Mr. Cuomo, who is running for a third term, has taken dozens of trips around the state this summer at the taxpayers' expense, planting trees to promote an environmental bond act and handing out economic development checks to many communities.
While those trips would clearly seem to help Mr. Cuomo's standing as a candidate, and while he has taken many more of them this year than he has in non-election years, aides to the Governor said the flights all involved legitimate state business and did not have to be paid for by his campaign.
''He doesn't advocate his own candidacy,'' explained a spokesman for Mr. Cuomo, Gary G. Fryer. ''Does he say, 'Vote for me'? Does he say, 'There's an election, and go to the polls'? He does not.'' 
When he starts doing that, Mr. Fryer said, Mr. Cuomo will start paying for use of the plane.
 
Free Fund-Raising Trips
That policy has upset Mr. Rinfret, his Republican opponent, who released a letter to the Board of Elections demanding that Mr. Cuomo at least be forced to pay for those parts of his trip, like fund-raisers, that could clearly be construed to be ''political.''
''I am absolutely astonished by the blatant abuse of taxpayer dollars by Mr. Cuomo as he campaigns throughout the state at public expense,'' Mr. Rinfret said in a prepared statement. ''Cuomo is really taking the taxpayers for a ride with his use of state airplanes, limousines, and personnel on his political junkets.''
Under Mr. Cuomo's policy, even day trips that include appearing at a Democratic fund-raising event can still be paid for entirely with state money if his aides determine that Mr. Cuomo spent most of the working day on official business.
For instance, on Aug. 20 Mr. Cuomo flew on state aircraft to Long Island, where he attended a bill-signing ceremony for environmental laws and met with local officials. That night, he attended a fund-raising dinner in Southampton for Sherrye Henry, a Democratic candidate for the State Senate.
 
No Plans to Pay for Trips
The next day, Mr. Cuomo flew on a state plane to Buffalo, where he toured a General Mills plant, was interviewed by a newspaper reporter, and attended a fund-raising cocktail party with his wife in Cheektowaga, a community near Buffalo.
Mr. Fryer said there were no plans to charge any part of either trip to the campaign, because he said both days involved ''a preponderance of state business.'' But he said he expected that there would be several days during the fall campaign when Mr. Cuomo would pay for his use of the plane.
Records of flights on state aircraft by top state officials, and the bills, are supposed to be compiled quarterly by the State Department of Environmental Conservation, which maintains the state's air fleet. But Arthur Woldt, a department spokesman, said today that he had no records available for any of Mr. Cuomo's flights this year.
 
Stricter Monitoring Is Urged
Reporters and others not employed by the state are often charged a fare equivalent to full-fare coach on a commercial airline when they use a state airplane. A journey to Buffalo, for example, would cost about $300 round trip. But those charges make up only a tiny part of a flight's cost.
Aside from Mr. Rinfret, several groups that scrutinize government, including Common Cause, have asked for stricter monitoring of state planes and helicopters during election years and possible creation of a special billing for any incumbent's use of a plane for a political event.
The state's election law does not mention using state aircraft, although it does require that all free services a candidate receives be reported as income with the Board of Elections. Also, the constitution bans using state property for personal affairs.
A spokesman for the Elections Board, David Flanagan, said Mr. Rinfret's complaint would be reviewed. He had no comment on whether board officials believed it was warranted.

CORRECTION-DATE: August 31, 1990, Friday, Late Edition - Final

 CORRECTION:
An article on Wednesday about Gov. Mario M. Cuomo's use of state aircraft referred incorrectly to payment for trips that are partly political or unofficial. During campaigns, his campaign committee or the State Democratic Party will pay at least half the cost on any day that involves such activity.

HEADLINE: Douglas Manager in Low Profile

BYLINE: By PHIL BERGER

 BODY:
John Johnson, the manager of the heavyweight champion, James (Buster) Douglas, was out of character last week in Columbus, Ohio.
In the past, Johnson has been a feisty and garrulous sort, clearly enjoying his role as the country boy from Red Jacket, W. Va., who against all odds succeeded in the buccaneer business of boxing.
As manager of the sport's most prestigious champion, Johnson appeared to relish his maverick image and talked easily and at length about the pleasures and perils of guiding the heavyweight champion. In often salty language, he railed against perceived enemies and assailed traditional notions of boxing economics, insisting that the fighter deserved the biggest share of the purses since he, and nobody else, absorbed the punches. 
But last week, following a shake-up earlier this summer that saw Johnson and his son, John Jr., move out of the Columbus offices of James (Buster) Douglas Inc., the manager no longer seemed as assertive as he had been.
When a reporter, just arrived in Columbus, called to interview him last Thursday, Johnson said the request had to be cleared by Steve Brener, a publicist working for the Mirage, the Las Vegas, Nev., site of Douglas's first title defense, against Evander Holyfield, on Oct. 25. It was the first time since Douglas won the title six months ago that the talkative Johnson had felt obliged to get permission to speak to the press.
Later, after Brener had cleared him for conversation, Johnson gave his version of the events that led to his exodus from the Douglas Inc. office. But after saying - as Douglas would later - that there were certain philosophical differences about their business aims, he told a reporter, ''Look, if James doesn't want to talk about this, then all I told you is off the record.'' What does it all mean? Just this: Douglas has decided he can speak for himself from now on and has passed the word to Johnson. Where Johnson used to attend to the myriad details of the heavyweight champion's business, Douglas now has longtime friends, Rodney Rogers (as camp coordinator) and Larry Nallie (as financial director), looking after his affairs .
Johnson, who was at Douglas's Thursday night training session and was cordially received by the champion and his co-trainers, John Russell and J. D. McCauley, will continue to get his 23 percent managerial share but less of the spotlight.
''James basically has come into his own, and taken the reins ,'' said Rogers. ''You'll start seeing more of the real James Douglas, the more verbal James Douglas, the more outgoing type.''
Stage Role for Breland Mark Breland, the former welterweight champion, will play the role of a fighter in Clifford Odets's play ''Golden Boy.''
Earlier this month, Breland auditioned for the Trinity Repertory Company of Providence, R.I., which will stage the play in October. Soon after, Dennis Conway, general manager of the theater, said: ''We'd love for him to do it. What it comes to is whether we can negotiate salary.''
Over the weekend, the fighter's manager, Shelly Finkel, said that Breland had agreed to work for scale and would go into rehearsals after Labor Day.
Whether the acting job is a prelude to a career change remains to be seen. The prospect of Breland's retirement was raised in July, after he was knocked out by Aaron Davis in nine rounds in Reno, Nev.
Breland is said to want to continue boxing. Finkel said that he and the fighter would discuss Breland's boxing future this week in Norfolk , Va.
 
Argentine Next for Foreman
George Foreman's next opponent in the 42-year-old heavyweight's bid to extend his 22-fight winning streak will be Walter Masseroni of Argentina on Sept. 25 at the London Arena. Masseroni, 26 years old, had 9 victories, 4 losses and a draw before this year, all in Argentina. A spokesman for the promoter, Frank Warren, said Masseroni's record this year was not available. Taylor-Chavez Again? There's a chance that Meldrick Taylor, the former junior welterweight champion, could end up in a nontitle over-the-weight rematch with Julio Cesar Chavez , the International Boxing Federation-World Boxing Council junior welterweght champion. There were discussions on that possibility last week between Home Box Office and the fighters' handlers. Taylor, who has been having problems making the 140-pound junior welterweight limit, had planned to move up to the welterweight division and challenge Davis, the World Boxing Association champion, and he still might. . . . Pernell Whitaker, the lightweight champion, will be interested in the outcome of the Sept. 22 bout between the I.B.F. junior lightweight champion, Tony Lopez, and the I.B.F. featherweight titleholder, Jorge Paez, in Sacramento, Calif. Whitaker might fight the winner. And, according to the fighter's promoter, Dan Duva, if that man turns out to be Paez, Whitaker could end up with his first million-dollar purse.

HEADLINE: To a Child, Staff of Life Is Surely Peanut Butter

BYLINE: By MOLLY O'NEILL

 BODY:
LIKE many primordial preferences, personal taste in peanut butter is idiosyncratic.
Some believe that early-childhood experience establishes a lifelong predilection for a sweet or a salty, a creamy or a crunchy peanut butter. Others believe that tastes in peanut butter mature, just as palates seem to, moving from an early yen for the sweet and bland into a yearning for more robust flavors. Others are convinced that an individual's propensity in the peanut butter department is as irrevocable as the color of one's eyes.
''Congenital, absolutely congenital,'' said William F. Buckley, the syndicated columnist whose single attempt at poetry is the sonorous couplet: I know that I shall never see A poem lovely as Skippy's peanut butter. 
But whether cleaving to the early-childhood theory, the progressive development model or the construct of pre-ordained taste in peanut butter, no one argues the national taste for peanut butter.
For decades the spread has carried Americans through childhood as certainly as children have toted peanut butter sandwiches to school. It is an economical, nutritious and convenient food that parents and children can agree on. Recent samplings by a group of three adult food writers and a panel of preschool children showed that preferences in peanut butter can vary from generation to generation. Nevertheless, 85 percent of American homes stock peanut butter, according to the Peanut Advisory Board, a trade group in Atlanta.
Half the peanuts consumed in the United States are eaten in peanut butter, about 800 million pounds a year. That amount could smoothly coat the Grand Canyon, according to the Peanut Advisory Board. It is not clear whether this would be a mere smear or a nice thick coating.
Peanut butter is a $1 billion annual business and it is growing. Mitch Head, executive director of the board, said peanut butter consumption has grown by an average of 5 percent a year for each of the last five years, even as the school-age population has been declining. ''Our research indicates that the growth is coming from senior adults,'' people over 55, Mr. Head said.
A historian of peanut butter, Mr. Head sees a certain poetry in the graying of peanut butter. It was invented, he said, either by an unidentified physician in St. Louis or by Dr. John Harvey Kellogg of Battle Creek, Mich. Dr. Kellogg filed a patent for the ''process of preparing nut meal'' in 1895. That same year, the unsung physician is said to have encouraged the Bayle Food Products Company in St. Louis to produce a similar paste. Both doctors envisioned high-protein food for elderly people.
Mr. Head doesn't claim that pulverized peanuts were the creation of a health-conscious turn-of-the-century American. Peanuts themselves were probably native to Bolivia or Peru, where they were discovered by Spanish explorers. The Spanish carried them to Africa where they were cultivated and used in cooking, Mr. Head said.
While researching them at Tuskegee Institute, George Washington Carver developed 300 uses for peanuts and, in 1925, published 125 peanut recipes, several of which called for peanut pastes that were distant cousins to modern smooth and spreadable peanut butter.
In the early 20th century, improved technology produced an increasingly smooth, gritless peanut butter. By 1928, the addition of hydrogenated vegetable oils was found to keep the oil from separating from the peanut paste. The hydrogenating process was first used commercially by the company that later became Peter Pan. Skippy soon followed. In 1958 Procter & Gamble began producing Jif. Its benchmark advertising campaign - ''Choosy mothers choose Jif'' - established peanut butter as far from geriatric fare; peanut butter had become synonymous with childhood.
It still is. ''Per capita, children are the biggest consumers,'' Mr. Head said. Jif, Skippy and Peter Pan are still the top selling brands, sharing 65 percent of the market.
According to Mr. Head, the boom was precipitated by World War II. ''The Government provided peanut butter to the G.I.'s, they came home with a taste for it and started the baby boom,'' he said.
This could support Mr. Buckley's congenital theory of the origins of personal preference in peanut butter. Yet, it does not rule out the early-childhood-experience theory. C-ration peanut butter and 90 percent of those sold today are hydrogenated, sweetened and salted. Therefore a taste for a sweeter or a smoother spread could be an effect of environment.
Studying adult peanut butter eaters sheds little light on the origins of personal preference. Mr. Head said studies show that most Americans eat peanut butter until their early 20's, stop until they have children of their own and resume until their children have left home. ''They start eating it again in their later years for the exact same reason it was invented, good cheap, easily digestible nutrition,'' he said.
Despite this pattern, adults tend to treat their passion for peanut butter as aberrant, throwback behavior. In a vain effort to divert attention from their lapse, they rely on cute props. There are now 60,000 members in the Peanut Butter Lovers Fan Club that Mr. Head helped establish in 1986. His quarterly newsletter, Spread the News, circulates among 10,000 people. About 2,000 people are expected to attend the club's celebration on Labor Day weekend at Disney World to commemorate the 100th anniversary of the first manufacture of peanut butter in St. Louis.
Other grown-ups bring more serious concerns to bear on peanut butter. Those involved with dietary issues point out that two tablespoons of peanut butter contain about 190 calories, 5 grams of polyunsaturated fat and 3 grams of saturated fat, and that peanuts are a good source of protein.
Adults have also raised questions about aflatoxin, a carcinogen present in some moldy peanuts that was found to damage the livers of laboratory animals. In its September issue, Consumer Reports magazine said it found traces of aflatoxin in all the peanut butters it tested, although it said all its samples had levels below that permitted by the Food and Drug Administration. Based on its studies, the magazine estimated that with ''an intake of one peanut butter sandwich every 10 days, the lifetime risk might be about seven cases of cancer per million consumers.''
Mr. Head said that aflatoxin occurs in nature in various grains and that in the peanut industry the incidence of aflatoxin is below the safety standard set by the Government.
So far, these concerns have not deterred adult peanut butter lovers. And their pattern of consumption appears to prove the developmental theory of peanut butter preference. Generally, grown-ups choose one that best reflects their evolving self image. At the height of the I-am-as-upscale-as-I-can-eat 80's, for instance, Tromply's Peanut Butter Fantasies of Boston made a splash with products like chocolate raspberry and banana crunch.
Then, ''fun'' and ''trendy'' gave way to ''pure'' and ''healthy'' in the nation's food vocabulary, and the pattern spilled over to peanut butter. ''The fastest-growing area is in the all-natural category,'' said Paul Burnish of Kroger Stores in Cincinnati.
Craig Sonksen, owner of the Krema Products Company, in Columbus, Ohio, the nation's oldest peanut butter manufacturer, said a public desire for ''good old-fashioned flavors'' may also be fueling his skyrocketing sales. Krema is still made the way it was 100 years ago, by roasting peanuts, grinding them twice and hand-packing the butter into jars.
Krema, once strictly a regional product, is now sold throughout the Midwest and it is headed for stores nationwide. ''We can't keep it on the shelves,'' said Mr. Sonksen. ''Baby boomers want their babies to have the real thing.'' At least they think they do. In a recent test at The New York Times, three adults who sampled nine peanut butters all ranked a New York store's fresh-ground natural peanut butter No. 1. But opinion diverged when it came to second choice. Although the samples were tasted from unmarked containers, the tasters ranked the peanut butter brand of their childhood or the brand they currently keep at home as a close runner-up. Personal taste in peanut butter can be educated, refined and matured. But backsliding is just a memory away.

OIL AND THE MIDEAST
 
Oil prices turned up, with the contract for October delivery rising 97 cents, to $27.88 a barrel, as traders searched for a new balance in the market after the $4 plunge on Monday. [Page D1.]

HEADLINE: Mobilizing for Desert Warfare

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK

 BODY:
As American soldiers mobilize in the Saudi Arabian desert, hundreds of American companies are mobilizing in their own way to deliver to the armed forces everything from bullets to bug spray. While no one is hoping for a war, many companies have a chance to reap unexpected profit from the military buildup.
One company, Sterling Foods Inc. of San Antonio, received an $8 million contract in March to make 18 million small loaves of bread to be served with soldiers' rations. Now with the movement of American troops to Saudi Arabia, the Pentagon's contract for the bread, which can last several years in sealed pouches, has been greatly expanded.
''It's almost like there's no lid on it; make as many as you can,'' said Roy D. Terracina, president of the privately owned company. ''We are running two full shifts, six days a week. It's good for business, yeah, but it's also kind of scary.'' 
 
Scramble by Pentagon
Businesses that supply the armed forces stand to benefit immediately and in the long run from the Middle Eastern conflict. Companies like Sterling Foods and Edcar, a Puerto Rico-based uniform company, will gain first from the short-term burst in sales as the Pentagon hastily scrambles to feed, dress and equip tens of thousands of soldiers for life in the desert.
In the last two weeks, Tyson Foods of Springdale, Ark., has supplied 240,000 pounds of frozen chicken to the armed forces. As is the case with other large companies, though, that amount represents a small amount of Tyson's business.
Still, as of last week, the Pentagon had bought 150,000 bottles of sunscreen lotion for $219,000; 6,000 boxes of lip balm for $99,000; 230,000 cans of foot powder for $80,000, and 40,000 cases of insect repellant for $76,400. The bill for canteen covers came to $470,000. And then there were more than 70,000 pairs of sunglasses, 100,000 pairs of goggles, 100,000 sewing kits and 17,000 handkerchiefs.
In the long run, analysts and executives say, military contractors and weapons suppliers will gain. That is because Pentagon spending will very likely not be trimmed as sharply as was being planned only a few months ago, when the cold war ended and it seemed peace was breaking out all over.
Experts say the Middle East conflict will not immediately lead to new orders for major weapon systems, partly because big military weapons purchases in the 1980's left the nation well equipped. ''You could go to war with Iraq for a year without buying a thing,'' said Lawrence J. Korb, senior fellow at the Brookings Institution.
So the companies that will see the most new business at first are those that make items like ammunition, spare parts and military clothing. Those goods will be used up more quickly than they would have been before troops were deployed overseas.
Some of the biggest increases in business have gone to companies that make uniforms needed for desert combat or chemical warfare.
The Pentagon has not had anyone making desert battle uniforms since 1986, and many of the soldiers were sent to Saudi Arabia in camouflage outfits designed to blend into European forests or Asian jungles. So two companies in Puerto Rico, Edcar and Propper International, are rushing to turn out 75,000 desert uniforms by October.
 
Protection Against Chemicals
Before Iraq invaded Kuwait on Aug. 2, Sac and Fox Industries Inc., owned by the Sac and Fox Indians, was one of four companies with contracts to make chemical warfare uniforms. Now, with concerns about Iraq's possible use of chemical weapons, the Pentagon cannot get the protective gear fast enough.
''I'm supposed to be making 2,000 pairs a day, but I'm only making close to 500,'' said Louise Fields, plant manager at Sac and Fox's plant in Idabel, Okla., although she said her sewers were working 52 hours a week. Even though the company is producing green forest-camouflage suits instead of tan desert ones, the Pentagon is ''tickled to death to get them,'' she said.
As a result of the activity the stock prices of two publicly traded small companies that specialize in military equipment have climbed rapidly. They are Survival Technology of Bethesda, Md., which makes an antidote for some nerve gases, and Engineered Support Systems in St. Louis, which makes decontamination equipment needed by troops facing chemical weapons. $41.5 Million for Food Food suppliers can also expect more business. Already the Pentagon has bought 500 tons of fresh fruits and vegetables and spent $41.5 million on meat and other food, according to the Defense Personnel Support Center in Philadelphia. The center acts as the Pentagon's main agency for coordinating the purchases of food and clothing for troops.
But in some cases, what appears to be an oasis of new business is actually a mirage. The bottled water concession in the desert would seem to be a great opportunity, but the Pentagon insists it is not actually buying that much water, and is instead relying on tap water, supplemented by desalinization plants it is building in Saudi Arabia.
Some of the first units to go to Saudi Arabia did buy bottled water because they were in a hurry, said Frank Johnson, a spokesman for the Defense Personnel Support Center. That water is being replaced with locally available water, but soldiers continue to use the old bottles as containers, making it appear to television viewers that huge quantities of mineral water are being consumed.
The water problem did provide a relatively small order for the Wisconsin Pharmacal Company of Jackson, Wis. It just received a $147,00 order for 260,000 bottles of its Potable Aqua water purification tablets.
 
Bad for the Bottom Line
Sometimes, what appears to be a bonanza can even be bad for the bottom line. Moyco Industries Inc. of Philadelphia had a contract to supply one million cans of its Itch-a-way foot powder to the armed forces over the course of a year. Now the Pentagon wants the cans all at once, forcing the company to add overtime costs to speed up production.
''It's bid at a price that I can't economically afford to go overtime,'' said Frank Palm, sales manager of the company. He said that after he appealed the Pentagon agreed to cover the extra costs of accelerated production.
But for many specialized military contractors, the increase promises to stave off severe cutbacks, even possible closings, that were on the way.
''We could have been looking at a layoff of some people had this not come along,'' said Keith Hane, general manager of the hand protection division at Siebe North Inc. in Charleston, S.C., The company, a subsidiary of Siebe P.L.C. of Great Britain, makes vinyl gloves to protect soldiers from chemical attacks. But Siebe's factories have been operating in recent years at a rate that barely kept them in business.
 
A Second Wind for Some
The same is true of three companies that make rations, which the Army now calls M.R.E.'s, or meals, ready to eat.
The Pentagon had planned to cut its M.R.E. orders to 2.2 million cases next year, from 3.1 million this year. That would have made it impossible for all of the companies to stay in business, said Laura Jansen, president of one of them, Cinpac Inc. of Cincinnati. But on Friday, even as she was being interviewed, Ms. Jansen received a facsimile transmission from the Pentagon saying it would increase its purchases for next year to 3.4 million cases.
''Is this a change or a stay of execution?'' Ms. Jansen asked. Like other contractors, she said she hoped the Middle East crisis would convince the Pentagon that it needed to sustain its suppliers, even through times of peace. But she is not sure that will be done.
 
An Economic Stimulant
The mobilization of industry in times of war has often been an economic stimulant and a sustained rise in military spending and in industrial production by related industries this time would do the same, many economists say.
''What it will actually do to the economy near term is to help prevent a recession,'' said Allen Sinai, chief economist for The Boston Company. He still predicts a mild recession later this year, but not as severe as it would have been without the increased military spending.
But there are forces at work this time that might counteract a large part of the economic gains.
One is the huge Federal budget deficit that will be worsened by increases in military spending, hurting the economy more in the long run. The other is that the rise in oil prices caused by the Middle East conflict, if sustained, will hurt the economy.
Airlines and shipping companies, for instance, are leasing planes and ships to the Pentagon to ferry troops and supplies to the Middle East. But that income has been offset by higher fuel costs, and could be further dampened by a decline in trade and tourism that would result from a recession.
 
Not Really Good News
Many analysts are still saying that the Defense Department's budget will continue to be cut, and call the current developments not really good news for the military industry, only less bad news.
''Right now we're still operating under the assumption that the defense budget will be reduced in the 1990's in a big way,'' said David Hensley, acting director of the business forecasting project at the University of California at Los Angeles. ''It will be unlikely that the 20,000 aircraft industry layoffs announced for the second half of this year will be reversed.''
Still, individual weapons programs and companies could benefit. The C-17 cargo plane, built by McDonnell Douglas, might now be saved from scheduled cutbacks because the Middle East crisis pointed out a shortage of airlift capacity, said Lawrence M. Harris, military industry analyst at Bateman Eichler, Hill Richards in Los Angeles. The company's F-15 fighter might also receive extra orders, he said.
Raytheon, which makes the Patriot missile system being used in Saudi Arabia, could also benefit, as could General Dynamics, which might see more orders for its M-1 tank, he said.

HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
The Oil Reserve: Big Is Beautiful

BYLINE: By Peter Passell

 BODY:
HOW much energy insurance is really enough?
Uncle Sam has already socked away 600 million barrels of oil - 75 days' worth of imports -in the nation's strategic petroleum reserve. And an interagency government study, completed in February, estimated that the likely benefits of yet another 150 million barrels, to reach the current target of 750 million barrels, would exceed the costs.
But setting targets by such calculations, argues Philip Verleger, an energy specialist at the Institute for International Economics, is ''like using a micrometer to measure a pillow.'' The value of the reserve as a deterrent to panic, Mr. Verleger believes, depends as much on soft perceptions as on hard economics. And with a quarter of the world's oil production capacity within range of Iraq's MIG-29's, a bigger reserve is looking better and better. 
To economists the size of the optimal oil reserve turns on a handful of variables. Best guesses must be made about the likely magnitude and duration of future supply disruptions, and how temporary shortages would affect the gross national product. The costs of buying and storing more oil are then weighed against the benefits of extending emergency supplies. Avoiding a $100 billion disruption that has a 1 percent chance of occurring next year would, for example, be worth $1 billion.
Such analysis, which the Administration is using to justify its reluctance to go beyond 750 million barrels, can be misleadingly precise. No one really knows, for example, whether it would take a $5-a-barrel or a $10-a-barrel price increase to tip the economy into recession. No one can know because much depends on how the Federal Reserve responded to the shock. Nor can the long-term cost of stockpiling oil be calculated accurately. If the price of oil were to increase each year by as much as the interest on the money for the stockpile, this cost would be nil.
But there are yet more fundamental reasons to treat the cost-benefit calculation with a grain of salt. Once ordinary supply patterns are disrupted, the amount of oil needed to keep the market in one piece depends on perceptions of both the Government's capacity to replace the lost fuel and its willingness to sell enough to prevent price inflation. Thus while more oil is undoubtedly better than less, the relationship between the size of the reserve and the market's response to a crisis is as foggy as spring in Kennebunkport, Me.
In the first few days of the Kuwait crisis, with about four million daily barrels lost to the market, prices barely budged. But the Administration, reportedly worried that a quick release from the reserve would give Saudi Arabia an excuse not to increase production, declared that it was saving the oil to cover ''physical shortages.'' Once the market absorbed that ambiguous message, prices began to dance to the rhythm of the war drums. For Mr. Verleger, the lesson is clear. America's 600 million barrels, combined with another 300 million to 400 million barrels held by other oil importing nations, would probably be enough to cover any but the most severe disruption. But with a reserve in this range, there will always be a rationale for not dipping into it. Mr. Verleger would thus opt for a stockpile of two billion barrels - enough ''so even President Bush wouldn't hesitate to use it.''
Purchased outright, another 1.4 billion barrels would cost tens of billions. But the Administration and some Congressional leaders are intrigued by the economist's favorite alternative: leasing it.
An exporter with very low production costs -like Saudi Arabia - might be persuaded to deliver oil to the American reserve in return for a modest annual fee. Simply moving a tiny fraction of Saudi Arabia's quarter-trillion barrels from one storage site to another would not affect the market value of Saudi oil in the long term; the Saudis would still own the oil and get the eventual proceeds. But Washington would gain control over its disposition - and thus a lever to dampen oil shocks.
Leasing would obviously serve American interests if the rent were low enough. And the rent that ought to satisfy the Saudis would be low indeed. It would cost, at most, $5 a barrel to produce and transport a barrel of Saudi crude to a storage site in Louisiana. Thus the cost to the Saudis would be the interest lost on the $5 investment - no more than, say, 50 cents annually.
In fact, Mr. Verleger believes that it would be in the Saudis' interest to absorb the full cost of a larger American stockpile. After all, moving a $25 barrel of oil out of the reach of Saddam Hussein (and the Iranian mullahs) ought to be worth 50 cents a year to the desert royals.
If you doubt it, consider how much the Kuwait princes might now wish they had paid to have an extra $30 billion or $40 billion in liquid assets tucked away in Louisiana. Oil, as the G.I.'s now hunkering down in the Saudi heat are learning, makes strange bedfellows.

HEADLINE: U.S. Foreign Trade Deficit Declined by 14% in Quarter

BYLINE: Reuters

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 28

 BODY:
The gap between United States imports and exports shrank to its lowest level in six and a half years between April and June, the Commerce Department said today, but the improvement was largely caused by cheaper oil imports that have since shot up in price.
The nation's total deficit in trade with the rest of the world fell 14 percent, to $22.6 billion in the quarter, compared with $26.3 billion in the first three months of the year. It was the lowest quarterly deficit since a $21.3 billion shortfall in the fourth quarter of 1983. 
The nation's trade performance has been improving steadily with record exports in the past several quarters, but analysts warned that the shock of higher oil prices may set back progress in the second half of the year.
 
Higher Oil Prices
Since Iraq's invasion of Kuwait Aug. 2, oil prices have moved violently, rising 50 percent to a seven-year high of $32.35 a barrel before dropping sharply this week. Several members of the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries are expected to raise production, which should help stabilize prices.
The Commerce Department said the average price of imported oil fell from $19.47 in the first quarter to $15.81 in the second quarter - a price level that is unlikely to be repeated.
''Oil prices are going to take some of the glow off this report because it represents the situation pre-oil shock,'' said Jean Sundrla, an economist with the Washington forecasting firm Evans Economics Inc. ''Definitely, the trade balance has been moving in the right direction, but these numbers are about to be hit hard by higher oil prices.''
 
Peak of $41 Billion
The United States quarterly trade deficit, calculated on a balance-of-payments basis that excludes military trade, peaked at $41 billion in the third quarter of 1987.
Since then, a weaker dollar and cheaper oil have made United States goods cheaper for foreigners, increasing American exports. In addition, the lower oil prices have cut the value of imports.
Total exports in the second quarter reached a record $96.7 billion, up from $96.3 billion in the first three months this year and higher than the $91.1 billion in last year's second quarter.
Imports fell between April and June to $119.3 billion, from $122.6 billion in the first quarter, though they were roughly equal to the value of imports in the second quarter of 1989.
 
Biggest Import Component
But the improvement in imports was accounted for entirely by cheaper oil, as oil imports fell to $12.1 billion from $15.6 billion in the first quarter. Non-petroleum imports were up slightly, to $107.2 billion from $106.9 billion.
The United States deficit with Japan increased slightly, to $10.4 billion from $10.3 billion in the first quarter. It was down, however, from $12.2 billion in last year's second quarter.
The deficit with Canada, the United States' largest trading partner, widened to $1.9 billion in the second quarter, from $1.7 billion in the first three months this year, but was down from a $2.3 billion deficit in last year's second quarter.

HEADLINE: American Acquisitions In Europe Up Sharply

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER

 BODY:
American acquisitions of European companies, led by the purchase by Philip Morris of the Swiss chocolate maker Jacobs Suchard, rose sharply in the second quarter after a sluggish performance in the first three months of the year.
The 53 deals, up from 37 in the first quarter, showed that American companies were still positioning themselves for European economic unity, scheduled for late 1992, by getting a foothold in Britain and on the Continent. The total value of the 17 transactions for which prices were reported was $5.5 billion. 
Other data show, however, that total acquisitions by American companies worldwide slowed slightly in the first six months of the year.
 
One Big Deal
With the Jacobs Suchard deal worth nearly $4 billion, or more than two-thirds the quarterly total of all United States deals in Europe, the value of reported American acquisitions in the second quarter was the second highest in the last 18 months.
Without the Suchard deal, the value of acquisitions, although up in the second quarter, was not as strong when compared with other quarters also adjusted for removal of large deals. Nevertheless, the number of second-quarter deals was far greater than in the first quarter and more than the average quarterly pace of the last six quarters.
''It definitely is not evidence of a slowing down,'' said Mark Dixon, editor of Translink's European Deal Review, which compiles the merger and acquisition data. ''If anything, it is evidence of a speeding up.''
According to Translink, American, Japanese and European companies were involved in a record 394 mergers or acquisitions in Europe in the second quarter. The total value of the deals in which prices were reported was 17,847 European currency units - an E.C.U. is a basket of European currencies - just below the record 17,933 E.C.U.'s in deals in the third quarter of last year.
Because the dollar has weakened against major European currencies since last year, the dollar value of the acquisitions rose to $21.7 billion in the second quarter, compared with $19.6 billion in the third quarter of 1989.
The Jacobs Suchard deal, at about $3.8 billion, accounted for 68.9 percent of the total value of the American deals in the second quarter (17 percent of all the deals) in which prices were reported. It was the largest single deal in the last 18 months. Switzerland is not one of the 12 countries in the European Economic Community, but Jacobs Suchard gives the Philip Morris Companies access to the community because of the large business it does there.
The remaining 16 priced American deals in the quarter totaled $1.7 billion. The largest of these was a $506 million purchase by the GTE Corporation of Thom Lighting in Britain.
While this $1.7 billion was more than twice the $731.7 million in deals during the anemic first quarter, the level was still below other recent quarters. The total of all deals for the fourth quarter of 1989 was $4.2 billion; the third quarter was $7 billion, and the average of all deals in the first two quarters of 1989 was almost $2 billion.
The Translink data also show that the Japanese continue to be relatively small players in Europe. Japanese companies ranked 10th on the list of acquirers there in the second quarter. Their 27 European deals in the first six months of the year, with a reported value of $924.6 million, were about the same as last year.

CORRECTION-DATE: September 1, 1990, Saturday, Late Edition - Final

 CORRECTION:
An article in Business Day on Wednesday about American acquisitions in Europe included an incorrect report from Translink's European Deal Review about a transaction between the GTE Corporation and Thorn Lighting in Britain. While GTE is discussing the acquisition of Thorn Lighting, no agreement has been reached.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Cummins Engine Slows Production

BYLINE: Reuters

DATELINE: COLUMBUS, Ind., Aug. 28

 BODY:
The Cummins Engine Company said it would reduce engine assembly to one shift at its headquarters operation here. Cummins said North American demand for heavy-truck engines had significantly declined because of the prevailing economic uncertainty and the Persian Gulf crisis.
The company recently took other measures to reduce costs, including voluntary layoffs and shutdowns of its Columbus plant on July 20 and Aug. 10. A shutdown is also scheduled for Friday. The Columbus plant previously operated two shifts, a spokeswoman said. Cummins has many similar engine assembly plants.

HEADLINE: Dow Edges Up 3.22 Points on Slow Volume

BYLINE: By ROBERT J. COLE

 BODY:
Stock prices moved ahead slightly yesterday on slow volume as some investors and traders took to the sidelines. Many said they were waiting for further signs as to where oil prices and the Middle East crisis might be heading.
The price of crude oil moved ahead by about $1 a barrel yesterday, to $27.88. But traders argued that while oil seemed to be the key for the short term, now was not the time to put money in the market - particularly after the Dow Jones industrial average has bounced back to regain 131 of the 173 points it lost in three days last week. 
The Dow ended the day at 2,614.85, up 3.22.
Trading volume slowed to 127.65 million shares, the lowest in two weeks, and down from 160.2 million the day before. Advances narrowly outstripped declines 837 to 664. Three stocks touched new highs, but 66 hit new lows.
 
New Low for Cummins Engine
One was Cummins Engine, which traded as high as 55 1/2 this year. It sank yesterday to a new low of 40 1/8, ending the day at 40 1/2, down 7/8. The company said economic uncertainty and the situation in the Middle East had accelerated the decline in demand for truck engines and that it cut plant operations in Columbus, Ind., to one shift.
The mood of the market is ''wait and see,'' said Ronald J. Hill, investment strategist for Brown Brothers Harriman.
''We're still at risk in the Middle East,'' he said, ''and potential developments could still send oil prices higher again. So long as that possibility remains, you have a ceiling on how high stock prices will go.''
The market's strength, although slight, seemed concentrated in the big-name blue chips and to a slightly greater extent in the smaller stocks in the Nasdaq over-the-counter market and on the American Stock Exchange.
While the Dow rose 12-hundredths of a percent, the broader Standard & Poor's index of 500 stocks rose barely one-tenth of a point to 321.34. But the Nasdaq composite index rose 1.59 points to 382.86 and the Amex market-value index rose 1.05 points to 321.78.
 
Cautious Optimism
Caution, or perhaps cautious optimism, seem to be the market bywords for the present. Traders adopted an attitude that now might be the time to buy on weakness. Many hunted for bargains, while others took profits.
Among the Dow industrials, most changes were relatively small because of the small change in the Dow. Even so, General Motors headed the Big Board list of the 10 most-active stocks, rising 1 1/8, to 40 1/4, on 1.7 million shares.
A.T.& T., another big Dow stock, fell 1, to 32 1/8, on 1.3 million shares, and Procter & Gamble rose 1 1/8, to 77. The only two other Dow stocks to show a point or more change were Chevron, which rose 1 1/8, to 75 5/8, and Texaco, which rose 1 3/4, to 62 3/4.
Other oil shares also registered strong gains. Royal Dutch rose 1 3/4, to 80 3/4; ARCO 1 7/8, to 134 1/8; British Petroleum 2 1/2, to 81 1/2; Amoco 1 3/8, to 55 3/4; Pennzoil 1 1/4, to 77 1/2, and Amerada Hess 1, to 52 3/8.
Companies that serve the oil producers also advanced briskly. Schlumberger climbed 1 1/4, to 63 1/4; Kerr McGee 1, to 47 3/4 and McDermott 1 1/4, to 27 1/4.
 
Weak Insurance Group
In a weak insurance group, General Re, a leading reinsurance company, fell 3 5/8, to 69, a new low, in brisk trading. Analysts said the reinsurance market might not turn profitable for a year.
On Nasdaq, In-Store Advertising plunged 7 1/2, to 7 1/4, in heavy trading after it announced that revenues this year would not come close to analysts' forecasts.
Although many foreign markets advanced, the Dow fell 23 points almost from the opening bell yesterday, seesawing its way back up again, then back down and back up again.
 
Recession Fears
Overhanging the market most of the day was strong Wall Street sentiment that while the situation in the Middle East had not deepened into a shooting war, the market's basic weakness was still the developing recession. That, in turn, led many investors to feel that further weakness could be developing.
Birinyi Associates, which tracks program trading, said that buying and selling programs continued for most of the day but that the net effect was to lift the Dow barely 10 points. In the biggest move of the day, a 12-minute series of buying programs starting around 12:40 raised the Dow just 7.5 points, Birinyi said. ----World Markets Mixed By Reuters The world's stock markets turned in a mixed performance yesterday, with Japanese and British shares rising and German and French markets turning lower.
In Tokyo, stocks continued their broad advance, picking up from Monday. Falling oil prices and a stronger yen fueled advances but persistent market rumors of trouble in the Persian Gulf capped gains.
The 225-share Nikkei average was up 569.00 points, or 2.26 percent, to 25,710.76 after gaining 976 on Monday. But at the midday recess today, the average lost almost 453 points, or 1.76 percent, to 25,257.93.
In London, after a long holiday weekend, shares advanced sharply after Monday's gains elsewhere. Volume was light. The Financial Times index ended up 39.7 points at 2,126.1.
In Frankfurt, profit taking knocked shares sharply lower after Monday's record 6 percent rise. The DAX 30-share index of leading West German shares ended off 36.91 at 1,617.89.
Share prices also fell in Paris after previous strong gains. The market waited for a new sense of direction with traders nervous and volumes thin. The CAC-40 index closed 19.14 points lower, at 1,680.58 after Monday's record rise of more than 5 percent.
In Zurich, shares fell in active trading as uncertainty about the Gulf crisis and profit taking combined to bring prices down. The all-share Swiss Performance Index closed 17.3 points lower at 1,006.8.
Hong Kong prices climbed sharply after rallies in New York and Tokyo, closing near the day's peak. The Hang Seng index surged 160.93, or 5.5 percent, to 3,078.94.

HEADLINE: FUTURES/OPTIONS;
Grain and Soybeans Mixed; Livestock Prices Are Down

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
Grain and soybeans futures prices were mixed yesterday, and livestock and pork futures fell.
Analysts said traders were taking a wait-and-see attitude after Monday's sharp decline of prices in the grain market. The selloff was tied to the drop in crude oil and precious metals prices. 
''There was very little action in the stocks, oil and precious metals, so the grains traded both sides of the market,'' said Jerry Zusel, an analyst with the Balfour Maclaine Corporation in Chicago.
Grain and soybean prices were pushed higher last week on fears of war in the Middle East and fears of inflation in the United States, he said.
''People are watching events in the Middle East,'' Mr. Zusel said. ''If we get a new spate of news on a negative note, then grain prices will rise again. Otherwise grains will be on the defensive.''
The latest state crop reports indicate the condition of corn and soybeans has improved because of hot, dry weather. Prices are also being pressured by a lack of export demand.
Wheat was 1 3/4 cents lower to 1 1/2 cents higher, with September at $2.79 3/4 a bushel; corn was 1 1/4 cent lower to 1 3/4 cents higher, with September at $2.53 3/4 a bushel; oats were 3 1/2 cents to 5 cents lower, with September at $1.08 1/2 a bushel; soybeans were 3 cents lower to 1 1/4 cents higher, with September at $6.19 3/4 a bushel.
Livestock and pork futures were lower on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange as profit taking set in after Monday's price climb.
Sluggish retail demand for beef weighed on livestock futures as expections for lower cash hog prices pushed pork futures lower.
Live cattle were 0.30 cent lower to 0.15 cent higher, with October at 76.65 cents a pound; feeder cattle were 0.02 cent to 0.25 cent lower, with August at 88.57 cents a pound; hogs were 0.13 cent to 0.77 cent lower, with October at 48.45 cents a pound; frozen pork bellies were 1.80 cents to 2 cents lower, with February at 52.37 cents a pound.

HEADLINE: CONFRONTATION IN THE GULF;
Japan Promises Grants and Food, But Lack of Arms Aid Nettles U.S.

BYLINE: By STEVEN R. WEISMAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, Thursday, Aug. 30

 BODY:
Japan unveiled a long-awaited program on Wednesday night to help finance the international effort against Iraq, but the package drew unusual criticism from American officials for the absence of direct tangible aid to the military buildup in the Persian Gulf.
The package includes an unspecified amount of loans and grants to Egypt, Turkey and Jordan, the dispatching of 100 medical specialists to the region, and a pledge to supply refrigeration equipment, water and other goods to help soldiers cope with the desert heat.
Officials said the aid program might ultimately amount to billions of dollars, but that any estimate would be vague because Tokyo plans long negotiations on the terms of loans and grants and the specific projects to be financed.
The Finance Ministry said last week that Japan would provide financial aid to countries hit hard by a loss in revenue because of the gulf crisis. But this week it stipulated that such help would be granted only in coordination with other countries and the International Monetary Fund, which is already pressing Egypt to adopt difficult austerity measures. 
These requirements would almost certainly delay the aid and limit the amount of money available, offering little immediate relief to countries that are short of cash because of the cutoff in Middle Eastern oil or the loss of remittances from nationals working in oilfields there.
Tokyo also pledged Wednesday night to transport food, water and medical supplies to the gulf on Japanese civilian aircraft and ships, and to pay for military equipment to be flown there on planes and ships chartered from other nations.
Today, Japanese officials said the cost of chartering ships and planes and shipping medical and other supplies to the Middle East would be about $1 billion.
 
Constitution Bans Force
Despite public pressure from Washington to do more than simply underwrite the military efforts undertaken by others, Prime Minister Toshiki Kaifu said it would violate Japan's Constitution for the country to transport any military personnel or equipment to a region of potential combat.
''There are things we can do and cannot do,'' the Prime Minister said, adding that the package ''represents the maximum extent we can go.''
Japan's Constitution, written by the United States after World War II, renounces ''the threat or use of force as a means of settling international disputes.'' It has long been cited by Tokyo as a barrier to action, even in using military aircraft to aid earthquake victims in Armenia in 1988.
Mr. Kaifu, who looked grim and fatigued at his news conference Wednesday night, seemed to be more concerned with Japanese opposition to his package as too radical than with American concerns that it is too modest. He appealed to the Japanese to abandon their aloof approach to world political crises.
''If Japan's response is delayed and the world gets the impression that Japan does nothing for peace in the region, when its own important national interest is at stake, Japan's future will be lost,'' the Prime Minister declared.
There was no official comment from the United States immediately after Mr. Kaifu announced the package. But American officials made it clear earlier in the day that they were ''very unhappy,'' as one put it, with the omission of an indirect or direct Japanese military role.
''If you look at the situation in the Middle East, we judge it to be basically a military situation,'' said an American official, his voice edged with sarcasm. ''There's a limited number of Girl Scout cookies that can be used there.''
In another unusual move, the United States Ambassador, Michael H. Armacost, told Japanese editorial writers on Tuesday evening that Americans were feeling ''impatience, bewilderment and exasperation'' with Japan's delays in announcing its plans in the gulf, a spokesman for the American Embassy said.
He said Mr. Armacost expressed concern that Japanese assistance would be ''so circumscribed as to hinder its operational usefulness and thus its political value.'' The Ambassador has made it clear that he does not agree that the Constitution prevents further action.
 
U.S. Wants Minesweepers
Among the steps urged by Mr. Armacost and other officials is the use of Japanese minesweepers to patrol waters in the gulf. They also have asked that Japanese ships normally used for transporting automobiles to the United States be commandeered to send tanks and other equipment to the Middle East.
Aides to Mr. Armacost, who only last week were praising Japan for its swift expression of support in the days after Iraq's invasion of Kuwait, had a different tone on Wednesday, saying that Tokyo's inaction would stir resentment in Congress that Japan is not putting itself at risk like other nations.
Some also warned that Japan's response might set back Tokyo's quest for a seat on the United Nations Security Council.
Taizo Watanabe, a spokesman for the Foreign Ministry, said he could not say whether the aid to Turkey, Egypt and Jordan would be ''in the millions or billions'' of dollars. He said the assistance would likely be in the form of low-cost loans or grants for projects to be identified later.
Project loans, however, are not what the countries of the Middle East need right now, many experts say. These countries are suffering rather from a loss of cash flow amounting to billions of dollars. Mr. Watanabe said it was his understanding that Japan would not be meeting the Middle Eastern country's ''cash needs.''
Officials and experts said there were several reasons for Japan's failure to come forward with a bolder package. Among them is the Japanese people's reluctance to violate the spirit or letter of a Constitution that has taken on an almost sanctified status.
Some Japanese officials are said to fear that a greater military role for Japan would alarm Asian neighbors that remain wary about the country's intentions 45 years after the end of World War II. Others say that the Japanese oppose giving more authority to the military, whom they blame for the disaster of World War II.
The Foreign Ministry and Mr. Kaifu himself have been described by officials in the last few weeks as the most eager for Japan to step up its role in the Middle East crisis. But wider measures were opposed by other ministries and by political rivals of Mr. Kaifu, who do not like to see him succeed or improve his image.
Mr. Watanabe said on Wednesday night that as a practical matter, Japan would be prevented from transporting combat-related personnel or equipment on commercial aircraft because the employees of shipping and aircraft companies have made it clear they would refuse to carry such cargo into a war zone.
Japan Air Lines is said to be worried about several of its employees being held hostage by Iraq.

HEADLINE: THE U.N.'S WATERSHED: A SPECIAL REPORT:
CONFRONTATION IN THE GULF;
Putting Teeth in an Embargo: How U.S. Convinced the U.N.

BYLINE: By ELAINE SCIOLINO with ERIC PACE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 29

 BODY:
The phone call to Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d on Aug. 22 at his Wyoming ranch was not the first to interrupt his vacation, but it was one of the most important. The Soviet Foreign Minister, Eduard A. Shevardnadze, called Mr. Baker's remote mountain retreat to say Moscow was leaning toward supporting a United Nations resolution giving the world's navies the right to enforce the international trade embargo against Iraq.
It would be two days before the Soviets gave their full approval, but the call was a pivotal point in a six-day round-the-clock diplomatic effort by the United States and its Western allies to convince the Soviet Union, China and defiant third-world countries that urgent action was needed to enforce the United Nations sanctions imposed after Iraq's invasion of Kuwait.
On the basis of the call, the United States delegate to the United Nations, Thomas R. Pickering, and other Administration officials redoubled their efforts to build an international coalition to restrain President Saddam Hussein of Iraq.
 
An Improvised Effort
At 4:10 A.M. on Saturday Aug. 25, the Security Council voted 13 to 0, with two abstentions, to approve Resolution 665, calling on member states with naval forces in the area to take action ''as may be necessary'' to ''insure strict implementation'' of the embargo on trade with Iraq. 
It was the first time in the United Nations' 45-year history that such sweeping military authority had been conferred without a United Nations flag or command. And it was the most striking example of Soviet-American cooperation within the international organization.
But according to interviews with senior Administration officials and foreign diplomats involved in the negotiations, the effort to enlist the United Nations was largely improvised, and did not flow from lofty principles of international unity.
Administration officials said the United States turned to the United Nations primarily to deflect objections from other countries that Washington was acting alone, and to avoid problems if ships from countries sympathetic to the sanctions against Iraq were stopped by American warships.
''There was no prescribed game plan,'' a senior Administration official said. ''We were thinking things up moment by moment.'' The adoption of the resolution depended on a blend of delicate diplomacy and arm twisting by the United States and its allies, rising Soviet impatience with Iraq and close consultation between Mr. Baker and Mr. Shevardnadze, whose working relationship and budding friendship were instrumental in obtaining support for the resolution.
 
The Embargo
 
Going It Alone, Thinking Again
An hour after Iraqi tanks rolled into Kuwait at 2 A.M. New York time, Aug. 2, Robert M. Kimmitt, Under Secretary of State for Political Affairs, was on the phone to Mr. Pickering at the United Nations, instructing him to call an urgent Security Council meeting to condemn the aggression and demand Iraq's immediate and unconditional withdrawal. A resolution to that effect sailed through the Security Council on Aug. 3, as did later resolutions imposing trade sanctions against Iraq, declaring the annexation of Kuwait null and void, and challenging Iraq's seizure of foreign hostages and its closure of foreign embassies in Kuwait.
Given this extraordinary cooperation from the Security Council, world leaders were startled when Mr. Baker announced on Sunday Aug. 12, that the United States had decided to go it alone with an ''interdiction'' of Iraqi commerce at sea. Mr. Baker argued that the action was legally justified by a formal Kuwaiti request under Article 51 of the United Nations Charter. Article 51 recognizes members' right to ''individual or collective self-defense'' while waiting for Security Council action.
But by Monday Aug. 13, several members of the Security Council were asserting in a closed meeting that an impatient American President was imposing a blockade, calling it something else, using a questionable legal formula to justify it and doing it without telling anybody. The diplomats, led by Yves Fortier, Canada's representative, argued that the Security Council should instead take a step-by-step approach under the Charter's Article 42, which provides for blockades and other military actions but only with explicit Security Council approval.
''I tried to make the point that Canada is Washington's best friend on the Council, and that if your best friend can't bring you bad news, who can?'' said Mr. Fortier. ''I said that these are uncharted waters, that there are no precedents, so why not play it as the framers of the Charter had envisioned it?''
Another diplomat cabled back to his capital, ''For first time since the beginning of the crisis the United States looked isolated.''
At that moment, the Bush Adminstration began a concerted campaign of damage control. Mr. Baker telephoned the French Foreign Minister, Roland Dumas, and Mr. Shevardnadze, saying yes, perhaps the United States should have consulted more and assuring them that the United States wanted their help in enforcing the embargo.
Mr. Shevardnadze repeated what he had told Mr. Baker the previous week, that perhaps the time was right to reinvigorate a moribund United Nations mechanism known as the Military Staff Committee as a collective way to resolve the Persian Gulf crisis.
The United States, jealously guarding its own prerogatives, has long resisted committing American forces to a military operation under a United Nations umbrella. But on Tuesday Aug. 14, Mr. Kimmitt called in the representatives of the other permanent members of the United Nations to pursue the Soviet idea and discuss putting warships in the gulf under United Nations auspices. One diplomat who attended the meeting said, ''The Americans threw the Soviets the bone they wanted.''
 
The Challenge
 
A Chinese Vessel And Iraqi Tankers
The Bush Administration still had not decided to push hard for a Security Council resolution legitimizing American ''interdiction.'' ''Our strategy was that we were going to do whatever we had to do on our own, and if we could bring along the U.N., then fine,'' said a senior Administration official.
But by the end of the week, American intelligence officials warned that the United Nations sanctions were about to be broken as an Iraqi tanker, loaded with Iraqi oil, headed toward Yemeni waters. The United States would have to decide whether to stop the tanker or allow it to steam into port.
Complicating matters was the fact that intelligence officials spotted a Chinese cargo ship believed to be carrying fertilizer moving from Iraq's only functioning port at Umm Qasr. Although the Chinese vessel had to return to port because of mechanical problems, the incident highlighted the risks of the American decision to block Iraqi shipping on its own. The Bush Administration knew that to seize or disable a Chinese vessel would probably destroy any chance of further collective measures at the Security Council, where China has veto power, and could turn world opinion against the United States.
On the evening of Monday Aug. 20, Mr. Pickering was instructed to call an urgent Security Council meeting to seek a resolution allowing the use of force to enforce the sanctions. His explicit order was to keep the Security Council in session however long it took to get a resolution passed.
Mr. Pickering, 58 years old, a former Ambassador to Israel, Jordan and El Salvador and one of the State Department's most senior career ambassadors, began what other diplomats described as a heroic effort to put a positive face on a terrible brief. He argued that the Iraqi tanker, the Khaniqin, would reach Yemeni waters by 6 A.M. New York time on Tuesday Aug. 21, and urged Security Council members to immediately accept a joint American-British draft resolution.
But the members of the Council had no instructions from their capitals on how to vote and demanded evidence of the Khanaqin's whereabouts that Mr. Pickering could not provide. The meeting adjourned after 1 A.M., after Yemen's chief delgate informed the Council that his country would not allow the tanker to unload its cargo. Mr. Pickering thanked his Yemeni colleague in fluent Arabic.
''Pickering is a prince of a man but many of us felt that the Americans lost a lot of good will that evening,'' said a Western ambassador.
On Wednesday, Aug. 22, Mr. Pickering presented an ad hoc sanctions committee of the Council with an array of maps showing the locations of a number of Iraqi tankers, while Sir Crispin Tickell, the chief British representative, offered evidence of Libyan complicity in sanction busting.
''It wasn't quite Bay of Pigs stuff, but at that point, a number of us were convinced,'' said a participant.
 
The Strategy
 
Moving China And the Soviets
The United States and its Western allies had also carried out an extraordinary diplomatic campaign to keep the Soviets and the Chinese involved in the negotiating process.
The eventual success of the resolution was largely the result of the telephone diplomacy of Mr. Baker and Mr. Shevardnadze, whose relationship has bloomed in the last year. Their cooperation has been so close that it was Mr. Baker who first informed his Soviet counterpart of the invasion while the two of them were meeting in Siberia.
In the weeks that followed, the two men stayed in contact, sometimes several times a day, either directly by phone or through their envoys in each other's capitals.
But after the Americans began their push for a resolution to enforce the embargo, Mr. Shevardnadze made it clear that the Soviet Union wanted to exhaust its own diplomatic initiative with Mr. Hussein before embracing the punitive resolution.
For more than two decades, Moscow has supplied Baghdad with the bulk of its weaponry, trained its military and helped build its infrastructure.
In meetings in Moscow with Saadun Hammadi, an Iraqi Deputy Prime Minister and veteran diplomat, Soviet officials blamed Iraq for the crisis and urged Mr. Hussein to observe Security Council calls to withdraw from Kuwait and release foreign hostages. If he did not, the Soviets said, Iraq would face tougher measures by the world body.
By Wednesday Aug. 22, Mr. Shevardnadze told Mr. Baker by phone that Moscow needed to give President Hussein 48 more hours, but that the United States should go forward with a resolution. In a phone call the next day, Mr. Shevardnadze told Mr. Baker that President Mikhail S. Gorbachev had sent an urgent personal appeal to President Hussein.
But on Friday, Aug. 24, Aleksandr M. Belonogov, a Soviet Deputy Foreign Minister and former chief envoy to the United Nations, urgently summoned the American Ambassador, Jack Matlock, to the Foreign Ministry and gave the go-ahead.
All the Soviets wanted, said Mr. Belonogov, were two small textual changes. The first was the deletion of the specific reference to the use of ''minimum force'' and substitution of the more general phrase, ''measures commensurate to the specific circumstances.'' The second was inclusion of the phrase ''maximum use of political and diplomatic measures.''
The American side saw the changes as strengthening, not weakening the final resolution, because they did not preclude the use of force, minimum or otherwise.
'' 'We've heard from the Iraqis and the answer is unsatisfactory,' '' said a senior Administration official, paraphrasing Mr. Belonogov's words. With a few small amendments, he said, the Soviets were ''ready to go.''
''The fishing buddies stayed in real close touch and in the end that was the key,'' a senior Administration official said.
The Chinese also needed convincing, but of another kind. From the beginning, China had opposed the use of force to back sanctions and stated its belief that great-power involvement in the Persian Gulf made the crisis worse.
In a Presidential message to Chinese leaders, and in meetings between American and Chinese officials in Washington, the United Nations and Beijing, the Bush Administration drove home the point that it would be unthinkable for one of the five permanent members to veto the resolution. China, eager to resume a role as an important player in the world and delighted at having established diplomatic relations with Saudi Arabia in July, went along.
 
The Final Night
 
Temporary Delay, Ultimate Victory
The evening of Friday Aug. 24, the Security Council met for a marathon session that would end in their predawn resolution.
Still, the vote to approve the resolution did not come easily. The Council had to wait for the Chinese to get final instructions from Beijing. Cuba, one of the 10 rotating members of the 15-member Council, tried a last-ditch effort to convince other third-world Council members to delay the vote. Other third-world delegations tried to change the language to effectively establish a joint United Nations command over the naval activities. Mr. Pickering and Sir Crispin were seen huddling in a corridor near the Security Council trying to get the full backing of recalcitrant Colombian and Malaysian envoys.
At 4:10 A.M., as the vote was taken, some of the delegates turned to China's chief delegate, Li Daoyu, to see if he would abstain, as they had expected. He did not, but raised his hand with 12 others to vote for the resolution. Cuba and Yemen abstained.
Even in the face of unity, however, there were gradations in tone and different interpretations of what the resolution actually meant.
''United States naval forces, in coordination with other naval forces in the area, would use minimum force only as necessary'' to enforce the trade sanctions structly, Mr. Pickering said in praising the Council in his formal remarks after the vote. Valentin V. Lozinskiy, the deputy Soviet representative, struck a more cautious note, urging ''prudence and caution and not to permit reliance on forcible methods.'' And China's envoy, Mr. Li, said that his country believed that the wording of the adopted resolution ''does not contain the concept of using force.''
