HEADLINE: In New York, Low Count Draws Chorus of Criticism

BYLINE: By MIREYA NAVARRO

 BODY:
Preliminary 1990 census figures show New York City with a population of 7,033,179, significantly smaller than the Census Bureau estimated in 1988 and at least 600,000 people short of estimates by city planners and at least one outside expert.
The numbers released yesterday drew a loud chorus of criticism from state and local officials and were expected to fuel the debate over the accuracy of the 1990 count and renew calls for a statistical adjustment to compensate for any undercounting.
The disputed figures could mean fewer Congressional seats for New York State and the loss of millions of dollars in Federal aid.
New York City officials, who estimate the city's population at 8.1 million, have been among the most vocal critics of the census and have filed a lawsuit against the Department of Commerce, of which the Census Bureau is a part, to win an adjustment.
''This is unadulterated nonsense,'' Mayor David N. Dinkins said of the latest figures. ''The Census Bureau itself admitted that its 1980 count missed some 450,000 New Yorkers.'' 
Gov. Mario M. Cuomo, speaking in Albany, repeated a widespread belief among Democrats that the Republican Administration sees an undercount as a way to erode Democratic representation and it ''has no real interest in changing the way we count.''
''The numbers are low, period,'' he said. ''We'll do anything we can to challenge it because it's wrong, in my opinion.''
Initial numbers released yesterday by the Census Bureau put the city's population 38,460, or 0.5 percent, less than the 1980 total, 7,071,639. In 1988, the bureau estimated the city's population had grown to 7,352,700, while studies by city planners and outside experts have estimated a population closer to 8 million. A breakdown of the population by borough was not available.
Discrepancies between the preliminary data and the bureau's own projections have also emerged in tallies for New York, New Jersey and other states, although the reasons are still unclear. Officials from the Census Bureau would not comment yesterday on the differences between the latest figures and earlier estimates, but they stressed that the information should be considered ''working numbers'' subject to change.
Rough figures also released yesterday showed that the population of New York State rose by 0.4 percent, or 68,421, to 17,626,586. New Jersey and Connecticut also gained population -by 3.4 percent and 3.8 percent, respectively - but the figures for these two states, released last week, were also lower than expected.
The census data, which are being sent to about 39,000 local governments for possible corrections during a phase called post-census local review, is already being questioned by New York City officials and at least one expert outside government.
 
Demographic Indicators
City officials said immigration and records from utilities, the Housing Authority and other sources indicate that New York City's population has grown in the last decade. Emanuel Tobier, a professor of planning and economics at the Robert F. Wagner School of Public Service at New York University, estimated in a study scheduled for release next week that the city's population was 7,622,000 in 1987. He said the figure should be closer to 7.8 million now.
Mr. Tobier said he used the 1980 census as a base and then projected population growth to 1987 using information about births, deaths, enrollment in public and private schools, Social Security recipients and other demographic indicators. The number of births alone, he said, increased by 28 percent between 1980 and 1989.
''It's inconceivable to me that this type of increase could take place without a significant increase in the population,'' he said. ''Since births are a recorded event, and the census figures are based upon their finding people, I have to say that the census figures have to be wrong ones, and by a lot.''
States and cities have three weeks to challenge the estimates by matching the census tally against their own lists. The bureau is then supposed to recanvass areas where discrepancies occurred.
 
Information Is 'Snapshot'
''Once we see the workload we have, there'll be reallocations of money to serve those areas that are challenging'' the preliminary figures, said John J. Connolly, assistant to Bureau Director Barbara Everitt Bryant.
Mr. Connolly said the preliminary information ''is a snapshot of an ongoing process.''
''We expect these numbers throughout the country to be higher,'' Mr. Connolly said. ''We want the localities to help us find housing units we might have missed.''
New York City officials said they had identified several instances where the count might have been inaccurate. During their review of the numbers, they said, they will first look at whether the Census Bureau accounted for 256,000 housing units that the city had pointed out as missing or misplaced in the bureau's original address list.
 
Time Is a Concern
''We have a substantial number of blocks that weren't on their list and that we submitted to them and when the mailing came out didn't get the forms,'' said Hulbert James, census project coordinator for the Mayor's office.
Mr. James said another area that merits close inspection is the number of units listed as vacant, or that were altogether deleted, during the door-to-door count that followed up on people who did not mail in census forms. He said at least 176,421 units were classified as vacant and 337,613 units were deleted.
Another concern is the time allowed to review the figures. Evelyn S. Mann, director of the population division of the New York City Department of City Planning, said a staff of two to three dozen computer specialists, geographers and demographers would spend the next two weeks making a computer match of the bureau's numbers with the city's own list of housing units in 29,000 to 30,000 city blocks. But because time is short, she said, the workers can research only big differences between the two lists.
 
'Back to the Maps'
The city's list is based on utility records, housing authority and real property records, land use maps and aerial photographs, new construction and demolitions.
''We could go back to the maps, aerial photos and start all over again to build up data for a particular block to recheck both sets of numbers, but we won't be able to do that except in a very limited way for really outstanding differences,'' she said.
The numbers are troubling for the the city and the state because they are used to allocate Federal aid and Congressional representation.
In addition, Jeffrey M. Wice, Washington counsel to Mel Miller, the Speaker of the State Assembly, which is controlled by Democrats, predicted that the state could lose three seats in Congress based on the figures released yesterday. The state could hold that loss to just two seats, he added, if future adjustments boosted New York's population count by about 250,000 and the rough tallies for six other states remained the same.
But these projections are not certain, he said. ''I wouldn't want anyone to think that this is definitely no more than a three-seat loss,'' he said. ''The chances of losing a fourth seat are now somewhat remote, but they're not out of the question.''
New York Republicans, who now control the State Senate, also expressed concern that the numbers released today did not adequately portray New York's true population count but said they would review the numbers more closely before deciding on any legal action.
''We haven't had a good enough look at the overall state numbers to decide on that,'' said John McArdle, a spokesman for Ralph J. Marino, the majority leader of the State Senate.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1, Column 1; National Desk

HEADLINE: Census Data Show Sharp Rural Losses

BYLINE: By FELICITY BARRINGER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 29

 BODY:
From the Mississippi River delta to the Appalachian mountains of Kentucky to the high plains of North Dakota, rural America suffered far greater losses in population during the 1980's than had been expected, according to preliminary figures from the 1990 census.
The rural losses, combined with big gains in thriving Western and Southern metropolitan areas like Seattle, Los Angeles and Orlando, Fla., are expected to mean that 19 of the country's 435 Congressional seats will change hands among the states, with New York losing three and California gaining seven. That would give California 52, or 12 percent of the nation's total.
The preliminary estimate for the country's population was about 245.8 million, based on the mail return of census forms and follow-up door-to-door head counts. But Census Bureau officials said efforts to count people missed in those initial surveys had already added or will add at least three million people, bringing the national total near 250 million. That figure would be about even with the bureau's earlier estimates. 
 
Millions of Immigrants
More than at any time since World War I, the population increase was driven by immigration, both legal and illegal. Immigrants - 7 million to 9 million over the decade, largely from Asia, Latin America and the Caribbean - represent at least one-third and perhaps as much as 40 percent of the estimated increase of 23 million people, according to data from the Immigration and Naturalization Service and analysis by the Urban Institute, a Washington-based research group.
That fact may be a slight damper on Republican strategists' enthusiasm for the new census numbers. The areas that are the biggest gainers, particularly around Los Angeles and throughout Florida, are areas that helped put Ronald Reagan and George Bush in the White House. But at least in California, as much as 30 percent of the growth is due to immigration. Whether the new Asian and Mexican immigrants there will follow the immigrant tradition of voting Democratic is not yet clear.
The portrait of America that emerges from the preliminary data reveals a country continuing the trends of the 1960's and 1970's, when more and more of the population turned southward and westward and once-powerful manufacturing industries of the East and Middle West crumbled.
In fact, the new data show, at least 55 percent of Americans now live in the South and the West, up from 52 percent in 1980 and 48 percent in 1970. By contrast, only 20 percent of Americans live in the Northeast, down from 21.7 a decade ago, and 24 percent live in the Middle West, down from nearly 26 percent in 1980.
 
Following the Sun
In the 1980's, the new data show, a growing number of retirees went looking for sun, sea and mountain peaks, and the new worlds of high-technology manufacturing and a growing service economy created boom towns along the California and Florida coasts.
''In the 1970's, you had Sun Belt growth, but a lot of it was in the small metropolitan areas and non-metropolitan areas,'' said William H. Frey, a demographer from the University of Michigan. ''In the 1980's, we've flipped in the other direction, toward more metropolitan growth. The Sun Belt is still growing faster than the snow belt, but in general the growth in the 1980's centered on the metropolitan areas around the coasts.''
All the decade's demographic trends seemed to converge in California, which, according to Jeffrey Passell of the Urban Institute, became home to 25 to 30 percent of the decade's immigrants. But the high-technology industries and the expanding service economies in everything from software development to law to recreation to real estate also drove dramatic growth rates in Los Angeles, San Diego and San Jose. Seven counties in California have more than a million people. Only four other states, New York, Florida, Texas and Pennsylvania, have more than one county with a million people.
Los Angeles also passed Chicago as the country's second-largest city, while San Diego moved past Detroit and Dallas to become the sixth-largest.
 
The Winner: California
Indeed, the decade's biggest winner was California, which accounted for nearly a quarter of the nation's population growth - and will gain political power to match - for a total of more than 29 million people. Florida grew by about 31 percent, to a total of just under 13 million, in the preliminary data, while Texas increased by 2.6 million, or 18.3 percent. The five southwestern states of Nevada, New Mexico, Arizona, Utah and Colorado grew by 22 percent, up to a total of 11 million.
Meanwhile, Pennsylvania and Illinois, powerhouse industrial states at mid-century, were among the states to lose population between 1980 and 1990, according to the preliminary figures. Illinois decreased from 11.4 to 11.3 million, and Chicago lost as many as 275,000 people, about 9 percent of its population, as it dropped below 3 million.
Over all, the population totals of Southeastern region - Alabama, Louisiana, Mississippi and Tennessee, as well as Arkansas - came in well blow expectations.
The preliminary figures, which have been released in stages over the past two weeks, will increase before the final figures are delivered to President Bush at the year's end, according to John Connolly, an assistant to the Census Bureau Director, Barbara Bryant.
 
Sent Out for Review
The current figures, being sent to 39,000 local jurisdictions for review and possible correction, fail to reflect late counts of people in some hard-to-count areas, the continuing counts of up to one million parolees and probationers and the allocation of about 1.2 million dependents of overseas military personnel in communities around the country, according to another Census Bureau official.
Local jurisdictions have 15 working days to review the numbers and provide evidence showing areas that were missed by enumerators. After the local review, which is being done on a far greater scale since its inception a decade ago, revised local figures will be combined with figures obtained in other efforts to find people missed in the initial count.
Texas, which picked up at least 2.6 million people, still grew less than expected. But Houston, with at least 1.6 million people, climbed from fifth to fourth in the rankings of the largest cities. Dallas, with 991,000 people, retained seventh place.
 
Pennsylvania Loses Out
Pennsylvania, by contrast, was one of the states to lose population, according to the preliminary figures. The state, which dropped from 11.8 million to 11.7 million people, seemed to suffer the convergence of many of the negative trends - getting little benefit from immigration, new industry or the growth of retirement communities, and falling victim to the continued decline of manufacturing and the renewed decline of rural areas.
The rural decline over the decade reversed the trend of the 1970's, when a back-to-the-land movement fueled a modest increase in rural population around the country, according to Dr. Frey of the University of Michigan and Calvin Beale, a demographer with the Agriculture Department.
Census figures released today showed great-than-expected losses all across rural America. The total loss for rural areas, Mr. Beale estimated, could be as high as 1.4 million in the 1980's. Rural losses were apparently a major factor in tipping at least four states, Iowa, North Dakota, Wyoming and Louisiana, below their 1980 population totals.
''The prolonged agricultural crisis and the general economic recession that affected the nation as a whole, particularly in 1982 and 1983, was much worse in the rural areas and was more prolonged in the rural areas,'' said Mr. Beale.
 
Even Weather Is Factor
The economic factors varied, he said. They include the downturn in mining in Western Colorado, Wyoming and the southern Appalachian regions; the loss of population in some heavily black counties in the rural South, and the months of searing heat and drought that drove people from the dry grain fields of Iowa and the Dakotas. All but six counties in Iowa lost population, according to preliminary figures.
However, he said, the magnitude of the losses in hard-hit areas of the South and Middle West will be masked to some degree in overall figures by pockets of rural boomlets, almost all associated either with population spilling out from suburbs as young people seek affordable housing, or with the growth of retirement communities.
Examples of the continuing growth of the counties near the outer boundaries of metropolitan areas could be found all over the country, most dramatically in Riverside and San Bernardino Counties east of Los Angeles, but also in Dutchess County, north of Westchester, or Worcester County, 40 miles west of Boston, or Snohomish County, north of Seattle.
 
Politics of Population
Because the membership in the House of Representatives is fixed at 435, it is possible for a state to have a population gain - as New York did, albeit a slight one - and still lose seats in the House as faster-growing states get new ones in accordance with their greater share of the population.
In the complicated formula by which the 50 states compete, however impersonally, for their shares of the House seats, Mississippi and Kentucky are currently in contention for the last Congressional seat among the 435 to be allocated. At least that is the opinion of Kimball Brace, president of Election Data Services, a consulting firm which works for many state Democratic organizations, and Thomas Hofeller, redistricting of the Republican National Congressional Committee.
Both men agree on the estimates of which states will be winners and losers in the allocation of Congressional seats. Aside from California, the gainers will be Florida, gaining four seats for a total of 23, and Texas, whose delegation will increase by three for a total of 30. Other likely gainers of one seat each are Virginia, North Carolina, Georgia, Arizona and Washington.
Losers, aside from New York, include Pennsylvania, Illinois, Ohio and Michigan, each likely to lose two seats, and Louisiana, Iowa, Kansas, Kentucky, Montana, New Jersey and West Virginia, each likely to lose one seat.
But the remaining three million or more people who have not yet been factored into the count could skew slightly those predictions, Mr. Brace and Mr. Hofeller cautioned.

An article and a map yesterday about the 1990 census referred incorrectly in some copies to the percentage increase in Texas' population.

The chart was also credited incompletely. Election Data Services was a source for changes in Congressional representation.

HEADLINE: Sununu Tutors the Kremlin's Staff

BYLINE: By FRANCIS X. CLINES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MOSCOW, Aug. 29

 BODY:
The Kremlin moved ever closer in spirit to the White House today as President Bush's chief of staff worked within on the Soviet ''paper flow,'' and a tent city of disgruntled citizens maintained its encampment in a park just across the way.
The tent-city residents, remarkably akin to the year-round camp of aggrieved Americans who picket opposite the White House in Lafayette Park, kept their own flow of petition paper moving on the edge of Red Square.
They are in their second month of freely offering assorted complaints around the clock about a Soviet bureaucracy that not long ago swallowed dissidents whole.
''Sure this is progress - we're no longer afraid to complain,'' said Valentina Dzhaitova, one of the tent dwellers hoping to get President Gorbachev's attention. ''But so far it's useless.'' 
 
Kremlin's Paper Flow
The mission of John H. Sununu, the White House chief of staff and expert on getting a President's attention, is being kept under wraps. Soviet and American officials explain that the visitor from the West Wing is concerned with discussing not the substantive issues but only the process by which they reach the desk where the ruble stops.
Mr. Sununu stayed on the closed side of the Kremlin walls, offering no public comment and only private advice, embassy officials said, on how to govern the Government.
Whether a White House-style paper flow might bolster the Kremlin's ability to face a raft of pending critical problems is debatable, at least, in a nation that daily suffers fresh touches of eeriness. This week, for example, features not only increasing Armenian guerrilla violence and another attempt at creating a national economic recovery plan, but also such lower-priority problems as anthrax in Khirgizian sausage and quicksand in the Moscow subway.
A vivid summary of those problems, from the plight of refugees of interethnic violence to the poverty and homelessness of economic failure, is at hand among the 200 residents of the tent city. They vow to stay permanently as they complete their second month amid the flower bed and lawn of the Rossiya hotel just opposite the Kremlin.
 
'Capital of Absurdistan'
The visible routine of their lives, from the old lady who walks her dog outside her plastic tent to the family glimpsed getting dressed in their cramped enclosure, only heightens the human wonder suggested by the striped domes and swirls of nearby St. Basil's Cathedral.
''This is the capital of Absurdistan,'' an approaching Muscovite said as he took the tent city in stride.
Another passer-by said: ''Do not stop here. Go all the way through the Kremlin gates.'' He felt the Soviet bureaucracy must be scolded full face, not briefly heckled as the Government limousines race past the 20 tents lining what residents have dubbed the Street of the Victims of Arbitrary Government Rule.
The tenters' individual complaints range from jobs and apartments lost to the machinations of vindictive bureaucrats to accusations of coverups of neighborhood slayings.
 
Uprooted Baku Family
Soviet passers-by, connoisseurs of the difference between a crazed street loner and one of the nation's many despairing victims of old totalitarianism, are quietly curious and often sympathetic, dropping coins and paper rubles into the begging cups outside the tents.
''I understand them well,'' said a Moscow visitor, Stanislav Bagdasarov, who stood watching with his 8-year-old son, Arsen, taking a break after three fruitless days of visiting Government bureaucracies with his own problem. ''There are many just demands here.'' His family was uprooted a year ago from home, jobs and possessions in the anti-Armenian pogrom in Baku. They fled to kin in Armenia and now live in a tent in Yerevan as Mr. Bagdasarov, one of the nation's 600,000 ethnic refugees, petitions the Government for a job and minimal shelter.
''Winter is coming,'' he said, staring hard at the tent dwellers. ''Soon it will be cold out here. I could come to this before I'm done, holding out my own begging cup.''
 
Candor as a Public Plecebo
A banner announcing the tent city, which has taken root with the encouragement of the new insurgent city government, demands investigations and trials of abusive Government bureaucrats.
''Before we would have been buried alive by the Government for doing this,'' said Dmitri Dukov, a 65-year-old former sailor who said he suffered decades of official abuse, from job loss to unjust psychiatric incarceration, after publicly complaining that the seaman's union demands bribes as a condition of membership.
''Now the Government lets us stay here and complain, but it does not do anything about the problems,'' he said.

SECTION: Section A; Page 14, Column 4; Foreign Desk

LENGTH: 1023 words

HEADLINE: CONFRONTATION IN THE GULF;
Peace Dividend: Casualty in the Gulf?

BYLINE: By RICHARD L. BERKE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 29

 BODY:
The Persian Gulf crisis is giving members of Congress second thoughts about making major cuts in military spending for the next fiscal year and has muted but not silenced the hopeful talk of a peace dividend from the end of the cold war.
The crisis has also handed lawmakers a real-life test case, leading some to rethink the nation's military spending priorities, particularly whether more money should be spent on hardware needed for contingencies like the American military buildup in the Persian Gulf and less money on preparing for a European war or a nuclear conflict with the Soviet Union.
The Defense Department has traditionally been slow to embrace major changes in spending priorities because the military services are wed to weapons programs already in development or production.
Last year, for example, Congress approved $600 million for buying the kind of high-speed transport ships that have been in short supply in the sealift of troops and equipment to the Persian Gulf. 
 
Cheney Proved Agile
But Dick Cheney, the Secretary of Defense, was able to maneuver around Congressional dictates - as Cabinet secretaries sometimes do - and used more than a third of the money for new M-1 tanks and other purchases. The Pentagon has still not spent the remaining $375 million.
''Cutting the defense budget by the big numbers is going to be difficult under the best of circumstances,'' said Robert D. Reischauer, director of the Congressional Budget Office. ''But it is certainly much harder when you have troops on alert in the field and a clear threat out there.''
The chairmen of the House and Senate Budget Committees have proposed cuts of $12 billion to $15 billion from the Bush Adminstration's proposed military budget of slightly more than $300 billion for the fiscal year that begins Oct. 1. The request represents an increase over current military spending that is slightly less than the inflation rate.
But lawmakers and Congressional aides said that in private conversations now, there appeared to be less support among key legislators for these proposals for cuts. Support is growing, they said, for a more modest plan by Senator Sam Nunn, chairman of the Armed Services Committee, for cuts of less than $10 billion.
 
Support for Nunn Seen
Senator Pete V. Domenici of New Mexico, the ranking Republican on the Budget Committee, said he expected the Nunn proposal to have ''far more support - I think there will be far broader support for less cuts.''
Specifically, Mr. Domenici and other lawmakers said there will be new momentum for protecting some programs that had been the targets of budget cutters, like the B-2 stealth bomber, which could be used against ground targets in Iraq, and the V-22 Osprey, a tilt-rotor aircraft that can swiftly carry troops to a battle area.
Advocates of major military cuts expressed concern that their opponents would use the Persian Gulf conflict as an excuse to keep appropriating money for the Pentagon.
''We're not going to nuke 'em,'' Representative Marty Russo, an Illinois Democrat and member of the Budget Committee, said of the Iraqis. ''We ought to spend more money on rapid deployment, conventional warfare. ''You can do all that and still cut the budget. The modern threat is not all-out nuclear war.''
Representative Bill Frenzel of Minnesota, the ranking Republican on the Budget Committee, added: ''I would admit that those people who never wanted to see our military build down at a rapid rate now have a much better excuse to defend their position.''
Before Congress can resolve the more complicated debate over weapons systems, lawmakers returning from their monthlong recess must decide how to come up with money for the Persian Gulf military operation, which the Pentagon says will cost $2.5 billion through September, twice as much as initially estimated.
President Bush has not decided whether to seek additional money from Congress to pay for the increase or to find the money in existing programs, Pentagon officials said. But Congressional aides, noting that there will be a full legislative agenda next month, predicted that there would be little enthusiasm for a supplemental spending bill.
Yet budget negotiators from Congress and the Bush Administration face an even more daunting challenge: how to comply with the legally required spending ceilings for fiscal 1991 when the growing military costs in the gulf come on top of the rising cost of the savings and loan bailout.
Some lawmakers and analysts say they fear that the negotiators will use these spending pressures and the fear of a recession as an excuse to revise the Gramm-Rudman Act in order to accommodate bigger deficits.
''This whole thing may get them off the hook,'' Respresentative Les Aspin, chairman of the Armed Services Committee, said of the negotiators. ''They may say, 'O.K., the recession is imminent, what we need to do now is not have a $50 billion deficit-reduction package, but something smaller.' ''
 
With Combat, Less Chance Yet
With the deficit and the savings and loan bailout, some analysts and lawmakers say prospects have dimmed for a significant peace dividend in which military spending could be shifted to domestic programs, and would diminish even more so if a shooting war breaks out.
''The peace dividend?'' Senator Frank R. Lautenberg, Democrat of New Jersey, asked Tuesday after joining more more than 170 lawmakers at a meeting with President Bush. ''It looks like it's evaporating in front of our eyes.''
Others argue that there will still be a peace dividend. Representative David E. Bonior, the deputy majority whip, argued, for instance, that he saw no need for 300,000 American troops in Europe. ''I still think we can make serious reductions,'' he said.
But Mr. Cheney said at a recent veterans' convention here that it was a ''faulty conclusion'' that the country could realize a peace dividend for an array of domestic programs. Citing the Mideast conflict, he said, ''The best way to maintain the peace is to be prepared for war, and the 'peace dividend' is peace and it's worth every penny we paid for it.''

HEADLINE: Cleanup Begins At Tornado Site Where 25 Died

BYLINE: By WILLIAM E. SCHMIDT, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PLAINFIELD, Ill., Aug. 29

 BODY:
When the black wind stopped howling and the terrible roar faded, the first thing Bernice Lea noticed, as she huddled inside her bedroom, was the rain, cold and hard on her back. She looked up and saw that the roof and most of the walls of her house were gone.
Even worse, so was her husband's car, with her husband in it.
''Dave had just pulled in the driveway when the tornado hit,'' said Mrs. Lea, standing today amid the flattened debris of her house, one of more than a dozen ranch-style houses on Pratt Lane demolished by the tornado that scoured a track through the neighborhood on Tuesday. ''It picked up his car and tossed it into the bean field at the end of the block. He's hurt, but, thank God, he's alive.'' 
At least 25 people were killed and more than 350 were injured when the most lethal tornado to strike Illinois in two decades dropped suddenly from the heavy August sky. It shattered more than 50 homes and 500 apartments, and crumpled automobiles like tinfoil as it churned an eight-mile course through the cornfields and subdivisions of Will County, 35 miles southwest of Chicago.
Despite its ferocity, the tornado struck before the National Weather Service issued either a watch or a warning of a tornado hazard.
Late this afternoon, President Bush declared Will County a disaster area, qualifying residents and businesses to receive low-interest loans and economic assistance.
Some victims were killed when they were quite literally sucked into the sky and their bodies tossed into a cornfield adjoining an apartment complex in the nearby town of Crest Hill. At least nine residents of the apartment complex were killed, local officials said.
With six other residents of the Crest Hill Lake Apartments still missing today, police divers dragged a nearby lagoon, and a National Guardsmen searched nearby farm fields for bodies.
 
No Warning
Although Will County and much of Chicago had been under a severe storm warning, with predictions of hail and dangerous lightning, the National Weather Service did not issue tornado watches or warnings until after the storm had already touched down and caused severe damage.
Fred Ostby, director the National Severe Storm Forecast Center in Kansas City, Mo., said one reason the tornado was not detected was because current weather radar at Chicago and most other American cities is outdated. A Doppler radar system, which will be installed in Chicago in the next two years, is much better at detecting tornadoes, although he could not say for certain whether it would have picked out the tornado on Tuesday.
Paul Dailey, a meterologist in charge of the Chicago Forecast Office of the National Weather Service, says Chicago does have a older, experimental Doppler radar, but it was not working on Tuesday.
 
A Wrong Conclusion
Lacking radar data and reports of funnel clouds from spotters on the ground, Mr. Ostby said forecasters did not believe that the storm cells crossing Illinois on Tuesday, at the trailing edge of a cold front, were potent enough to produce tornadoes.
Gov. James R. Thompson toured the damaged towns this morning, and declared Will County a state disaster area. He dispatched more than 150 National Guard troops to the county to assist in clean-up operations and to patrol against looters.
Much of the area remained without telephone or electrical service late this afternoon. With skies today cloudless and temperatures in the mid-80's, volunteers, clean-up crews and residents moved through shattered neighborhoods, looking to pick up the pieces.
In the background there was the whine of chain saws cutting through fallen trees and the rumble of bulldozers pushing crumpled automobiles and debris off roads. On some highways, traffic backed up in mile-long snarls, as sightseers out to ogle the storm damage became entangled with utility trucks and convoys of National Guard trucks transporting bulldozers.
The storm churned a swath nearly 700 feet wide across miles of countryside, flattening anything in its path. At the Crest Hill Lake Aparments, the storm sheared the walls off some buildings, leaving the interiors of the apartments standing exposed, like an open-air stage set.
Cornfields ripe for the harvest looked like a giant had trod across them, the green stalks mashed into the earth. Houses exploded, littering nearby streets with splinters of lumber, glass and roofing. Trees that were not shattered by the wind had been stripped of their leaves, and were entwined instead with crumpled sheets of metal and shreds of clothing.
On Pratt Lane, down from the Leas' house, Gene Fassiotto stood in what used to be his garage, and pointed toward what was once his living room. Atop the litter of broken bricks and broken lumber was his two-month old Oldsmobile, tossed there by the storm, wheels up, like a dead turtle.
 
'We Lost Everything'
''We lived in this house 25 years,'' said Mr. Fassiotto, his eyes glazed with tears. ''And in 25 seconds, we lost everything.'' Less than 500 yards from the Fassiottos's house was the shell of St. Mary Immaculate Church and school, where at least two people, including a nun, were killed.
Officials at the National Weather Service in Chicago say they still do not know for certain whether more than one tornado moved through the area.
Temperatures on Tuesday had built into the 90's, and the air was heavy and humid when storm clouds formed just after noon. The first hint of severe weather had come nearly two hours earlier, when there was a report that a tornado had touched down near Rockford, about 80 miles to the northwest.
Late today, Don Gould, a regional office of the Illinois Emergency Services and Disaster Agency, said he would investigate why the National Weather Service chose not to issue a tornado warning or watch as a result of the sighting in the Rockford area.
But Mr. Dailey, at the Chicago Forecast Office, said the sighting was not confirmed, and all other radar and forecast data gave no hint of tornadoes. ''I've done a lot of second and third guessing on myself, on the way home from work last night and today,'' said Mr. Dailey. But he said there just was not any evidence to suggest that a tornado was spawning.
 
Storms Pummel New York State
 
By The Associated Press
Powerful storms roared across upstate New York Tuesday night and early yesterday, uprooting trees, damaging buildings, flooding roads and knocking out electricity to thousands of people. Tornadoes were suspected of causing the damage in several areas. One of the worst storms struck the New York State Fairgrounds in the Syracuse suburb of Geddes at about 8 P.M. Tuesday. The storm swept up pieces of tents and stables.

GRAPHIC: Photo: A section of Plainfield, Ill., that was leveled on Tuesday by a tornado that touched down 35 miles southwest of Chicago. (Associated Press)

SECTION: Section A; Page 21, Column 1; National Desk

LENGTH: 625 words

HEADLINE: Judge Orders Tuna Import Ban Over Dolphin Kill

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, Aug. 29

 BODY:
A Federal district judge has ordered a ban on imports of tuna fish caught by five nations until they can show that they are not killing more dolphins than United States law allows.
The judge, Thelton E. Henderson, ruled on Tuesday that the Bush Administration was taking too long in determining whether foreign fleets were complying with the United States law.
The 1988 law required an embargo on any nation whose dolphin-killing rate was more than twice the rate of the American tuna fleet.
August Felando, president of the American Tunaboat Association, the largest group of American-flagged tuna boats, said the embargo could disrupt the supply of tuna in the United States. ''The U.S. fishermen cannot supply all the tuna that's required for the U.S. market,'' he said. 
The embargo affects the nations whose boats use nets to catch yellowfin tuna in the eastern Pacific - Mexico, Panama, Venezuela, Ecuador and Vanuatu, an island nation in the Southwest Pacific.
Brenda Killian, associate director of the Earth Island Institute in San Francisco, an environmental group that brought the lawsuit, said yellowfin tuna was included in the fish canned and sold under many national and supermarket brand names as ''chunk light.''
Ms. Killian said it accounted for 20 to 30 percent of the canned yellowfin sold in this country and 5 to 8 percent sold worldwide.
 
Tuna Prices Likely to Rise
She said the ban could cause canned tuna prices to rise a few pennies a can because canners would be forced to buy tuna caught farther away in the western Pacific by methods that did not endanger dolphins.
The law imposed the embargo at the end of 1989 unless the Commerce Department determined that a nation's dolphin kill rate was under the limit, Judge Henderson said. But before enforcing the embargo, the department wanted to evaluate figures from each nation for 1989, and department officials said an assessment had not been completed.
But Judge Henderson, who was appointed to the Federal bench by President Jimmy Carter in 1980, wrote that under the agency's plan, dolphins continued to be killed and American fishermen were bearing a competitive burden as a result of delays in providing figures that were the fault of the foreign governments.
''The continued slaughter and destruction of these innocent victims of the economics of fishing constitutes an irreparable injury to us all,'' the judge wrote. In April the United States' three largest sellers of canned tuna stopped buying tuna that is caught in nets that trap dolphins. The companies - the H. J. Heinz Company, which makes the Star-Kist brand; the Van Camp Seafood Company, which markets Chicken of the Sea tuna, and Bumble Bee Seafoods Inc. - account for nearly 70 percent of the canned tuna sold in the United States.
 
How Dolphins Are Killed
Dolphins are killed in tuna fishing because they swim with schools of yellowfin tuna. Fishermen trap and drown the air-breathing dolphins as they pull in their nets around the schools of tuna.
The United States cannot regulate foreign fishing practices in international waters, but it can limit imports.
A National Marine Fisheries Service official testified at a Congressional hearing last month that foreign dolphin kills last year rose by more than 40 percent to 84,000, while United States dolphin kills dropped to 12,643.
The Commerce Department argued that the law required only that it compile information from foreign governments, which was submitted by a July 31 deadline, and decide whether each country met the legal standard.
But the Earth Island Institute contended that the law required an embargo of any nation that had not shown it met the limit by the end of last year, and Judge Henderson agreed.

PUB-DATE: August 30, 1990

SECTION: Section A; Page 22, Column 1; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 638 words

HEADLINE: Loose Ends in Gulf Policy

 BODY:
Despite President Bush's nearly flawless handling of the Persian Gulf crisis, several key issues still dangle dangerously, unresolved or unclarified.
One is the central question of whether the allies are carrying their fair share of the military and economic burden. Though Bush Administration officials are trying hard to arrange wider participation, the enterprise seems to be losing momentum. Nothing would do more to alienate ordinary Americans from the entire endeavor than the discouraging sense that the allies are making little more than a token contribution. 
A second imperative is to sort out quickly who's in charge in Saudi Arabia and surrounding waters. Separate Arab and American commands have been established, almost certainly with separate policies for using force. Unless lines of authority and policy are quickly clarified and coordinated, the costs of confusion will be high if force has to be used.
Third, there remains the complex issue of negotiations with Iraq. All the talk about victory leaves the impression that there's no room for compromise. But it's important to comprehend, before misunderstandings solidify, that at the right time certain bargains will and must be struck.
A combination of fear, outrage and Mr. Bush's rapid diplomacy persuaded many nations to respond to Iraq's invasion of Kuwait with troops, ships, money and increased oil supplies. But the depth of that help has been disappointing.
The Saudis seem to be pulling their weight - as might be expected of the country most directly threatened by Baghdad. The British have been contributing, but they, along with France, could send more troops to Saudi Arabia. Egypt's dispatch of several thousand troops simply isn't enough. President Hosni Mubarak, as the beneficiary of billions in U.S. aid and as a leader of Arab moderates, owes much more in tanks and troops.
As for Germany and Japan, neither has begun to contribute at a level equal to its need for a secure oil supply. To be sure, Bonn is preoccupied with reunification. Yet Germany would be shortsighted to underestimate its debt to America's sacrifice. That's even more true for Tokyo. Yet Japan's leaders are hesitating. They seem to have decided against honoring even their modest idea of providing up to $2 billion in critical aid to Turkey, Jordan, Oman and Egypt, four of the countries hardest hit by the crisis.
Problems also seem to be developing on the military side. There are at least two centers of authority: an Arab-Islamic command headed by Riyadh, and a joint American-Saudi command, presumably including non-Arab countries.
To further complicate matters, the Saudis say Mr. Bush has promised to consult with King Fahd -whatever that means - before launching any action from Saudi territory. Nor is it clear whether or how these joint commands are supposed to consult with other countries that have contributed forces to the region. Such confusion could be fatal in an emergency.
Of all the question marks, the biggest involves the terms of a settlement with Saddam Hussein. Certain objectives defined by Mr. Bush and the United Nations - Iraqi withdrawal from Kuwait, for example - are clearly beyond compromise. But other objectives, like promoting regional stability by destroying Baghdad's chemical and nuclear facilities, for example, or creating an interim government for Kuwait, will require persuasive collective diplomacy in the event these ends are not achieved by military force.
It's important that Americans realize this and don't balk when it comes time to talk. U.S. officials have repeatedly, albeit quietly, made just that point to an unlistening public.
Any one of these issues, mishandled, could cause substantial harm to the world's cause in the gulf. Mr. Bush would serve the common cause to show he's on top of them.

HEADLINE: Transit Agency Seeking To Buy Grand Central

BYLINE: By DAVID W. DUNLAP

 BODY:
The Metropolitan Transportation Authority is trying to smooth the way for development of a huge office tower on Madison Avenue as a necessary first step in its efforts to buy Grand Central Terminal and begin a $400 million renovation of the mid-Manhattan landmark.
Until it owns the 77-year-old terminal, the authority will not proceed with most of the renovation that it announced last April because, like any renter, it is reluctant to pour money into a property that it does not own.
The M.T.A. leases the terminal for use by its subsidiary, the Metro-North Commuter Railroad, from the Penn Central Corporation of Cincinnati, the successor to the New York Central Railroad, which built the terminal and rail yard. Penn Central also owns Grand Central's unused development rights, which amount to anywhere from 1.6 million to 1.9 million square feet.
''The key initial obstacle to the Grand Central master plan is getting clear title to the building,'' said Frederick S. Harris, director of real estate for the authority. 
 
Development Rights Add To Cost
But the M.T.A. does not want to buy the terminal while so many unused development rights are still associated with the property. Those would add tens of millions of dollars to the purchase price. The authority also does not want to see those development rights used to build a skyscraper directly over the landmark terminal, as Penn Central has proposed in the past.
So the authority is now trying to find a way to make it easier for a partnership of the developer G. Ware Travelstead and First Boston Inc. to use some of the 1.5 million square feet of development rights they have contracted to buy from Penn Central.
This would mean trying to break an impasse over an office tower proposed by Mr. Travelstead, and First Boston at 383 Madison Avenue, between 46th and 47th Streets. If the partnership could use enough development rights at that site, which they own, they would pledge that the balance would not be used to construct anything over Grand Central Terminal.
As proposed in 1986, their 383 Madison Avenue tower was 72 stories tall and would have used up almost 800,000 square feet of development rights. But it was rejected last year by the City Planning Commission because of its extremely high density, among other reasons, and is now caught up in a lawsuit by the developers against the city.
''I think there were a lot of good intentions on all sides,'' Mr. Harris said when asked why the M.T.A. had become involved in this tangled affair. ''But I felt everybody was locked into a position where nothing could happen. Obviously, the first place to start was the Travelstead side, because they're in litigation.''
''The M.T.A. has the potential to play a role that people hadn't recognized,'' he said.
There is no way to predict what the eventual price tag on the terminal would be, and no way of saying what effect such a purchase would have on M.T.A. operations. Depending on how the value was calculated, and depending on whether the development rights were still part of the property, the cost might be anywhere from $10 million to $150 million or more.
If 383 Madison Avenue were built under the aegis of the M.T.A., as part of the overall Grand Central transportation network, authority officials think it would be exempt from zoning rules, enabling development to proceed at a greater density than would ordinarily be permitted.
Under this scenario, part of the base of 383 Madison Avenue would be used as the entrance ramp to a new subsurface north-south truck route leading from 47th Street to a 12-bay loading dock inside the terminal. The route would be created out of a 1,000-foot-long maintenance yard that is soon to be phased out.
It is unclear how big 383 Madison Avenue might be. Under existing zoning, the structure could automatically have about 650,000 square feet of space. Under the maximum density that city planners have ever permitted on a single zoning lot in mid-Manhattan - in which the floor space is 21.6 times greater than the lot size - the building could have 930,000 square feet.
 
Debt Service an Issue
Mr. Travelstead, whose offices are in Manhattan, and First Boston are seeking a structure with 1.45 million square feet of space - the 650,000 allowed automatically, plus 800,000 in air rights. Only in that way, they argue, could they afford to own the additional 700,000 square feet of air rights that would come in their deal with the Penn Central Corporation and for which there is no development site immediately available.
''We would like 800,000 square feet at 383 Madison,'' said Frederick W. Rovet, an associate of Mr. Travelstead and a former Penn Central executive. ''If we could build a building like that, we could carry the other 700,000 square feet. We would pay the debt service on those 700,000 square feet from the profits of 383.''
But Mr. Harris said the authority believed there might be some other point - above the effective density limit set by the city and below the density sought by the developers - ''where the economics would work.''
 
City's 'Concordance' Sought
Moreover, he noted that while the proposal might not be subject to zoning rules, the authority did seek at least the acquiescence of city officials.
''It was always our expectation that all of this was unlikely to happen without the concordance of the city,'' Mr. Harris said. ''We are not the arbiters of density. We could not possibly usurp the density-setting role.''
Con Howe, the executive director of the City Planning Department, noted that ''the commission's problem was not with the applicant.'' Its problem, he said, ''was with the scale and impact of the building.''
The Planning Department has proposed its own solution to the problem of Grand Central's unused development rights, which would involve the expansion of a zoning district in which those rights would be transferrable.
 
'Vastly Out of Scale'
Kent L. Barwick, president of the Municipal Art Society, which has long been a leading voice in the preservation of Grand Central Terminal, said that even a structure built at the existing density limit - 930,000 square feet - would be ''vastly out of scale and totally inappropriate.''
Mr. Barwick, however, applauded the M.T.A. for seeking to resolve the ownership question. ''Neither the state, the city nor the public is going to sit still for putting $400 million into a property that belongs to what is, in effect, a bankrupt railroad,'' he said.
''It seems to be a good time for the M.T.A. to be examining its options,'' Mr. Barwick said, ''but it would be foolish for them to hitch their wagon to a star that seems to be going nowhere'' - meaning the Travelstead-First Boston proposal.
 
No Hint of Price
Robert W. Olson, senior vice president of the Penn Central Corporation, said: ''At the appropriate time, we'd be willing to sit down and negotiate with M.T.A. over the potential sale of the terminal. Obviously, it would have to be at the right price.'' He declined to provide even a hint of what the right price might be.
The actual owner of Grand Central is the New York & Harlem Railroad Company, the vestige of an operating railroad that currently has no employees and conducts no business. New York & Harlem leases the terminal to Penn Central, of which it is a subsidiary.
A spokesman for Amtrak, R. Clifford Black, said there was no discernible pattern of station ownership nationwide. Some are owned by public authorities, like South Station in Boston; some by municipalities, like the Union Passenger Terminal in New Orleans; some by Amtrak, like Pennsylvania Station in Manhattan; some by freight railroads; some by corporations, and some by individuals.

LENGTH: 1305 words

HEADLINE: Try to Tell a Widow That Life Goes On

BYLINE: By CAROL LAWSON

 BODY:
HOME is the two top floors of a condominium apartment building in Manhattan, near Central Park, which were demolished and rebuilt over the past year as a French palace. It is a work in progress: a butler in a white jacket serves soft drinks in crystal goblets as workers on tall ladders adjust the wiring for a chandelier in the rotunda.
Rooms are filled with gilded French furniture, crystal sconces, heavy silken fabrics, towering Greek columns and hand-painted murals. It has all been planned ''so you shouldn't think you're in America,'' says its owner, Joan Rivers.
But the minute she begins talking about her life during the last three years, the far-fetched trappings of celebrity and fortune fade away. As she speaks about life on her own without her husband of 23 years, Edgar Rosenberg, Ms. Rivers sounds like widows everywhere who are groping for a way to go forward despite what nearly all agree is the most painful, wrenching and self-defeating experience they have ever faced. Even though she did not become a widow under typical circumstances - her husband committed suicide - Ms. Rivers's description of her passage through grief is not all that different from that of other women whose husbands have died. 
''You don't ever get over the loss of your husband: you survive it,'' said Ms. Rivers, who is Mrs. Rosenberg to her household staff. ''I was 52 years old and had to totally start a new existence.'' At first, she said, she turned to close friends and a psychologist for help. ''But nobody can really help you,'' she said, her brash speaking voice toned down to a soft, serious level. ''You have to help yourself. This is a solo trip.'' Few married women will escape widowhood. There are 11.5 million widows in the United States, and projections from census data suggest that nearly 80 percent of all married women can expect to be widowed, and that they will survive their husbands by about 16 years. Underlying those projections are two trends: women live longer than men, seven years on average, and men tend to marry women younger than themselves.
Ms. Rivers and other widows who have written new books on the subject all describe life as a black pit immediately after their husbands died. Philomene Gates writes in ''Suddenly Alone: A Woman's Guide to Widowhood'' (Harper & Row, $18.95) that she ''wanted to die too.'' She was married for 38 years to Samuel E. Gates, a lawyer who died of a heart attack in 1979.
Mrs. Gates, herself a lawyer in New York, goes on to write that she ''felt vulnerable, unattractive, unloved and hideously alone,'' and that ''the first few months of widowhood almost flattened me.''
''I felt my life was over,'' said Xenia Rose, whose husband the cellist Leonard Rose, died of leukemia in 1984; they had been married 20 years. ''There was no future.
''It was the worst thing that ever happened to me,'' added Mrs. Rose, a psychotherapist in New York. Her account of her experience, ''Widow's Journey: a Return to the Loving Self,'' will be published in October by Henry Holt ($19.95).
Ms. Rivers described herself as virtually starting over from scratch. Her husband was found dead from an overdose of prescription drugs in a Philadelphia hotel room on Aug. 14, 1987. His death came three months after Ms. Rivers was dismissed by the Fox Broadcasting Company from ''The Late Show,'' a ratings failure that was an attempt to topple Johnny Carson. Her husband had managed her business affairs and was the show's executive producer.
''I was totally unstrung, totally fragmented,'' Ms. Rivers said. ''I had no career. No husband. Nothing.''
''I didn't know how to turn on the outside lights or where the burglar alarm was,'' she said. ''Edgar did everything, so I could concentrate totally on my child and my career. I didn't even know the name of my bank.''
At the same time, she said, she was dealing with other issues typical of early widowhood: anger toward her husband, loss of self-esteem, friends' expectations of how she should and should not behave, rejection by married friends who felt uncomfortable socializing with a single woman, and worries about finances. She wanted to go back to work, but the combination of her talk-show failure and the circumstances of her husband's death made her unemployable.
''Nobody wants to see someone whose husband has killed himself do comedy four weeks later,'' she said.
Some widows say that initially they are so exhausted by grief that they can hardly get out of bed in the morning. ''Putting on my makeup, deciding what to wear, thinking of what I had to do - it all took as much energy as climbing Mount Everest,'' Mrs. Gates writes.
Ms. Rivers had a very different reaction. Unable to work, she funneled her energy into frenetic travel.
''I went all over,'' she said. ''To Dallas for a party, the South of France for a weekend. It was better to be in the air for 14 hours than sitting at home.''
Like many women, she has never liked being called a widow. ''It is a terrible word,'' Ms. Rivers said. ''The connotation is a lady in a long black dress who has retired from the world. And then, when you start to go out, and you laugh in a restaurant, people look at you as the Merry Widow. That's even worse.
''Part of the healing process is that you can laugh at something, even the next day, and then you start to cry again. There are terrible highs and terrible lows.''
For every widow, recovery follows an individual path. ''There are no rules for getting through this,'' said Mrs. Rose, who has remarried.
''I expected to feel 100 percent better a year after Leonard died. I felt worse.''
Mrs. Rose, who encourages widows to join support groups, said many women feel so much pressure to recover by the end of a year that ''they think they are weird if they don't.''
Laurie Graham, the author of ''Rebuilding the House: One Woman's Passage Through Grief'' (Viking, $17.95), said it was ''at least a year before I felt tentatively hopeful, but I still had terrible episodes of sadness.'' Ms. Graham, a former editor at Scribner's, was married for eight years to George Shieffelin, a retired chairman of Scribner's, who died of a heart attack in 1988.
''I was not ready to meet new people until two years after George died,'' she said.
Ms. Rivers, who has resurrected an ''ice cold'' career and is writing a book about her life, said she was ''very proud'' of what she had done on her own. ''My life now is very busy, very full and terribly lonely,'' she said.
She came close to remarrying about a year after her husband died. ''I was so desperate, so left out,'' she said. Now she sees someone on a ''semi-regular'' basis, she said, but has no plans to marry.
Mrs. Gates has rebuilt what she called ''a co-ed life.'' Although she said she still thinks of her husband ''40 times a day,'' she has worked hard to establish friendships with single men whom she can call to accompany her to parties and dances.
''I like men,'' she said, ''and I have made the most enormous effort not to have a circle of friends who are all widows.''
Ms. Rivers said she was now in ''the final phase of widowhood: you have adjusted, you are able to go on but you do not forget your husband.''
''I miss the person you can say everything to,'' she said, her eyes welling tears. ''I miss having a history with somebody. I miss getting into a car after a party and saying, 'Could you believe that?' I miss the friend, the buddy who is really in your corner. There is no one to say: 'I'm going downstairs. Do you want a sandwich?' Those are the things of widowhood that are just killers, even now.''
She took a deep breath. ''He's not going to come back, so you have to get your life going again. You must get to it, and don't wallow. A life can be made. It can even be terrific. But it's never going to be the same.''

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:
The Oil Jolt
After weeks of deliberations, OPEC authorized its key producers to begin immediately pumping all the oil they can, averting a feared world shortage that has sent prices surging on world markets. The decision is expected to add some four million barrels a day to current world supplies, making up for nearly all the exports lost in the Iraq embargo. [Page A1.] 
Oil prices fell sharply as traders responded to the OPEC decision to lift output as well as to reduced fears of war. The contract for October delivery fell $1.96, to $25.92 a barrel. [D1.]
Venezuela, a ''safe source'' of supply, is expanding its oil industry in hopes of playing a larger role as an exporter, especially to the United States. [D1.]
The drilling of test wells in the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge should be considered, Interior Secretary Lujan said. [B10.]
 
 
 
The Economy
Frightened Americans might finally be pushing the U.S. into recession, after months of weak economic growth. Two leading indexes of consumer confidence, which for many economists is the most sensitive measure of the nation's vulnerability to recession, plunged sharply in August. [D1.]
Resolution Trust will name an overseer to ferret out corruption among Federal officials and private firms managing the assets of failed savings institutions. [D2.]
Mired in a slow back-to-school season, America's retailers have expressed deep concern that the Middle East crisis will hurt their business even further in the weeks heading into the all-important Christmas season. [D4.]
The index of leading indicators was unchanged in July from June, the Commerce Department said. [D4.]
New-home sales fell 2.3 percent in July, the fifth drop in seven months, the Government said. [D4.]
 
 
 
Companies
Donald J. Trump is about to enter Round 2 of his financial crisis, and the sale of the Plaza Hotel is likely. [D1.]
A patent said to cover the microprocessor has been issued to a virtually unknown inventor, shocking the industry. [D1.]
Fujitsu introduced two powerful supercomputers and an operating system that can run all the company's computers. [D3.]
The M.T.A. is setting out to buy Grand Central Terminal from its owner, the Penn Central Corporation, in a complex deal. [B1.]
 
International
The Bank of Japan raised its discount rate for the fifth time in a little more than a year, to 6 percent from 5.25 percent. [D1.]
 
 
 
Markets
The stock market continued to gain, posting its fourth advance in a row. The Dow rose 17.58, to 2,632.43. [D8.]
Interest rates fell and the price of Treasury securities rose, with the 30-year bond jumping more than a point. [D7.]
The dollar moved mostly higher in the U.S. after a mixed performance overseas. [D14.]
Precious metals futures halted their plunge, largely disregarding a sharp drop in energy futures prices. [D14.]

HEADLINE: Japan Lifts Discount Rate to 6%

BYLINE: By JAMES STERNGOLD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, Thursday, Aug. 30

 BODY:
Despite denials just a day earlier, the Bank of Japan said this morning that it was raising its discount rate for the fifth time in a little more than a year. The new rate is 6 percent, from 5.25 percent, effective today.
The news was a clear signal that the Japanese Government remained concerned about inflationary pressures building in the solidly expanding economy.
The pressures were growing even before Iraq invaded Kuwait on Aug. 2, but the subsequent rise in oil prices heightened worries among policy makers in Japan that they would have to act to squelch rising prices. Japan imports nearly all its energy supply and is thus particularly vulnerable to oil price rises. 
 
Higher Prime Rate
The official announcement came after increases in other important market-related rates. Last week, banks announced that they were raising their long-term prime rate to 8.5 percent, from 7.9 percent, a steep increase that will have a more direct effect on borrowers, ranging from corporations to home buyers, than the increase in the discount rate, which affects certain short-term loans to banks.
The discount rate, which also acts as a benchmark for other interest rates, has been increased five times and has more than doubled since May 1989, when it stood at 2.5 percent. It was last increased on March 20, to 5.25 percent.
The increases in various Japanese rates could have some impact over time on the American financial markets. With the United States discount rate at 7 percent, the gap between the countries has narrowed.
Richard Koo, an economist at the Nomura Research Institute here, said Japanese bonds would now be more attractive to Japanese investors, and would thus reduce the flow of capital to the American bond market.
Although it is unclear just how much of an effect that could have, it might put some slight upward pressure on American interest rates, he said.
 
Unexpected Timing
There had been hints that the announcement was coming, but the timing was unexpected. The central bank issued its statement early on Thursday in Tokyo, before the markets here opened. The central bank usually makes its announcements in late afternoon after the markets close.
As is often the case in Japan, the news had been leaked to Japanese newspapers, two of which reported on Wednesday that the rate rise was imminent. The central bank governor, Yasushi Mieno, denied the reports on Wednesday afternoon, but few believed him.
Since the move had been anticipated, the announcement had little effect on financial markets here. The Nikkei index of 225 issues rose initially, then began to slide, ending the morning session up just 34.68 points at 24,929.47. It had fallen sharply on Wednesday amid the reports that a rate rise was imminent. The dollar also firmed slightly early today.
 
Classic Signs Abound
Mr. Koo, the economist, and several others also said the increases were likely to slow the Japanese economy, which has been showing classic signs of overheating in recent months. Consumer prices are expected to rise less than 3 percent this year, but the labor market is tight, factories are running at capacity and overall growth is still expected at about 5 percent.
''The Bank of Japan did a good job moving so fast because if they had delayed, the situation would have deteriorated fast,'' said Susumu Kato, the chief economist in Tokyo for Goldman, Sachs & Company.
Mr. Kato added that the jump in the prime rate meant that real interest rates, the cost of borrowings after inflation is discounted, were now about 6 percent, much higher than the expected growth rate for the economy. The result, he said, is that growth may slow in the coming months. Implications for U.S. The rate increase may prove particularly important for policy makers in the United States, who are struggling to keep the American economy out of a recession. Some economists have said they fear that higher Japanese rates may lift the value of the yen against the dollar.
Should the American currency fall too fast, they have contended, the Federal Reserve may be forced to raise American interest rates, or at least not permit them to decline, to defend the dollar. The worry is that that could help push the economy towards a recession.
But several days may be needed to determine the overall reaction. The dollar actually strengthened slightly, to 143.45 yen from 143.20 yen, before the announcement this morning. Traders said that was because the move was expected.
''For the time being, it's just a big yawn,'' a foreign exchange trader said. ''Wait a while and see what the U.S. does.''

HEADLINE: Venezuela's New Oil Prominence

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LAGUNILLAS, Venezuela

 BODY:
The lines of oil wells that stretch across Lake Maracaibo here are pumping higher volumes of crude to help offset the shortage caused by the embargo of oil from Iraq and Kuwait.
By the end of September, an extra 350,000 barrels a day should be flowing from Venezuelan oilfields, and by the end of December nearly 500,000 more barrels a day. Two-thirds of that oil is likely to be sold to the United States, Venezuela's largest customer. Last year, Venezuela sold a million barrels a day to the United States, 18 percent of American oil imports and about half of the country's total production. 
With war preparations gripping the Persian Gulf, it is no surprise that American eyes are suddenly turning to Venezuela, which likes to style itself as a ''safe source'' nation, unlike those in the troubled Middle East. And Venezuela holds the world's largest proven oil reserves outside the Middle East - 59 billion barrels.
''When you buy the cheap oil of the Middle East, you're not calculating the cost of defending the Middle East,'' said Antonio Cassella, planning manager for Petroleos de Venezuela, the state oil company, known as PDVSA.
But beyond the short-term production increases, Venezuela, one of Latin America's oldest and most stable democracies, is poised to play a newly prominent role in the United States energy scene well into the 1990's.
Moves that Venezuela is already making in that direction include a $24 billion six-year capital investment plan by PDVSA that was started months before the current Middle East troubles, and new policies to encourage foreign investment. There is also the geographical proximity to the United States.
Most of the oil will come from Maracaibo, an oil-soaked region in western Venezuela where decades of production have caused the ground to sink by as much as 15 feet.
An oil tanker leaving Maracaibo takes just four days to reach the East Coast of the United States. That compares with the 30 days it takes a tanker to reach the United States from Saudi Arabia, Venezuela's traditional competitor as America's top foreign supplier. That means not only lower transportation costs, but more flexibility for changing orders on short notice. 4th-Largest Oil Company The story of Venezuela's energy exports in the 1990's will largely center on PDVSA, the fourth-largest oil company in the world, according to Petroleum Intelligence Weekly, which bases its rankings on ownership of reserves, production and refining capacity, among other factors. The company ranks behind only Aramco of Saudi Arabia, Royal/Dutch Shell of the Netherlands, and Exxon.
In contrast to widespread negative stereotypes of state companies, the Venezuelan company is profitable and is considered to be professionally managed. But it has found itself hemmed in by production quotas.
Not known as a successful negotiator within the Arab-dominated Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries, Venezuela has seen its quota share of OPEC production drop almost in half over 20 years, from 16 percent in 1970 to 8.6 percent today. But Venezuela was a prime engineer behind OPEC's decision to authorize its members to start pumping all they could to make up for the embargo against Iraq and Kuwait.
 
New Technologies a Concern
Noting that many Arab oil producers cheat on their quotas, oil company executives here had been bitter about Venezuela's regular OPEC quota of 1.945 million barrels a day.
''The question of how long our oil will last is irrelevent,'' said Alberto Quiros Corradi, a former president of one of PDVSA's 11 subsidiaries. ''The question is: Will new technologies replace oil before we run out of oil?''
The company has invested aggressively in extending control over production, refining and marketing. It ranked as the second most profitable company in the world in 1989, after a Brazilian state mining company, according to Fortune magazine's survey of the world's 500 largest corporations. Using the same formula, profits as a percent of assets, the survey ranked the company as the most profitable oil concern in the world.
The company startled Americans by paying $700 million last November to complete its purchase of Citgo Petroleum. Through Citgo, Venezuelan oil has priority access to 8,500 affiliated gasoline stations.
 
More Profitable Than Pemex
When compared with Latin America's other oil-exporting giant, Petroleos Mexicanos, the contrast is striking.
Both companies had roughly similar sales volumes in 1989 - $15.2 billion for Pemex, and $13.7 billion for PDVSA. But Pemex had more than triple the labor force - 165,000 workers compared with 47,000 for PDVSA. The Venezuelan company was 8.5 times as profitable as Pemex in 1989: $2.7 billion, compared with $320 million for the Mexican company.
''For a state-run company, PDVSA is admirably run,'' said an American diplomat in Caracas. ''There is very little smell of corruption, and they think in terms of businessmen.''
Much of the profit comes from the extraordinary energy wealth of Venezuela, which is larger than Texas and Oklahoma combined.
The cost of oil exploration averages 10 cents per discovered barrel - literally pennies when compared with the $5-a-barrel average in the United States. Oil production costs here average $2.50 a barrel - less than 10 percent of the current international sales price.
Last year, the company accounted for 75 percent of Venezuela's $12 billion export earnings and for 17 percent of the nation's $79 billion gross domestic product.
In 1975, when PDVSA was formed from multinational companies, the company refined 40 percent of its product. Now it refines 80 percent.
 
One Soft Spot
The company's only major soft spot is domestic sales. Those operations had a $286 million loss last year. High-octane gasoline is sold here for 26 cents a gallon, cheaper than bottled water. Street riots have greeted Government efforts to raise the price, which may be the world's lowest.
The company is in the midst of an ambitious expansion program. These are some of the goals for 1995:
* An increase in crude production potential by 30 percent, to 3.5 million barrels a day.
* An increase in domestic refining capacity by 25 percent, to 1.5 million barrels a day.
* A tripling of petrochemical production, to 10 million tons a year, and a doubling of natural gas production, to the equivalent of 200,000 barrels of oil a day.
Plans also call for building, with foreign participation, a $3 billion liquefied natural gas plant and increasing coal production sixfold, to 10 million tons a year.
 
Price Relief Is Sought
TEGUCIGALPA, Honduras, Aug. 29 (AP) - Five Central American countries have asked Mexico and Venezuela for relief from the increase in oil prices prompted by the Middle East crisis.
''The military invasion of Kuwait by Iraq, which deeply concerns our people, will affect the world petroleum market and will bring inevitable political and economic consequences for Central America,'' the President of Honduras, Rafael Leonardo Callejas, said yesterday.
Under a 10-year-old agreement, the five countries have been buying Venezuelan and Mexican oil for an average of $17 a barrel, paying 80 percent in cash. The other 20 percent is used as loans for development projects, or to finance imports from Venezuela and Mexico.
The proposal made today calls for payment to be made in 70 percent cash and 30 percent loans. The other four nations are El Salvador, Costa Rica, Nicaragua and Guatemala.

HEADLINE: Oil Drops Nearly $2 A Barrel

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER

 BODY:
Oil prices fell sharply yesterday as traders responded to the decision by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries to allow an immediate increase in oil production, as well as to reduced fears of war in the Persian Gulf.
The contract for October delivery of oil fell $1.96 a barrel, to $25.92, on the New York Mercantile Exchange. So far this week, the October price for oil has plunged $4.99 a barrel.
Gasoline and heating oil prices also retreated yesterday. 
The energy market has focused on diplomatic moves to ease Middle East tensions and on the OPEC decision, which is expected to add some four million barrels a day to current world oil supplies, making up for nearly all the oil exports lost as a result of the embargo imposed on Iraq and Iraqi-occupied Kuwait.
News of the OPEC decision, reached yesterday in Vienna, drove prices down by about $1 a barrel in early trading, said Bradford H. Hughes, an energy futures trader at Cargill Investor Services.
 
Offer by Hussein Reported
Prices fell another dollar on reports that Saddam Hussein, Iraq's president, was taking a more conciliatory stance on his country's occupation of Kuwait, Mr. Hughes said. The Iraqi leader was reported to have offered to allow all Western men to join Western women and children in leaving Kuwait and Iraq, if the United States would guarantee that it would not attack Iraq.
But yesterday's fall in oil prices is unlikely to cause an immediate reduction in gasoline prices, refiners said. Even at $25.92 a barrel, the Chevron Corporation is still paying 17.3 cents a gallon more for oil than its average payment in July, about double the 8 to 10 cents a gallon increase in the wholesale prices it charges dealers for gasoline, said James E. Huccaby, the manager of pricing for Chevron USA.
The Amoco Corporation said the prices it charged dealers for gasoline had risen 16 cents since July 1, while oil costs had climbed 22 cents.
In futures trading yesterday, the contract for September delivery of gasoline fell 3.81 cents a gallon, to close at 84.72 cents, while the contract for September delivery of heating oil tumbled 7.88 cents, to close at 71.64 cents a gallon.
Prices of oil for delivery next year fell less sharply yesterday than the October price of oil. This is an indication that the markets are becoming less anxious and less willing to pay a premium for oil available soon, said Philip K. Verleger Jr., an energy economist at the Institute for International Economics in Washington.
''It's the first small step back toward the situation at the end of July,'' he said. ''That'll last until the first gunshot is fired.''
Chevron and the Shell Oil Company, a unit of the Royal Dutch/Shell Group, announced yesterday morning that they were immediately cutting by $3, to $26.50, the posted price they would pay for West Texas Intermediate crude oil, the American benchmark grade.
Bonner & Moore, Houston, a consulting firm to the oil and petrochemical refining industries, said the announcement in Vienna underscored a consensus among the 10 OPEC members voting for it that they wanted to avoid a sustained surge above $30 a barrel, for fear that such prices could push the industrial nations - their largest customers - into recession.
 
'Possibility of a Powder Keg'
At the meeting, only Iran opposed the accord, as did Iraq and Libya, which did not attend the meeting. Iraq has said it would regard higher production by other OPEC members as an act of aggression.
''We still have the possibility of a powder keg as long as there is a heightened military presence in the Middle East,'' said Craig Whitley, a futures market analyst at Bonner & Moore. ''We are still holding to our belief for the long term that OPEC will make up most of the shortfall in production.''
Bonner & Moore forecasts that oil will cost $24 a barrel during the first half of 1991 and $22 for the second half.
Saudi Arabian officials said yesterday that they had already increased production to 7.5 million barrels a day from 5.5 million and would lift their output to 8 million barrels a day by September. Venezuela and the United Arab Emirates are also commited to raising production.
Other OPEC members are expected to raise output to keep up their oil revenues, including Iran and Libya, which opposed the accord.
