HEADLINE: Consumer Confidence In Worrisome Drop

BYLINE: By LOUIS UCHITELLE

 BODY:
Two major indexes of consumer confidence, which for many economists is the most sensitive measure of a nation's vulnerability to recession, plunged sharply in August, indicating that frightened Americans might finally be pushing the United States into a recession, after months of weak economic growth.
The indexes, from the University of Michigan and the Conference Board, measure the willingness of consumers to make purchases. Sharp plunges in consumer confidence, if they endure for several months, have set off recessions in the past because unexpected shocks, like the Iraq crisis, have prompted Americans to cut back suddenly on purchases, even though they had the income and the borrowing power to keep on buying.
 
A Possible Chain of Events
The August plunge in consumer confidence might herald such a blow. If the Middle East shock were to endure, then the sudden pullback in buying could cause inventories to pile up at stores, warehouses and factories. Orders for new goods would be canceled, thousands of workers would be laid off, production would fall as unemployment rose, and a recession would be born, perhaps before the end of the year. 
''In the coming month, we will know whether this sudden drop in confidence will endure or even worsen,'' said Richard T. Curtin, director of Consumer Surveys at the University of Michigan. ''If there is no rebound, then we must talk of recessionary cutbacks in consumer spending. The economy is certainly vulnerable.''
Alan Greenspan, chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, considers consumer confidence to be an important indicator of what is ahead for the economy. His concern might translate into lower interest rates if the indexes were to remain at their current low levels through September.
Similar lasting blows to consumer confidence played major roles in the 1974-75 and 1980 recessions. The Arab oil embargo, which started in October 1973, was soon followed by recession as fuel costs rose abruptly and Americans cut back on their spending.
President Jimmy Carter's credit restrictions, in early 1980, also helped to bring on a recession. Trying to reduce inflation, the President and the Fed restricted the use of credit cards, and millions of Americans responded by putting their cards away. A six-month recession developed, in which the gross national product contracted at an alarming annual rate of 9 percent.
The impact of the Iraq crisis on consumer buying is not yet certain. So far, only scattered sales data are available for August to gauge whether the drop in consumer confidence actually translated into a cutback in purchases. The initial data show that already weak auto and truck sales fell a notch in August and that sales of household goods have also declined from July levels. In addition, some department stores reported slow back-to-school sales of clothing and supplies.
The Michigan Survey for August, compiled yesterday for formal release later this week, shows that Michigan's consumer confidence index fell 11.8 points, from 88.2 in July, to 76.4, the lowest level since the 1982 recession. The index is based on survey questions that probe how 500 people across the country feel about job prospects, wages and the economy in general, both currently and for the next six months. During the first seven months of 1990, the index declined by only 4.8 points, from 93 last January.
A separate index in the Michigan survey that breaks out only the expectations for the next six months -an index that has foreshadowed recessions more accurately than the overall index - fell even more sharply, to 62.9 in August, from 77.3 in July. This index had also declined gradually in the first seven months of the year, from 83.4 in January.
 
Conference Board Report
The Conference Board, a business organization, reported similar results for its August survey of 5,000 people. The overall consumer confidence index dropped to 83.8 in August, from 101.7 in July, and the index measuring what people think will happen to salaries, jobs and the national economy over the next six months plunged to 73.4, from 91.8 in July. In each case, the decline over the first seven months of the year was gradual: a loss of less than 6 points.
''What happened in August may be an initial panic reaction and we may get a rebound in September,'' said Jason Bram, a Conference Board economist. ''If the indexes bounce back more than half way, then we can look back and say it was only an initial shock reaction.''
That could be the case. The Conference Board's survey questions were mailed to households on Aug. 1, the day before Iraq's invasion of Kuwait. Replies received toward the middle of the month were slightly more optimistic than earlier responses, Mr. Bram said. The Michigan survey, a telephone poll, was spread out over the entire month, and the replies toward the middle of the month were also more optimistic.
''But the improvement then halted,'' Mr. Curtin said. ''We have not seen any improvement for the past two weeks.''
 
Contrast With October 1987
For either survey, the August plunge was the sharpest for a single month since the stock market crash on Oct. 19, 1987. Confidence quickly rebounded in 1987, but the crash occurred during a period of strong economic growth. What's more, the Fed, concerned about damage to the nation's banks and brokerage houses, quickly lowered interest rates.
Almost the opposite is the case now. The Iraq crisis comes after 15 months of sluggish economic growth. And the Fed, apparently more concerned about inflation than recession, has resisted lowering interest rates, out of concern that inflation might be a greater risk than recession.
The surveys help to support this concern. Aside from the plunge in consumer confidence, both surveys showed that Americans had suddenly decided that inflation would rise because of higher oil prices. In both cases, consumer inflation forecasts of less than 4 percent a year were raised to 5.5 percent or more.

HEADLINE: Talking Deals;
'Paralyzed' Contel In GTE's Grasp

BYLINE: By Keith Bradsher

 BODY:
THE Contel Corporation, which earlier this month signed a definitive agreement to be purchased by the GTE Corporation, provides another example of a company forced into a deal at least in part because of debt it took on to make acquisitions.
The $5 billion deal with GTE, which is scheduled to close by the end of the year, will create the nation's largest provider of local phone service and the second-largest cellular phone company, with $25 billion in annual revenues and both good and bad implications for employees and consumers.
Although it ended up as a friendly deal, Contel initially sought to preserve its independence.
GTE first approached Contel with a merger proposal in April, the chairmen of both companies said in separate interviews. Russell E. Palmer, the chairman of the GTE board's strategic planning committee and the dean until July 1 of the Wharton School at the University of Pennsylvania, informally suggested the idea over breakfast at the Intercontinental Hotel in New York to a friend, I. W. Burnham 2d. Mr. Burnham, a founder and retired chairman of the now-bankrupt investment firm, Drexel Burnham Lambert, is a member of the Contel board's executive committee and a trustee of the University of Pennsylvania. 
James L. Johnson, the chairman and chief executive of GTE, said the decision to send Mr. Palmer to broach a possible merger was not made casually by GTE's board. ''Knowing he had a good relationship with Mr. Burnham, we all as a group figured it might be a good opportunity to explore it,'' he said.
Contel's senior management quickly rejected the idea, which did not include a specific offer, said Mr. Burnham and Charles Wohlstetter, Contel's chairman, in separate interviews. Mr. Wohlstetter said he opposed a merger then, and remains unhappy that the company he built from a single employee in 1961 is likely to lose its independence by the end of the year. ''We didn't want this,'' he said. ''We didn't invite it.'' What made the merger necessary, Mr. Wohlstetter said, was a steep fall in Contel's share price in early summer. The stock dropped to $27 a share, from $32, just as the company was preparing to issue $400 million worth of stock to redeem short-term debt incurred in a $1.3 billion acquisition of cellular telephone franchises last year from McCaw Communications Inc.
The $5 drop would have forced Contel to issue many more shares, reducing earnings per share, to raise the $400 million. Mr. Wohlstetter said that GTE then ''came back through the same director and said, 'Why do you want to sell stock at a price like that?' '' This second approach was also tempting to Contel's board because of GTE's willingness to issue 1.27 shares for each share of Contel stock, so that the annual dividend received by Contel shareholders would climb to $2.01 from $1.10, Mr. Burnham said. The McCaw deal had made it virtually impossible for Contel to raise its dividend for at least five years, he said.
Two other concerns were a possible downgrading of Contel's debt rating and the company's inability to finance any further large acquisitions for years to come. ''We're paralyzed,'' Mr. Wohlstetter said. ''There's nothing Contel can do alone because we can't extend ourselves. we're not an RJR Nabisco or ''junk bond'' company.'' He noted Contel is a regulated phone company with fairly cautious shareholders who expect regular dividends.
Contel's board accepted GTE's terms and agreed in principle on June 12 to a merger. Some Contel employees and Wall Street analysts have argued that Mr. Wohlstetter should have demanded a higher price.
The merger's effect on consumers will vary. The greater size and visibility of the combined company will probably make it more difficult to win rate increases for local phone service, said an investment banker involved in the deal. But the combination of their many cellular phone franchises might reduce competition in that industry, because a move by a large cellular phone chain to cut cellular service prices in one city can provoke the rival chain in the city to retaliate with lower prices where the two companies compete.
When two companies compete in many different markets, they are more likely to avoid cutting prices in any one market for fear that a price war will spread, economists say.
To eliminate redundant operations and shave costs after the merger, the combined company would have to cut its work force, Charles R. Lee, the president and chief operating officer of GTE, said at an analysts' meeting last week. GTE has already had some layoffs. ''There will be more pain, and there will be additional reductions in employees as a result of this merger,'' he added.

HEADLINE: Slow Back-to-School Sales Hurting U.S. Retailers

BYLINE: By ISADORE BARMASH

 BODY:
Mired in a slow back-to-school season, America's biggest retailers have expressed deep concern that the Middle East crisis will hurt their business even further in the weeks heading into the all-important Christmas season.
The industry has been in difficult straits all year, and new gloom has been added by soft sales of school supplies and clothing, although traditionally the bulk of these sales are not made until the Labor Day weekend.
Still, the slow start and declining consumer confidence stemming in part from the Persian Gulf dispute are strong new blows for heavily indebted retail chains like Macy's, Federated/Allied stores, which operates Bloomingdale's, and Ames Department Stores.
 
Poor Season Is Feared
These new factors could mean that the 32-day Christmas shopping period that begins after Thanksgiving Day could be even more crucial than usual for many retailers. For some it may be the difference between normal operations and insolvency, or even liquidation. 
In fact, many store executives and industry analysts are already predicting a poor holiday season, one that may be even worse than last year's. They said they expected to see a wave of price cutting as great as or even greater than that during the Christmas season last year. That would be good news for consumers looking for low prices, but would severely hurt retailers' profits.
While most Christmas goods have already been ordered, some stores are trying to cut back on the remainder until the situation in the Middle East has clarified.
 
Costs Increasing
If there is no change, most retail executives expect the sluggish consumer buying to worsen. They are being told by their suppliers that merchandise prices will rise because of higher oil prices and they say their operating costs, particularly for fuel, are already on the way up.
Perhaps more important, the Middle East crisis is having an adverse effect on the consumer's buying attitude, now emotionally charged by the callup of reservists, said retailers and consultants in interviews this week.
''The research data we are getting tell us that a large portion of the population will reduce its spending,'' said Bernard Brennan, chairman and chief executive of Montgomery Ward. ''On top of the Mideast crisis itself, the callup of reserves is creating a lot of emotion. It is adding to the concern many people feel.''
Walter F. Loeb, a New York retailing consultant, said he has surveyed major catalogue company business and found fall volume off at Sears, Roebuck, J. C. Penney and Spiegel Inc. ''They are running behind last year at those companies, and other cataloguers also see a slowdown,'' Mr. Loeb said. ''Catalogue business usually gives a signal of retail sales, so the forecast isn't good.''
 
A Fuel Bill Soars
The Middle East crisis has already brought about these developments:
* Wal-Mart Stores, the Arkansas-based discounter, says its fuel bill for diesel trucks will jump by $1 million a month because of a rise of 15 cents a gallon.
* J. C. Penney estimates slippage of at least one-half to 1 percent in expected sales gains, which translates into millions of dollars, for the rest of the year and into 1991.
* Montgomery Ward reports that suppliers have said they will raise their prices by 5 to 7 percent for spring merchandise.
But some of these retailers also see the possibility of a better outcome. If the Middle East situation eases or there is assurance of peace, consumer attitudes could improve considerably for the Christmas season, they say. That would lead to a strong wrap-up in sales and profits that could turn 1990 into a better year than 1989.
Some executives, like David Glass, Wal-Mart's president and chief executive, feel that the Middle East confrontation will be short-lived. But all those interviewed believe that the longer the crisis lasts, the greater the harm to sales, employment and the economy in general.
 
'Perception Is Reality'
''The longer it lasts, the more consumer psychology will be affected,'' said Tom Gould, president and chief executive of Younker's, a Des Moines chain of 36 department stores operating in five states. ''People already are thinking of the possibility of a recession with inflation. When they see gasoline going up from $1.35 a gallon to $1.55 in 10 days, they begin to worry that they can afford things. In our business, perception is reality.''
Robert Gill, vice chairman and chief operating officer at J. C. Penney, said, ''We have sensed a slowdown in general in the retail sector.'' He added, ''Business is spotty around the country, and particularly weak in the Northeast area, and our sales have been less than we planned for.''
John J. Schultz, president of retail services at the National Retail Federation, a trade association, said, ''What would have been a slam-dunk affair this Christmas against last year's terrible season now makes for a pessimistic outlook.''
''If the Mideast affair continues unchecked, sales will lose a couple of expected percentage points and retailers will be cautious on building inventories,'' he added. ''In that case, they will be lucky to make last year's Christmas figures, and with inflation, actually lose business.''
The Dayton Hudson Corporation, based in Minneapolis, seems to be bucking the trend. Karol D. Emmerich, vice president and treasurer of the department store chain, said: ''So far, we are surprised by the lack of impact on our sales. We feared some immediate shock, as we did in 1974 during that oil crisis. But perhaps it is because our promotional divisions concentrate on basics, which are less affected by downturns.''
 
Grim Retail Forecast
CHICAGO, Aug. 29 (Reuters) - Because too many stores are competing for consumers' dollars, the next 10 years will be among the toughest for retailers since the Depression, according to a study released this week. ''Some industry observers predict that as many as 50 percent of existing retailers will no longer be in business by the end of the decade,'' the Harris Bank of Chicago said in its annual retail study.

HEADLINE: Leading Indicators Unchanged

BYLINE: Reuters

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 29

 BODY:
The Government's main guide for forecasting the direction of the economy was unchanged in July from June, the Commerce Department said today, and economists predicted it would soon point downward because of the Persian Gulf crisis.
The index of leading indicators, a basket of measures for gauging future economic activity, was unchanged in July after rising a revised one-tenth of 1 percent in June and six-tenths of 1 percent in May.
The index showed a glimmer of strength in higher orders for manufactured goods, which analysts said could keep the economy out of recession. But the July number did not reflect the plunge in consumer confidence, a component of the index, since Iraq's invasion of Kuwait on Aug. 2. 
 
Optimism Fades
The Conference Board, a New York business research group, said Monday that consumer optimism about the economy was at its lowest level in more than seven years because of the Middle East crisis, which has sent energy prices soaring.
''We still think the economy has enough momentum to avoid a recession,'' said Darryl Delano, senior economist with Cahners Economics in Newton, Mass. ''But this index is likely to be down sharply in August.''
The index of leading indicators is designed to forecast the economy's direction six to nine months in advance. Financial markets showed little response to the report because the July data have been overshadowed by events in the Middle East.
 
Sluggish G.N.P. Growth
Mr. Delano said he had revised downward his forecast for total 1990 growth in the gross national product to 1.4 percent, from 2 percent, but believed stronger overseas sales would keep the United States out of recession. The declining dollar has made United States products more competitive in foreign markets.
The G.N.P. grew at a sluggish 1.2 percent annual rate between April and June, down from 1.7 percent in the first quarter.
In addition to higher plant and equipment orders in July, there were also increases in commodity prices and consumer expectations.
But seven other indicators were negative, including stock prices, which fell, and jobless claims, which rose. One indicator, orders for consumer goods, was unchanged.
Gordon Richards, an economist for the National Association of Manufacturers, said the report understated the economy's weakness.
''While these figures indicate that the economy was already sluggish prior to the Middle East crisis, they do not point to the emergence of a recession,'' Mr. Richards said. ''Since this time, however, the sharp rise in world oil prices has made a downturn almost inevitable.''
He said consumer confidence has been ''fatally undermined'' by concern over possible war in the Middle East.
 
Economy 'Going Sideways'
Lynn Reaser, senior economist with First Interstate Bancorp in Los Angeles, said the index in July was at the same level as it was in January 1989. ''It points to an economy that has been going sideways since then,'' she said.
Other negative indicators in July included longer delivery times for vendors, a lower money supply, fewer unfilled orders, a shorter workweek and fewer building permits.
The June index was previously reported as unchanged and May was reported up seven-tenths of 1 percent, but those figures were revised on the basis of updated data.

HEADLINE: CONSUMER RATES;
Funds Show Mixed Yields

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO

 BODY:
Yields of tax-exempts and taxable money market funds monitored by IBC/Donoghue's Money Fund Report were mixed for the week ended yesterday.
Donoghue's analysts said that while short-term interest rates were steady in the reporting week, long-term rates were up considerably because of the uncertainties created by the crisis in the Persian Gulf, the weak dollar and fears of higher inflation caused by rising oil prices.
Tax-exempt money fund managers appeared to reposition themselves in the week by increasing the average maturity of the securities they held by 8 days, to 57 days. 
 
Securing Higher Rates
Analysts said the managers may have been trying to lock in the higher yields of the last few weeks for a longer term. They said a move by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries to increase production would take pressure off oil prices and might lead to lower interest rates.
Cash flow into tax-exempt funds during the past few weeks has been erratic because of volatility in the financial markets. Assets fell by $393 million in the week ended yesterday, to $80.7 billion. They had risen $755 million in the previous week.
The average seven-day tax-exempt compounded yield increased by 8 basis points, to 5.34 percent, and the 30-day average yield rose by 3 basis points, to 5.25 percent. A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point.
The equivalent taxable seven-day compounded yield of tax-exempt funds was 7.41 percent for investors in the 28 percent tax bracket and 7.97 percent for the 33 percent bracket.
 
The Highest Return
Among tax-exempt funds with assets of more than $100 million survyed by IBC/Donoghue's, the USAA Tax Exempt Money Market Fund showed the highest return for the second consecutive week, with a yield of 6.02 percent. The fund is distributed by the USAA Investment Management Company of San Antonio.
Of the funds investing primarily in New York State securities, the General New York Money Market fund distributed by Dreyfus had the highest yield, 5.69 percent.
For taxable funds, assets increased by $475 million in the week, to $400.9 billion, a new high. Institutional assets declined by $528 million, to $85.1 billion, while consumer funds added more than $1 billion, to $315.8 billion.
The average days to maturity of securities held in managers' portfolios was unchanged, at 45.
Donoghue's said the seven-day compounded average yield for taxable money market funds fell by 4 basis points, to 7.76 percent in the week.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasury Bonds Post Solid Gains

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Interest rates fell and the price of Treasury securities rose yesterday, with the benchmark long bond jumping more than a point. The gains were fueled by a drop in oil prices and rising hopes among market participants that the Federal Reserve Board may move to ease short-term interest rates soon, perhaps by the end of the week.
In conjunction with a drop in oil prices, which fell to $25.92 a barrel, their lowest level in three weeks, traders said that an apparent softening of Iraq's negotiating stance seems to have reduced the chances of war in the Persian Gulf. The third positive for market participants was a successful auction of $8.5 billion worth of new five-year Treasury notes. 
''It could all change with one gunshot, but right now everything is going in the right direction for the market,'' said Mitchell Stafman, head of the government trading desk at Chemical Bank.
If oil prices stabilized at current levels over the next few days, the Federal Reserve Board might be persuaded to refocus its attention on the weak economy and to lower short-term interest rates to help stave off further weakness, traders said.
Since the start of the crisis in the Persian Gulf, the run-up in oil prices has led to rising fears among credit market participants of sharply higher inflation. In large part, those same fears have effectively prevented the Fed from taking any steps to help the economy.
''If oil holds at these levels over the next couple of days, I wouldn't be surprised to see the Fed move on Friday,'' another trader said.
 
Efforts to Ease Fed Policy
''The temptation for the Fed to ease policy will certainly increase if oil prices stay stable,'' said Raymond W. Stone, a managing director at Stone & McCarthy Research Associates, a Princeton, N.J., firm that provides research and commentary on developments in the credit and foreign exchange markets.
''I would like to see them ease policy over the next few days,'' Mr. Stone added, ''but the situation in the Middle East looks so tenuous that it makes it hard to predict if oil prices will stay down.''
The positive tone in the market provided a useful backdrop for the five-year note auction.
At the sale, the new notes were sold at an average yield of 8.57 percent, up slightly from the 8.54 percent average yield at the last five-year note sale on May 24. It is the highest yield on a five-year note since the auction in May 1989.
Also brightening the outlook for traders is the fact that, with the exception of the weekly bill auctions, there will be no new Treasury securities to bid on until late September.
''The fact that we don't have to face any more new paper for a month is a positive,'' said Robert Chandross, chief economist at Lloyd's Bank.
In the secondary market for Treasury securities, the 8 3/4 percent 30-year bonds of 2020 were offered late yesterday at a price of 98 1/4, up more than 1 point, or more than $10 for each $1,000 face amount of securities, to yield 8.91 percent. Late Tuesday, the bellwether issue was offered at a price to yield 9.03 percent.
Among note issues, the 8 3/4 percent ten-year notes were offered at 99 5/8, up 22/32, to yield 8.80 percent. The 8 1/2 percent seven-year notes were offered at 98 29/32, up one-half point, to yield 8.71 percent. And the 8 1/8 percent two-year notes auctioned Tuesday were offered on a when-issued basis at 100, to yield 8 1/8 percent.
 
Short-Term Bill Rates
Short-term Treasury bill rates eased slightly.
By late in the day, three-month bills were offered at a discount rate of 7.45 percent, down 2 basis points, or hundredths of a point. Meanwhile, six-month bill rates fell by 5 basis points, to a late offered rate of 7.42 percent.
The positive tone in the market for Treasury securities was also evident in the secondary markets for investment-grade corporate and tax-exempt municipal securities.
Municipal bond traders said that prices of most widely quoted issues rose by about three-fourths of a point.
Participants in the corporate bond market said that prices of most issues rose by anywhere from one-half point to a full point, in line with the increase in the price of Treasury issues.

HEADLINE: FUTURES/OPTIONS;
Precious Metals End Slide; Grain and Soybeans Drop

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
Precious metal futures halted their plunge on the Commodity Exchange in New York yesterday, largely disregarding a sharp drop in energy futures prices.
Gold was 10 cents lower to 30 cents higher, with the August contract up 30 cents, to $385.30 an ounce. Silver was 1.6 cents to 1.8 cents lower, with August at $4.826 an ounce. 
Trading volume fell from Tuesday's level because of increased margins on gold and silver contracts demanded by the exchange. The move makes trading more expensive and dampened market enthusiasm, said Don Tierney, an analyst with Stanley Bell & Company in New York.
Mr. Tierney said selling hit the market early in the session as energy prices fell. But moderate buying by speculators and investors in London helped stabilize prices.
After losing more than $30 in recent trading sessions, gold is coming out of its oversold condition, with some new buying beginning to support the market, Mr. Tierney said.
Grain and soybean futures prices were lower on the Chicago Board of Trade, with aggressive commodity fund selling coming late in the session.
The sharp selloff was attributed to the good growing conditions in the Middle West and the perception that crop yields will be larger than predicted by the Agriculture Department, said William Biedermann, an analyst with Allendale Inc. in Chicago.
''There is also the perception that the Soviet Union will not enter the U.S. market for grains and soybeans until October,'' Mr. Biedermann said.
Also contributing to the fall in prices was the lessening of inflationary pressures, as indicated by a drop in energy futures prices.
Livestock futures were lower and pork futures mostly higher on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange.
Cattle prices were under pressure from light retail demand for beef and expectations of a decline in cash prices.
Pork futures were higher on speculation that demand for pork products will increase.
Live cattle were 0.13 cent to 0.58 cent lower, with October at 76.07 cents a pound; feeder cattle were 0.20 cent lower to 0.20 cent higher, with August at 88.77 cents a pound; hogs were 0.02 cent to 0.37 cent higher, with October at 48.82 cents a pound; frozen pork bellies were 0.22 cent to 0.35 cent higher, with February at 52.60 cents a pound.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Mostly Higher in U.S. After Ending Mixed Abroad

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar moved mostly higher in United States trading yesterday after a mixed performance overseas as currency dealers began adjusting their holdings ahead of the long holiday weekend in the United States.
Domestic currency dealers said the dollar's advance came as a byproduct of trading in German marks and Japanese yen, which captured most of the attention.
Dealers sold marks and bought yen. The buying interest in the Japanese currency coincided with an OPEC agreement to increase oil output to compensate for lost exports from Iraq and Kuwait. 
At a meeting this week in Vienna, the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries decided on the action in response to the international embargo on Iraqi and Kuwaiti oil.
Earlier this month, dealers had dumped yen amid speculation that Japan's economy, heavily dependent on oil imports, would suffer because of a disruption in oil supplies from the Middle East.
 
Markets Still Nervous
A nervous tone continued in foreign exchange markets as traders awaited developments in the Persian Gulf.
Michael Schaufler, a currency trader at the Bank of Boston's New York office, said foreign exchange markets were preparing for the three-day Labor Day weekend in the United States. Efforts are being made to balance currency holdings in case any market-moving developments occur while United States trading is closed.
European foreign exchange dealers similarly reported that with prospects uncertain for a diplomatic solution to the Persian Gulf crisis, trading focused more on consolidating accounts before the end of the month.
The dollar rose to 1.55925 marks, up from 1.5560, in New York trading yesterday. And it rose against most other major currencies except for the yen and the British pound.
The dollar closed in New York at 143.55 yen, down from 143.75 on Tuesday. Earlier in Tokyo, it closed at 144.35 yen, up 1.15 yen from Tuesday's close. Later in London, the dollar was quoted at 143.65 yen.
The British pound was quoted at $1.9475 late in London, up from $1.9470 on Tuesday. In New York the pound rose to $1.94575 from $1.9429 on Tuesday.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with Tuesday's late quotes, were: 1.28875 Swiss francs, up from 1.2860; 5.2300 French francs, up from 5.2175; 1,155.00 Italian lire, up from 1,149.25, and 1.13865 Canadian dollars, down from 1.1416.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Healthiest S.& L.'s Show Sharp Decline In Latest U.S. Data

BYLINE: By STEPHEN LABATON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 30

 BODY:
Providing troubling evidence that the savings and loan deterioration is worsening, the Government reported today that even the healthiest segment of the industry has sharply declined in the last three months.
Losses mounted and loan problems increased at more than 7 percent of the strongest savings institutions.
''The numbers do not bode well for the thrift industry,'' said James Barth, a finance professor at Auburn University who has been studying the savings and loan debacle.
Analysts and lawmakers have assumed that the future of the industry would be dependent upon how the healthiest savings associations weather the current crisis. They expressed concern today about the latest figures, saying the data raised questions about whether the industry would be able to survive or would need to be absorbed by the commercial banking industry to reduce the enormous burden that the savings and loan bailout has placed on the Federal budget. 
In May, the Office of Thrift Supervision issued a report in which it divided all the savings institutions not taken over by the Government into four groups, based on their financial standing that month. Group One consisted of the well-capitalized and profitable institutions, and Group Two comprised those expected to meet the requirements on capital, which provides the institution's cushion against losses. Group Three included those troubled with poor earnings and low capital, and Group Four was made up of those associations expected to be seized by regulators.
Today, the thrift office updated that data into August. And out of the 2,453 savings associations in the industry that have not been taken over by the Government, the thrift office removed 106 institutions from Group One and added 17. That left a net loss of 89 from the top rank and a final Group One total of 1,175.
At the same time, the return on equity of the top group dropped from 10 percent to 9.6 percent, a move that some analysts said would further discourage investments in savings institutions. All told, 205 institutions out of the total were either demoted to a lower category or seized by the Government over the period, while 101 institutions earned a higher rating.
Officials at the Office of Thrift Supervision said that the reduction in Group One institutions was caused by the inability of the savings associations to meet capital requirements and by their mounting losses. They said some had also had their ratings lowered after more of their assets were identified as being in trouble, which required them to post additional reserves on their financial statements.
''Given the slowdown in the economy and the real estate market in many sections of the country, we are not surprised by the slippage in the healthiest group,'' said T. Timothy Ryan Jr., director of the Office of Thrift Supervision.
 
No Expectation of Improvement
He warned that there was no sign that the industry would be much better in the near term. ''If current economic conditions continue or get worse, it's very possible we will see additional deterioration when we report on the groups again in three months,'' he said.
Mr. Ryan said it was too soon to reach any conclusions about the future of the industry but added that trends should begin to emerge by earlier next year.
''What we have said about the healthiest thrifts is that we believe they are in good shape and by and large they should be survivors,'' he said. ''But we are not in control over the world. These thrifts will move in and out of groups based on macroeconomic factors.''
 
Need for Change Cited
Professor Barth of Auburn, who is working on a Congressional study of the Federal deposit insurance system, said that the numbers ''underscore the need for Federal deposit reform and raise again the question of who do we want to give access to Federal deposit insurance and what do we want them to be doing with federally insured deposits.''
In the wake of the savings bailout - which by the most conservative of estimates is expected to cost taxpayers more than $130 billion - both Congress and the Bush Administration have been considering ways to change Federal deposit insurance. A series of Congressional hearings on the issue will be held in the coming weeks, and a report by the Treasury Department is expected to be completed by the end of the year.
''The weaker the savings and loan industry, the more likely are the prospects for homogenization and accleration of bank and thrift mergers,'' said Representative Jim Leach, Republican of Iowa, who serves on the House Banking Committee. ''Consolidation will be a hallmark of the 90's.''
 
Accounting Changes Noted
Mr. Leach said the numbers in today's report by the thrift office might not necessarily illustrate an industry in decline but could to some extent reflect accounting changes that have been imposed upon the savings institutions.
''My sense is that it does not reflect a deteriorating circumstance as much as a greater honesty in accounting,'' he said. ''The figures are being looked at much more realistically. One has to remember that behind some of the scandals have been poor accounting practices. Those practices have changed.''
Officials in the savings industry questioned the accuracy of the Government's figures, although they acknowledged that soft real estate markets had continued to take a toll on even the healthiest savings associations.
 
Data Are Questioned
''If there is going to be a debate on the continuation of thrift chartering, we would hope that it would be on the most accurate data obtained,'' said James H. Grohl, senior vice president of the United States League of Savings Institutions, the industry's largest trade association. ''We have serious questions about today's data and are not confident that the shifts are accurate.''
He said that he believed that there were fewer problem institutions than the Government report indicated and added that his organization would be examining the Government's data over the next few weeks.
A lot of institutions, even the healthiest ones, he said, are adjusting to the sour real estate market and to the new rules of the bailout legislation that tightened capital standards. ''If we are headed into a recession, or indeed are in one, then we are no safer than commercial banks or other segments of the economy,'' he added.

HEADLINE: Complaints About Census Mount As Many Areas See Low Counts

BYLINE: By FELICITY BARRINGER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 30

 BODY:
The complaints of New York City about undercounting in the 1990 census figures grew to a chorus today as officials from cities and states across the country argued that they, too, were underrepresented in the preliminary data.
The loudest cries came from the areas whose populations sank furthest below expectations, like Philadelphia, which lost 8.6 percent from its 1980 figure; New York, which lost 0.5 percent, and small Southern cities like Meridian, Miss., which lost 13 percent.
Of those who claim an undercount, some, like Joseph Chow, a Houston planning official, were able to offer specific evidence. Other officials, like those in Philadelphia, said they were sure the numbers were low but had not yet determined what houses or apartment buildings had been left off the maps of the areas covered by the Census Bureau's count thus far.
 
The Highest Count Possible
Because the final census figures will be used by the Government to determine everything from Congressional representation to Federal benefits, state and local officials want the census to reflect the highest count possible. And this year, officials have been given more opportunity than ever before to review census findings before the data are made final. 
While the angry responses could be heard most clearly, there was another, less audible reaction of contentment in cities like Colorado Springs, where census tallies confirmed local perceptions of the growth and economic good times experienced in the 1980's.
''If there's anything that's a trend it's that there's this kind of diversity in the way people are perceiving the success of the census,'' said Alice Watland, an official of the National Association of Regional Councils, which represents thousands of local governments around the country. ''Just when I think I can go to the Census Bureau and say, 'This is the way it is playing across the country,' I'll call three or four people and get three or four more different opinions.''
Kevin Vandenbroek, press secretary for Gov. Ray Mabus of Mississippi, said state officials would meet today and Friday to examine their legal options for challenging census figures, which showed the state with a gain of just over one-half of 1 percent.
In most jurisdictions around the country, however, the emphasis is not on arguing about the figures, but on changing them. In many cases local governments do not have the computer capability to run cross-checks of census housing data and their own data on utility hookups or tax rolls.
Miss Watland said about 14 percent of the nation's 39,000 state and local governments were actively participating in the post-census local review.
And, while a few local officials expressed skepticism about the possibility that there was a political motivation behind some of the unexpectedly low numbers, far more were concerned that flaws might be caused by imperfect maps and haphazard field work.
''The thing that disturbed us worst was that we ended losing population in the county as a whole,'' said Mayor James Kemp of Meridian, Miss. ''We just don't believe that. We can believe that population in the city decreased. People are moving out of the city to the outer county, but they're still there.''
Meridian is the seat of Lauderdale County, which had a population of 77,285 in 1980. The preliminary count in the 1990 census put the county's population at nearly 74,000.
 
Disappointment in Philadelphia
David Baldinger, deputy director of the Philadelphia Planning Commission, said city officials were ''really disappointed'' with the preliminary figures, which showed a population decline as great as 135,000. But he said the Census Bureau initially sent Philadelphia the wrong computer tapes, so his department has had no chance to see where the problems are.
Mayor Maynard Jackson of Atlanta, whose preliminary count was down 9.6 percent from 1980, was more blunt. ''Way out of line,'' he said. In Los Angeles, which gained at least 450,000 people, city officials were nonetheless skeptical of the figures.
''Superficially, the count looks to be pretty much on the money,'' said Gregory Lipton, a technical expert with the city government. ''But the estimates were built on the 1980 census, and we know that was under by 4.7 percent. We know right away in this one that we're under by 4.7 percent, assuming nothing has changed. And we have more immigrants and more undocumented immigrants. We're very concerned about the count in minority neighborhoods.''
Among the cities that found problems with the census count are these:
* In Houston, Mr. Chow, assistant director of the city's Departmnet of Planning and Development, said an initial review of housing data and census maps showed that about 20,000 Houston housing units had been allocated to a neighboring county. The people in those developments were found but were counted as part of another jurisdiction.
* In upstate Michigan a demographer working with Kimball Brace of Election Data Services, a Washington consulting firm, found that in areas where the count fell well below expectations a high percentage of housing units were listed as having only one resident. In some census districts elsewhere there were reports of frustrated census counters listing a single person in a home when they were unable to make contact with the household.
* In several areas of central and South Chicago, which as a whole posted a 9.3 percent decline in the preliminary census figures, from 14 to 17 percent of all population data was taken by ''last resort'' means - asking someone outside the household, like a mail carrier or a neighbor, for information about the residents, said Bill Hunt of the General Accounting Office, an investigative arm of Congress. More than 10 percent of all housing units were enumerated by ''last resort'' methods elsewhere in the country, he said, including at least two local census offices in the Bronx, two in Manhattan, one in Los Angeles, one in Washington and one in Philadelphia.
 
The Least Reliable Data
''Last resort'' data, Census Bureau officials agree, are the least likely to be reliable. In a report prepared earlier this month, Mr. Hunt said data from 3.5 percent of all housing units were derived by these procedures.
The number of vacancies showing up in census data has some local officials suspicious. ''A few communities we have talked to, like Wilmette, think the vacancy rate is too high,'' said Max Dieber, who works as a consultant to communities in the Chicago area asessing the accuracy of census data.
But he added: ''What we were telling communities is that they're going to see these numbers, but don't sweat it. The number is preliminary and unfinished. Their task is to make sure all their housing units are counted.''
Houston, Detroit, Los Angeles and Chicago joined New York City several years ago in an ongoing lawsuit against the Commerce Department seeking to force a fundamental change in census methodology that would require the bureau to adjust the final count to correct for the undercounting in areas with hard-to-count populations of minorities and the poor. Officials from those cities have indicated that the initial counts will do nothing to dissuade them from pursuing that legal effort.
But while the losers - and even apparent winners like Los Angeles - believe the count was seriously flawed in their areas, the reaction in many parts of the country was closer to simple boosterism and civic pride at the way their communities were growing.
In Northern California, for example, newspapers tended to focus on the way the city of San Jose south of San Francisco Bay had vaulted ahead of San Francisco in it population.

LENGTH: 1522 words

HEADLINE: Confrontation in the Gulf;
BUSH URGES ALLIES TO BEAR 'THEIR FAIR SHARE' OF COSTS OF MILITARY EFFORT IN GULF

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 30

 BODY:
Asserting that the ''shape of the post-cold-war world'' was at issue in the Persian Gulf crisis, President Bush said today that he was asking other countries to help pay the multibillion-dollar cost of military operations and economic sanctions in the region.
As the emergency entered its fifth week and Secretary General Javier Perez de Cuellar of the United Nations arrived in Jordan for a mediation effort, Mr. Bush again said he was pessimistic about the search for a diplomatic solution. Mr. Perez de Cuellar, who is to meet on Friday with Iraq's Foreign Minister, Tariq Aziz, described the situation in the Middle East as explosive.
 
Hostage Release Delayed
In Iraq and Kuwait, American and other Western women and children, whom President Saddam Hussein had promised on Tuesday to release at once, encountered further delays. But late tonight, the Foreign Office in London said Iraq was arranging to fly some British women and children directly to London, and Virgin Atlantic Airways said it had been given permission to fly to Baghdad to pick up others. 
Soviet officials, after three weeks of quietly tolerating the buildup of United States military forces in the Persian Gulf, sharply challenged the American presence and questioned whether it might be intended as a permanent foothold in the region. [Page A13.] At a news conference before returning to his vacation home at Kennebunkport, Me., Mr. Bush said, ''The United States has large interests in the balance and has undertaken commitments commensurate with them, and we're more than willing to bear our fair share of the burden.'' He added, ''But we also expect others to bear their fair share.'' [Excerpts, page A11.] Brady and Baker to 'Twist Arms'
American delegations led by Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady and Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d are to be sent soon to West Germany, Saudi Arabia, the United Arab Emirates, Japan, South Korea and the Kuwaiti exile Government to ''twist their arms a bit,'' a ranking official said, for contributions of as much as $25 billion.
The money would defray some of the costs of American troop deployment and naval patrols and help Middle Eastern nations that are suffering hardships because of the United Nations trade embargo against Iraq.
Turkey, Egypt and Jordan are the principal nations that will benefit from the aid program, American officials said. The plan to solicit contributions was put together rapidly, under strong Congressional pressure to set up some form of burden-sharing, and it remains somewhat nebulous.
At a dinner at the White House on Wednesday night for a group of 11 senators and representatives, all old friends of the President, several pressed him to adopt a more aggressive policy, including the removal of President Hussein as the leader of Iraq, several guests said.
Representative John D. Dingell, Democrat of Michigan, said there was ''a clear appreciation that further goals have to be established.'' Senator William V. Roth, Republican of Delaware, commented: ''There were some who spoke quite strongly that we ought to go beyond the goals we have set, that the aggressor ought to be removed.'' Senator Richard G. Lugar, Republican of Indiana, who attended the dinner, has recently expressed that view in public and in private.
But Mr. Bush said today that he had ''stopped short of adding'' the removal of the Iraqi leader to the list of aims of the United States.
 
New Conditions for Release
The roughly 1,000 American women and children in Iraq and Kuwait have been told that they must submit letters requesting permission to leave, accompanied by Arabic translations and bearing official stamps attesting that all local taxes have been paid, according to Margaret D. Tutwiler, the State Department spokeswoman.
''Once again, Iraqi promises have been followed by new restrictions on foreigners and burdensome procedures,'' Miss Tutwiler said. ''This emotional roller coaster is inhumane and disgraceful.''
The Foreign Office in London said that even as Mr. Hussein was talking about letting people go, he was detaining others. A spokesman said Iraqi troops took 32 Britons from their homes in Kuwait on Wednesday, grouped them with seven others and transported all 39 to Baghdad under armed guard.
At his news conference, Mr. Bush said he was worried about the hostages but declined to say that their vulnerability would deter him from launching any attack on Iraq.
''We cannot permit hostage taking to shape the foreign policy of this country,'' he declared. ''I will not change the policy of the United States to pay homage or to give credibility to this brutal move of staking out citizens and a brutal move of holding people against their will.''
 
Perez de Cuellar Meeting Delayed
Mr. Perez de Cuellar had been expected to begin his meeting with Mr. Aziz this evening, but the session was postponed until Friday morning. He said he thought the Iraqi Government was ''as eager as we all are to find a just and lasting solution of the problem.''
But he also emphasized that he had no choice but to push Baghdad to accept Security Council resolutions demanding Iraqi withdrawal from Kuwait and release of all hostages.
''These are not my resolutions,'' the United Nations leader said. ''They are Security Council resolutions. I cannot make any concessions on these. I am not a merchant to negotiate.''
In Cairo, officials of 13 countries of the Arab League gathered for an emergency meeting that is given little chance of finding a solution. The deep divisions within the group, which split when 12 countries voted on Aug. 10 to send troops to help defend Saudi Arabia, were reflected by the absence of eight member countries.
As the diplomatic activity continued, so did the military buildup. A unit normally based at Torrejon in Spain, the 401st Tactical Fighter Wing, which flies F-16's, arrived in Qatar on the Persian Gulf. Army aviation units flying Apache, Blackhawk, Huey, Chinook and Kiowa helicopters were dispatched from West Germany to the gulf region.
The Pentagon reported that it had intercepted about 250 ships since the naval blockade of Iraq begun. Most of those were simple radio contacts, but about 10 ships have been boarded, none of them flying the Iraqi or the Kuwaiti flag.
Pete Williams, the Pentagon spokesman, said that in the last 48 hours, three ships - Sri Lankan, Panamanian and Liberian - had been searched in the Gulf of Aqaba and then allowed to proceed.
A fourth ship, a Sri Lankan freighter called the Kota Wirama, was searched and prevented from proceeding toward Jordan. Lloyd's List International, the shipping newspaper, said the vessel had been suspected of carry chemicals bound for Iraq.
 
Commerce at 'a Trickle'
At his news conference, President Bush said that Iraqi commerce through Jordan, which has been described as quite bustling in reports from the scene, ''has come down to a bit of a trickle.''
In a speech at the National Press Club this morning, the Jordanian Ambassador, Hussein Hammami, said that no ship with goods bound for Iraq had entered Aqaba harbor since Aug. 19, but that Jordan would continue to allow food and other basic commodities into Iraq until it was told not to do so by the United Nations.
Mr. Bush refused to comment directly on reports that the United States is aiding the Kuwaiti underground with weapons, equipment, intelligence and training, but he added: ''I support the Kuwaiti underground. I support anybody that can have a hand in restoring legitimacy there in Kuwait.''
 
De Facto Aid Reaches Guerrillas
Intelligence officials who spoke on condition of anonymity said Mr. Bush had been asked to sign a document authorizing formal cooperation but had so far declined to do so. Nonetheless, they said, numerous kinds of de facto aid is reaching guerrilla forces.
In discussing his plan to raise money to help Middle Eastern and other countries and to help defray American operational expenses, the President said he did not want American soldiers to appear to be mercenaries.
He said that he would send Mr. Baker and Mr. Brady, two of his closest Cabinet colleagues, to talk to the countries from which he hopes to raise most of the money. Mr. Brady is expected to leave next week for London, Paris, Tokyo and Seoul; Mr. Baker will travel to the Middle East, to Moscow and then to Europe.
 
'A Real Selling Campaign'
Although Mr. Bush denied that he was dissatisfied with what anyone had contributed to the multinational effort so far, the fact that he felt obliged to send emissaries indicated otherwise. Senior officials made no secret of the fact that they had what one called ''a real selling campaign'' ahead of them. They said, for example, that Japan's promised $1 billion was not enough.
Asked about the chances of war, Mr. Bush said: ''It's so hard to answer that question, ''because of the unpredictable nature of Saddam Hussein himself.'' The American buildup in the region, he said, ''would be a deterrent to anybody with any degree of rationhality. Having said that, I don't know what is on this man's mind.''

HEADLINE: Confrontation in the Gulf;
Excerpts From President's News Conference on Gulf Crisis

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 30

 BODY:
Following are excerpts from President Bush's news conference in Washington today, as provided by the Federal News Service, a transcription company:
 
OPENING STATEMENT
The United States is engaged in a collective effort involving the overwhelming majority of the member states of the United Nations to reverse the consequences of Iraqi aggression. And our goals, enshrined in five Security Council resolutions, are clear: the immediate and unconditional withdrawal of Iraqi forces from Kuwait, the restoration of Kuwait's legitimate government, the stability of Saudi Arabia and the Persian Gulf, and the protection of American citizens.
What is at stake here is truly significant -the dependability of America's commitments to its friends and allies, the shape of the post-postwar world, opposition to aggression, the potential domination of the energy resources that are crucial to the entire world. This effort has been truly international from the very outset. Many other countries are contributing. 
At last count, 22 countries have either responded to a request from Saudi Arabia to help deter further aggression, or are contributing maritime forces pursuant to United Nations Security Council Resolution 665. And still others are providing other forms of financial and material support to these defense efforts or to countries whose economies are affected adversely by sanctions or by higher oil prices. And still others are paying a heavy economic price at home for complying with the United Nations sanctions.
It is important that the considerable burden of the effort be shared by those being defended and those who benefit from the free flow of oil. Indeed, anyone with a stake in international order has an interest in insuring that all of us succeed.
The United States has large interests in the balance and has undertaken commitments commensurate with them, and we're more than willing to bear our fair share of the burden. This includes above all the thousands of men and women in our armed forces who are now in the gulf, but we also expect others to bear their fair share.
A number of countries already have announced their willingness to help those adversely affected economically by this endeavor. And it's essential, though, that this be a concerted and coordinated one and that all affected countries participate. It is important to get the priorities right and make sure that those most deserving of assistance receive it and that those most able to contribute do so. . . . Our approach calls for substantial economic assistance to those states - in particular, I'd single out Turkey and Egypt, who are bearing a great part of the burden of sanctions and higher oil prices. The plan also targets additional countries, including Jordan, the countries of Eastern Europe and others for special assistance. The United States will also seek burden sharing for part of our own effort.
At the same time, we will be asking other governments, including Japan, the Republic of Korea, the Federal Republic of Germany, Saudi Arabia, the Emirates, Free Kuwait and others to join us in making available financial and, where appropriate, energy resources to countries that have been most affected by the current situation.
To facilitate this undertaking, I've asked Secretary of State Jim Baker and the Secretary of the Treasury Nick Brady to lead high-level delegations to the Persian Gulf, Europe and Asia. . . .
Let me close by repeating what I said the other day in meeting with the congressional leaders. The basic pieces of our policy are in place. The Iraqi regime stands in opposition to the entire world and to the interests of the Iraqi people. It is truly Iraq against the world. But I want to make this point clear, we have no argument with the people of Iraq.
Another area where there has been unprecedented international solidarity is OPEC's willingness to take up the slack in oil production created by the embargo on Iraqi and Kuwait's oil. In this connection, I met this morning with our energy advisers who are watching the oil production situation very, very closely. And we are pleased with OPEC's decision to help take up the slack in crude oil production. And although we are in what I would see as a transition period, the situation appears manageable.
At the present time, we don't anticipate major imbalances in the oil market. But we do have the Strategic Petroleum Reserve tested and available if it is truly needed. And our energy policy is resulting in increased oil production and fuel switching to natural gas and to other fuels. I also repeat my previous request for Americans to conserve and for all parties to act responsibly. Right now, the situation, I would say, is relative stable, and I am very pleased by the coordination that has taken place with so many countries in maintaining adequate fuel levels.
 
QUESTIONS AND ANSWERS
 
Hostages and Impasse
Q. Mr. President, Saddam Hussein has rejected demands that he pull his troops out of Kuwait, and he's holding several thousand foreigners hostage to keep the world at bay. You say you don't see much chance for diplomacy to work. How long can the West allow this impasse to go on, and would you take any action that might endanger the lives of those hostages?
A. It will go on as long as it takes to have these United Nations sanctions fully implemented, and I am glad that these diplomatic efforts are taking place. Perhaps one will hit pay dirt. But as of now, I must say, I'm not optimistic, because the man keeps reiterating terms that are simply - fly in the face of the United Nations action.
And on the second question, look, I feel very concerned about Americans that are held against their will, but we cannot permit hostage-taking to shape the foreign policy of this country, and I won't permit it to do that.
 
Priorities of U.S.
Q. Mr. President, there are reports that there's a split in your Administration; some who want to expand the goals to include the ouster - eventual ouster of Saddam, and also there are many, many suggestions for a Middle East conference that would include what you would call the post-postwar shape of the world, the perennial problems of the Middle East. What do you think on both of those?
A. What I think on the second part of the question that we ought to get on with the business at hand, the shorter-run business, which is the solution to this question, or the making right the situation in Kuwait; meaning the pulling out of forces obviously, and the restoration of the rulers.
I do - as I look at the countries that are chipping in here now, I think we do have a chance at a new world order. And I'd like to think that out of this dreary performance by Saddam Hussein there could be now an opportunity for peace all through the Middle East. But we have to be sure that what's been undertaken so far is successful before we can - we can move to that other agenda, it seems to me.
Q. Well would you support, then, a conference afterward? I mean, this may be premature, but the question is, are you shooting for that?
A. I haven't - that's not an objective - a conference. Peace through the Middle East is an objective. And as you know, we have never ruled out a conference of any nature. In fact, it was part of an active part of our diplomacy just several years ago. But I don't want to get out ahead of the - where we are right now on this. The question right now is what do we do to get Saddam Hussein to comply with international law. . . .
Q. . . . Do you want to get him out of his job? . . .
A. Well it wouldn't disappoint me if the Iraqis got up and said, Look, this man is our problem. I've said right here the problem is not with the people in Iraq And - but I - I've spelled out our objectives here, and I - I would - I stopped short of adding to them what - you know, the answer that you were seeking from me on the President.
 
Israel and Jordan
Q. Mr. President, some have expressed the fear that Saddam Hussein might seek to inflame the Arab world against the United States by drawing Israel into the conflict here, perhaps by a strike against Jordan. Can you tell us if you're prepared for such a contingency, and if so, how?
A. . . . I can't predict what he's going to do. . . .
Q. Well let me just follow up by asking a question about Jordan's participation in the U.N. sanctions. There are numerous reports coming out of the East - some quoting Israeli intelligence - to the effect that Jordan is a highway, really, for supplies still reaching Iraq. Are you aware of those reports? And what --
A. I'm aware of some. But, it is my view that based on what I've seen most recently, that commerce has come down to a bit of a trickle there. There were reports of enormous numbers of trucks being - being laid up - no goods to transport. So I don't know how effective it is right now. I do know that King Hussein told me, looking me right in the eye, that they were going to comply with the sanctions. But I've seen reports that indicate there's some leakage there. But I just can't give you the quantity. . . . It's my feeling that commerce through the Aqaba, the port of Aqaba, and indeed through Jordan going to Iraq and vice versa has slowed down. Regrettably, there's a lot of refugee traffic. And I think that's hurting the Saddam Hussein image, because people see the humblest being brutalized the most. . . .
 
Fishing and Golfing
Q. Sir, you're going to return to Kennebunkport this afternoon. May I ask how bothered you may have been by the opinion of many Americans . . . who are bothered by you going out and fishing and golfing while in command of the troops in the gulf?
A. No, I'm not bothered by it. I've expressed myself on that. If I were bothered I wouldn't be going back there for the Labor Day weekend with my family. And I think the American people are supporting strongly what I'm doing.
 
Help From Allies
Q. Mr. President, on the question of burden sharing, since you're sending your envoys out, it sounds like you have not gotten the voluntary contributions you might have liked to have gotten. Can you give us a sense of how much you're looking for, and where you expect to find it?
A. No, I don't think it's a question of doing this because we haven't gotten what we think is fair for other countries and for the - and for burden sharing generally. What we're talking about here . . . is a consulting and coordinating effort, and we've had strong indications of support. But now we are moving up a little bit and trying to take . . . leadership, in helping sort out who should help whom. And somebody has to do that, and we've made a significant commitment in various ways, and so it seemed appropriate that we take the lead in working with our friends and allies. . . . Prime Minister Kaifu called me last night . . . and made a significant contribution, and then pledged to do more in terms of support for other countries. Now that is very good, and that was voluntary. But it needs to be coordinated. Somebody needs to take the lead on saying, Look, we don't put all the money to this one country. . . .
Q. If I could follow up, there have been concerns expressed about the Japanese not making any military contribution. They could send minesweepers or something like that. Is money not enough in the Japanese case? And what has happened to your good friend Helmut Kohl, who seemed extraordinarily silent?
A. Well, I don't - I wouldn't say money is not enough. I'm fully aware of the constraints on the Japanese, and I have not pressed him to go beyond what his Constitution provides.
Helmut Kohl - I think they'll be very responsive, and part of what we're talking about here is to follow up on comments like the ones Helmut Kohl made to me about we want to be a part of this, we want to help. So I have no argument with the Germans at all.
 
Kuwaiti Underground
Q. Is the United States doing anything to help the Kuwaiti underground, the Kuwaiti rebels? Training, supplies, anything?
A. . . . I wouldn't comment on it . . . but in a broad way - I support the Kuwaiti underground. I support anybody that can have a hand in restoring legitimacy there to Kuwait
 
Iraqi Opposition
Q. . . . There are some Iraqi opposition groups in London and elsewhere and the Kurds. And they've all said in recent weeks they've heard nothing from your Administration. If anything . . . the United States only wants covert contacts with them. Why not, if as you say, you want the Iraqi people to rise up, why . . . has this administration not done anything with the opposition groups?
A. We've got a plan, and the plan is to work diplomatically, and the plan is to put on the ground a significant military force. And if these comments I made today about anybody who wants to help the United Nations and those of us who want to see Iraq out of Kuwait succeed, so much the better.
 
Effects on Iraq
Q. If I could just follow up, you said also today you don't want to hurt the Iraqi people. But isn't this embargo and these sanctions only hurting them, and hurting them first before they hurt Saddam?
A. There's nothing that's painless, David, when you get into a situation like this, when you have a leader that is - could brutalize his own people.
 
Talking With Iraq
Q. On the question of negotiations, Mr. President, are all channels still open? Specifically, have there been any back channel contacts or proposals from White House officials that are worth pursuing?
A. None that I know of.
Q. If I could follow on that, sir. Saddam Hussein has suggested that you and he and Margaret Thatcher go on TV to debate this. What do you say to that?
A. I say you can put an empty chair there as far as I'm concerned.
 
Reduction in Troops
Q. A suggestion has been made that some reduction in the troops might be made in the days ahead to give a more international tinge to the force over there. Would you support such a move?
A. I'm more interested in seeing the fulfillment of commitments made.
 
Hussein and TV
Q. Mr. President, do you have any problem with the live TV coverage of Saddam Hussein media events, which a lot of people complain that this gives him a propaganda platform?
A. No, I have no complaints about it. I think that it hasn't helped him very much with world opinion. . . .

PUB-DATE: August 31, 1990

SECTION: Section A; Page 11, Column 2; Foreign Desk

LENGTH: 743 words

HEADLINE: Confrontation in the Gulf;
In Tallying Its Gulf Costs, U.S. Counts Impatience

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD KRAUSS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 30

 BODY:
In drawing up the burden-sharing plan announced today by President Bush to help sustain United States forces in the Persian Gulf, the Administration was mindful that the American people and Congress might become impatient with a drawn-out crisis, State Department officials said.
The officials said that political pressure is likely to grow as the American public starts taking notice of the expanding costs of the operation, especially when many economists say a recession is imminent. The plan is intended to bolster the argument that the sacrifices required to stop Iraqi aggression are truly international.
The Pentagon said this week that American operations in the Persian Gulf will have cost $2.5 billion beyond normal military operating expenditures by Sept. 30. 
The political ramifications have been driven home in recent days by members of Congress, who have argued that Japan and other allies should contribute more to the enforcement of the United Nations trade sanctions against Iraq.
 
Concern for Mideast Allies
The officials said another element of the plan, aid to nations suffering economic hardships, was motivated by the Administration's belief that Turkey, Egypt and Jordan, need financial assistance to make up for the more than $3 billion in annual trade they will lose collectively as sanctions against Iraq take hold.
The State Department officials said that as the crisis deepens, the Administration hopes to assure the political stability of these nations, which are among those most affected by the Iraqi invasion of Kuwait. All three countries face a rise in unemployment and a wave of business failures linked to Iraqi commerce.
Drafts of policy papers prepared by the State and Treasury Departments over the last week have differed in their breakdowns of the needs of the poorer Middle East countries and of the contributions that should be sought from the Administration's wealthier allies.
But officials said the Administration hopes eventually to gain commitments of $2 billion a month or more, including military and economic aid, with the largest amounts coming from Saudi Arabia and Kuwait's exiled Government.
 
Measuring Jordan's Compliance
The officials suggested that the amount of aid allotted to Jordan would depend on how it fulfills its pledge to observe the embargo against Iraqi trade. President Bush said King Hussein of Jordan had promised to support United Nations sanctions. But there have been continued reports of food and other shipments leaving the port of Aqaba or crossing the Jordanian border into Iraq.
The State Department officials said Bangladesh, Morocco, the Philippines, India and some countries in Eastern Europe would also receive assistance. The Administration does not believe that Bangladesh and Morocco should have to pay for sending several thousand troops to Saudi Arabia when wealthier countries are sending no troops at all, they said.
They noted that the Philippines, India and Eastern European nations are expected to lose vital oil supplies and trade as long as they support the embargo against Iraq and occupied Kuwait.
The Administration's primary goal, a State Department official said, ''is to sustain the worldwide support for effective pressure on Iraq. At the same time, we have an important stake in helping countries to respond to the adverse economic affects of the Gulf crisis.''
He added, ''The idea is not to hit up countries for specific sums, but to build on the consensus we have already achieved and insure an appropriate sharing of the burden.''
 
Nations to Be Pressured
The Administration particularly wants long-term commitments of economic aid from West Germany, Japan, South Korea, Saudi Arabia, the United Arab Emirates and the exiled Government of Kuwait. Officials greeted Japan's announcement that it would commit $1 billion in financing, transportation and medical aid to the effort, but said that much more would be needed from Tokyo in the months to come.
Announcing the program at the White House, President Bush said: ''We are more than ready to bear our fair share of the burden, and we also expect others to bear their fair share.'' He added, ''We're trying to take the lead here in helping sort out who should help who.''
Mr. Bush said he was sending separate delegations led by Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d and Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady to Europe, Asia and the gulf to solicit contributions.

PUB-DATE: August 31, 1990

SECTION: Section A; Page 13, Column 1; Foreign Desk

LENGTH: 707 words

HEADLINE: Confrontation in the Gulf;
THATCHER WARNS EUROPE OVER GULF

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: HELSINKI, Finland, Aug. 30

 BODY:
Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher of Britain said today that Europeans were in danger of losing the global military protection of the United States unless they took a broader view of their security interests than they have in the Persian Gulf crisis.
''It is sad that at this critical time Europe has not fully measured up to expectations,'' Mrs. Thatcher told a gathering of European conservative party leaders.
''The only countries in Europe which have done significantly more than the minimum are Britain and France,'' she said, adding, ''It's not what you say that counts but what you do.''
''That was a jab at West Germany,'' said Carl Bildt, leader of Sweden's opposition Moderate Center Party. Mrs. Thatcher's remarks came 12 hours bfore President Bush's call for countries, like West Germany, that have not contributed much directly to the military operations in the gulf, to reimburse the United States for up to $12 billion in costs. 
The NATO allies and the 12-nation European Community have supported United Nations resolutions condemning the invasion and annexation of Kuwait by Iraq, imposing an international trade and military embargo on the Iraqi Government, and authorizing enforcement of the blockade. But NATO itself, formed to defend Europe against aggression by the Soviet Union in the cold war, has not taken any role, and the alliance has left action or inaction to its members.
 
Widely Different Views
Their positions reflects their widely differing notions of who they are and what they can do with military force. Germany, though soon to be united and the most powerful military and economic force on the European continent, has shown itself deeply equivocal about using that strength in any way except to defend its territory.
Britain, which likes to remember when it ruled the waves, has sent four warships and three minesweepers to the gulf, deployed scores of aircraft and sent 2,200 soldiers. France, which likes to show that it can defend its interests all alone, has an aircraft carrier, an elite commando unit, and seven other navy ships in Indian Ocean waters near the gulf.
The United States has sent thousands of troops to Saudi Arabia and neighboring states, several aircraft carrier task groups, and several hundred combat aircraft to the gulf. Other European countries have sent the odd ship or two for logistical support or economic help to Arab nations suffering from the effects of Iraq's aggression, but not as part of a single American or NATO military campaign.
 
Speaks at Conference
With West Germany's Chancellor, Helmut Kohl, taking center stage in NATO councils for the last year as German unity suddenly became a possibility, Mrs. Thatcher had been something of a wallflower until the gulf crisis erupted.
But today at the conference of the European Democrat Union she said: ''There is no place in future for an inward-looking NATO, any more than there is for an inward-looking European Community. We have all this rhetoric about a common security policy as part of the political union, yet when it comes to something practical which affects us fundamentally, some countries are hesitant.
''We cannot expect the United States to go on bearing major military and defense burdens worldwide, acting in effect as the world's policeman, '' she warned, ''if it does not get a positive and swift response from its allies when the crunch comes - particularly when fundamental principles as well as their direct interests are just as much at stake.''
The NATO treaty, signed in 1949, created the alliance to respond to ''an armed attack on the territory of any of the parties in Europe and North America,'' but specified that the collective security guarantee applied only to the North Atlantic area north of the Tropic of Cancer.
 
Calls for Redefining Aims
That, most of America's allies agreed during two Arab-Israeli wars, clearly did not include the Middle East. But now that the threat of Soviet attack is gone, at least for a good while, people like Mrs. Thatcher are arguing that it is time to redefine NATO's aims to include defense against threats to security or economic stability from places like the gulf, where they have arisen repeatedly in the last decade.

GRAPHIC: Photo: ''We cannot expect the United States to go on bearing major military and defense burdens worldwide,'' Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher of Britain said. (Agence France-Presse)
