LENGTH: 1057 words

HEADLINE: Japan's Price Tag on Gulf Aid Bears U.S. Imprint

BYLINE: By STEVEN R. WEISMAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, Friday, Aug. 31

 BODY:
A decision by Japan to pledge $1 billion in aid for the military buildup against Iraq came after United States officials expressed unhappiness over the original package announced by Prime Minister Toshiki Kaifu, American and Japanese officials said today.
The officials said the American unhappiness - and a personal request for more by President Bush to Prime Minister Kaifu on Wednesday - led to wrangling session that night among Japanese bureaucrats and politicians to produce the price tag on Japan's aid program missing in the original announcement.
A major confrontation was also said to have occurred between Mr. Kaifu and his Finance Minister, Ryutaro Hashimoto, who was reported to have resisted the Prime Minister's desire for a specific dollar figure to satisfy Washington's demands.
After producing the $1 billion figure, Mr. Kaifu called President Bush again on Thursday. That call led Mr. Bush to say in Washington that the United States was satisfied with Japan's efforts. ''We're now evaluating the package more positively than before,'' an American official said. 
 
Military Vehicles Requested
The extraordinary deliberations within Japan provided an unusual glimpse into the way the Japanese political and bureaucratic system functions - extremely sensitive to high-level pressure from Washington, and yet insistent on maintaining Japanese prerogatives.
Officials said today that although Mr. Kaifu had also originally ruled out the shipment by Japan of any military equipment to the Middle East, discussions with the United States were continuing to see if Tokyo could send military vehicles and equipment.
The decisions this week culminated nearly a month of wrestling over Japan's role in the international effort to press Iraq economically and militarily to pull out of Kuwait. In the end, Japan showed itself divided and perplexed about protecting its interests and cooperating with its major ally.
Pressed by many countries and by many Japanese to exercise leadership commensurate with its status as an economic superpower, Tokyo was widely seen today as hampered by bureaucratic rivalries, political infighting and a long-standing ambivalence toward a larger role on the world stage.
 
'Japan Was Really Tested'
Many Japanese themselves are expressing disappointment that Japan moved too late with too little, and doing it too much under foreign pressure.
''This was a time when Japan was really tested to see if it could bear its international responsibility,'' said Moto Shiina, a leading Japanese defense analyst and former member of Parliament. ''I am disappointed that Japan did not show the will and the courage to do something more.''
Like Americans and Europeans, Japanese have been moved and angered by broadcasts of women and children hostages in Baghdad.
But widespread concern focused on two facets of the Japanese aid scheme announced on Wednesday. First was an apparent scaling back on earlier expectations of a large, quick infusion of cash for Middle East countries from Japan's Foreign Ministry.
 
Aid With Strings Attached
Second was the point that after a searching public debate with everyone agreeing that Japan's interests were at stake, Mr. Kaifu concluded that Tokyo could not provide the assistance to the military buildup in the Middle East that Washington had sought.
What Japan did come up with was not inconsiderable, Japanese and American officials agreed.
Mr. Kaifu offered to spend $1 billion to send 100 medical specialists, and food, water and medical supplies aboard chartered ships and planes to the Middle East, and to provide an unspecified sum of economic aid to Turkey, Egypt, Jordan and other countries strangled by the crisis.
But the economic aid to hard-pressed Middle East countries came with strings attached, guaranteeing that the money would not be available for their immediate cash needs.
 
Watered Down by Ministry
Government officials explained that initial promises made by Foreign Minister Taro Nakayama were watered down when turned over for implementation to the Finance Ministry, which like all budgetary agencies is extremely reluctant to spend money.
Clashes and delays caused by bureaucratic infighting have long been a feature of Japan, which traces the existence of a powerful professional bureaucracy back a thousand years.
In Japan's Government system, moreover, the Prime Minister is usually too weak politically to force an issue through the way he wants or reconcile differences among various agencies. Mr. Kaifu is considered an especially weak Prime Minister, lacking a power base of his own.
As for Japan's foreign aid program, it is also controversial because it relies less on health and welfare for individuals than on large construction projects - power plants, harbors and the like -often awarded to Japanese contractors.
In addition, Finance Ministry officials asserted this week that the financial aid would have to be carried out in coordination with other nations and with the International Monetary Fund, making it likely to require austerity measures of the recipients.
 
'Airlift Capacity Is Strong'
The more serious debate in Japan was whether Tokyo could for the first time since World War II use its ships and planes to transport military equipment and personnel.
The United States has made its view clear that such aid would not violate either Japan's Constitution - written by the American occupation after World War II - or laws enacted since then.
In rejecting the American request, Mr. Kaifu said Japan could not do such things without amending the laws governing its armed forces. Many defense experts argue that Japan could have done more and still complied with its legal and Constitutional restrictions.
''We cannot send combat forces. That's true,'' said Mr. Shina, who until his defeat for re-election this year was the dominant Liberal Democratic Party's leading defense analyst. ''But our airlift capacity is strong, and so are combat-ready medical teams who know how to set up field hospitals.''
Aside from the Constitutional issue, a practical barrier to Japanese action emerged this month when owners and unionized workers with the airlines and shipping companies told the Government that they would refuse to carry military personnel or equipment into a combat zone.

HEADLINE: Confrontation in the Gulf;
Soviets Issue Their First Criticism Of American Presence in the Gulf

BYLINE: By BILL KELLER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MOSCOW, Aug. 30

 BODY:
After three weeks of quietly tolerating the buildup of United States military forces in the Persian Gulf, Soviet officials sharply challenged the American presence today and questioned whether it might be intended as a permanent foothold in the region.
''There are no guarantees that the United States will leave Saudi Arabia after the crisis is over,'' Deputy Foreign Minister Aleksandr Belonogov told a parliamentary committee today.
Mr. Belonogov, who has been a leading architect of Moscow's collaboration with the West in efforts to roll back Iraq's conquest of Kuwait, said the Soviet Union had never approved of the American deployment of troops in Saudi Arabia, and described the situation in the region as ''becoming more and more explosive.''
In an interview with the official Tass press agency, General of the Army Vladimir N. Lobov, Chief of Staff of the Warsaw Pact alliance, said the American presence had drastically changed the strategic balance in the region and could scuttle disarmament talks in Europe. 
 
Pressure on Moscow
General Lobov, who said he was expressing his personal views, speculated that the United States might plan to extend its forces along the Soviet Union's southern flank, establishing a bridgehead from which to control Middle East oil flows and put pressure on Moscow.
The remarks of two Soviet officials were by far the most critical Soviet reaction in the gulf crisis, which has brought the two superpowers into the closest working relationship since they were allies in World War II.
There are several possible reasons for the Kremlin's choice of this moment to distance itself somewhat from American policy in the region.
One is the mounting impatience of top military officials about the massing American armies in a region 700 miles from the Soviet Union's southern border. The Kremlin may have felt the time was ripe to issue a warning before the Americans got too settled.
Another reason may be to address fears in the public and the Parliament of a widening conflict that could draw in the Soviet Union. #85% Support Anti-Iraq Stand A poll published today in the weekly Moscow News showed that 87 percent of those with a clear opinion support President Mikhail S. Gorbachev's policy of siding with the West and Arab moderates in the campaign to isolate Iraq.
But the Kremlin knows that a public scarred by the war in Afghanistan and obsessed with its domestic economic woes would have little appetite for sending Soviet troops to fight in the Persian Gulf.
Since the first joint Soviet-American statement condemning Iraq on Aug. 3, Moscow has joined Washington to support five United Nations resolutions against the former Soviet ally, including one endorsing use of force to police an economic embargo.
While the Kremlin has repeatedly urged that military measures be delayed until economic and political pressure has had a chance to work, Soviet officials have refrained until now from publicly criticizing the American-led naval buildup and the assembly of land forces in Saudi Arabia.
Today's comments came as the Soviet Parliament's International Affairs Committee met to review the Iraq-Kuwait conflict.
 
'Buildup Cannot Please Us'
Mr. Belonogov, who was quoted by Tass, said, ''The buildup of American military cannot please us, either in the short run, since the situation is becoming more and more explosive, nor in the long run, since there are no guarantees that the United States will leave Saudi Arabia after the crisis is over,''
''The United States, in answer to an inquiry, notified the Soviet Union of its intention to sent troops to Saudi Arabia, but the Soviet Union did not express approval of that step,'' he added.
General Lobov, who spoke to Tass after the parliamentary hearing, offered a ''likely scenario of further developments in the region'' that may have been intended as a case for bolstering the Soviet military budget.
The general hypothesized that with the United States likely to scale back its presence in Europe as a result of conventional arms talks in Vienna and the reunification of Germany, ''the current crisis over Iraq and Kuwait opens new possibilities.''

HEADLINE: Romanian Leader Is Seeking Fall Visit With Bush

BYLINE: By DAVID BINDER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BUCHAREST, Romania, Aug. 30

 BODY:
Prime Minister Petre Roman wants very much to come to Washington this autumn and has let the Bush Administration know of this desire. But so far, the Romanian official has received no positive response.
''I would like to visit the United States,'' he said in an interview in his office here on Wednesday. ''I want to go because I want to be questioned by Congressmen and Senators. I want to be really there to answer every question and every criticism directly in the pure American style.'' 
Mr. Roman, who is 45 years old, is to meet on Friday with Senator Bob Dole, the Republican minority leader, and six of his colleagues. He will be able to answer their questions in English, which he speaks quite well.
But at this point, it is not clear whether the Bush Administration, which has repeatedly and harshly assailed the Romanian Government for most of the last eight months, will officially endorse a visit by Mr. Roman.
''We have certain benchmarks,'' a United States Embassy official said when asked about the possibility of a visit by the Romanian head of government. ''We are watching University Square,'' the scene of violent clashes between anti-Government demonstrators and the police earlier this year. ''We are watching the newspapers and the development of a press law.''
The Romanian Government has been strongly criticized for its conduct during a demonstration in June, when a call for help brought to the capital a flood of miners who beat up students, demonstrators and others.
But American diplomats here acknowledge that the Romanian Government, whose United Nations representative is now chairman of the Security Council, has been extremely supportive of United States initiatives in the Persian Gulf crisis. ''That is a positive development'' in the still sour relationship with Washington, a diplomat said.
Mr. Roman indicated that he intended to go to New York for the annual session of the United Nations General Assembly that starts in September and that ''if I have a clear signal from Washington, I'll go.''
He was frank in acknowledging mistakes by his Government, which was overwhelmingly endorsed by Romanian voters in elections last May that were generally acknowledged as fair.
Referring to clashes in mid-June that left six dead, Mr. Roman said the Government was ''to blame for the fact that we did not succeed in controlling the violence of June 13 and the fact that some miners got out of control the evening of June 14 also.''
He said that the events of June were still the subject of investigation, but that it was evident to him that some former members of the Nicolae Ceausescu's secret police had committed acts of violence at the time. ''Some former Securitate men were involved in provocations to force us to return to their methods,'' he said, using the Romanian name for the deposed dictator's state security force.
Mr. Roman rejected the notion that Romania was, saying, ''I think you have to view our political stability within the framework of the legacy inherited from the Communist regime.''
''It was clear to us that we would have a very difficult economic situation in this transition,'' he continued. ''It may be worse than we expected.'' He said other East European countries ruled by Communist governments until last year had the same problems.
He said his Government had pushed through the necessary legal changes to turn state enterprises into privately owned companies and to encourage small businesses, including a law that every apartment house built after 1948 could be placed on the open market.
That and other laws encouraging privatization ''haven't operated very well,'' he said, because brakes were being applied ''by ancient members of the bureaucracy.''
''We need to accelerate the autonomy,'' he said, although ''even those that want to put things in order, most don't have the means.''
The only real bright spot in the economy, Mr. Roman said, was in agriculture, where a record grain harvest of 11.5 million tons is being brought in -with 3 million tons being kept by farmers for sale on the free market.

HEADLINE: Loss of Paychecks From Kuwait Makes Families Hostages of Crisis

BYLINE: By LISA BELKIN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: HOUSTON, Aug. 30

 BODY:
Saying the Persian Gulf conflict has eliminated its only source of income, a Texas company has cut off paychecks to the families of six oil workers who are hostages of the Iraqi Government.
The company, OGE Drilling, says paying the men would mean financial ruin. But the families say losing those paychecks means they might lose their homes and medical care.
''Our revenue has been cut. and if we continue to pay these people we will be bankrupt,'' said Bill Schaub, drilling director of OGE. ''Then we would no longer be helpful to anyone.'' 
 
'In His Mind I'm Safe'
That explanation is little comfort to Donnita Cole, of Odessa, Tex., whose husband, John, is trapped in Kuwait. ''My husband thinks I'm fine right now,'' she said. ''In his mind I'm safe and OGE is taking care of me, but in actuality I may be out on the street.''
OGE has 18 employees and one source of income: the Kuwait Oil Company, whose assets were among those frozen by the United States Government after Iraq invaded Kuwait earlier this month. OGE's business is to find employees for Kuwait Oil. It pays their salaries and expenses and is then reimbursed by Kuwait Oil.
Even before the invasion, Mr. Schaub said, Kuwait Oil owed OGE $500,000. The salaries of the six employees being held by Iraq cost the company $30,000 a month, he said. Though he would not reveal the revenue of OGE, or its parent company, OGS Engineering, he said ''those kind of losses are more than we can handle.'' OGS provides workers for other aspects of the oil business.
 
No Steady Job for Years
Patricia Hale of Spring, Tex., says loss of the paycheck is more than she can handle. Before her husband, Edward, left for Kuwait in January, he had not had steady work for several years, because of the decline of the oil drilling business, in which he had worked for 35 years. By the time he left, the couple had sold their home north of Houston, and most of their furniture. Their savings were gone.
In February, Mrs. Hale, who has severe asthma, was hospitalized for a month. Because hers was a pre-existing condition, insurance did not cover the $18,000 bill, which she has been paying in installments. Still recuperating at home, she requires oxygen equipment, which costs nearly $3,000. She was about to apply for a loan to pay for the equipment, she said, when she heard rumors that OGE might stop her husband's paychecks.
''That equipment is my lifeline,'' she said. ''The company that owns it couldn't be nicer, and they said they understand but they can't go forever without being paid. One day they'll want the money or the equipment back.''
 
Search for Ways to Help
Jay P. Simpson, vice president of OGE, said the company was looking at ways, short of paying the full salaries, to help the families of the six employees.
He said his staff was investigating ways to allow the families to keep their insurance benefits, which are due to expire on Saturday. The company has queried the Texas Employment Commission to see if the families could apply for unemployment benefits for the employees in their absence.
Bill Grossenbacher, the commission's Administrator, said the most the families could receive would be $214 a month if they qualified. A recipient of unemployment benefits must be able to actively look for work and since the hostages cannot do so special waivers of state regulations would be required.
Mr. Simpson said OGE was trying to collect the money it was owed by Kuwait Oil and had approached the Treasury Department, asking that some of its assets be freed so its debts could be paid.
Finally, he said, the idea of other OGE and OGS employees' taking pay cuts is being discussed, and a fund is being set up for anyone who wished to donate money to the families.
Still, Mrs. Cole said she was close to the breaking point.
''I'm playing three waiting games now,'' she said. ''I'm waiting to see if my soldier son in Saudi is going to war. I'm waiting to see if my hostage husband comes home from Kuwait. And now I'm waiting about the paycheck. That last one, I didn't need.''

GRAPHIC: Photo: Patricia Hale of Spring, Tex., whose husband, Edward, is being held hostage by Iraq. She is dependent on oxygen equipment that she can no longer afford because Mr. Hale's employer, a Texas oil personnel company, has stopped paying his salary and those of five other hostages. (F. Carter Smith for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Tokyo's Share of World Leadership

 BODY:
Faced with Washington's wrath, Japan stopped some of its hemming and hawing yesterday and pledged at least $1 billion for the multilateral effort in the Persian Gulf. But regrettably, Tokyo remains vague about its promises to help beleaguered front-line gulf states.
In any event, the total package disclosed so far doesn't begin to approximate what Japan's contribution should be - given that nation's substantial dependence on gulf oil and its new desire to become an important actor in world affairs. 
Tokyo cannot hope to be a great power unless it acts like one. And as long as Prime Minister Kaifu and other political leaders defer to cautious financial bureaucrats, Japan cannot fulfill its clear obligations to thwart Iraqi aggression.
To be sure, Washington unwisely complicated Mr. Kaifu's problems by urging Japan to make a military contribution to the gulf effort. Specifically, it was a mistake to ask Tokyo to send minesweepers. That would violate the spirit of Japan's anti-militarist Constitution. It would also alarm Japan's Asian neighbors, many still traumatized by Japanese aggression during World War II.
But the United States is right to press Japan to live up to its promises of immediate and large-scale financial aid, with no strings attached, to countries like Turkey and Egypt. Provided Jordan fully respects the United Nations sanctions, it should receive aid as well. These countries face heavy economic burdens from the showdown with Iraq, and they have no realistic hope of coping without substantial outside help.
Japan did much better yesterday, albeit belatedly, in stepping up to its responsibilities in sharing the financial burden for the multilateral force. The $1 billion pledge over the next six months is a good start. Even without major combat, U.S. troop deployments are expected to cost more than $1 billion a month. The Administration hopes that Germany will also make a heavy contribution. Reasonably enough, the largest share is expected to come from the rich Arab gulf states that American forces are there to defend.
As Mr. Bush rightly noted yesterday, ''the shape of the post-cold-war world'' is at stake in the resolution of the gulf crisis. A critical element of security in that new world will be united action. And for there to be unity, different nations will have to contribute according to their strengths.
Mr. Kaifu seems to understand the point well as it applies to his own nation. ''If Japan's response is delayed,'' he said Wednesday night, ''and the world gets the impression that it does nothing for peace in a region where its own national interest is at stake, then its future will be lost.'' So far, Japan is doing more than nothing, but not nearly enough to set an example of how to unite for peace.

HEADLINE: NATO Can Get Back on the Right Track

 BODY:
To the Editor:
It is not true that the North Atlantic Treaty Organization was ''founded in 1949 to counter the Soviet threat'' (''NATO, Bereft of a Military Role, Redefines Itself as the West's Political Galvanizer,'' news article, Aug. 9). Only after the Korean War did NATO become the United States-led military bloc it is. West Germany was incorporated into the North Atlantic Alliance in April 1955; on May 5 that year the Soviet Union retaliated by forming the Warsaw Pact. 
Louis St. Laurent, Lester Pearson, Escott Reid and NATO's other Canadian architects conceived the Western alliance as an economic, political and cultural confederation of Canada, the United States and Western Europe. In August 1948, Pearson gave the definitive statement of Canada's vision of the Western Alliance:
''A North Atlantic treaty will create a new living international institution which will have within itself possibilities of growth and adaptation to changing conditions. The North Atlantic Community is a real commonwealth of nations which share the same democratic and cultural traditions. If a movement toward its political and economic unification can be started this year, none of us can forecast the extent of the unity which may exist 5, 10 or 15 years from now.''
In the Canadian view, shared by Britain and an influential minority in the State Department of the United States, the treaty's military aspect was only to provide the psychological security for reconstruction of Western Europe. This position is enshrined in Article II of the North Atlantic Charter. Pearson expected the alliance's military aspect to be temporary, until relations between the Western world and the Soviet world had improved. The European peace that proved evasive in 1948-49 is finally in sight.
The end of the cold war, and with it NATO's exclusively military existence, should be welcomed to return NATO to its original principles of economic, political and cultural cooperation.
 
GREGORY FELTON
Vancouver, Canada, Aug. 13, 1990

HEADLINE: Our Towns;
Lobster Bargain Is No Bargain For Lobstermen

BYLINE: By NICK RAVO

DATELINE: STONINGTON, Conn.

 BODY:
Here on the fishing docks in this seaside village near the Rhode Island border, stacks of empty lobster traps bake like driftwood in the noonday sun. ''Must mean people are buying lobster, else they wouldn't be empty,'' said Arthur Medeiros, the captain of the Rosemary R., a 40-foot dragger that combs Fishers Island Sound and Little Narragansett Bay.
Indeed consumers, restaurantgoers mainly, are buying lobster these days; fishing industry statistics say that the nation's annual lobster consumption has remained at 40 million pounds for the last 10 years.
Among New England lobstermen, however, there is rising concern that cheap Canadian imports, higher fuel prices and the fickle tastes of diners could scuttle their business. Already, boat prices (the prices paid to lobstermen) have hit 10-year lows, making lobster almost as cheap as hamburger in some parts of New England. In Maine and Massachusetts, lobstermen have gone on strike this summer in an effort to push up prices. 
''We've been having a hard time for the past year,'' said John Healey, captain of the 27-foot lobster boat Tina Marie. ''People aren't spending money like they used to, so there is no demand. Not even at low prices.''
Even in good years, lobster prices rise and fall like the oil or gold markets. This year, though, boat prices in Stonington dropped to $2.25 a pound in April. Last summer, the average price fluctuated between $3 and $4 a pound. Lobstermen generally cite $2.50 a pound as their break-even point.
In the past few weeks, boat prices have rebounded to a recent high of $3 to $3.50 a pound. The increase, however, is linked mainly to recent bad weather limiting supplies already languishing because of the annual August lull in catches.
''August is always the worst month,'' said Mr. Medeiros, a fisherman here for the last 43 years and the current president of the Southern New England Fishermen and Lobstermen's Association.
Lobstermen sometimes attribute the summer price drop to the price of fuel, which has increased by up to 25 cents a gallon in the past month.
''It doesn't pay to go out anymore,'' Mr. Healey said. They also cite imported lobsters from New Brunswick and Nova Scotia that are selling for less than $3 a pound. More often, they point to the worsening economy for what they perceive to be a drop in demand. Eighty percent of all lobsters are consumed in restaurants.
''We were delivering 150 lobsters a day to some restaurants last year and now they are getting 75 or 80,'' said Bill Paul, manager of DiMaggio's Lobsters, a wholesaler on the town dock.
Because of the decline in wholesale prices, some restaurants in Connecticut have been offering lobster dinners at under $10 this summer; SoNo Seafood in South Norwalk recently was charging $8.95 for one-claw lobsters. Others, though, have been less willing to pass along the price cuts. For example, the cheapest price for a one-pound lobster at the HarborView, a dockside restaurant in Stonington, runs $14.95 at the bar.
''It's a short season, and we have to make as much money as we can off the tourists,'' said the restaurant's head chef, Bill Geary. ''Besides, a lot of our customers are from New York, and this is still cheaper than what they are used to.''
Catching lobsters has also come under criticism from crustacean-rights advocates, who contend that boiling lobsters alive is a cruel method of killing. ''We hear it every day,'' Mr. Paul said. ''People come in here, they want the lobster, but they don't want to cook them. They hear them kicking in the pot.''
''All they got to do is make sure the water is hot enough,'' Mr. Paul said. ''They'll be dead in a second.''
Curiously, the troubled lobster market has not forced lobstermen into other less-volatile ventures. The trade has become almost trendy. On his latest album, the rock singer Billy Joel released a song about bay men, and he is often spotted cruising in a working lobster boat.
Old-timers around Stonington, a former whaling and shipbuilding capital that still houses Connecticut's largest fishing fleet, noted, too, that an increasing number of part-timers have been taking out lobster traps.
''Every year, there are more and more out there,'' Mr. Medeiros said. ''It must be the romance.''
Mr. Healey, however, had a better explanation. ''With the economy the way it is around here,'' he said, ''it is getting so there is nothing else to do.''

HEADLINE: Bargemusic: Brooklyn's Floating Concert Site

BYLINE: By ALLAN KOZINN

 BODY:
''Actually, we're practicing great economy of motion here,'' said Olga Bloom, the founder of Bargemusic Ltd. Since 1977, Mrs. Bloom has been presenting weekly concerts in the refurbished coffee barge that she transformed into a floating concert hall on the Brooklyn side of the East River, near the Brooklyn Bridge.
''Water exerts a universal pull on humanity'' Mrs. Bloom said. ''People commune with nature here, to a degree. They even see a soaring seagull occasionally, and I have my scraggly, brave pines on the deck. We also offer an unmistakably warm social occasion. Our audiences are small - we can hold 140, but I usually try to cut off at 130 - so even though they don't know each other, they speak to each other readily during the intermissions. They are sharing values. And they are experiencing a noble performance of great music. All in one visit.''
Bargemusic, which is moored at Fulton Ferry Landing, at the foot of Furman Street, presents its concerts every Thursday evening and Sunday afternoon, with Friday evening concerts during the summer. This week's program, which can be heard tonight at 7:30 and on Sunday at 4 P.M., includes the Mozart Clarinet Trio in E flat (K. 498), Walter Piston's Duo for Viola and Cello, and Mendelssohn's Piano Trio in C minor (Op. 66). 
The performers are Toby Hoffman, violist; Mihae Lee, pianist; Mark Peskanov, violinist; David Shifrin, clarinetist, and Ronald Thomas, cellist. Tickets are $15, or $12 for students and the elderly. Information: (718) 624-4061.
 
Intimate Setting
Some of Bargemusic's appeal has to do with the idea that hearing a serious concert on a barge is a slightly quirky thing to do. But its principal draw is the intimacy of the performances. Now that chamber-music concerts are offered frequently in such large spaces as Avery Fisher Hall and Carnegie Hall, New York's smaller halls have lost some of their proprietary claim on the genre. But even the smallest ones have several hundred seats. And current practice aside, chamber music's historical roots are in small rooms, where most of the repertory is heard to best effect.
Bargemusic has changed a good deal since Mrs. Bloom started it 13 years ago. Her original plan was to present concerts by students at New York City's conservatories. She found immediate support from the New York State Council on the Arts. But when the council sent someone to critique the series, after a few seasons, the reviewer found the performances insufficiently rehearsed. So in 1984, Mrs. Bloom appointed Ik-Hwan Bae, a violinist who is now 33 years old, as her project's artistic director, and he helped Bargemusic set a new course.
''I got involved purely by chance,'' Mr. Bae said the other day. ''One of my colleagues who was supposed to play at the barge got sick, and asked me to sub for her. I was a last-minute thing, but I went and did it, and I fell in love with the place. Olga and I talked, and we hit it off instantly.
''But artistically and financially, the barge was not in good shape, and I saw that Olga's dream for it was not being realized. She wanted to create an environment where musicians could come and play as they would in their own living rooms. And she wanted to give opportunities to students.
''That's a very honorable dream,'' Mr. Bae said, ''but I convinced her that in order to be a successful musical organization in New York City, we simply could not depend on students alone. It's fine to bring in young talented students from time to time. But we are presenting about 120 concerts a year, and unless you have professional musicians giving concerts of first-class quality, you cannot draw the kinds of audiences and support that you need. Once we began inviting professional musicians, things got better very quickly.''
 
Friendly Get-Togethers
As artistic director, Mr. Bae is responsible for putting together the 52 weekly programs.
''I always go after the artists first, then I put together the programs,'' he said. ''I think the reason we are successful now is that we have a pool of 80 or 90 players who come from around the world, and who look at the barge the same way I do. We don't think of it as a normal concert scene, or like the places we play when we are touring. It's more like our student days, when we played chamber music with our friends, and had fun. Not that I don't have fun when I play elsewhere. But the intimate ambiance seems to hit everyone in the right place.'' Some of the players scheduled to perform over the next few months are the pianists Edward Auer, Christopher O'Riley and Paul Schoenfield; the violinist Pamela Frank; the flutist Ramson Wilson; the cellist Carter Brey, and the violist Steven Tenenbom.
Mrs. Bloom, whose husband, Tobias, was a violinist in Toscanini's NBC Symphony Orchestra, and who played the violin herself, began thinking about presenting concerts in 1975, after her husband's death. The first two barges she bought - a wooden vessel and a metal one - proved acoustically problematic. But on her third try, she found the 102-foot coffee barge, built in 1899. After spending a year refurbishing it on Long Island, she had it towed to Brooklyn, near the Brooklyn Maritime Museum and the River Cafe.
 
Weddings and Donations
When she began giving concerts on the barge, Mrs. Bloom ran the series on a shoestring budget, and she raised money partly by renting the barge out for weddings and similar events on Saturdays, and partly through donations from foundations, corporations and individuals (including many musicians). But costs, she said, have grown enormously in recent years. In 1985, her budget was $350,000. This year, it is close to $1 million.
''And I'll tell you why,'' she said. ''We have seen many changes, but one of the most important is the change in river traffic. It used to be exclusively huge freighters, which slid by without a ripple. They have all but vanished. And what we're getting now are ferries and fast-moving pleasure craft. They throw up a huge wake, which causes erosion on the shore, and loosens our line. Now we bob around quite a bit, and every time we have to call a man with a welding machine, we've got a sizable expense. But as they say, if you have to ask what it costs to run a boat, you shouldn't buy one.''
One thing Bargemusic's budget cannot cover yet is the cost of rehearsal. Usually, the musicians get together just before the Thursday evening concert. For Mrs. Bloom, there's a certain excitement to this approach: ''When you're always on the brink of disaster, the performances have a certain spontaneity.'' ''Well, Olga has a way of saying that,'' Mr. Bae said, ''but let me explain how it works. Most of the musicians who play at the barge see each other regularly at festivals around the world during the year. And in many cases, we've known each other, and played concerts together, for 10 or 15 years, ever since we were in school. When you know each other, and you know the music, you are in familiar territory. And I find that positive things can happen when you have not had endless hours to rehearse and to argue about specific points. Usually, everyone brings out the best in each other.''
 
Thoughts of Retirement
In recent years, Mrs. Bloom, who is 72 years old, has been thinking about retiring. She has appointed what she calls a board of overseers, separate from the board of directors. She and Mr. Bae are among the overseers; the others are Fred Sherry, the cellist and artistic director of the Chamber Music Society of Lincoln Center; David Shifrin, the clarinetist who is also the director of Chamber Music Northwest, and Mr. Thomas, the cellist in tonight's concert, and also the director of the Boston Chamber Music Society.
''I have turned some of the administration of the barge over to these four young musicians in order to ensure the future,'' Mrs. Bloom said. ''Very often when a founder leaves, an organization founders. But with these artists, who were chosen both for their artistic integrity and their entrepreneurial skills, I'm hoping to create a sense of continuity.''
But Mrs. Bloom still has projects in mind. Recently, she and Mr. Bae visited Seattle, where they are considering starting a West Coast version of Bargemusic. And to hear Mrs. Bloom tell it, the New York and Seattle barges would be the start of an armada.
''One problem is that there are not many cities that have the steady variety of artists that New York has,'' Mrs. Bloom said. ''But if we organized the cities on the East and West Coasts, and put barges on various bodies of water, then we could circulate the artists. I think this could work not only in music, but in all the art forms. There is a need for grandiose events. But there is also a need for intimate ones.''

THE OIL JOLT
 
The Administration has a ''short-term strategy'' for stabilizing oil markets by raising domestic production and encouraging conservation, Energy Secretary James D. Watkins said. It will not speed up a new national energy policy, he said. [Page A1.] 
 
Wholesale gasoline prices surged, reflecting concerns about low supplies and strong demand. Crude oil prices also rose, with the October contract up 85 cents a barrel, to $26.77. [D1.]
 
A substantial oil shortage will develop late this year, predicted officials at the International Energy Agency, which represents 21 oil-consuming nations. [D1.]
 
A Texas company, OGE Drilling, has cut off paychecks to families of six oil workers who are hostages of Iraq. [A18.]
 
THE ECONOMY
 
Even the healthiest segment of the savings industry has sharply declined in the last three months, the Government reported, providing troubling evidence that the crisis is deepening. [A1.]
 
Many officeholders who got contributions from Silverado are looking for a way to get rid of the money. [A17.]
 
Orders to factories for manufactured goods grew 1.6 percent in July, the Government reported. [D2.]
 
A capital gains tax cut ''could not be counted on'' to significantly increase American economic growth, the Congressional Budget Office said. [D2.]
 
MARKETS
 
Stocks fell in the slowest trading in three months as many investors lightened their holdings before the Labor Day weekend. The Dow slid 39.11, to 2,593.32. [D1.]
 
Prices of Treasury securities were mixed in light trading. An oil-price rise helped persuade bond traders to sell securities, but anticipation of Fed easing helped shorter-term issues. [D12.]
 
The dollar gained against leading currencies, even the yen, despite the Bank of Japan's decision to raise its discount rate. [D13.]
 
Grain and soybean futures prices dropped on continuing good growing weather in the Midwest. [D13.]
 
COMPANIES
 
Not even Morgan Stanley has been able to insulate itself from the vicissitudes of the securities business. [D1.]
 
Morgan Stanley has been selling a large number of ''junk bonds'' as part of a liquidation. [D1.]
 
Newmont Mining said it plans to sell 3.6 million new common shares in a public offering. [D1.]
 
A Federal appeals court overturned a $5.2 million libel award to the singer Wayne Newton from NBC. [A16.]
 
Harold C. Simmons said he had raised his Lockheed stake to 19.8 percent, as high as he can go without bumping into a company ''poison pill.'' [D3.]
 
INTERNATIONAL
 
Tokyo's financial markets reacted positively to the increase in the Bank of Japan's discount rate, but a number of economists warned that it was unlikely to be the last such jump in Japanese interest rates because of growing inflationary pressures. [D6.]

Weight Watchers will start a yearlong national TV campaign next week for its weight-loss programs, again using its persuasive selling tool - Lynn Redgrave. Kim Foltz: Advertising. [D5.]
 
The Mideast crisis drew a surprisingly muted reaction from mutual fund investors. Floyd Norris: Market Place. [D6.]

HEADLINE: Newmont to Offer Stock; Hanson Seeks to Cut Stake

BYLINE: By JONATHAN P. HICKS

 BODY:
In a move to take advantage of the current boom in the gold industry, the Newmont Mining Corporation, the large gold mining concern that is 49 percent owned by Hanson P.L.C. of Britain, said yesterday that it plans to sell 3.6 million new common shares in a public offering.
Newmont also said it intends to sell warrants to buy 1.8 million common shares. Analysts said the sales could bring more than $160 million. 
Meanwhile, Hanson said that its United States affiliate, Hanson Industries, intends to sell 8.4 million of its Newmont common shares and 4.2 million Newmont warrants. The sale could bring Hanson more than $600 million, if all the warrants are exercised, and could reduce Hanson's stake in Newmont to about 26 percent.
''Ultimately, Hanson will dispose of the remaining Newmont shares,'' said William G. Siedenburg, an analyst with Smith Barney, Harris Upham & Company. ''But it's not clear how they will dispose of them.''
 
Financing for Acqusitions
Newmont is based in Denver and is the largest gold producer in North America. On the New York Stock Exchange yesterday, its shares fell $3.375 each, to $44.50. The company has 67.6 million shares outstanding.
With uncertainties over the direction of the economy aggravated by the current crisis in the Middle East, some investors have turned to gold as an investment. At the same time, several mining companies have announced plans to finance additional capital expenditures or acquisitions of gold properties.
Two days ago, Amax Inc. said it planned to offer 9.5 million units in its Amax Gold Inc. subsidiary. Also, the American Barrick Resources Corporation, a Toronto-based mining concern, said two weeks ago that it planned to sell five million common shares in a public offering.
 
Shareholder Rights Plan
Newmont also announced yesterday that it had adopted a shareholder rights plan aimed at making it prohibitively expensive to make a hostile offer for the company. Jim Hill, a spokesman for Newmont, said that the company was not aware of any parties contemplating such an offer.
Newmont has shifted gears several times in its nearly 70-year history, changing its emphasis from copper to coal to precious metals.
Earlier this summer, it sold its 54.9 percent stake in the Peabody Coal Company to Hanson for $725.6 million. It recently sold a 19.9 percent stake in Newmont Australia to Poseidon Gold of Australia for $137 million and indicated that it wanted to sell its remaining stake. 1-Year-Old Stake Hanson acquired its stake in Newmont in August 1989 as part of its acquisition of Consolidated Gold Fields. Its sale of Newmont shares could help it recoup some of the costs of acquiring the Peabody Coal stake.
Sir Gordon White, chairman of Hanson Industries, said the offering ''will permit Newmont to raise additional capital and, at the same time, afford Consolidated Gold Fields the opportunity to monetize a portion of its investment while continuing to maintain a significant stake in Newmont.''
Hanson's holdings in Britain include Imperial Tobacco, Ever Ready batteries and London Brick. In the United States, Hanson's interests include the SCM Organic Chemicals Corporation, the Peabody Holding Company, Jacuzzi whirlpool bath products and Farberware cookware.

HEADLINE: Year-End Oil Shortage Predicted

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PARIS, Aug. 30

 BODY:
Officials at the International Energy Agency, which represents 21 oil-consuming nations, predict that a substantial oil shortage will develop late this year. At present, however, daily worldwide demand for oil is only slightly greater than supply, despite the embargo on Iraqi and Kuwaiti crude.
As the agency's governing board prepared for a meeting on Friday, an official said the worldwide shortage in September would be only 100,000 barrels a day, despite the loss of some 4.3 million barrels a day from Iraq and Kuwait.
As a result, a senior agency official predicted in an interview, the United States, West Germany, Japan and other members of the agency at Friday's meeting would probably postpone any decision to begin drawing oil from their government-controlled strategic reserves, saying only that they will act at the appropriate time. 
 
Known as Consumers' Club
Industrialized oil-consuming nations formed the agency in 1974 to help protect against oil shocks. The agency has worked to develop policies, like increasing strategic reserves and putting in place emergency conservation programs, to help cope with an oil crisis. It has also encouraged alternative energy forms like coal and nuclear power.
The agency - often called the oil consumers club - includes the United States, Canada, Japan, most Western European nations, Australia and New Zealand. The group, which acts by consensus and is charged with coordinating the industrialized world's response to an energy crisis, is highly regarded for the work it does in monitoring oil supply and demand.
Agency officials said a serious shortage had not yet developed because oil companies had drawn down their petroleum stocks, production had increased in some countries and demand had dropped modestly.
But a senior agency economist said that - despite Wednesday's decision by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries to allow members who can produce more oil to begin pumping immediately - the worldwide shortage could rise to 200,000 barrels a day in October and to more than 500,000 barrels a day in December as demand swells with cold weather and as oil companies and some countries feel they should stop drawing down their stocks.
 
Refusal to Tap Reserves
OPEC officials said their action would replace nearly all the oil lost from Iraq and Kuwait, but agency offcials expect the move to add only four million barrels a day.
''A shortage of 100,000 barrels a day is manageable, but uncomfortable, but a shortage of 500,000 barrels a day is unmanageable,'' said an agency official. ''If it gets up above that level, then the shoe really starts to pinch.'' Member governments might then decide to draw down their strategic reserves, agency officials say.
Many oil experts and economists have criticized the agency's members for their refusal so far to tap their emergency reserves. They say such a move would have gone far to hold down oil prices, forestalling damage to the world's economic growth.
But agency officials say that the United States and other leading agency members contend that the reserves should be drawn down only when there is a serious shortage. ''The philosophy, particularly among the major holders of emergency stocks, is to look at the situation in terms of physical volume, rather than influencing the market,'' said an agency economist.
 
'A Little More Hairy'
Peter Bogin, a Paris-based oil economist with Cambridge Energy Research Associates, agrees with the I.E.A. that a significant shortage will develop by late fall. ''In the November-December period things will start to get a little more hairy,'' he said. ''Even with the OPEC decision, you're still not going to get the full four million barrels a day, and the quality of the replacement oil is not as good as the missing Iraqi and Kuwaiti oil.''
Saudi Arabian officials said they had increased production to 7.5 million barrels a day, from 5.5 million, and would lift their output to 8 million by September; Venezuela plans to increase its output by some 500,000 barrels a day. But much of the Saudi oil is medium and heavy, and the Venezuelan oil is also heavy. This, oil experts say, will mean a shortage of light oil and its derivatives - kerosene, airplane fuel and gasoline - and possibly an excess of heavy products, like sludgy oil derivatives used for paving roads.
Agency officials calculate that all but 100,000 barrels of the 4.3 million-barrel-a-day September shortfall will be made up in the following way:
* Oil companies will draw down their stocks by about one million barrels a day, and OPEC nations will reduce their stocks on the seas by some 200,000 barrels.
* OPEC is expected to have increased daily production by 1.3 million barrels by late September, Mexico by 100,000 barrels a day and industrialized nations by 300,000.
* Non-OPEC non-industrialized countries like Malaysia and India are expected to draw down their reserves by some 600,000 barrels a day.
* Demand will be reduced by 150,000 barrels a day as the industrialized countries stop building their strategic reserves, by 200,000 barrels a day as power plans switch to substitute fuels from oil and by 300,000 barrels a day as a result of higher fuel prices.
* Government-encouraged conservation could pare demand by another 50,000 barrels a day.
Robert Mabro, director of the Oxford Institute for Energy Research, said the agency's scenario might be overly optimistic. He questioned whether oil companies would be willing to draw down their commercial stocks to the extent the agency foresees. He also said that even if they did, they would ultimately move to rebuild those stocks, which could increase daily demand, and thus aggravate the supply imbalance.

HEADLINE: Stocks Fall As Trading Dries Up

BYLINE: By ROBERT J. COLE

 BODY:
Stocks fell yesterday in the slowest trading in three months as many investors lightened their holdings before the Labor Day weekend.
Program traders were extremely active, knocking the market down sharply.
The Dow Jones industrial average slid 39.11 points, or 1.5 percent, to 2,593.32, while the broader Standard & Poor's index of 500 leading stocks fell 5.48, or 1.7 percent, to 318.71.
The smaller stocks, as measured by the Nasdaq composite index and the American Stock Exchange's market value index, fared a little better. 
The Nasdaq fell back 3.10, or eight-tenths of 1 percent, to 378.68, while the Amex slipped 1.32, or four-tenths of 1 percent, to 322.91. 120.89 Million Shares Traded Trading volume on the New York Stock Exchange slowed to 120.89 million shares from 134.24 million on Wednesday. Declining issues outnumbered advancing ones 998 to 482, and 66 issues reached new lows while only 3 hit new highs.
Among the new highs was Kansas Gas and Electric, which hit 25 7/8 before ending at 25 3/4, up 1/4. Kansas City Power and Light, which is offering $27 a share for Kansas Gas, said Wednesday that it might raise its bid.
One trader said yesterday's market drop was ''really not very meaningful.'' He said the market's moves next week, when a flurry of economic data are scheduled for release, could be ''much more important.''
''It's not because you're bearish or because war could break out,'' he said, referring to yesterday's selling. ''It's because you're coming into a three-day weekend and you certainly aren't going to hold positions until Tuesday.''
He maintained that many professional traders took this week off and that many others, in anticipation that investors would sell stocks today, decided to beat them to it.
 
Thin Market Expected
The trader predicted that because volume would probably be light today, stocks would decline. But he said the news today could have more of an impact on prices than on a normal trading day because the market would be so thin.
Birinyi Associates, which monitors program trading, said selling programs began barely 10 minutes after the opening bell yesterday, continued for more than a quarter of an hour and knocked close to 19 points off the Dow. A 12-minute series of selling programs starting at 3:25 P.M. knocked 15 more points off the Dow.
Throughout the day, Birinyi said, seven selling programs clipped a total of 72 points off the Dow.
Program trading is a technique that involves simultaneous trading in stock-index futures and the underlying stocks that make up the index to profit from price differences between the two markets.
With crude oil up 85 cents a barrel yesterday, to $26.77 a barrel, the Dow Jones average of 20 transportation stocks fell 9.87, or 1.08 percent, to 901.26. Among airline stocks, AMR fell 1, to 45 1/4 and UAL fell 2 1/4, to 93.
 
Blue-Chip Stocks Decline
Among the blue chips in the Dow industrial average, A.T.&T. fell a point, to 31 1/2; Chevron 1 1/2, to 75 1/4; General Motors 1 3/8, to 39 1/4; Coca-Cola 1 1/2, to 41 1/4; I.B.M. 2 1/8, to 101 3/4; Merck 2, to 80, and Texaco 1 1/8, to 62 1/8.
Among the 10 most-active stocks on the Big Board, Wal-Mart Stores fell 1 1/4, to 27 3/4; Philip Morris 1, to 44 1/2, and General Electric 3/4, to 61 7/8, all in heavy trading.
Home Shopping Network gained 3/4, to 5 3/4. The company, which reported sales last year of $774 million, said sales for the year ended today topped $1 billion.
Newmont Mining fell 3 3/8, to 44 1/2, after Consolidated Gold Fields, which owns 49 percent, said it would sell some of Newmont's stock. After the sale, Consolidated's stake in Newmont dropped to 26 percent, a sign that its interest in taking over Newmont had cooled. Newmont Gold, controlled by Newmont Mining, fell 2, to 46 1/2. Meanwhile, ASA, a leading gold stock, rose a point, to 49 5/8, as gold rose $1.20 an ounce, to $386.70.
 
Lower Earning Estimates
Melville, the shoe and apparel chain, fell 3, to 42 after William N. Smith, a retailing analyst for Smith Barney, Harris Upham, cut his earnings estimates.
Intel, the semiconductor producer, was among the most-active Nasdaq stocks, falling 1 7/8, to 33 1/8, as two million shares were traded. Gilbert P. Hyatt, a Southern California inventor, won a microprocessor patent that could bring him royalty payments.
Sierra Tucson, which operates alcohol and drug rehabilitation centers, plunged 4 3/4 in heavy trading, to 7 1/2, after forecasting lower profits.

HEADLINE: Wholesale Gasoline Price Soars

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER

 BODY:
Wholesale gasoline prices surged yesterday, reflecting traders' concerns about low supplies and strong demand. Crude oil prices also rose, with the contract for October delivery up 85 cents a barrel, or 3.3 percent, to $26.77 on the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Also unsettling the markets were remarks by Saddam Hussein, Iraq's President, yesterday and late Wednesday. Traders feared that war remained a possibility in the Middle East. 
Even small news items, like reports of large gasoline contract purchases by an unidentified Wall Street brokerage house at midday, produced large price swings in tense trading, said Michael Wilner, the president of Hilltop Trading Company Inc., an independent trading and brokerage firm on the floor of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
 
'Getting Battle Weary'
''The main thing that's happening in these energy markets is that people are incredibly, incredibly nervous, and the activity is absolutely frenetic,'' he said, adding, ''Judgments tend to be a little less than keen right now, because people are getting battle weary.''
Trading of gasoline, heating oil and other petroleum products was particularly heavy on the eve of the expiration of some futures contracts today. Traders who had committed to delivering oil scrambled to find cargos to meet these contracts. Large oil companies are holding most of the available stocks and are selling little, Mr. Wilner said.
The gasoline contract for September delivery rose 4.35 cents a gallon, or 5.1 percent, to close at 89.07 cents. The contract for September delivery of heating oil increased 3.9 cents, or 5.4 percent, to close at 75.54 cents a gallon.
Traders and analysts said there were widespread concerns about possible shortages of refinery capacity. Kuwait and Iraq, which exported fourmillion barrels of oil exports a day before a United Nations embargo was imposed, sent out 630,000 to 800,000 barrels of that as refined products.
Although the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries authorized all production necessary to stabilize energy markets on Wednesday, a move that could lead to an extra four million barrels a day, almost all of that will be shipped as crude for lack of extra refinery capacity, said Fareed Mohamedi, an economist at the Petroleum Finance Company in Washington.
''I think that'll result in stock drawdowns,'' he said.
Another factor is that Kuwaiti and Iraqi oilfields produce a lighter oil that, when refined, yields a higher proportion of gasoline than oil from most other countries.
Military activities in the Persian Gulf and reduced refinery production there could trim exports of such petroleum products as jet fuel, household kerosene and home heating oil by 500,000 barrels a day, Bernard J. Picchi, an oil industry analyst at Salomon Brothers in New York, said. A lack of refinery capacity will become ''particularly noticeable'' as winter approaches, with shortages possibly resulting, and gasoline and heating oil prices almost certainly rising, he said.
The Strategic Petroleum Reserve, a Government emergency stockpile, consists of crude oil, not petroleum products, Mr. Picchi added.
 
Down to 210 Million Barrels
But some experts contended yesterday that enough refinery capacity could be found. William D. Hermann, the chief economist of the Chevron Corporation, said that while refineries in the United States were already very busy, there was extra capacity elsewhere.
Some of the 120 older, mostly small refineries in the United States that have been closed in the last 10 years could probably be reopened if needed, he said.
The American Petroleum Institute estimated Tuesday that the nation's gasoline stocks were down to 210 million barrels, not far above the 200 million barrel minimum that oil companies try to have on hand for routine operations. A three-week moving average shows American demand for gasoline set a new record last week, and demand will probably be strong over the Labor Day weekend, said Robert S. Jonke, an energy futures trader at Cargill Investor Services.

HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
Thai Stock Boom Grinds to a Halt

BYLINE: By Steven Erlanger

DATELINE: BANGKOK, Thailand

 BODY:
BRASH and booming Thailand, which has been enjoying some of the world's highest growth rates in the last few years, has suffered something of a nervous breakdown this month, as the shock waves set off by President Saddam Hussein of Iraq have ricocheted around the world.
After surging 85 percent in a year, the Thai stock market fell farther faster than any other market in the world. In the 17 trading days between Aug. 1, the day before Iraq invaded Kuwait, and last Friday, the Securities Exchange of Thailand index lost 39.1 percent of its value, falling from 1,142.63 to 695.81, its lowest level since Oct. 31, 1989.
The move was almost enough to stop the pile drivers that are forming the foundations of the condominiums and office buildings that are supposed to service Bangkok's boom. The biggest concern during the market's tumble, economists say, was that the real estate sector might collapse, leading to a larger loss of confidence. The sector is already regarded as a little too hot and speculative for anyone's good. 
The Government became so worried that it decided to intervene. It established a $195 million mutual fund, to which all brokers had to contribute, to buy shares and provide liquidity to the market. If necessary, officials said, the sum could be doubled.
The fund, called Ruam Pattana II (''Joint Development II''), has a five-year maturity, and is modeled after Ruam Pattana I, set up after the stock market collapse of October 1987. The first fund was one-fifth the size, but seemed to help, prompting a rapid recovery of the market.
Fund II seemed to help this time, too. The S.E.T. index, as it is called, made gains of some 60 points a day early this week, and there was a general easing of concern. On Thursday the index closed at 839.80. Nevertheless, some economists contend that the Government intervened too quickly. ''I think the fund created an artificial bottom faster than necessary,'' said Chesada Loha-unchit, managing director of Tara Siam Business Information Ltd., a leading Thai research institute. ''The bottom was very near, but a lot of people with confidence in the market didn't want to buy before the bottom. I think the authorities were too nervous.''
Mr. Chesada, like most economists here, forecasts that the rise in oil prices will curb the growth of Thailand's economy, but says that Thailand is better positioned to adjust than it was during the last oil shock, in 1979. It is also probably better off than many of its neighbors, who depend more directly on exports to the United States, which seems to be veering into recession.
But no one knows if there will be a war, or what the price of oil is going to be next year. Narongchai Akrasanee, vice president of the Thailand Development Research Institute, the country's leading institute, said he expected that if oil prices average about $25 a barrel, Thailand's growth in 1991 will slow to 5 percent or 6 percent from an initial forecast of 9.3 percent, while inflation will reach 7.5 percent, instead of 6 percent.
Mark Greenwood, managing director of Asia Equity Thailand Ltd., said that with oil at $30 a barrel, inflation would be near 10 percent and growth would be cut to 6 percent. At $25 a barrel, he said both growth and inflation would be about 8 percent.
The main worry, he said, is inflation in real estate, ''which has a lot of froth.'' But compared to 1979, he said, when imported crude oil filled 80 percent of Thailand's energy needs, the figure today, after natural gas development, is 40 percent, representing only 9 percent of total imports.
Mr. Chesada agreed, saying that 1979 found a weak Government here with a large budget deficit and the baht tied to the dollar. Today, with a budget in the black and a floating baht, he said, Thailand has much more room to use fiscal policy. If there is a recession and a drop in exports, he said, the Government could put money into badly needed infrastructure projects - telephones and building mass transit to ease the paralyzing traffic, which happen to be most businessmen's biggest complaints.
Thailand also has a new Finance Minister, Virabongsa Ramangura, who when appointed Monday was in Vientiane, Laos, finishing a contract to advise the Laotian Government. In the face of rising oil prices, he said, he would concentrate on more austerity in Government spending and perhaps, finally, a tax overhaul. A nonpartisan technocrat and economic adviser to former Prime Minister Prem Tinsulanonda, Mr. Ramangura said he was known as an ''austerity economist.''
Almost more important, Mr. Virabongsa is regarded as a far less stubborn and difficult man than his flamboyant predecessor, Pramual Sabhavasu, a party regular in Chart Thai, the leading party in the ruling coalition. Mr. Pramual was named to a deputy prime minister's post. Mr. Pramual, known for his toughness, said he had not been told in advance of the change, and he left the ministry in tears. A lot of bankers were happy.

HEADLINE: Reduction In Gains Tax Is Questioned

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
A reduction in the Federal capital gains tax ''could not be counted on'' to increase American economic growth significantly, the Congressional Budget Office said today.
''It is unlikely that the increase in income would generate enough additional tax revenue'' to offset losses to the Treasury, said the report, which was requested by the Congressional committees that write the nation's tax laws and prepare the budget.
During his campaign for the Presidency in 1988, President Bush said cutting the capital gains tax rate was a top priority. But the Democratic-controlled Congress has refused to enact the proposal. 
 
Gains Tax Reduction Sought
The Administration has been seeking a reduction in the capital gains tax rate during this year's budget negotiations with lawmakers.
Representative Dan Rostenkowski, the Illinois Democrat who is the chairman of the House Ways and Means Committee, said the study was ''an objective assessment'' of previous reports on the capital gains tax cut the President proposed in his fiscal 1991 budget. Mr. Rostenkowski has opposed reducing the rate.
Capital gains are profits from selling stock, real estate and other investments. They are taxed at the same rates that apply to wages and other income. Legislation supported by the Administration and its Congressional backers last year would have excluded up to 30 percent of capital gains from taxes.
The new report questioned economic assumptions used by the Bush Administration, including a projection that such a cut would reduce the deficit by $12.5 billion over the years 1990 to 1995.
The Congressional Budget Office, a nonpartisan agency, said the Administration's studies ''made optimistic assumptions about how much cutting capital gains taxes would raise the real after-tax return received by savers and reduce the cost of capital faced by businesses.''
''Under plausible assumptions,'' it continued, ''cutting taxes on capital gains could even slow capital formation and slow growth if the deficit was increased by more than the increase in private savings.
''Taken together,'' the report said, ''the studies thus raise doubt about whether cutting taxes on capital gains can be counted on to raise saving and investment enough to significantly increase the gross national product.''
 
Different Projections
The Treasury Department has projected that the cut would raise tax collections every year, and by $12.5 billion in the initial five-year period. But Congress's Joint Committee on Taxation has forecast annual losses in revenues after the first two years and losses totaling $11.4 billion over the period.
In a study conducted in February, the committee also concluded that more than 83 percent of the direct benefits of the tax cut would go to people with annual incomes greater than $100,000 a year.
The report released today was based on an analysis of eight previous studies on the effects of a cut in the capital gains tax, including two by the Congressional Budget Office.

HEADLINE: Recovery Is Seen for Texas

BYLINE: Reuters

DATELINE: DALLAS, Aug. 30

 BODY:
Economists are starting to see proof that the Texas economy is recovering from the deep slump caused by the collapse of oil prices in 1985, a Federal Reserve Bank official said today.
''Things are indeed getting better,'' Harvey Rosenblum, senior vice president of the Federal Reserve Bank of Dallas, told a group of business leaders. 
Employment, banking statistics and credit figures show that over all, the state's economy is much healthier than it was late last year, although some areas, notably the Houston region and south Texas, remain depressed.
Non-agricultural employment has reached seven million workers, up from the 1986 trough of 6.2 million, Mr. Rosenblum said.
The Dallas area has fared well, with two jobs having been created for every job lost in the recession, he said.
After several years of credit shrinkage, Texas ''is on the verge'' of credit expansion, Mr. Rosenblum said, adding that ''lending will probably expand in 1990 for the first time in several years.'' But he added that troubled banks still outnumbered healthy ones in Texas.

HEADLINE: Factory Orders Up 1.6% in July

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 30

 BODY:
Orders to factories for manufactured goods grew 1.6 percent in July, the Government reported today. But analysts said the fluctuating performance so far this year suggested an anemic industrial economy even before the Persian Gulf crisis.
''It confirms that before the situation in the Middle East, things were sluggish but growing,'' said Thomas Runiewicz, an economist with the WEFA Group in Bala-Cynwyd, Pa. But now, with the Middle East problem and energy prices higher, he said he would expect to see factory orders down in August and ''especially this fall.'' 
The Commerce Department said orders for both durable and nondurable goods rose $3.8 billion, to a seasonally adjusted $240.3 billion, after declining 1.9 percent in June. The gain was attributable mostly to a jump in orders for durable goods, particularly aircraft.
 
Up Three, Down Four
So far this year, factory orders have been up in three months and down in four, including a 5.5 percent drop in January, the sharpest decline since a 7 percent plunge in the recession month of December 1974.
''Basically, orders have been flat because of the month-to-month volatility,'' said Sung Won Sohn, chief economist at the Norwest Corporation in Minneapolis. ''It's consistent with a slowing, weakening economy, which could get into a recession shortly.''
Today's report said orders for durable goods - items expected to last more than three years - rose 2.8 percent, to $128.2 billion, after falling 3 percent a month earlier. The increase was a slight revision from the 2.9 percent advance reported last week.
Orders for nondurable goods rose two-tenths of 1 percent, to $112.1 billion, after falling four-tenths of 1 percent a month earlier.
Among the durable categories, transportation orders were up 4.9 percent, to $34.9 billion, after a 6.3 percent decline the previous month. Much of the increase was in aircraft orders. Excluding the transportation category, orders would have risen 1.1 percent.
In the important category of nonmilitary capital goods, a barometer of business investment plans, orders gained 10 percent, to $39.8 billion. But most of the increase also was in aircraft, and aircraft orders are expected to slow.
Military orders fell 3 percent, to $7.6 billion after a 21.8 percent decline in June. Excluding military, factory orders were up 1.8 percent.
Inventories increased seven-tenths of 1 percent, to $373.3 billion, the largest one-month increase since a rise of eight-tenths of 1 percent last January.
''At first glance, this would appear quite disturbing,'' said Evalina Tainer, an economist with the First National Bank of Chicago, since a pileup of unsold goods could bring about a drop in production and job layoffs.
But much of the stockpile was automobiles in anticipation of a possible strike this fall, she explained. The United Automobile Workers contract with the Big Three auto makers expires Sept. 14.
Unfilled orders rose six-tenths of 1 percent, to $520.4 billion, while shipments declined eight-tenths of 1 percent, to $237.5 billion.
In addition to transportation, new orders rose in other durable-goods categories including primary metals, up 3.5 percent, to $11.9 billion; non-electrical machinery, up 2.3 percent, to $22.3 billion, and electrical machinery, up 1.5 percent, to $20.3 billion.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
Fund Investors Remain Steadfast

BYLINE: By Floyd Norris

 BODY:
THE Iraqi invasion of Kuwait, and the subsequent plunge in stock market prices, drew a surprisingly muted reaction from mutual fund investors, several mutual fund companies said yesterday. Some managers said investors even stepped up their purchases of stock funds.
''Surprisingly enough, we had a pretty good month here, probably the best month since April,'' said Henry Schmelzer of New England Securities.
While other fund organizations, particularly ones that sell directly to consumers, reported that cash was taken out of the funds on a net basis, they noted that new sales of stock funds remained strong, as many investors sought to profit by buying funds at depressed prices. 
''Over all, the cash outflow in August was just a little larger than July's net inflow,'' said Jane Nelson, a spokeswoman for T. Rowe Price. At the Vanguard Group, Brian Mattes, a spokesman, referred to ''an incredibly small outflow'' from stock funds. ''The reaction has been very muted, given the extreme stress the market has been under,'' he added. Stock prices fell sharply for much of August, although they recovered to some extent early this week.
Another sign of investor confidence that the turndown in stock prices is temporary came in a survey of Fidelity Investments customers. Michael Hines said it showed that 21 percent of stock fund owners planned to buy in the next three months, up from 16 percent in a similar survey a month earlier. The percentage who said they planned to sell fell to zero from 4 percent in July, he said. ''It shows that the people who wanted to sell have done so,'' he added.
The statements came as the Investment Company Institute, a trade group, released figures for July, showing that only $131 million flowed into equity funds that month, the poorest showing since January, when $242 million came out of such funds.
In July the Dow peaked at just below 3,000 and began to slide, and investors responded by withdrawing $991 million from funds that invest primarily in American stocks. But that outflow was offset by a record inflow of $934 million into international funds, which invest overseas, and a flow of $188 million into global funds, which can invest either overseas or in American markets.
Foreign markets have also suffered heavily this month, and some fund officials said money had flowed out of international stock funds in August.
The cash flow figures include new sales and redemptions of fund shares, as well as exchanges between different funds. They do not include reinvested dividends.
The big movements out of stock funds, in both July and August, came from the minority of investors who seek to time markets by moving from one fund to another within the same group of funds. In July, those investors moved a net $1.2 billion out of funds, and officials of fund groups that cater to such investors said the trend continued this month.
''There were heavy net exchanges out of equity funds,'' Mr. Hines of Fidelity Investments said. But he added that for the whole month, the exchanges out of stock funds might be less than in July.
In general, the heaviest withdrawals from stock funds this month came from funds that are marketed directly to consumers, like Fidelity and T. Rowe Price's, rather than from funds sold through investment advisers, like New England Securities, which is represented by both stockbrokers and insurance agents.
''We had net positive sales in both equity and fixed-income funds,'' said Jack Thomas, a senior vice president of IDS Financial Services, whose funds are sold by financial planners.
''We're not seeing people aggressively selling their funds,'' reported Robert Leo, the director of mutual funds at Shearson Lehman Brothers. ''I think a lot of people are aware this thing can turn very quickly.''
Many fund managers reported that investors moved cash out of long-term fixed income funds as interest rates rose during August. They also said that renewed talk of recession had caused investors to withdraw cash from ''junk bond'' funds. After heavy cash withdrawals from those funds earlier in the year, they had net cash inflows in May, June and July. Indeed, the July figure was $136 million.
Sales of municipal bond funds were reported to have continued strong in August, after seeing net inflows of $1.5 billion in July.
The professionals who manage mutual funds poured cash into the stock market in May and June, but grew a bit more cautious in July, lowering the cash proportion of total stock fund assets to 10.7 percent from a revised June figure of 10.8 percent. In April, the figure hit a record 12.5 percent.
Investors' reluctance to dump stock funds when prices fell stands in sharp contrast to the reaction in 1987, when there was intense selling after the crash and the flow of new purchases dried up. ''Everybody was concerned we'd run into one of those crunches like 1987,'' said Mr. Schmelzer of New England, ''but it was pretty much business as usual.''
