PUB-DATE: August 31, 1990

SECTION: Section D; Page 6, Column 3; Financial Desk

LENGTH: 704 words

HEADLINE: Japanese Markets Rally After Central Bank Raises Rate

BYLINE: By JAMES STERNGOLD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, Friday, Aug. 31

 BODY:
The financial markets here reacted positively to Thursday's increase in the Bank of Japan's discount rate, but a number of economists warned that it was unlikely to be the last such jump in Japanese interest rates because of growing inflationary pressures.
In trading today on the Tokyo stock exchange, share prices rose strongly and the Nikkei index was up more than 500 points at one point. But during the last half-hour of trading the market dropped back somewhat, and the Nikkei ended with a gain of 308.41, or 1.2 percent, to 25,978.37. Reuters reported that, late in the day, the Bank of Japan had recalled 300 billion yen in loans to banks. 
The stock market rose and bond prices rallied on Thursday after the early-morning announcement that the central bank had increased the discount rate to 6 percent from 5.25 percent, the fifth increase since May 1989. On Wednesday, the markets were swept by rumors that the rate rise was coming, so traders were prepared when the announcement came.
The bellwether rate is now more than double its level a year ago, but it has lagged behind even sharper increases in market-determined interest rates.
Though the move was anticipated, there was some criticism and concern on Thursday. Kabun Moto, the Minister of International Trade and Industry, said inflationary pressures had not yet even begun to force prices higher and thus the move was premature. He added that retail oil prices were unlikely to rise until later this year and that the real burden of higher rates will fall hardest on small and medium-sized businesses.
 
Concern Over U.S. Reaction
Others worried that the move might put upward pressure on American interest rates as the American economy is weakening and vulnerable. In fact, there were reports that the central bank had purposely waited until the Government announced its package of measures to support the multinational force in Saudi Arabia trying to enforce the embargo on trade with Iraq.
The Nihon Keizai Shimbun, a leading economic newspaper, said the central bank had been seeking approval from the Finance Ministry for several weeks to initiate the rate rise. It had delayed, the report said, out of concern that an early move might stir criticism in the United States that Japan was putting its own interests ahead of the common front needed to confront Iraq.
Prime Minister Toshiki Kaifu announced Japan's aid package late Wednesday evening, less than 12 hours before the Bank of Japan said it was increasing the discount rate.
Eishiro Saito, the chairman of the Federation of Economic Organizations, which represents Japan's biggest companies, noted that the Japanese economy was generally healthy and thus could withstand higher rates. But he added, ''The problem is how the higher rate will affect the U.S. economy and foreign exchange rates.''
 
A Pre-emptive Move
Yasushi Mieno, the governor of the Bank of Japan, described the move at a morning press conference as pre-emptive, saying it was an attempt to halt inflationary pressures even before prices began to rise sharply; consumer prices are expected to rise by less than 3 percent this year.
But Mr. Mieno indicated that the central bank would have to remain ''flexible'' and consider further rate increases if the Persian Gulf crisis pushes oil prices even higher. Japan imports nearly all of its energy supply.
''The central bank is catching up with the the market, but they're not ahead of it,'' said Robert Alan Feldman, an economist with Salomon Brothers Asia. ''At this point, three-quarters of a point is not enough, and you could well see another move later.''
On Thursday, the stock market fidgeted immediately after the announcement, then moved much higher in the afternoon. The Nikkei index jumped 775.17 points, to 25,669.96. An estimated 530 million shares were traded, a moderately active day. The rise reversed most of Wednesday's 816-point decline.
Meanwhile, the 10-year Government bond yield slid to 8.115 percent from 8.3 percent Wednesday, a relatively large decline.
And despite the rise in the discount rate, the yen fell against the dollar on Thursday. Because the move had been anticipated, it had little impact on currency markets.

The New York Times

PUB-DATE: August 31, 1990

SECTION: Section D; Page 12, Column 4; Financial Desk

LENGTH: 763 words

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Short-Term Treasuries Advance

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities were mixed in light trading yesterday. An 85-cent rise in oil prices, to $26.77 a barrel, helped persuade bond traders to sell securities, but anticipation that the Federal Reserve Board might be on the verge of easing monetary policy helped push up prices of shorter-term notes and bills.
''The Government market is gearing up for an easing in Fed policy,'' one trader said. ''The market is expecting the Fed to move, and positions seem to reflect it.'' 
Weakness in the economy, coupled with relative stability in oil prices over the past few days, has raised hopes that the Fed might move soon.
Those who were betting the central bank would move very quickly were disappointed yesterday; the Fed's open market operations gave no indication that policy had been altered.
Nevertheless, a number of analysts said that a lowering of the overnight rate for bank loans in the Federal funds market, the short-term rate most closely controlled by the Fed, was imminent.
 
Awaiting 2 Reports
''We look for the Fed to ease policy sometime next week,'' analysts at MMS International, an economic information and forecasting service, said in their daily market commentary. ''The best window of opportunity would be after the purchasing managers' report and before the employment data.''
The August survey of the nation's purchasing managers is expected to be released on Tuesday. A report on employment conditions during August is expected from the Labor Department on Sept. 7.
''If oil prices stabilize at around current levels, I think that would provide an opportunity for the Fed to move,'' said Rudolf Thunberg, an economist at Ried Thunberg & Company, an investment advisory firm in Westport, Conn.
Dealers said that activity was fairly brisk early in the session, but trading slowed as the session progressed and market participants began to look toward the Labor Day weekend. In observance of the holiday, trading will stop at 2 P.M. today. The credit markets will be closed on Monday.
By late in the day, the Treasury's 8 3/4 percent 30-year bonds of 2020, were offered at a price of 97 3/4, down 17/32, to yield 8.96 percent, up from a yield of 8.91 percent late Wednesday.
Yesterday's price move was one of the smallest for the issue in several weeks. Movements in oil prices and developments in the Persian Gulf since Iraq's invasion of Kuwait on Aug. 2 have recently made the most speculative Treasury issue even more volatile.
Among note issues, the 8 3/4 percent 10-year notes were offered at 99 13/ 32, down 9/32, to yield 8.84 percent.
The two issues that were auctioned earlier this week continued to trade at close to their auction prices.
Late yesterday the 8 1/8 percent two-year notes sold on Tuesday were offered on a when-issued basis at a price to yield 8.09 percent. And the 8 1/2 percent five-year notes were offered at a price to yield 8.51 percent in when-issued trading.
Short-term Treasury bill rates fell slightly, reflecting anticipation of an easing move by the Fed.
In late trading, three-month bills were offered at a discount rate of 7.42 percent, down 3 basis points, or hundreths of a percentage point. Six-month bill rates fell by 4 basis points, to a late offered rate of 7.38 percent. And one-year bill rates fell by a similar amount, to a late rate of 7.25 percent.
Dealers said that the increase in Japan's discount rate to 6 percent,from 5.25 percent, during overnight trading in Tokyo had been anticipated and was not a factor in the fixed-income markets yesterday.
Unlike a growing number of his colleagues, Michael J. Moran, the chief economist at Daiwa Securities America Inc., does not expect the Fed to move quickly to lower interest rates.
''I think the Fed will wait and look at the employment report, and if that provides any new evidence that the economy is even weaker than previously, then they will ease,'' Mr. Moran said. ''I think the Fed won't move until it has a clear case to adjust interest rates.''
 
Corporate and Municipal Issues
Activity in the secondary markets for investment-grade corporate and tax-exempt municipal bonds was light, dealers said.
Prices of most corporate bonds dipped by 1/4 of a point to 1/2 of a point, market participants said. Meanwhile, prices of actively quoted municipal securities fell a by a range of 1/8 of a point to 1/4 of a point.
In other developments, the Federal Reserve reported that all three measures of the nation's money supply rose in the week ended Aug. 20. The report had no impact on prices in the credit markets.
